# ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS

![](images/23c5106395f876346750cb0c7d28564f86f9b9819c2b6d034fbdbb8546a0df87.jpg)

HIS book began almost accidentally in 1978 when I devoted a sunmer at the Library of Congress to poring over nineteenth-century English printers' trade magazines, trying to discover what they had to say about bookdesign. My quest was largely fruitless, because Victorian printers seemed inarticulate on that subject, but as I turned the pages and reflected

upon the typography of the last century, I frequently stumbled upon the name of William Morris. At frst I resisted the lure of Morris, with his enormous reputation among bibliophiles, since it appeared selfevident that there could be nothing new to say about the Kelmscott Pres, r I s wae that it ad been  y  y English-speaking printing historian of this century. Eventually, however, I realized that there was no avoiding Morris  who seems to many of us the most significant figure in modern book typography and I then discovered, to my great pleasure, that much fresh evidence about the Kelnscott Press, unavailable to earlier scholars, was now to be found in public and private collections. Only gradually did I grasp the larger cultural significance of Morris's 'little typographical adventure' and understand that the neaning of this episode extended far beyond the boundaries of printing history.

That moment of illumination came to me a decade ago, and since then I have had the pleasure of examining the handsone books of the Kelscot Press in many British and American libraries, of stuying a od del manut aeial latn to thehistry  the res, and of receiving innumerable courtesies from libraries and collectors. I am keenly aware of the inadequacy of merely supplying simple, undifferentiated lists of institutions and persons who have given me assistance along the way, yet I could not record here all the nuances of generosity and friendship without writing a memoir of that decade. I feel confident, however, that many of the persons mentioned will recognize the warm memories that lie behind this litany of names.

Without several fellowships and travel grants, it would have been impossible for me to complete my research. They were supplied by the American Philosophical Society, the Division of Arts and Humanities of the University of Maryland, the John Sinon Guggenheim Memorial Foundation, and the National Endowment for the Humanities.

Those who have given me permission to quote unpublished documents and to reproduce illustrations are cited elsewhere in this book, but I must register my special indebtedness to the Society of Antiquaries (London), the owner of the William Morris copyright.

John Dreyfus, Joseph R. Dunlap, and Julia Markus read drafts of the book and offered stimulating criticisms, from which I hope I benefited.

My wife Eileen, my daughter Heather, and my son Glenn have cheerfully tolerated my prolonged preoccupation with the Kelmscott Press and have given me much love and support. At an early stage in thrc Heateohelpei traiwnbooks at teLby of Congress.

# THE KELMSCOTT PRESS

![](images/ebaca30dab68e2eea12f01b667c96e604dd85648b12d9846f7d23b544f6bbd67.jpg)  
Fie  Many the Kclmcott Press books hadengrav title-pages, such as this onc in News from Nowhere (1893), which appeared opposite thc first page of text. Thc house (drawn by C. M. Gere) is Kclmscott Manor, after which Morris's Press was namcd.

![](images/9648ce2fda13826c300d47abd6f45b9b22d5105bc510eb8f61d3d7810ab70916.jpg)

BEGA N printing books with the hope of producing some which would have a definite claim to beauty, while at the same time they should be easy to read and should not dazzle the eye, or trouble the intellect of the reader by eccentricity of form in the letters.'

With this brisk introduction William Morris began writing on 11 November 1895

a brief explanation for an American admirer of how and why he had established the most famous of all private presses, the Kelmscott Press. The essay first appeared in Modern Art, a Boston magazine, in 1896 and was later reprinted, under the title A Note by William Morris on his Aims in Founding the Kelmscott Press, as the last publication of the Press in the spring of 1898. Morris's formulation of his purposes was characteristically terse: 'it was the essence of my undertaking', he wrote, 'to produce books which it would be a pleasure to look upon as pieces of printing and arrangement of type.' The rest of the essay was devoted to a discussion of paper, type-design, spaces between words and lines, margins, and ornaments.

Morris's Note is an undeniably important document. As one of his few public statements about the Kelmscott Press, it has been quoted frequently since 1898 in histories of the Press; indeed, in 1924 Bernard H. Newdigate went so far as to suggest that the Note was one of the three books that every student of English book-production ought to read.' Yet one might argue that the essay is cryptic, even evasive, leaving a host of questions unanswered. How did Morris develop an interest in book-design and production? Why did he turn to fifteenthcentury German and Italian books for his models? What was the relationship between the Kelmscott Press books and the best work of other nineteenth-century English printers? How exactly did Morris rediscover some of the fundamental principles of fine printing? How did he find the craftsmen to carry out those principles for him?

We need the answers to these and other questions, for there is nowadays general agreement — even among those who dislike his style of typography - that Morris is one of the most important printers of the modern era and that the establishment of the Kelmscott Press was an event of major cultural significance. Throughout northern Europe and the English-speaking world, particularly in the

United States, the impact of Morris's typographical theories and of the Kelmscott books theselve was pervav. Histoians are stil ung about the natureof that influence (whether, in particular, it was always a good or enlightening influence), but no one disputes that the forces set in motion at the Kelmscott Press eventually spread round the globe.

Fron a merely quantitative point of view, the achievement of the Kelnscott Press does not seem especially impressive. Between 1891 and 1898 it produced fifty-two books and a set of specimen pages of another book that was never conpleted. The books were designed by Mor a ing about 3o0 copies. All were ornamented with initials and borders by Morris, and some were illustrated, usually by Edward Burne-Jones. So why are these few Kelmscott books famous everywhere today, and why have they profoundly affected twentieth-century theories of book-design?

One explanation of their potent appeal lies in the fact that Morris's ideas, like his ornamented books, are richly suggestive. When he appears to be speaking about typographical matters, he is in reality examining how we ought to live.'If I were asked to say what is at once the most important production of Art and the thing most to be longed for, I should answer, A beautiful House; and if I were further asked to name the production next in importance and the thing next to be longed for, I should answer, A beautiful Book,' he wrote in an unfinished essay. 'To enjoy good houses and good books in self-respect and decent comfort, seems to me to be the pleasurable end towards which all societies of human beings ought now to struggle." That is a most characteristic passage by Morris: ot content with treating typography as 'a minor art' (to quote Stanley Morison's phrase),3 Morris insists upon enlarging the context and seeing the beautiful book as an essential part of his attempt to redeem the late-Victorian world from dreary mechanical ugliness. It is surely this passion for moral, atic, an scial or whch lns rec o Mos yographical pronouncements and which makes the history of the Kelmscott Press a matter of some importance even to non-bibliophiles.

Morris, like many of his contemporaries, had grave misgivings about a world that was incresingly being shaped bydutrialis. H looked about him at the Victorian city  smoky, polluted, crowded, and gloony — and asked himself whether the human race was really in a better state now than it had been in the Middle Ages. In an essay published in 1890 Morris took his readers on a tour of an unidentified but typical English industrial town (apparently Peterborough) and reveal streeby sre uilding bybuding te heeorr urban landscape. With great eloquence Morris described the dreary terraced houses, the small, shabby shops, the 'ugly modern iron brie the anewok nhe  he T s y  a found about him; the past which he then immediately conjured up was amedival worlsiplicty, purity,ancleanness. Morris Hamlet-like injunction to 'Look here, upon this picture, and on this' reminds one of that famous pair of plates which had appeared in A. W. N. Pugin's Contrasts (second edition, 1841): 'Catholic town in 14and 'The Same Town in 1840' In the lower plate we see a srene city of spires pointing towards heaven; in the other plate the multitude of lovely churches has been supplanted by factories, belching smokestacks, and a particularly grim Victorian workhouse.

Pugin's pictures, in turn, evoke Carlyle's Past and Present (1843), the very title of which vibrates with the fervour of Victorian moral idealism, and its implied contrast, like Pugin's, between a lost Golden Age and the bleak world of nineteenth-century England may help us to grasp the passionate intensity of Morris's rejection of the present. We may perhaps wish to protest that Pugin, Carlyle, Ruskin, Morris, and others of their outlook idealized the Middle Ages and ignored the material benefits of our mechanized civilization; yet we fail to do them justice unless we recognize that the Gothic Revival was not ultimately an attempt to resuscitate a period style of architecture or art but rather a morally powerful protest against the psychological and social disintegration produced by the modern world.

The Kelmscott Press was, in effect, the final phase of the Gothic Revival. Burne-Jones compared the Kelmscott Chaucer (1896) to a cathedral; by the same analogy, the smaller books can be regarded as parish churches, each faithfully rendered in quasi-nedieval style. But such a sweeping rejection of contemporary life brought with it apparently insoluble problens. How, for example, could one reproduce the general appearance of a fifteenth-century book and still escape the dangers of miere imitation? By turning one's back on contemporary printing machinery, how could one inprove the appearance of the ordinary book? In reverting to methods of hand-production, how could one print books that readers with modest incomes might afford?

These doubts about the Kelmscott Books — which are so redolent ofVictorian revivalism and historicism — were in fact being expressed

Pugin's Contrasts (1841) is a pictorial rendcring of that nostalgia for a medieval Goldcn Age which pervades thc writings of many Victorians, particularly Carlyle, Ruskin, and Morris. The mood of Pugin's drawings is connected with Morris's 'typographical advcnturc', for thc Kelmscott Prcss represcnts the final phasc of thc nincteenth-ccntury Gothic Revival.

already during Morris's lifetime. Why, it was asked repeatedly in the press, did Morris, a committed socialist, not print books for the poor? Why did he insist upon producing anachronistic books whose glamour derived primarily from their resemblance to medieval volumes? Morris was conscious of such accusations, as we shall see, and he offered persuasive answers to some of them. The dilemmas he faced at the Kelmscott Press are of more than technical interest today, because they illustrate both the rewards and dangers of a reversion to handicraft methods. If, as Morris argued, our complex technological civilization bears within it the seeds of its own destruction, then the story of the Kelmscott Press — possibly the best-known example in the Western world of a serious, thoughtful attempt to revive a traditional craft — becomes a fascinating parable of the extent to which we may be able deliberately to turn the clock backwards.

One suspects that Morris has been written about more extensively than any other printer except Gutenberg. Even before the Kelmscott Press was officially opened, enthusiastic reports about Morris's 'typo-British and American periodicals. By 1893, in distant Chicago, the Inland Printer was prepared to proclaim that 'a new era has dawned in English printing'® Reporters flocked to Kelmscott House, Morris's home in Hammersmith, for interviews with 'the Master Printer'. The Kelmscott edition of Chaucer was widely described as the most beautiful printed book since the fifteenth century. In the last volume produced at the Press, Sydney Cockerell supplied 'A Short History and Description of the Kelmscott Press' and 'An Annotated List of All the Books Printed at the Kelmscott Press in the Order in Which They Were Issued'. In 1924 H. Halliday Sparling, who had been Morris's son-in-law and, like Cockerell, a Secretary of the Press, published a book-length history entitled The Kelmscott Press and William Morris Master-Craftsman. Since then many other more specialized studies of the Press have appeared in print.'

How, then, can one justify still another book on the Kelmscott Press? The short answer is that there is really only one extended history, Sparling's, which is by now very much out of date; since 1924 a great quantity of manuscript material relating to the Press's history - including letters, diaries, manuscripts, and corrected proofs of Kelmscott books - has become available to scholars. The dispersal of Sir Sydney Cockerell's collection in 1956 and the acquisition by the Pierpont Morgan Library of the Kelmscott materials of John M.

## Introduction

Crawford, Jr., are only two notable instances of the emergence in recent decades of important new docunentary evidence. With these fresh records in hand, and with the calner perspective that the passing of time usually brings, it ought (one hopes) to be possible now to write a history of the Kelmscott Press that avoids both the embarrassing proselytizing tone of Sparling's book and the biliousness of certain anti-Morris polemics published in the past.

The Kelmscott Press, despite — or perhaps because of — its fame, is a subject encrusted with myths and misconceptions. It is surely worth the effort to try to set the record straight, if only because Morris's curious typographical experinent may force us to reflect on which threatens today to envelop the world.

# ONE \* THE WORLD OF VICTORIAN PRINTING

![](images/bc17929ddac34a972bb9409b0cdedd46bea796bc41a1a9de45d04588fbaea945.jpg)

HE history of the printed book may seem, at first glance, to be a perfect confirmation of Morris's view that the older crafts had steadily deteriorated in quality since the Middle Ages. 'Printing', as Richard Durnford, Bishop of Chichester, put it trenchantly, is an art which beginning perfect, ever afterwards declined."

Ana wno, comparing tne typical massproduced volume of the nineteenth century - nastily printed in greyish ink on shoddy, acidic paper - with the Mainz Psalter or Gutenberg's Bible, could doubt that Morris and the Bishop were right?

Yet the story of the decay of printing is a good deal more complex than Morris ever acknowledged. For him, it was all quite simple: the earliest printed books were still the best because they were produced at a time when the spirit of medieval craftsmanship was strong, but, with the advent of false historicism and a weakness for pseudo-classical ornament in the sixteenth century, printing, like architecture, began to decay. Only in the second half of the eighteenth century, according to Morris, were the consequences of this typographical dry rot fully revealed in the 'sweltering hideousness of the Bodoni letter, the most illegible type that was ever cut'. Bodoni and Didot, even more relentlessly than Baskerville (whose achievements Morris certainly underestimated), carried the development of the 'modern' typeface to its logical conclusion. 'It was reserved for the founders of the later eighteenth century', wrote Morris, 'to produce letters which are positively ugly, and which, it may be added, are dazzling and unpleasant to the eye owing to the clumsy thickening and vulgar thinning of the lines.  The nineteenth century's further contributions to this melancholy tale were increased mechanization and cheaper paper, ink, and binding materials. When he reflected upon the contemporary printing scene, Morris found a small source of optimism only in the growing popularity of Caslon's old-face type and its imitations.

Because of the wide influence of Morris's typographical writings, this sweeping indictment of nineteenth-century printing became accepted dogma for several decades following his death in 1896. There came in time the inevitable reaction against his oversimplifications and his doctrinaire hatred of the modern world. Writing in an American typographicaljournal, the Dolphin, in 1938, Peter Beilenson remarked with obvious irony that 'there had developed the general opinion that between the early great classical period of Didot, Bodoni, and Bulmer, and the late arts-and-crafts period of Morris, the printing of the century was one continuous sacrifice on the altars of Machinery and Mainmon'. In fact, he argued, many nineteenth-century printers had been preoccupied with the problem of quality, and 'technically bookmaking averaged at least the level of preceding centuries (except that papermaking and binding sought and found lower commercial levels) and rticallythenturyffre reter vartygthings, which on the whole unfortunately suit our present taste less than those of any preceding century' Recently a less cautious revisionist, Ruari McLean, has insisted that 'more exciting things happened in book design between 1837 and 1890 than in any other comparable period in the history of the world's printing; and most of them happened in London' (In fairness to McLean, it must be added that he is referrig to the most sumptuous Victorian books; he elsewhere acknowledges that the ordinary commercially produced book of the last century was a very dismal thing indeed.)

Not surprisingly, nineteenth-century printers themselves did not share Morris's low estimate of their achievements. The trade journals of the time were filled with hymns of self-praise. 'What wondrous strides have been made in almost every department of the Art!' the Printers' Register (6 July 1881) declared. 'Hand-presses have become almost exterminated, pushed out by power-driven machines; typefaces . . . may now be counted by hundreds in their varieties; the uniformity of black ink, relieved on rare occasions by red initials, now gives way to mosaics ofevery imaginable colour, combined with gold, silver, and many-shaded bronzes; while borders, ornaments, picturetypes, engravings, and lithographs are made use of to an extent never before conceived.' The naive enthusiasm for machinery — and the implication that technological advances always produce aesthetic triumphs  in this representative pronouncement may make us reluctant to attend very seriously to the testimony of Morris's contemporaries in the printing trade; but at least it should be observed that they rejected his assessment of their craftsmanship, for Victorian bookwork was as fine as that of any preceding era.

New technology  which Morris was to reject so dramatically at the Kelmscott Press  had completely changed the character of the printing trade in his day. Before the third Earl Stanhope perfected an iron press in bout 8oo, all printing fro Gutenberg'daywar — had been done on a wooden press which was so simple in structure etilpiriaaions tere i o bcllp thery  the c d they show he me unsohisicated mehanim that was sil b us nearly three hundred years later. If Morris had really beenmotiv ated solely by the antiquarian impulses that sometimes are attributed to him, he presumably would have reverted to the wooden press; there were still a few of them around in late-Victorian England, and they were occasionally seen in operation on special occasions such as the Caxton Exhibition in 1877. But Morris recognized that the older presses were actually inferior to the iron hand-press of the nineteenth century. 'One of the great drawbacks to wood-block printing in those tm he y s the h . Their only resource was to print with the paper very wet, and with books . .". have suffered much from this cause."

Lord Stanhope's press, despite some enhancements, was in fact only a slightly improved version of the medieval press. More dramatic technological developments appeared in the printing trade within two decades. Friedrich Koenig invented a steam-driven cylinder press which was first used to print The Times on 29 November 18 14, and for the rest of the century the presses that were employed by newspapers and mass-circulation magazines grew ever more elaborate and imposing. By the 184os the machine-minders who tended these clanking monsters had come to resemble flies crawling over the skin of an enormous elephant. In the latter half of the century the handpress (always iron by now) was used much less frequently even in book-printing; when Morris founded the Kelmscott Press in 1891 such presses were still readily available but were regarded as useful mainly for the printing of posters and the taking of proofs.

The growing mechanization of printing led to a reduction in the cost of books and periodicals. Some observers at the time were overjoyed by this means of spreading literacy. Charles Knight (a printer and publisher as well as a writer), in The Old Printer and the Modern Press (1854), argued fervently that the new technological developments were a heaven-sent gift that would lead to the wider distribution of books through book clubs and public libraries. The Society for the Diffusion of Useful Knowledge, of which Knight was an active leader, was symptomatic of the energetic, cheerful Victorian response to the everfaster spinning cylinders of the steam press. It is also signifcant that a number of the edifying examples cited in Charles Babbage's On the Economy o MachineryanManuactures (fourt edition, 183 w drawn from the world of printing.

![](images/f78de8f266fb419d92eb05f10da16af8fdf4150aeb8b22682a5353d8b16c502a.jpg)  
Figure 3 By thc middle of the last century, this sort of massiv, clanking press was commonly used for the printing of newspapers and magazines; even books were produced on soimewhat smaller steam-driven cylinder presses. Morris's adoption of the hand-press was a direct reaction to such technological devclopments.

Of course there were other voices, sometimes from within the trade, warning that the growing acceleration of the printing process was leading oa debasmenttraditional standard i typoraphy mals.TCHari Ta manual for printers, gave an example of the problems that grew out of the new preoccupation with speed: 'The system of hurrying works through the press, lately adopted by soe booksellers, by giving them among a variety of houses, is destructive of uniformity. . . . It is not unusual to discover, in volumes, so divided, one to contain more lines in a page than another, or, even if the number of lines are the same, the page differs in length; or, should the type agree in depth, it is very probable that it varies in thickness; and still more probable that it varies in the quantity of work it has done; l of whic produce a garng graphic art' (p. 394).

At the same time there was a noticeable cheapening of inks and papers. Until the third decade of the century ink continued to be made from the usual ingredients — lampblack and linseed oil  according to the traditional recipes (one of which can be found in Moxon's Mechanick Exercises on the Whole Art of Printing, first published in 1683—4). Soap began to find its way into ink, because ink thus diluted gave a sharper impression, and soon the chemists were able to provide a whole range of artificial substitutes for the old ingredients. When Morris in the 189os insisted upon using an ink similar to that employed by printers of earlier centuries, it was seen as a revolutionary gesture.

Developments in paper-manufacturing were even more alarming. With the supply of good white rags declining, paper mills began, in the final decades of the eighteenth century, to bleach inferior rags and to introduce gypsum and alum into the fibre. The disastrous consequences were evident long before the Kelmscott Press cane on the scene. John Murray (not related to the publisher), a popular writer on scientific subjects, in 1823 contributed to the Gentleman's Magazine an article which was reprinted as a book the following year under the title

Observations and Experiments on the Bad Composition of Modern Paper. After describing the disintegration of particular books, Murray suggested that 'the chief causes of destruction consist in the employment of sulphate of lime, &c. in the pulp, and bleaching the rags previously, or the paper subsequently, with oxymuriatic acid gas. (Chlorine.)' (p. 8). Worse was to come. In the latter half of the century book papers were more frequently made from various rag substitutes, the poorest of which was niechanical woodpulp. As a general rule (with naturally a few exceptions), the older a book, the more likely its paper is to be in good condition today; librarians around the world are overwhelmed by the prospect of the steady disintegration of nearly all books printed since the mid-nineteenth century. To Morris, the most insidious aspect of modern book papers was that they frequently masqueraded as something better, with fake wire and chain lines (to suggest that they were handmade) and adulturants (to add weight and shininess). In certain kinds of books and periodicals, he suggested, cheap paper was acceptable, but only if it frankly acknowledged its own cheapness and made no effort to disguise itself.

Probably the ordinary reader a century ago paid no more attention to such matters than readers nowadays do; the most appalling woodpulp paper, when it is new, may seem decent enough to the unpractised eye. But changes in type-styles were sufficiently drastic to be obvious to even casual observers. Everywhere around him - in posters, advertisements, shop signs, sometimes even in books - the nineteenth-century Englishman witnessed an unprecedented efflorescence of new type-designs. Egyptians, grotesques, Gothics, Tuscans: their very names were mysterious and exotic, their shapes heavy and insistent, perfectly suited to the frenetic commercial atnosphere of the times. Book-typography, meanwhile, had moved in the opposite direction, with letterforns that grew ever more delicate and fragile.

Historians of printing usually classify serifed typefaces designed primarily for bookwork into three major categories. Except to specialists, however, the first two groups - Venetian and old face - look very much alike; on the other hand, the differences betveen old face and moer face re vius t ll. Until the cigeet cetuy, despite a slow evolution in the shape of the Roman letter, typefaces used in books changed remarkably little. Caslon's Roman types, for example, which began to appear in the 173os, are not fundamentally dissinilar from the type used by Aldus in his Hypuotomachia Polipili (1499), even though they are separated by more than twvo hundred

![](images/74da7d93d3801442a6a267945a7c716bcb4e473d73c494832be460acff5d96b7.jpg)

Fiur 4 Victorian typefounders produced a bewildering variety of new These decorative, sometimes bizarre typefaces were used mainly in posters and advertisements, though occasionally they crept into books as well.

years. But in the generation after Caslon a new style of type-design was introduced throughout Europe, subsequently known as 'modern face', which was to have an immense influence on the appearance of books in Morris's century.

Although a Frenchman, Philippe Grandjean, is usually regarded as the progenitor of the modern face, it was left to Giambattista Bodoni, an Italian designer, and the French foundry of Firmin Didot to carry the new style to its logical conclusion. In England, at about the same time, John Baskerville produced a transitional version of the modern face that seemed better suited to conservative tastes. What all these varied type-designs had in common was vertical rather than oblique shading, unbracketed (i.e. unsupported) serifs, and greater contrast between thick and thin strokes. The effect was — as Morris always said disparagingly — dazzling to the eye. Whereas old-face types had grown directly out of the letterforms used by Renaissance scribes and Roman stone-cutters, the newly fashionable letters were geometrically precise and brilliant in their effect.6 Old-face designs convey a mellow charm, like that of an ancient building; modern-face types suggest the smooth regularity of a well-made machine. It is not difficult to understand why Morris repudiated modern face.

Though they are not to everyone's taste, the books of Bodoni and Baskerville are extremely handsome. (Morris, needless to say, would v avithatY eeh-nry ys must be judged not only by their own types and books but also by the mainly unwholesome effect they had on their successors. As Daniel Berkeley Updike remarked, 'Grandjean, Baskerville, Bodoni, and the Didots had a mischievous influence on type-forms; for the derivations from types that their work made popular culminated in a kind oletter whiw cpablaungata a the case with old fonts. The ordinary English, French, o Italian book printed between 1830 and 1850 was very often a cheap and mean-looking production. Perhaps Bodoni and other great persons were not wrong in their day; but they put type-forms on the wrong track.7

The modern-face types for books that evolved in the nineteenth cDBoo  an, and boring, and they changed very little as the century went on, except to become progressively lighter. It is also important to bear in mind that the evolution of type-designs is influenced by developments in technology as well as shifts in fashion. With the introduction of the steam press, it became necessary to cast types in a harder metal than

S there are so many objects of interest to be seen and described in the interior, it will be necessary to leave the coast for a time.

The Land's End, following the carriage road from Penzance, is about ten miles distant. Flundreds of visitors pass over this road in the summer time, and we also will now lake this route, not hesitating to step aside occasionally ome curious monument or legendary spot.

The beautifully wooded grounds of Trereife, about a mile from the town, first attract attention; the roadway is here arched with a long avenue of noble elms ; near its extremity it is crossed by another avenue,-the road turning from the left leads to Newlyn,--a few yards up on the right is Trereife Iouse, aimost buried in foliage; a yew tree is trained over the front of the bildinggivig it theappeaance oflig wall ol leaves. The walks and peeps of woodland scenery around this place are very beautiful. Later in the century it beceincreasingly coon or prs to use dry, smooth papers on presses with a hard backing; whereas the ink on hairlines had once spread slightly on damp, more coarsetexture papers, it did not  o when thenew teniqu o pg was employed. 'From an engraver's point of view, new types so printed were exquisitely sharp and clean,' Theodore L. De Vinne, the American printer, explained; 'but from a reader's point of view, the general effect of the print was relatively mean and wiry, gray and but weak presswork came in fashion.

De Vinne himself bore some responsibility for the developments that he lamented, for this style of printing came to England largely through the influence of American periodicals such as Harper's Magazine and Century Magazine (the latter printed by De Vinne), which had pioneered the technique of hard-packing in order to improve the reproduction ofillustrations. However, the progressively lighter typefaces, though they may have had their origin in technical decisions, exactly suited the taste of late-Victorian readers and printers, and there was much boasting in the trade journals of the time that the growing delicacy of types was a sign of increased 'refinement': 'in the present day we see the effect of an artistic taste, and appreciation for delicacy of outline and freedom of ornamentation developed in the excessively light types and floral embellishments now so popular with the best printers,' according to a contributor to the Printers' Register (6 April 1885). 'Even the heaviest styles were materially toned down to meet the current refinement of taste.' By contrast, older typefaces were condemned as heavy, clumsy, and crude. It is in the context of this widespread preference for delicacy and lightness that Morris's almost oppressively dark types must be seen: discarding what seemed to him the attenuation and weakness of nineteenth-century typography, Morris reacted (over-reacted, some would say) by designing faces that were solid, black, and aggressively self-assertive.

But Morris was to argue on more than one occasion that typefaces alone did not determine the beauty and harmony of the printed page: even with mediocre type, if certain other principles of design were observed, it was possible to produce a satisfactory page. Morris had profoundly held views on margins, for example, and nothing infuriated him more than the popular practice of placing the text virtually in the centre of a page. That Morris was fighting a firmly entrenched eneny is illustrated by a series of articles written by De Vinne, the most skilful and best-informed printer in America, on 'Book Margins' which appeared in the Printers' Circular (Philadelphia) between May and September 1871. De Vinne, who certainly ought to have known better, offered the unconvincing argument that 'the text or printed page is the picture, and the margin is the frame; all the proprieties require that the picture, when framed, shall be in the centre of the frame' (June 1871). In fact, he was merely endorsing what printers on both sides of the Atlantic already did. Similarly, there was a broad consensus - which was not to be seriously questioned until Morris and Emery Walker attacked it in the 189os — that generous spaces between words and lines of type improved legibility. The combination of lighter typefaces and ample spaces produced a page in which the text appeared to be, at best, greyish.

##

Yet such a damning summary of Victorian printing practices fails to take account that printers of the last century were sometimes, when not subjected to the usual commercial pressures, capable of producing very impressive work. All the printers' manuals of the day discussed what was variously known as 'fine press work', 'fine work', or (later in the century) simply 'fine printing" As the ters suggest, the printer who did this sort of thing earned his additional wages double or more the usual rate) by taking greater care in the presswork, especially the make-ready; there appears to have been no distinction made betiveen 'ordinary' and 'fine' composition.

The greatest achievements of printing during the last century undoubtedly are to be found in the striking technical skill with which illustrations were produced. In the eighteenth century woodcuts had disappeared almost completely from books, which were illustrated instead with copperplate engravings, often quite elegant in themselves but bearing no relationship to the typography. The engravings and the text pages were printed by entirely separate processes and were joined together only when the book was bound. Morris regarded this as a pernicious developnent, because it destroyed that harmony which seemed to him the supreme attribute of a well-printed book. The cautionary examples which Morris, Walker, and others in their circle nty c Ro' Italy  Pm both handsomely illustrated with steel engravings designed by Turner. Albert L. Cotton summarized the Morrisian view when he wrote in thConteorary Rev August 1898)Bautiful s picure, t bear no apparent relation to the volumes which they illustrate; their charm is independent and extraneous; the artist clearly did not concern himself to harmonise them with the text or with each other; taken k u. Ty e i      ell have been issued separately in a portfolio.'

With less justice, Morris brought the same accusation against Thomas Bewick,who had revolutinized English bookllustraton at the beginning of the century by perfecting the wood-engraving, which, unlike the woodcut, is done on the end grain of boxwood with graver's tools and is therefore capable of rendering very subtle effects. Morris did not especially admire subtlety in art; he preferred the bold, simple outlines of medieval woodcuts to the intricate delicacy of Bewick's vignettes of English rural life. Probably Morris also harboured an irrational prejudice against Bewick bécause the brilliant technique he had created degenerated very quickly into the facile, characterless style of wood-engraving that was to be found in so many English books and magazines for the remainder of the century. Within a generation Bewick's successors were producing, on an unprecedented scale, illustrations that were both technically dazzling and aesthetically empty. The 'facsimile' engraving reigned triumphantly in Victorian England. Its supremacy was challenged finally in the 1880s by a new mechanico-chemical process then known as zincography, which had been invented by Eberhard, a Bavarian. This technology bypassed the human engraver entirely, since the artist's design was transferred photographically on to a zinc plate, which was then engraved by immersion in a chemical bath. An illustration that previously had been engraved by hand over several days could now be prepared for the press in a few hours. By the time the Kelmscott Press came into existence, wood-engraving had disappeared almost overnight, and process engravings were to be seen everywhere, even in the most expensively produced books. Morris held zincography in contempt; it is one of the minor ironies of the Kelmscott Press that his chief adviser was Emery Walker, by profession a process engraver, who probably knew more about the latest developments in techniques of illustration than anyone else in England. In the end Walker was able to wring one small concession to modern technology from Morris: the borders and initials used at the Kelmscott Press were all printed from electrotypes, another new means of duplicating engravings.

Chromolithography was used less frequently in books, but it was capable of producing really splendid effects, as in some of the folios by Henry Noel Humphreys. Morris, curiously enough, seems to have been intrigued by the process: in 1894 he and Walker spent a day at the Bodleian Library, Oxford, to investigate the possibility of reproducing a beautifully ornamented fourteenth-century manuscript known as the Douce Apocalypse, no doubt under the Kelmscott Press imprint. Nothing came of their plans, but it is certainly significant that Morris regarded such an undertaking seriously. (Morris probably had chromolithography in mind on this occasion, though he may have intended to callinto service some form of photographic reproduction, such as the newly invented halftone process.) In a letter written earlier, in 1882, Morris suggested that an Indian carpet recently presented to the Boston Museum of Fine Arts might be reproduced by the same means: 'some of the chromo-lithographers are so good nowadays that a reproduction of a good & careful drawing of it would be of great service to those who are interested in such matters.'"

Despite these occasional fickers of interest in the latest methods of facsimile illustration, the one subject that most deeply preoccupied Morr was typeesign, and it ws there, he fe, that the conteprary book displayed its gravest weakness. Morris was not alone in his dissatisfaction with the modern-face type. There had been several efforts earlier in the century, both in England and on the Continent, to revive old-face designs," but the one attempt which had the most far-reaching consequences for English printers, including Morris, was the reintroduction by theChiswick Press o Caslon's old-ace Roman int. Nrs which books first appeared in Caslon type, recent scholarship has confirmed that it was The Diary of Lady Willoughby, a novel published by Longnans in the spring of 1844." At about the same time, however, the Chiswick Press was also setting up two other books entirely in Caslon: George Herbert's The Temple, which was issued T p ou  o h vis F modern printers in search of historic designs, Charles Whittingham the Younger, the proprietor of theChiswic Press, was not compelled to have the Caslon type recut; he simply went to the frm founded by

Coha  . Second, though the Caslon type was generally admired as attractive when it appeared in The Diary of Lady Willoughby, there was no rush by other printers to use it, for Caslon appeared too self-consciously archaic and odd to Victorian eyes. Charles T. Jacobi, who in Morris's day was manaerotheChiswick ress, said : 'the excellent dn of these different founts [i.e. the several sizes of Caslon] was not generally appreciated until about the year 1888, when the Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society first came into existence, and Mr. Emery Walker was perhaps more responsible than anyone else for making known this series of faces. Since that date these types . . . have gradually been adopted largely for the printing of the better class of bookwork, especially that of a more antiquarian nature. . . ."13

Caslon type does not look particularly remarkable to us today, because its frequent use in the early twentieth century has made it seem ordinary and very 'English'. (In time Caslon became so closely identified with Englishness that it was adopted by at least some printers for patriotic motives.) Bruce Rogers, the distinguished American bookdesigner, complained in 1917 that Caslon was, if anything, excessively familiar — amuch overrated type', he said; its best quality was a certain amount of irregularity, restful to the eye after the smoothness and monotony of our more modern letters'."4 England seemed to him at the time a 'Caslon-ridden country' It was precisely the occasional clumsiness and crudeness of Caslon, so offensive to Rogers, which recommended it to Morris, and in the last decade of his life he adopted it enthusiastically for his own commercially printed books. Morris in turn influenced Bernard Shaw to use it in his books from the late 1890s onwards; this had the effect of bringing Caslon into even more general circulation.

Because Caslon seemed appropriate only for 'period' typography to Victorian readers, the Edinburgh typefoundry of Messrs Miller and Richard offered their customers in 186o a design by A. C. Phemister called at first ´'Modernized Old Style' (later simply 'Old Style'), which attempted to capture the flavour of Caslon without sacrificing many of the characteristics of modern-face type. It also had the merit of being designed to work well with modern inks and papers. Other typefounders soon sold their own versions. Old Style was a deliberate compromise, and a good deal of scorn was poured on it through the years. One of the earliest to register a protest was Emery Walker, who, though he admitted that it was more suitable for smooth-surfaced papers than Caslon, described it as 'far inferior in beauty to types of the early printers'; similarly, Morris remarked that 'the design of the letters of this modern "old style" leaves a good deal to be desired, and the whole effect is a little too grey, owing to the thinness of the letters'.'s Bruce Rogers found it at best inoffensive, at worst monotonous." Whatever its limitations, Old Style proved to be an immensely popular typeface - by some estimates, indeed, the most widely used book face in England during the final decades of the century. (Modern face, however, continued to be the preferred type in periodicals, school-books, and scientific works.) A writer in the Printers' Register (6 November 1884) offered the opinion that 'probably no variety of type at present in use has so firm a hold upon the cultivated taste of the printer or of the public'. It was eventually supplanted in the second decade of the twentieth century by the revivals of historic types that began to emanate from the Monotype Corporation.

The resurrection of Caslon by the Chiswick Press in mid-century, followed by the introduction of Old Style by Miller and Richard, brought about gradual but important changes in the appearance of English books. We must remind ourselves that the rejection of modern-face types had begun long before 1891 and that the Kelmscott Press was, in a sense, merely the most visible manifestation of the antiquarian winds which had been stirring the bibliographical waters in England for a number of decades. The two figures who  apart, of course, from Morris - played the most decisive roles in sponsoring the revival of older types and ornaments were the publisher William Pickering and Charles Whittingham the Younger, the owner of the Chiswick Press. Stanley Morison, in fact, has suggested that Morris's celebrated achievements as a printer were firmly rooted in the less spectacular work of Pickering, Pugin, and certain publishers associated with the Oxford Movement. In a letter to Geoffrey Keynes, written in 1924, he remarked that 'Pickering was the first to design books in an eclectico-antiquarian spirit: I mean that he was the first to go back to XVIth century Venice& Lyons for inspiration. If I am right Pcker-th ibal hoRcttR hoIt assevat prtyorayha y typ which deliberately imitates the style of older books - has become so commonplace in the twentieth century that we assume it has always been with us, but the evidence suggests very strongly that it was invented by Pickering and several contemporaries of Catholic or Anglo-Catholic sympathies.

As early as 1830 Pickering acquired a set of wood-engraved initials designed by Thomas Stothard, but it was a decade later when the ornamentation of the Chiswick Press-Pickering books began in earnest. Inspired perhaps by the publication of Henry Shaw's Alabt  anDevi thi ,Whi ham commissioned more than a dozen sets of initials from Mary Byfield and launched a series of sumptuous reprints — including several versions of the English Prayer Book — adorned with blackletter titles and attractive borders, initials, and tailpieces. (Gladstone testified in 189o that 'the pages of their Prayer Book are a perfect picture, reminding one of those early printed books which are still marvellous examples of printing'") Though Whittingham turned occasionally to other printers, his finest books were produced by the Chiswick Press, which had the deserved reputation of being the most distinguished 'old-style' printer in England during the latter half of the nineteenth century. Even Morris had to serve what was, in effect, an apprenticeship at the Chiswick Press before he could set up a press of his own.

But there were other old-style printers, les frequently remembered today perhaps than the Chiswick Press but famous in their own time. The firm of George Falkner and Sons in Manchester, for example, were celebrated for the large collection of woodcuts, decorated initials, and head-and tailpieces with which they ornamented pseudoantique books. Falkner productions were invariably described by reviewers or the firm itself as quaint' — i.e. self-consciously antiquarian in favour. (It is significant that Morris never uses the word 'quaint' when writing about his own variety of old-style printing.) The Falkners had set up a wooden hand-press in 'the quaint Caxtonian Printing Offices in Old Manchester', and their printer's mark, designed by Walter Crane, showed a pressman at work on a hand-press. Printers like Falkner and Sons issued a good deal of ephemera as well as books: their 'Old Style Calendar for 1882', for instance, contained 'many delightful and entertaining particulars, very commodious indeed and profytable, worth the labour of your reading. (A reprint from many early Kalendars.)' Their book titles included The Shakespeare Show Book and The Old English Fayre Volume; many of the books were stained both internally and externally to suggest great age.° (This sort of fakery was, needless to say, the typographical equivalent of the fraudulent restoration of ancient buildings which Morris condemned near the end of his life.)

Noty tion, but even very respectable firms had only a muddled notion of wh  l ' The Gresham Steam Press, perated by he Unwin brothers, were among the first printers to follow the Chiswick Press's example in adopting Caslon type, yet in an 1862 type-specimen book they supplied this extraordinary note: 'Type of the old style of face is now frequently used, more especially for the first class of books and ornamentalworks.The ser in useat the Gresham Stem ress re from the original matrices which were cut at the beginning of the last century, and thus possess all the peculiarities of themediaeval letter."

It is easy to mock late-Victorian printers self-deluded by 'old-style' pretensions, with their verbose colophons in imitation Middle English, their confused appeals to antiquity, and their sham paper and leather (stai, o cour). Nor s it difful to point ut theconsistc their alleged commitment to the ideal of handicraft: the Falkners might display a hand-press in their device, but they were also notorious for owning the largest steam-driven presses in England. Nevertheless, old-style printers - and the publishers who patronized them - occasionally produced books which, while not possessing the massive authority of the Kelmscott volumes, still have considerable charm. The publisher Joseph Cundall, who was virtually forgotten until recently, issued a series of children's books, mainly in Caslon and often beautifully illustrated by Birket Foster and others, which are extremely handsome by any standard.²2 Did Morris ever see them? We do not know, because he is silent about the Victorian books he considered worthy ofadmiration, but certainly Cundal, like Pickering, belongs to a very small group of nineteenth-century publisher-designers whose work may have influenced Morris.

Likewise it would be fascinating to discover whether Morris was aware of the books published and printed by Andrew Tuer, whose Leadenhall Press ('Ye Leadenhalle Presse' was the quaint version) in London issued many lively, amusing exercises in pastiche. The founder of the Paper and Printing Trades Journal as well as a wholesale stationer, Tuer avoided merely imitative period typography by a playful approach to book-design that at times reminds one of the zestful spirit of Francis Meynell in our own century. (There is in fact a direct link: Meynell confessed to a special interest in Tuer because his father had been Tuer's literary adviser.²³) In works like Old London Street Cries (1885) and A History of the Hornbooks (1896) Tuer turned to the usual
