This el qualiyTuer'blin e semos viv  them beaok yl ublh duri he8 l wi pr ufiv y Joseph Crawhal and set in type that appears to have been deliberately battered. For many English readers in the fnal decades of the last century, Tuer's name was synonymous with old-style printing, which had by then become extremely fashionable.

Even in Tuer's own journal, writers occasionally expressed misgivings about the way in which 'old style'seemed to lead so often to an unhappy display of meretricious virtuosity in typography and spelling. Commenting on Ye Printers' Blotter: A Votarie of ye Wm. Caxton & ye Bn. Franklin, privately printed 'atween times' by Edwin C. Bell of Titusville, Pennsylvania, a contributor to the Paper and Printing Trades Annual ( June 188o) observed that it 'has evidently been projected, and its leading ideas copied, from Mr. S. L. Nussey's Ye Quaynt, published in this country, and it is only to be regretted that the imitation is not typographically more complete - for Mr. Bell, although evidently smitten with the idea of following the old-style, lacks the material, or more probably the necessary knowledge to carry it out, as his production is full of the grossest inconsistencies, literals, and bad imitations of early English spelling. . . . He is fond of the long f,but in every case makes an 'f' do duty, and worse than that he uses it for finals, as for instance in the word news' in his opening address, which he spells 'newf' - two mistakes in a word of four letters.' It was no doubt easier to criticize the amateurish blunders of a printer across the ocean, but in fact the English landscape was also littered with long 's's (frequently misplaced) and ubiquitous 'ye's. Quaintness had become a typographical disease. In Thé Art of Illustration (1894), Henry Blackburn complained bitterly about the fraudulence of imitative modern books in which process engravings masqueraded as woodcuts, machine-made papers with deckle edges pretended to be the product of hand-labour, and artificial binding materials were disguised as vellum. Such books, he said, cracked in half with a loud bang when they were opened the first time.

Old-style printing, then, was a mixed blessing. It originated with a commendable attempt to resist the tyranny of modern-face types by reviving the use of aslon, a superior eighteenth-century face, but it became very quickly associated with deception and cheap materials. When Ruskin subjected one of the most famous nineteenth-century English paintings, Holman Hunt's The Awakening Conscience (exhibited at the Royal Academy in 1854), to a characteristically close analysis, he seized upon one detail - the modern book in a papier-mâché binding which lies upon a table — as a particularly telling symptom of Victorian moral failure.² Ruskin was much interested in bookproduction: he tried his hand at designing bindings, and for the 1889 edition of Modern Painters he insisted upon a newly cast fount of type and painstakingly oversaw the printing of the plates.²6 Morris must have been aware of Ruskin's prolonged effort to control the printing of his own books.

Ruskin also played a minor role in launching what the Victorians calledartistic'printing, the only serious rival tooldstyle'printing in the 188os.2²7 Thomas Hailing, a Cheltenham printer who had achieved some celebrity by establishing a small trade journal called Hailing's Circular in 1877, proposed another periodical in which leaves printed by various printers would be bound together and circulated for their mutual edification.²8 The first number (188o) of the Printers' International Specimen Exchange, under the editorship of Andrew Tuer (who had a foot in both the artistic and old-style camps), contained a letter from Ruskin to Tuer: It sees to methat  lovely feld is open in the treatment of decorative type  not in the mere big initials in which one cannot find the letters — but in a delicate and variably fntentatio capital,andfillibla s or musically-divided periods of sentences and breadths of margin. Paper that won't break or won't mildew would be literally a "odsend" to me. I scarcly care to design an engraving to go on to modern paper. I have the most entire sympathy with your objects, but believe that people ill have bad paper nowadays, bad printing nowadays, and bad painting nowadays — and nothing else."

It is ly at odds with the upbeat tone of Tuer's introduction — and if Ruskin thought that Victorian printing was about to be regenerated by the Printers' Interational Specien Exchange, he was seriously mistaken. The artsc syle which dominated the Specimen Exchange from the beginning had been imported from the United States and was based upon a hideous (or so it now seems to us) combination of bizarre ornamental types and bent rules. The Printers' Register (7 July 1884), reviewing the fifth volune of the Specimen Exchange, argued that it represented a higher levcl of taste and workmanship than was to be found among any of the great printers of the past. But this was pure bluster (of the sort very common in trade journals of that decade), and the most sensitive printers recognized that the artistic style was a temporary aberration which could only be deplored. Jacobi remarked that 'the excessive rule-work, twisted and twirled in all manner of fantastic shapes, the adoption of grotesque types, together with the employment of diverse colours (not always harmonious) is work really overstepping the bounds of proper typography, and it appears to me but an emulation of lithography or chromolithography De Vinne, in a lecture delivered in Philadelphia, offered this sarcastic observation: 'What advances have we made in rule-twisting! What unknown possibilities in typography have been developed by our new race of compositors! . .  How it does delight us to employ a typographical gymnast who tortures brass rules and spends hours and days in experiments with borders, fancy job types, tint grounds, and flourishes!'30

![](images/4f02d875b5ff837b00177e3abfb3049aeeaab62032cff7ee8cbfe49e9f48903d.jpg)  
Figure 11 An example of 'artistic' printing in the Printers' International Specimen Exchange (188o), edited by Andrew Tuer. More sophisticated Victorian printers like De Vinne and Jacobi decried the obsession with bent rules and weird decorative types.

Fortunately the artistic style had its strongest impact on job-printing; it crcpt into books only occasionally, and then mainly on bindings and title-pages. Many book-printing houses such as Richard Clay and William Clowes were affected remarkably little by either the old-style or the artistic modes yet managed to produce typography that was at least neat, decent, and unobtrusive. Scottish printers in particular had a reputation for making books with these old-fashioned virtues. When Morris began to proclaim his typographical views in the 189os, it is u overshadowed by a showy upstart. In an interview published in the Printers' Register — Supplement (6 February 1894), Walter Blaikie, head of T. and A. Constable, Edinburgh, made a number of barbed comments that were clearly directed at Morris. 'Many machine papers are quite as durable as hand-made,he said; deckle eges had become an end in themselves (here Morris would have agreed with him), just as in some of Morris's books, he strives to perpetuate the embossing of papers by the type, which was one of the defects of the hand-press'. Caslon type, he added, was used chiefly 'for what I call quasi-facsimile reprints' And then the final veiled allusion to Morris: 'you must always remember that thc first objct of a book is to be read, not to become an article of brica-brac.' When a journalist from the periodical Bookselling interviewed Morris the following year, he found equal itust n the her i. The Constable e  pitersMoris in the headlines of their books, are simply hideous. . . . In my opinion the best printers in Scotand are the Clarks. They have done some really beautiful work and have done it cheaply.'

Among nineteenth-century English private printers, the only one whose work anticipated Morris' in any sense was the Revd C. H. O. Daniel, Fellow of Worcester College, Oxford. The term 'private', incidentally, is extremely troublesome: Emery Walker was even prepaergue that Kelt Pres was ot sri eg, a private press, since Morris was willing to print books for others. When John Martin, the librarian of Woburn Abbey, compiled a Bibliographical Catalogue of Privately Printed Books (second edition, 1854), he explained that 'by privately-printed books, the Editor means to designate such only as were not intended by the writers for sale, and the circulation of which has been confined entirely to their friends and connections, or to those who took an interest in the matter contained in them' (p. vi). The Daniel Press would fit such a narrow definition, but certainly not the Kelmscott Press — or most other modern private presses, for that matter.

Between 1845 and 1863 Daniel produced a large number of ephemeral pieces of printing, at first on a toy press and later on a small Albion press. In 1875, after an interval of neglect, he moved the press into his college rooms at Oxford and began printing again in earnest, and during this second phase he created a series of pamphlets and books which are admired and collected today. Falconer Madan, who has written the fullest account of Daniel's work as a printer, made a large claim for his press: 'Dates appear to show that the Daniel Press was the first attempt to raise the standard of English Victorian printing — a work which was carried on with higher artistic and professional ideals and better opportunities on the establishment of the Kelmscott Press in 1891. The use of the Fell type in 1877 and the production of the Garland of Rachel in 1881 may fairly be regarded as the first genuine signs of the Revival of Printing in this country.3

But is this true? Stanley Morison has shown that Daniel was not after all the first printer to revive the use of the splendid Fell types (owned by the Oxford University Press) in the nineteenth century, and Jacobi, among others, pointed out that Daniel's presswork was not as good as his typography.34 A close examination of The Garland of Rachel, which Madan describes as 'the most celebrated and valuable product of the Daniel Press' (p. 86), reinforces such misgivings. The little collection of eighteen poems, written by Daniel's friends to celebrate the first birthday of his daughter Rachel, is attractively set in the Fell types with ornaments by Alfred Parson, RA, and Mrs Daniel; yet the initials are weak, the inking is uneven, the register is erratic, and the paper has deteriorated badly. It is a charming production — indeed, intensely 'private' - but scarcely will stand up to a comparison with mallest  teelcott boks.T i ot  spe Daniel's printing: it is likely to appeal to precisely those collectors who are offended by Morris's penchant for the monumental book. But to see the Kelmscott Press as the direct descendant of the Daniel Press (as Madan suggests) is misleading.

Was Morris familiar with any of the Daniel books? He could easily have known about them, but as usual he seems never to have men-Dn s.Tzicuv in the diary of Sydney Cockerell, Morris's secretary, on 28 September 1898 (after Morris's death): 'Went to Bodleian & then to see Revd C.H. Daniel & his printing press (which has been idle for nearly a year) at Worcester House. Had a nice talk about printing &c.'

Just two years after Daniel began printing in Oxford, a major exhibition was held which was to have a more direct effect on the history of the Kelmscott Press. To observe the four-hundredth anniversary of printing in England, a Caxton Exhibition opened in South Kensington on 30 June 1877 with unprecedented attention from both the press and the public. Squeezed in between the large array of early printed books (mainly but not exclusively Caxton's) and modern printing equipment was a small exhibit which proved to be the hit of the show: while curious visitors watched him, a white-haired pressman in knee-breeches operated a wooden press approxinately two hundred years old. (At a Health Exhibit in 1884 Tuer's 'Ye Leadenhalle Presse' managed to put their hands on the same press for eonstrucion  medival printing offic Morison asserts that the 1877 exhibition 'had a decisive effect upon bibliographical and typoicl W the effect of elevating Caxton to the status of a national hero. William Blades's revised biography appeared in the sane year; in 1882 a Caxton Memorial Window was unveiled in St Margaret's Church, Westmsr. I s  tyaphil nt bfu the

Kelmscott Press, Morris used a modern imitation of one of Caxton's types, and fiv he Kelscott tit wer Caxton rrits. Thogh Caxton's books could not have appealed to him aesthetically, Morris bvously shared his countrymen's enthusiasm or England's ft printer.3

A growing interest in old books, facsimiles, and improved typography was evident on every side during the last two decades of the century. A writer in the Paper and Printing Trades Annual (December 1879) declared that 'fifty years ago a facsimile or an imitation of Caxton type could only be seen in the private library of the lover of books, but now the taste for Caxtonian and old-style works is so general that pamphlets and tradesmen's circulars are printed in type so nearly resembling the original that only the initiated can discern the difference'. One well-known London publisher, Elliot Stock, exploited this popular antiquarian taste by issuing a series of typefacsimiles of older English books, including Walton's Compleat Angler, Pilgrim's Progress, Paradise Lost, and Herbert's The Temple. They were attractively printed at the Chiswick Press and were well received by readers and reviewers. During the 188os Kegan Paul published a similar series, known as the 'Parchment Library'.

The cult of Caxton led also to a minor revival of Gothic types, at least for display purposes. Calbe Stower's The Printer's Grammar (1808) had been able to declare emphatically that blackletter 'is now abolished in England, except in a very few instances' (p. 41), and throughout most of the nineteenth century its use was restricted to legal and ecclesiastical books, usually on title-pages. In the 188os it began to appear again (though still for display only) in books and journals. The extent to which the Caxton exhibition had filtered down to the popular level is amusingly illustrated by this advertisement in the Antiquary magazine, 188o-I: 'Cash's Woven Initial Letters and Monograms for sewing on household linen, socks, and underclothing. The letters are manufactured in three sizes of Old English Type, in Turkey Red, and orders can also be executed in Black, both of which are warranted perfectly fast. . . . Sold by Drapers and Hosiers everywhere.' By the time Morris turned his attention to designing the Troy type, the Victorian Gothic Revival had already touched the Gothic typeface.

Another indication of the new interest in typographical experimentation was A. H. Mackmurdo's periodical the Century Guild Hobby Horse, which first appeared in 1884 but did not resume publication until two years later.3' Though its style was more art nouveau than Morrisian, the Hobby Horse was a distinguished journal that reminds us once again (if we need reminding) that many of the ideas usually attributed to Morris were in fact simply in the air at the end of the last century. With an engraved cover designed by Selwyn Image (whose Greek type Morris was to use in the following decade) and ornaments by Image, Herbert Horne, and others, the Hobby Horse - printed, not surprisingly, at the Chiswick Press - anticipates many of Morris's notions about the well-designed page. And, since Morris knew Mackmurdo and others associated with it, there can be no doubt that he was aware of the existence of the Hobby Horse. It is most unlikely that he was impressed by the Hobby Horse, though Mackmurdo in later years was to boast that his periodical was the true precursor of the Kelmscott Press. One can perhaps find a hint of Morris's probable attitude in a letter (undated) which he wrote to Charles Ricketts and Charles Shannon about their Dial, another journal, founded in 1889, with similar typography and ornaments: 'Thank you for the Dial which duly arrived. I confess that I looked at the art portion of it with somewhat mixed feelings, as the talent and the aberration of the talent seemed to me to be in about equal proportions.'38

The world of typographical scholarship — with which Morris had numerous connections — was also showing signs of renewed life in the final years of the nineteenth century. William Blades and Talbot Baines Reed were laying the foundations for the history of English printing; at the major British libraries figures like Robert Proctor and Henry Bradshaw were pioneering in the study of incunabula. During the 189os Kegan Paul published a bibliographical series under the general editorship of A. W. Pollard: the titles included The Great Book Colletors by Charls and Mary Elton, Book Plates by W. J. Hardy, Books in Manuscript by Falconer Madan, Early Printed Books by E. Gordon Duff, EarlyIllustrated Books by Pollard, and Book Bindings by Herbert P. Horne. Along similar lines, Elliot Stock inauguratedaris called 'The Book-Lover's Library' under the editorship of H. B. Wh 18801915; the Bibliographer, 1881-4; Book-Lore, 18857; the Bookworm, 188892). In the 189os the Bibliographical Society was formed L books.

M  Th Il 8

Chiswick Press also spoke on 'The Printing of Modern Books' Another organization with which Morrs was actively associated, the Art-Workers' Guild, devoted a substantial number of its meetings from 1886 onwards to the book arts; the topics discussed or demonstrate at the Guilmegincluded BooidiCal, Typography, and the Book Beautiful', 'The Manufacture of Paper' (this by Joseph Batchelor, Morris's papermaker, who brought along a washtub for his demonstration), 'Book Illustration' and 'Reformation of Printing Type'.

Shortly before his foundry cast Morris's Golden type in 1890, Talbot Baines Reed delivered an erudite lecture on 'Old and New Fashions in Typography' to the Society of Arts (the group to which Morris was later to speak about 'The Woodcuts of Gothic Books'). Reed's talk offers an excellent illustration of the enlightened climate of typographical opinion in which Morris's own theories were formed. Surveying the development of letterforms through the centuries, Reed praised the superiority of Venetian and old-face types — especially those of Nicholas Jenson and Caslon — and argued that 'the modern fshion [.e.modernface type]lacks a theracues and dignito the old. Its straight har serifs, its stiff interiors, its harsh contrasts of thick and thin, more than nullify the passing advantage of exact lining and delicate finish.'9 This assertion has a familiar ring, because it is precisely the doctrine that Morris was to promulgate within a year or two after Reed's lecture. Morris may not have been directly indebted to Reed, because there were others from whom he could have absorbed the same ideas (most notably Emery Walker, who was present that evening and participated in the discussion); but the lecture demonstrates that Morris's typographical theories did not evolve in isolation.

A slightly earlier lecture which eerily anticipates many of Morris's complaints about modern books was delivered by Henry Stevens, an American rare-book dealer long resident in London, to the Library Association at Cambridge in October 1884.4° Published under the title Who Spoils our New English Books Asked and Answered by Henry Stevens of Vermont (1884) and dedicated ´to the memory of two old friends Charles Whittingham and William Pickering', Stevens's lecture was a powerful attack upon declining standards in Victorian printing. 'The manufacture of a beautiful and durable book costs little if anything more, it is believed, than it does to manufacture a clunsy and unsightly one,' he declared in words that Morris was to echo (almost verbatin)

in the following decade.4' Like Morris, Stevens insisted that margins of classic proportions, which he specified, were essential to a beautiful printed page, and that good design was neaningless without good materials, especially ink and paper. He even made a stirring assault on the indiscrininate use of the binder's knife, which Morris later described as one of the chief enemies of civilization.

As always, we find ourselves asking the obvious question: was Morris familiar with this lecture? And, as in other instances considered earlier, there is no frn answer, though he undoubtedly had an opportunity to read it, for the booklet was on display at the 1888 Arts and Crafts Exhibition where Morris must have seen it. The issue, however, is not whether Morris borrowed particular ideas or attitudes from some of his contemporaries; what we need to keep in mind while examining the history of the Kelmscott Press is that Morris's reaction against the enfeebled Victorian book reflects a surprisingly widespread response to the typography of the day. Morris was not alone in protest ing against the modern debaseinent of printing, nor was he the only one to suggest sensible remedies. What sets Morris apart from Jacobi, Reed, Stevens, and other lesser voices is that he was able, during the 1890, tfuse  brilliantynthesis thendividual citiisms, tori the craft of printing back in touch with literature and art, and to move t books of unsurpassed beauty.

# TWO  WILLIAM MORRIS&THEARTS OF THE BOOK

![](images/7e587645b3384be664c50100d677ed36cbac6fb109c5f1669bd47273b8576da7.jpg)

HOUGH William Morris displayed a lively interest in the arts of the book at various times throughout his life, the first volume printed at the Kelmscott Press was completed only five and a half years before his death. Seen in isolation, the Press appears to be an inexplc able phenomenon of Morris's final decade, an undertaking brought to fruition only by the

desperate energy of a dying man. Yet Morris was able to achieve so much as a printer in so little time not only because of the obsessive intensity he brought to every task but also because of the profound understanding of the physical form of books that he had acquired over a lifetime. In fact, he made several noteworthy (but mostly abortive) efforts to produce lavishly ornamented and illustrated books before 1891. The Kelmscott Press, which to the casual observer perhaps seems to have sprung fully matured from the brow of Morris, had actually undergone a long period of gestation.'

The Press alone would have been enough to make the reputation of any other man or woman, but it was merely the last phase of a long, extraordinary career in which Morris achieved fame as a poet, revived a whole series of traditional crafts, established himself as the foremost English designer of the nineteenth century, and founded and managed a firm (Morris, Marshall, Faulkner, and Company, later reorganized as Morris and Company) which was to have a marked effect upon the furnishings of the middle-class Victorian home. In his later years he became, moreover, a pioneer in the architectural preservation movement and one of the most influential leaders of English socialism. Merely listing Morris's activities, however, cannot convey adequately the vitality of either the man or his ideas: born in an age of growing specialization, he was a brilliant generalist who did superlatively well whatever he turned his hand to. In the latter half of the twentieth century we have increasingly come to recognize Morris as one of the seminal fgures in the history of modern architecture, art, and design.

Though not disposed to introspection, Morris has left us in his work itself - the tapestries, stained-glass windows, wallpapers, poems, books — an unmistakable record of the growth of his inner life. 'I can't enter into politico-social subjects with any interest,' he wrote to his friend Cormell Price in July 1856, 'for on the whole I see that things are in a muddle, and I have no power or vocation to set them right in ever so little a degree. My work is the embodiment of dreams in one form or another."² In the 188os he did after all try to translate these dreams into political realities through socialist agitation, but he remained convinced, like Dickens's Stephen Blackpool, that nineteenth-century life was "aw a muddle', perhaps a hopeless muddle, and ultimately only his compelling personal vision of a simpler, purer world - located especially in the Middle Ages and, in moments of optimism, in the future - sustained him. Near the end of his life, reflecting on the comparative failure of the Society for the Protection of Ancient Buildings (of which he had been primary founder), he lamented, 'Now that I am grown old and see that nothing is to be done, I half wish that I had not been born with a sense of romance and beauty in this accursed age."3 The echo of Hamlet seems to be deliberate in this letter: finding himself living in a time that was out of joint, Morris, silently cursing, bent all his prodigious energies to setting it right. The Kelmscott Press was his ast great attempt to restore beauty nd santy to an ugly, mad world.

It is scarcely necessary to trace intellectual 'sources' of Morris's powerful, visionary attachment to the medieval past, for apparently from earliest childhood he was drawn to Gothic churches and cathedrals. The most intense experience of these years seems to have been a rapturous glimpse of Canterbury Cathedral at the age of eight. At M and ecclesiastical architecture, and invented stories about knights and fairies'; at Oxford he was intoxicated by 'a vision of grey-roofed houses and a long winding street, and the sound of many bells'.4 Burne-Jones, whose moods often mirrored Morris's, has left a revealing account of a walk across Oxford's Port Meadow from the ruins at Godstowe on a winter day when he (Burne-Jones, that is) forgot altogether the modern world and lost himself in a rapturous dream of 'the old days, the abbey, and long processions of the faithful, banners of the cross, copes and crosiers, gay knights and ladies by the river bank, hawking-parts and all the pgentry  the golde ae it made me feel so wild and mad I had to throw stones into the water to break the dream. I never remember having such an unutterable ecstasy, it was quite painful with intensity, as if my forehead would burst.'s

It is tempting to dismiss this sort of experience as adolescent connected with the growth of a comprehensive set of ideas which attepted to explain themoral, artisic,and social declineofVicorian England. They were 'Ruskinian' ideas, of course, yet one suspects that Morris was drawn so irresistibly to Ruskin's writings because he was y distant glamour of the Middle Ages more absorbing than the industrialized, urbanized world of the nineteenth century. In 1851—3 the three volumes of Ruskin's The Stones of Venice appeared, and they Mors was torerint i cl chaptr, The Nat Goth e fourth book of the Kelnscott Press in 1892: 'one of the very few necessary and inevitable utterances of the century,' he called it in his preface.'To some of us when we first read it,' Morris wrote, 'now many years ago, it seemed to point out a new road on which the world should travel. And in spite of all the disappointments of forty years, and although some of us, John Ruskin amongst others, have since learned what the equipment for that journey must be, and how many things must be changed before we are equipped, yet we can stil see no other way out of the folly and degradation of Civilization.

What exactly did Morris learn from Ruskin? Above all, that 'art is the expression of man's pleasure in labour' (as Morris put it in the NaG e; that h u the handicrafts, for 'the longing of simple people will take up the chain w, and . . . the academical art which has developed from that misreading of history which we call the Renaissance, will prove a barren stem'.? The modern artist (or craftsman, as Morris and Ruskin might say, since they did not recognize any distinction between the two categories) was compelled to look back to the Middle Ages for models and inspiration, though mere imitation of medieval art was to be discouraged. Writing to the illustrator C. M. Gere in 1893, Morris observed: 'One piece of advice I will now give you, which you must deal with according to your own mind, as it is I am aware a little dangerous. This it is; that you should now try to steep yourself, so to say, in mediaeval design; look [at] illuminations in 13th & 14th century books[,] at wood cuts and so on, and make sketches from them. . . But (there is always a but, you know) all this will be of no use to you unless you feel yourself drawn in that direction and are really enthusiastic about the old work. When I was a young-bear, I think I really succeeded in ignoring

# THE NATURE OF GOTHIC.

![](images/3652d6a24a8424555c0b11f7d04b69151d5840067af1d0fa055b9888c89a326b.jpg)

E are now aboutto enterupon the examination of that schoolof Venetian architecture which forms an intermediate step between the Byzantine and Gothic forms; but which I find may

be conveniently consideredinits connexion with the latter style. ¶ In order that we may discern the tendency of each step of this change, it will be wise in the outset to endeavour to form some generalidea ofits final result. We know already what the Byzantine architecture is from whichthe transition was made, but we oughtto know something of the Gothic architecture into which it led. ¶ I shall endeavour therefore to give the reader in this chapter an idea, at once broad and definite, of the true nature of Gothic architecture, properly so called; not of that Venice only, but univeral Gothic: or it will be one of the most interesting parts of our subsequent inquiry, to find out how far Venetian architecture reached the universal modern lie altogether. And it was of great service to me'Yet in his next letter to Gere, Morris admitted that this was 'doubtful advice'. Ning examples of derivative, historicist art and architecture, and Morris was fully conscious of the dangers in turning to the past. But he was convinced that only by rediscovering the older traditions could the Victorian artistreunibour npleasure; ny thus could eruil the decayed social foundations of art.

![](images/a420b3acb7cd9c112d86bee890fbf65afa1302f82f548a242555f15c7050cf9a.jpg)  
Figure 12 Morris, who as a young man had bccn swcpt off his fcct by Ruk The Stones  Venice rint  cenral chapter, The Nat Gothic', as thc fourth book of thc Kclmscott Press (1892).

At Exeter College, Oxford, in the 185os Morris, Burne-Jones, and several like-minded friends discovered Ruskin, Rossetti, and the Pre-Raphaelites, toyed with the notion of founding an Anglo-Catholic monastic community of craftsmen, visited country churches and rubbed brasses, and read Tennyson, Chaucer, Keats, Carlyle, and Charlotte Mary Yonge's The Heir f Redclyffe. After taking his egree Morris worked for a short time for G. E. Street, the architect, but within a few years he was married to Jane Burden and well launched on his own distinctive career as a renewer of traditional crafts. Architecture was always for Morris the supreme art, and throughout his life he never wavered in his conviction that all other arts were subservient it. Yet Morris came t realize that miorcrafts such as weving, wall-decorating, and printing, because they were less ambitious and involved simpler technologies, could be more easily restored to their former gloriés than architecture itself could. The book in particular, whether written or printed, appealed to him because it was a small, self-contained object; Morris doubted 'whether it were possible to have buildings generally good in the present day', but he noted that 'there are not the same difficulties with a book as with many other articles of commerce'.9

In the last year of his life Morris told Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, 'I remember as a boy going into Canterbury Cathedral and thinking that the gates of heaven had been opened to me. Also when I first saw an illuminated manuscript, these first pleasures which I discovered for myself were stronger than anything else in life." (This close identification of the Gothic cathedral and the Gothic book in Morris's mind is significant: in the 189os he was to speak of the arrangement of the Kelmscott Press Chaucer as purely architectural.) In the Bodleian Library at Oxford, Morris had his first opportunity to examine a large number of illuminated manuscripts, and he was to recall frequently in later years the pleasure he found in them, especially the 'Douce Apocalypse' and a fifteenth-century 'Romance of Alexander'."

![](images/ab03f63c5d179a01f0eb8fedad3197db9593d706c6331e7fd1bf77ab883abe2f.jpg)  
Figure 13 Morris, while an undergraduate at Oxford, admired the 'Douce Apocalypsc' in the Bodleian Library, and in November 1894 he and Walker camc to thc Bodleian in order to examinc it again; Morris considered the possibility of publishing a facsimile of the thirteenth-century manuscript.

Morris's passion for the decorated book never diminished: May Morris remarke that her fther and Burne-Jones were ormany years such frequent visitors to the manuscript room of the British Museum tha so familiar with its treasures as my father was'"² Indeed, Morris was prepared to argue that if all the other art of the early Middle Ages had disappeared, they might still claim to be considered a great period of art on the strength of their ornamented books'"³ In 1894 Morris contributed an article entitled 'Some Notes on the Illuminated Books of the Middle Ages' to the Magazine of Art, and at about the same time he began an essay, never completed, 'Some Thoughts on the Ornamented Manuscripts of the Middle Ages'. In both essays Morris displayed an impressive technical knowledge of medieval manuscripts; H.M. Hyndman claimed that Morris possessed such remarkable expertise that, during a visit to the Bodleian in about 1882, he was able to identify a whole group of manuscripts in rapid succession for the benefit of one of the librarians." What impresses the reader of Morris's eys today, however, is not his scholarly grasp ofa specialized subject (though that is considerable) but his fervently held conviction that such manuscripts represent the craftsmanship of the Middle Ages at its very highest level.

There is no evidence that Morris began to collect manuscripts seriously after he left Oxford, though in October 1858 he wrote from France that he was buying 'old manuscripts and armour and ironwork and enamel'." The earliest extant catalogue of his personal library, dating from about 1876, lists only six manuscripts, most of which he later sold."6 Nor did Morris systematically acquire early printed books in his youth, yet there are ample indications that he was drawn to them just as inevitably as he was to illuminated manuscripts. As a boy, for example, he found in his parents' library a copy of Gerarde's Herbal (1 597), which was to influence the floral patterns of his wallpapers and tapestries and which he was to consult again in 1875 when he studied the recipes of vegetable dyes. Greater pleasures lay ahead: during their undergraduate years Morris and Burne-Jones were introduced to the delights of fifteenth- and sixteenth-century wood-engravings. 'Two luminaries burst upon us at that time,' Burne-Jones remarked. 'One was Chaucer and the other was Dürer."7 Morris later felt vaguely embarrassed about his admiration for Dürer, who worked a century too late to be regarded as a medieval artist, but his ingenious solution to this problem was to assert that 'though his [Dürer's] method was infected by the Renaissançe, his matchless imagination and intellect made him thoroughly Gothic in spirit'."8 At about the time that Morris was founding the Kelmscott Press, he was able to put together within a few years an outstanding collection of incunabula, with particular emphasis on French and German illustrated books. He lectured and wrote enthusiastically on the subject in the 189os, and it is characteristic of how Morris's mind worked that his lecture on 'The Woodcuts of Gothic Books', delivered to the Society of Arts in January 1892,19 concluded with a discussion of the prospects for improving the modern illustrated book; for Morris never approached the history of printing or book-illustration from a merely antiquarian point of view. How, he wanted to know, could we learn from the printers and illustrators of previous centuries, and how could we match their achievements in our own day? As early as 18 we hear of Morris trying his hand at 'drawing on wood and cutting the designs himself', id  Th Mai  ElfenMere gravig des Rossetti which appeared in William Allingham's Day and Night Songs.20

It is clear what Morris and Burne-Jones found offensive in routine wood-engravings of the nineteenth century. Burne-Jones summedup their indictment of contemporary work in a letter written to his sister-in-law in 1862: 'As to scribbly work, it enrages one beyond endurance. Nearly all book and periodical illustration is full of it — drawings, you know the kind, that have wild work in all the corners, stupid, senseless rot that takes an artist half a minute to sketch and an engraver halfa week to engrave, for scribble is fearful labour torender. My dear, look at most things in "Once a Week" - the wasted time England with beautiful work"The solution, Morris and his frind came to believe, lay in stern simplicity: austere outlines in bold, vigorous strokes enhanced by only a minimum of shading, which was never to be rendered with cross-hatching. Such a formula, as Morris noteworthy that ll the wood-engravings produced by Morris or done under his direction — from the first tentative experinents of 1855 to the great Chaucer illustrations of 1896 - are, in their primitive strength, more closely related to the medieval woodcut than to the post-Bewick engraving.22

Morris an Bure-Joe weeoccionally pable  respnding sympathetically tmodern woodengravings. The publication

![](images/7d5db737b56ba6c0a4ceb4ce132012b625a1ad8b21a3fdf48a71b739e03bbfab.jpg)  
Figure 14 Though Morris praised the wood-engravings of Fred Walker (such as this one published in the Cornhill Magazine in 1863), he felt that their delicate lines, cross-hatching, and extensive use of shading made them unsuitable as book illustrations.

Edward Moxon's illustrated edition of Tennyson in 1857, for example, caused a great stir in Morris's circle, with Rossetti's contributions evoking special admiration. Rossetti himself complained bitterly that his designs had been ruined by the engravers, the Dalziel Brothers, whom Morris later called 'as big humbugs as any within the narrow seas'.² Burne-Jones, however, remarked more shrewdly upon the weakness of the book when, in 1896, he compared it with the Kelmscott Chaucer: ' As a book . . . it's nothing. There was no command over the type and printing such as Mr. Morris has, and there were so many hands engaged on the pictures as to make it impossible as a book."4

This was to be in later years the great central tenet of Morris's creed - the necessity of harmony in all aspects of a book, particularly between text and illustration — but it was one which he was capable of formulating only after much unhappy experience. In fact, his interest in the book as a physical object flickered on and off for several decades. Morris seems to have been consulted about such matters from time to time by Rossetti, who drew a highly successful title-page for his sister's Goblin-Market (1862) and designed attractive bindings for his own and others' books. Burne-Jones continued to contribute occasional illustrations to books and periodicals. One day in the early 187os Morris is reported to have picked up a volume printed by Aldus Manutius in about 149o and to have remarked as hé turned over the pages, 'Ah! I wish I could get my books printed like that!'s Surrounded by friends with a professional interest in book-production, Morris, as both a writer and an artist, felt much drawn to printing. On the whole, though, he resisted the temptation: one senses that, when the Kelnscott Press was established in 1891, Morris was able to throw himself into it with suc extrordinary pentp energy because it was something he had longed to do for decades.

##

In the meantime there were several forays into the world of bookcatch glimpses of Morris working his way, tentatively and circuitously, towards the goal, as yet unseen, of the Kelmscott Press. These episodes include the unsuccessful attenpt to produce an illustrated ThEartPris   8; t and illustrations for Love is Enough in 1871; Morris's calligraphic experiments, mostly dating fron the 187os; Morris's editorship of the

Commowal in the 188os; and the last three books designed by Morris The House of the Wolfings (1889), The Roots of the Mountains (1890), and The Sory Gunlaug the Worm-Tongue81 bef e opening of the Kelmscott Press.

Morris' frst volumeo verse,The Defence  Guenvere, was pub lisheby BellandDaldy i 88nprat theCiic Press.The typography was neat and inoffensive - precisely that sort of bland mid-nineteenth-century printed page that was later to make Morris apoplectic  ornamented only by the usual Chiswick Press woodengraved headpieces and decorated initials cut by Mary Byfield. When in the following decade Morris met F. S. Ellis, the antiquarian bookseller and publisher who was to play such a key role in the Kelmscott Press, he decided to allow Ellis to publish his books; and thus Morris's early connection with the Chiswick Press was broken, not to be renewed until the 1880s.

Morris's great literary undertaking of the 186os was a massive collection of poetic tales grouped together under the title The Earthly Paradise. (Th Life and Death of Jason, published in 1867, was originally conceived as a part of it.) Morris decided, with typical bravado, to illustrate it with hundreds of wood-engravings designed by Burne-Jones. "At dinner William Morris,' William Allingham recorded in his diary on I August 1866. 'The Big Story Book, woodcut of Olympus by N. Jones. M. and friends intend to engrave the wood-blocks themselves — and M. will publish the book at this warehouse.' A few weeks later Allingham noted that Burne-Jones "'founds his style . . . on old Woodcuts, especially those in Hypnerotomachia, of which he has a fine copy'.6 At first Morris turned to the services of one or more professional wood-engravers;2 he approached also George Wardle, who engraved two blocks, 'but after that I got no more,' Wardle recalled, 'and wondered for a while why, as I thought the second was certainly better than the first. The reason was, Mr. Morris became possessed by the idea of cutting the blocks himself: and he took them all in hand and carried them through, not without some lively scenes in Queen Square. He cut with great ardour and with much knowledge, but the work did not always go to his mind. It was necessarily slow and he was constitutionally qucWardeony is firmed amusing Burne-Jones caricature of Morris at work on a wood-engraving for The Earthly Paradise: he is shown bent over the block with a look of ferocious intensity, while engraving tools lie scattered about the table and floor in splendid disarray. When a premature announcement of Morris's plans appeared in the Athenaeum, he wrote to that journal, 'The time of publication . . . of this illustrated edition must, from the magnitude of the work, be very remote'.³

Fortunately ten trial pages of the book have survived (now in the Morgan Library, though they had dropped out of sight for many years), and these give us an accurate notion of the kind of volume Morris had in mind. Eight of the pages are set in Caslon type, with a long 's' and italic side-notes (of which Morris proved to be very fond urg e elott r ears; th ae e  e called Basle Roman which was available only at the Chiswick Press. (Morris was to turn to Basle Roman again in the Roots of the Mountains and The House of the Wolfings.) In both groups of pages the titles are in Caslon, since Basle Roman had been cut in only one size, and Mary Byfield's Renaissance-vintage initials make another appearance. The format in each instance is double-column folio: the leaves set in Caslon are $1 4 { \frac { 1 } { 8 } } \ \times \ 9 { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ inches, and those set in Basle Roman are $1 4 { \frac { 3 } { 8 } } \ \times \ 9 _ { 8 } ^ { \frac { 5 } { 8 } }$ inches. Morris bandoned the book priariy because ts productin (sially the engraving of the blocks would have proved interiable. Furthermore, the trial pages reveal another serious problem, the incompatibility between the typefaces and the illustrations (not to mention Miss Byfield's initials. The heavy, bold wood-engravings overpower the text, even when it is set in the slightly darker Basle Roman.

What this unsuccessful experiment must have demonstrated to Morris was that the revival of fne printing could not be brought about by the improvement of illustrations alone. At the conclusion of a lecture delivered in 1892, Morris summarized the lesson that he himself had learne, at least partially, in the 86s 'An illustrate book, where the illustrations are more than mere illustrations of the printed text, should be a harmonious work of art. The type, the spacing of the type, the position of the pages of print on the paper, should be considered from the artistic point of view. The illustrations should not have a mere accidental connection with the other ornaments and the type, but an essential and artistic connection. . . . This is the only possible way in which you can get beautiful books.'31

Morris apparently began cutting illustrations, of which he completed about thirty-seven or thirty-eight, for The Earthly Paradise in November 1865; the project was definitely put aside with the publication, in April 1868, of the first volume of the poem by F. S. Ellis.32 Its only illustration was a small wood-engraving of three women playing on musical instruments, and scholars are uncertain whether the engraving was designed by Burne-Jones or Morris. Yet the great illustrated edition of The Earthly Paradise, though it never materialized in exactly the form envisaged by Morris, was to have a curious afterhistory. With the help of Walter Crane, Burne-Jones, who often borrowed from his own earlier work, painted in 1872, a 'Cupid and Psyche' frieze, based on some of the Earthly Paradise designs, at No. I Palace Green, London, the Earl of Carlisle's town house.3³3 In 1897 there were plans afoot to publish the 'Cupid and Psyche' section of the poem, set in Troy type, with Burne-Jones's illustrations; a contract was drawn up with Longmans, Green, and seven trial pages were printed, but the undertaking was abandoned with Burne-Jones's death the following year. In 1935 the Gregynog Press published an edition of Robert Bridges's poem Eros and Psyche illustrated by newly cut engravings based on Burne-Jones's designs. Finally, in 1974 a portion of The Earthly Paradise was published in a style that Morris himself would probably have admired. ##

[Morris] makes a poem these days,' wrote Burne-Jones in 1871, " — in dismal Queen Square in black old filthy London in dull end of October he makes a pretty poem that is to be wondrously happy; and it has four sets of lovers in it, and THEY ARE ALL HAPPY and it ends well, and will come out some time next summer and I shall make little ornaments to it.What Morris had in mind for Love is Enough w mo bi an  rtt though he certainly was now thinking on a smaller scale than in the case of The Earthly Paradise. In addition to wood-engraved ornaments and illustrations, Morris intended that the pages should be decorated with much hand-illumination. (One can see, very imperfectly, what Morris had in mind in Harry Buxton Forman's copy — now in the Berg Collection of the New York Public Library - which has an illuminated title executed by Coventry Patmore's daughter in 1889. Was Morris ever shown this copy?) Morris designed a series of ornamental initial 'L's (which are much more delicate than the initials that were to appear in the Kelnscott books) and several borders closer in style to his calligraphic work than to the Kelmscott Press borders; Burne-Jones produced one or more borders and at least one illustration. The ornaments were all engraved by Morris. Trial pages, probably struck off in 1872, betray the same weakness that beset the Earthly Paradise trials, perhaps even more acutely in this instance: the pages are set in a conventional modern-face type that seems weak and enervated compared with Morris's vigorous ornaments.³° Like The Earthly Paradise, the book was published without decoration in 1873, yet Morris had a second opportunity to realize the right typographical form for it when he issued Love is Enough at the Kelmscott Press. The undistinguished typography of the 1873 Love is Enough is in marked contrast to its handsome binding designed by Morris. The title appears on the green cloth front cover in generously spaced gilt Roman apal  ssiilar  the ettg  ubequen many of the Kelmscott Press engraved title-pages) within a striking willow- and myrtle-pattern frieze.³° Morris from time to time professeindifference to binding as aminor'art (thus greatly distress ing Cobden-Sanderson on one occasion), but this was rather a pose. He was also responsible for another publisher's binding for a new edition of The Earthly Paradise (1890), and about 1870 he designed four tools for gold-tooled leather bindings. These were used on a sketch-book owned by Charles Fairfax Murray and on a manuscript written and illuminated by Morris for Georgiana Burne-Jones.³ The ornaments on the binding of the Saga Library likewise emanated from Morris.

Notwithstanding his intense (but sporadic) interest in printing, Morris was drawn even more strongly to the arts of calligraphy and iluination. When the frst sheet The Golden Legend me from the Kelmscott Press in May 1891, he was moved to remark: 'Pleased as I am with my printing, when I saw my two men at work on the press yesterday with their sticky printers' ink, I couldn't help lamenting the simplicity of the scribe and his desk, and his black ink and blueand redink, and I almost felt ashamed of my press after all. Morris often, to the surprise and amusement of his listeners, spoke disparagingly  printng, which hecalle  tilinnvin which he said he disapproved."' Much as he might admire the ill book  theehcntuy ps Mo  tha they represented medieval art in a state of decline; to discover the Gothic world in its wholeness and freshness, one had to return to the illuminated manuscript, preferably of the thirteenth century. Not surprisingly, Morrs during the 1870s, frustrated in his attempts to produce attractively printed books, resorted with increasing frequency to the writing and ornamenting of manuscrpts.

A long, relaxed letter to Charles Fairfax Murray, written in March 1874, conveys the sense of delight Morris found in this new activity: 'I wrote a book (on paper confound it) of about 250 p.p. translations of unpublished Icelandic stories with pretty letters to each chapter, which looked well on the whole. . . . Now I am at work at an Odes of Horace whic wil make about oo pp of vellum octavo of the bi page, which makes it a heavyish piece of work; however I have written about half &done  letters. To say the truth I have a mind to try and sell a book if I could find a customer: I work much neater now, & have got I think more style in the ornament, & have taken rather to the Italian work of about 145o for a type. . . . I am very keen on the thing just now, & really enjoy my Sundays very much at it, and as you may imagine am very like to neglect my other work for it. . . . I am rather thinking by the way of trying a Cupid & Psyche for the market, painting in some of the master's pictures with it which he [Burne-Jones] has given me leave to do: for belike the oddity of a poet illuminating his own poem might make it saleable. . .  (Morris did not systematically turn his work into a commercial enterprise; many o hismanuscipts instead became delightful gifts tofriends, partic larly Georgiana Burne-Jones).

A casual glance at Morris's foray into writing and ornamenting manuscripts may suggest that he was merely following the Victorian fashion for 'illumination' that had first been stimulated by the publication of A. W. N. Pugin's Glossary of Ecclesiastical Ornament and Costume (1844). In reality, Morris, with great independence of mind, charted his own course: the similarities between his manuscripts and the illuminated Biblical texts and uplifting proverbs done by middleclass Victorian ladies for display on the walls of their parlours are not very conspicuous. Morris of course was aware of the popular revival of interest in medieval illumination that swept across England in the 186os. He owned, for example, a copy of The Art of Illuminating as Practised in Europe from tlie Earliest Times, Illustrated by Borders, Initial Letters, and Alphabets (186o), with chromolithographs by W. R. Tymms and 'an Essay and Instructions' by M. D. Wyatt; and he was no doubt conscious of the other books on the subject flooding the market from writers such as Henry Noel Humphreys (Tle Art of Illunination and Missal Painting, 1849; The Illuninated Books of the Middle Ages, 1844-9), Freeman Gage Delamotte (A Book of Oruainental Alplabets, 1862; Medieval Alphabets and Initials for Illuminators, 1861; A

Primer of the Art of Illumination, 186o), and Henry Shaw (A Handbook of the Art of Illumination as Practised during the Middle Ages, 1866). (Morris also had in his library the 187o edition of Shaw's book.) In 1861 there was even a London periodical entitled The Amateur Illuminator's Magazine, and Journal  Miniature Painting.

Morris's three earliest surviving manuscripts, dating from 1856, are written in a weak Gothic script reminiscent of the style of the parlour illuminators, but in the third - a fragment of 'The Iron Man', a translation of Grimm's 'Der Eisenhans' - there is a fine historiated initial. Though these early efforts by Morris seem undistinguished in comparison with his later manuscripts, they made a strong impression on his contemporaries. 'In all illumination and work of that kind, he is quite unrivalled by anything modern that I know,' Rossetti wrote in December 1856; Ruskin declared at about the same time that Morris's 'gift for illumination is I believe as great as any thirteenth century draughtsmans'. Nearly all of Morris's more successful manuscripts, however, were done in the period between 1869 and 1875, and they display greater sophistication in both calligraphy and ornamentation. One explanation for this development, apart of course from Morrssincematurity as an arti, is that hehad in themenme acquired four of the most important Renaissance writing manuals, all published in 1525 or 1526 and bound together in a single volume: La operina di Ludovico Vicentino, da imparare di scrivere lettera cancellarescha by Arrighi; Il modo de temperare le penne, also by Arrighi; Lo presente libro insegna by Giovantonio Tagliente; and Thesauro de scrittori, a compilation of pages from various manuals. The volume as a whole, Alfred Fairbank has observed, provides 'a remarkable collection of sixteenth-century writing and lettering manuals' and 'marks the beginning of the italic handwriting reform now engaging world-wide tentionThere  inct  dct ine  decet omMo Edward Johnston, who initiated the modern calligraphic revival, for Johnston' career was effecively uc whenSydny Cockeell in 1898 described to him Morris's rediscovery of the older hands and showed him Morris's manuscripts.

Fairbank lists eghten manucripts by Morris (not includin tial since he published his census). Naturally they vary in style and achievement, and it is difficult to examine all of then today because they are scattered about in many libraries. (The largest group is in the Bodleian.) Still, it is possible to offer a few broad generalizations about

Morris's manuscripts of 186975. All are in either a cursive which owes something to the writing manuals or a more formal Roman. This is surprising, given Morris's known predilection for Gothic hands and types; he appears never to have explained to others what reasons lay behind such an unexpected choice. It must also be said that Morris's writing, though accomplished for its period, may strike us today as slightly primitive. Early in our century Graily Hewitt, when shown by May Morris quills cut by her father, concluded that they were not well cut, and it is obvious that on some occasions he used crow quills, which do not provide a broad nib, rather than goose quills.46 The ornamentation, which was supplied by both Morris and others, is often delightful and fresh: Morris was a master of decoration, as the Kelmscott Press books were also to demonstrate. Yet, as in the Kelmscott volumes, one feels that at times the ornamentation is so out of control that it threatens to crush the text. Morris wrote on both paper and vellum; the latter was sent to him from Rome by Fairfax Murray, who posted him such a large supply that in 1891 there was still enough left to print six copies of The Story of the Glittering Plain, the first Kelmscott Press book.

That Morriskillas calligraphergrewrapilyduring the 18 c be seen by looking more closely at two manuscripts from the beginning and end of the 186975 period. A Book of Verse (1870; in the Victoria and Albert Museum), an anthology of some of Morris's shorter poems (many of which were to be published in the Kelmscott Press Poems by the Way (1891)) intended as a birthday gift for Georgiana Burne-Jones, is written in a decent but unremarkable cursive; leaves and flowers by Morris that crawl up and down the margins and penetrate the blank lines of the text. Morris's colophon reflects the degree to which many of his manuscripts were collaborative productios:'As to those who have had a hand in making this book, Edward Burne Jones painted the picture [of Morris] on Page 1: the other o the title-page,and thefgure of Spring,Sue andAutu eo diss th pttnork, Grge Warl t    an I ornament I did, tether withll the wrtg

Figure 2o 'It is to be wonderful and put an end to printing,' Burne-Jones wrote of the Aeneid (1875), ornamented by himself and written by Morris.

fornerly in the Doheny Memorial Library, St John's Seminary, Canarillo, Calif.).47 Like many of his manuscripts, the Aeneid was left unfinished by Morris, who was an exceptionally busy man and was also notoriously impatient when confronted with time-consuming tasks. But before he abandoned the Aeneid Morris had completed 177 vellum pages, approximately half the text, in a fine, broad Roman hand; he also painted some decorated initials that are as accomplished as any he did before the founding of the Kelmscott Press. Burne-Jones began designing a group of illustrations (now in the Fitzwilliam Museum, Cambridge) which directly prefigure the Kelmscott Chaucer engravings. 'Every Sunday norning', he commented in a letter, 'you may think of Morris and me together - he reads a book to me and I make drawings for a big Virgil he is writing — it is to be wonderful a   lly o to Fairfax Murray, who later persuaded Graily Hewitt to complete it.

It is difficult to assess how much Morris's experiments in calligraphy and illumination may have affected his subsequent work at the Kelmscott Press. That the experience gave him valuable knowledge of Roman letterforms is undeniable, and anyone who is familiar with Morris's handling of borders and initials in the Kelmscott books will occasionally experience a shock of recognition as he turns over the leaves of the manuscripts. The main lesson to be drawn from Morris's calligraphic achievements, however, is that, paradoxically, the Kelmscott Press (which has drawn such superlatives from bibliophiles an pintighistorians) wasndetakeb Mor ostgruigy. His heart always lay with the manuscript rather than the printed book. Among the proofs and trial pages from the Press that are treasured today in library special-collections rooms, there are numerous little scraps of paper that betray Morris's secret hankering to abandon the printing press in favour of the pen: repeatedly he experimnented with hand-coloured initials, and when these ideas cone to nothing, he printed a fewcopies ofoe of the Kelmscott books with blank spaces l u hr calligraphers. Morris was not joking, merely exaggerating slightly, when he suggested that printing was a trivial invention. It was n   it   r ,  ee  e i bookpn  witt,has tendency to be a beautiful object'.49

Between 1885 and 1890 Morris was much absorbed again in printing, this time as editor of the Commonweal, the journal of the Socialist League. The Commonweal is a typographically unimpressive periodical, set in modern face and enlivened only by a wood-engraved, ornamented title designed and cut by George Campfield and two double-column headings designed by Walter Crane. Morris himself claimed that his work on the Commonweal contributed to his technical education, for he told a reporter in 1893 that in the course of my life I hadobtaine a good deal o knowlege of type. Particularly I was much among type when I was editor of the Comnmonweal.'o

Morris's connection with the Commonveal during this decade reflects his growing preoccupation with Marxism, which seemed to Burne-Jones, Wilfred Scawen Blunt, and some of his other friends an unfortunate aberration — a parenthesisin his life as a poet and artist, as Burne-Jones described it.Yet such a view fails to take into account how deeply intertwined Morris's social and aesthetic theories were. In an autobiographical sketch written late in life, Morris reflected that, 'in spite of all the success I have had, I have not failed to be conscious that the art I have been helping to produce would fall with the death of a few of us who really care about it, that a reform in art which is founded on individualism must perish with the individuals who have set it going. Both my historical studies and my practical conflict with the philistinism of modern society have forced on me the conviction that art cannot have a real life and growth under the present system of commercialism and profit-mongering.'² The central paradox of Morris's career, of which he was always painfully aware, was that, in attempting to restore the egalitarian basis of art, he unexpectedly found himself producing expensive objects for the wealthy. The opulent Kelmscott Press books were merely the final example of this dilemma.

There was, however, one final step before Morris decided to establish his own Press. Turning again to the Chiswick Press, which had produced his earliest books, he designed and closely supervised the printing of three volumes in which we can see him moving, now with greater assurance, towards the typographical principles of the Kelmscott Press. The Chiswick Press in 1889 was under the direction of Charles T. Jacobi, a man of sensible but cautious views on the printing of books.3 Though to Stanley Morison's generation he seemed a boring old fogy, 4 at the end of the nineteenth century he was one of the most knowledgeable persons in the London printing world.

Morris evomuchhis tie  iing th Comoal,  l journal. Exccpt for the wood-engravcd titl (dcsigncd and cut by Gcorge Campfield), it was typographically undistinguished.

'My own recollection of [Morris] goes as far back as 1867, when the Chiswick Press printed the first edition of his "Life and Death of Jason", Jacobi wrote later. 'It was not until 88 that I was brought into more personal contact with him, when I superintended the printing of his "A Tale of the House of Wolfings," in 1889; "The Root  theMountains,i 89o and also the Gunlug Sagi 1891.These were all printed in the Chiswick Press special types whilst he was considering and orulating his plans for starting h wn press at Hammersmith. . . .'ss

O H Old Face and another in Miller and Richard's Old Style type; but for both this book and The Roots of the Mountains Morris adopted instead Basle Roman (with the titles in Caslon), which he had used earlier in some of the Earthly Paradise trial pages. Basle Roman, cast for Charles Whittingham by William Howard's foundry, is a darkly coloured, relatively narrow old-face type based on a fount used by Johann Froben in Switzerland in the early sixteenth century. Printing historians have usually assumed that it was first employed in the Revd W. Calvert's Wife's Manual (1854), but recent research has shown that it appears in the preliminaries of Chronicles Selected from the Originals of Cartaphilus, the Wandering Jew, edited by David Hoffman (18534).7 Basle Roman is not likely to impress most readers today as an attractive face. Why, then, did Morris use it? We have already observed the strength of his atavistic impulses: if Caslon was superior to Victorian modern-face types, why should he not attempt to penetrate still farther into the past? When Morris began to design his own typefaces, he was not content until he had travelled all the way back to the fountainhead of typography, the fifteenth century. For the moment, he had to settle for the only commercially available replica of an earlier type (except for some facsimiles of Caxton's types, one of which he was to use in Gunnlaug), even if its model was too late for Morris's taste.

With the assistance of Jacobi and Emery Walker, Morris took pains to make The House of the Wolfings 'a pretty piece of typography for modern times', as he phrased it in a letter to his daughter Jenny. Harry Buxton Forman, who happened to be at the Chiswick Press when Morris revised the title-page, was startled to see him add a second 'in' to the phrase 'written in prose and in verse' so that the line would be longer. To Forman's objection, Morris replies, 'Ha! now what would you say if I told you that the verses on the title-page were written just to fill up the great white lower half? Well, that was what happened!

# A TALE OF THE HOUSE OF THE WOLFINGS AND ALL THE KIND-REDS OF THE MARK WRITTEN IN PROSE AND IN VERSE BY WILLIAM MORRIS.

WHILES IN THE EARLY WINTER EVE WE PASS AMID THE GATHERING NIGHT SOME HOMESTEAD THAT WE HAD TO LEAVE YEARS PAST; AND SEE ITS CANDLES BRIGHT SHINE IN THE ROOM BESIDE THE DOOR WHERE WE WERE MERRY YEARS AGONE BUT NOW MUST NEVER ENTER MORE, AS STILL THE DARK ROAD DRIVES US ON. Figure 22 Morris called The House of the Wolfings, which he carefully designed, 'a pretty picce of typography for modern tincs', and wrote the sho p aly  it il ffc n t .

It is a revealing anecdote, for, even at a time when the Kelmscott Press had not yet taken shape, Morris was already displaying his horror of white space and his determination to fill the printed page, if not with ornaments, then at least with text. What is noteworthy about The HouhWois hat t ei s eu than one might expect. The long 's' of Basle Roman was not used, and an 'e' with a conspicuous oblique cross-bar was abandoned by Morris (no doubt reluctantly) in The Roots of the Mountains. The binding, paper (machine-made laid), and treatment of the prelims are entirely conventional. There was also a large-paper edition, which Morris immediately regretted because it played havoc with the proportions of the marns and  e et dr  vast whi .

Morris's apprenticeship was rapidly drawing to a close. The moment The House of the Wolfings was off the press, he saw clearly that his attempt to darken the tone of the page had been subverted by the loose word-spacing which characterized nearly all Victorian typography. 'I am very glad that you like the new book,' he wrote to F. S. Ellis. I quite agree with you about the type; they have managed to knock the guts out of it somehow. Also I am beginning to learn something about the art of type-setting; and I now see what a lot of difference there is between the work of the conceited numskulls of to-day and that of the 15th and 16th century printers merely in the arrangement of the words, I mean the spacing out: it makes all the difference in the beauty of a page of print. If ever I print another book I shall enter into the conflict on this side also.'6

The design of The Roots of the Mountains, though broadly similar to that ofits predecessor, shows one or two more daring touches. (Morris, alas, won only a partial victory in his battle with the Chiswick Press compositors for tighter word-spacing.) The most significant change is that the proportions of the margins are closer to what Morris regarded as desirable; this was achieved in part by discarding headlines in favour ofside-notes. Emery Walker, who was beginning to influence Morris's typographical views, remarked in a lecture delivered in 189o that 'many of [the] early books had no headlines. The natural way of setting type was in rectangles, and he thought it looked best when composed in that way. A headline often destroyed the effect of unity in the page, and sometimes it was quite unnecessary from the reader's point of It was a lesson that Morris learned well: he never used a headline in a book again.
