Figure 23 Morris's last typographical experiment before the oundin  lc Theory Worm-Tongue, an excrcise in antiquarianism privately printed for him at the Chiswick Press in a replica of one of Caxton's typcs.

Instead of a large-paper edition, Morris arranged for 2 copies of The Roots ofthe Mountains to be printed on Whatman paper, which he bhny  ap stock was to be used by Morris in the earliest advertising circulars of the Kelotres Suc copi weun i  specal calenared chintz manufactured by Morris and Company ('It looks so nice and such fun,' he said³3) - an experiment that must have ultimately disappointed Morris, since he did not repeat it. Nevertheless, The Roots of the Mountains represented a measurable improvement upon its predecessor, and Morris was overjoyed: 'I am so pleased with my book — typography, binding, and must I say it, literary matter — that I am any day to be seen huggling it up, and am become a spectacle to Gods and men because of it.'64

Morris's last endeavour in bookmaking before the Kelmscott Press, The Story of Gunnlaug the Worm-Tongue (dated 1891 in its colophon but apparently completed in November 189o), was more radical in nature; significantly, it was never offered for sale, and most of the sheets remained unbound until after Morris's death.6 It is a deliberate attempt by Morris to approximate the appearance of an incunable, for there are catch-words but no page numbers, the spaces for initials are left blank, and, most extraordinary of all, the book is set in a precise replica of Caxton's fourth type which had been cast by William Howard for the Chiswick Press in about 185o. Ironically, the type was created in order to replace missing pages in Caxton books - the sort of 'restoration' which aroused Morris's fury when it was inflicted upon medieval churches.6 Though intriguing, Gunnlaug was a typographical cul-de-sac, too frankly derivative and antiquarian in style. Morris, when he was true to his own theories of art and craftsmanship, recognized that we must pay homage to the great achievements of the past not by imitating them but by closely studying their essential qualities so that we ourselves can fashion beautiful objects. It is this principle that was to make the Kelmscott Press something more than a quaint anachronism.

By the time the odd little Gunnlaug was printed late in 189o, Morris was at last ready to launch that most ambitious of all his 'typographical experiments'.

![](images/d21f4cc730ec2468a94d14daa759e7f41877c806e3cd3592723318fe22b946be.jpg)  
Figure 24 The Arts and Crafts Exhibition held at the New Gallery, Regent Street, in 189o. The Arts and Crafts Society promulgated Morris's handicraft ideals, and at the 189o exhibition the Chiswick Press case displayed the Whatman-paper edition of The Roots of the Mountains, one of Morris's pre-Kelmscott attempts to find a suitable typographical form for his books.

# THREE \* FOUNDING THE PRESS

![](images/dd4365ce7e2ac8a513a9b475142e8e76b95746403195e5bb17f583411463869d.jpg)

O W much might be done by the establishment of an exhibition, anywhere, in which the Right doing, instead of the Clever doing, of all that men know how to do, should be the test Of acceptance!' Ruskin exclaimed in a letter to Morris in 1878.' His proposal (or perhaps it was only a passing whin?) is rooted in the assumption that all the arts - even the so-

called minor or applied arts - have a common basis in the human creative faculty, and that the excessive honour paid to painting in the nineteenth century was unwholesome. In 1884 the Art-Workers' Guild was formed in London in order to allow the practitioners of various arts to come together for discussion: the Masters of the Guild wore a badge bearing the motto 'Art is Unity'. Morris joined the Guild in 1888 and was the Master in 1892.

When the Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society was created a few years later, in 1887, it drew both its goals and membership largely from the Art-Workers' Guild. But, whereas the Guild operated quietly behind closed doors, the Society mounted a series of widely publicized exhibitions and energetically promoted the arts-and-crafts ideal. In the preface to the catalogue of the first exhibition (which was held at the New Gallery, Regent Street, in October 1888), Walter Crane, the president, declared: 'The true root and basis of all Art lies in the handicrafts. If there is no room or chance of recognition for really artistic power and feeling in design and craftsmanship — if Art is not recognized in the humblest object and material, and felt to be as valuable in its own way as the more highly rewarded pictorial skill — the arts cannot be in sound condition. . . ."

The Arts and Crafts Society was founded on ideals that had their origin in Morris's exemplary career (and, at a second remove, Ruskin's writings), yet Morris was less enthusiastic about the Society than one might suppose. By this time in his life he had come to the unhappy conclusion that individual craftsmen - or even groups of craftsmen - were powerless to change the fundamental structure of a corrupt capitalist society; only political action, culminating in revolution, could bring about the root-and-branch transformation that was required.³ Despite these misgivings, he was active in the Arts and Crafts Society. At the first exhibition he lectured, while seated at a loon, on 'Tapestry and Carpet Weaving', and the products of Morris and Company were amply represented in the display cases. The Century Guild Hobby Horse and many books, including sixteen produced by the Chiswick Press, were shown at the exhibition; but significantly, not one of Morris's books could be seen at the New Gallery. This was only a temporary absence, of course. The House of the Wolfings made an appearance in the Chiswick Press case at the 1889 exhibition, and in 189o six sheets of The Roots of the Mountains, illuminated by E. G. Reuter, and the Whatman-paper edition of the book itself were exhibited. Nevertheless, that first exhibition of the Arts and Crafts Society must have served as yet another uncomfortable reminder to Morris that his own books were still inadequately printed.

Morris's lecture on weaving at the 1888 Arts and Crafts Exhibition was one of a series of six talks sponsored by the Society. Of these, two dealt with the book arts: on Is November Emery Walker spoke on 'Letterpress Printing and Illustration', and on 22 November T. J. Cobden-Sanderson lectured on 'Bookbinding'. It was this public exposition of the principles of good typography by Walker which, without warning, suddenly aroused Morris's dormant enthusiasm for printing and set off a chain of events that led directly to the founding of the Kelmscott Press. The evening of I 5 November was, therefore, an unexpectedly momentous occasion: many historians have argued that the modern revival of fine printing can be dated precisely from Walker's lecture. When Sydney Cockerell who was hardly likely to underestimate Morris's contribution, wrote an obituary of Walker for The Times (24 July 1933), he summed up the argument for Walker's importance in this bold generalization: It is scarcely too much to say that his infuence, directorindirect, can be discerned in nearly every well-designed page of type that now appears, and to him more than to any other man this century's great improvement in ordinary book production has been due.'4

Walkthecuir,rsplayeo and, while still an adolescent, stumbledupon abibliographical treaise which introduced him to the history of printing; from booksellers' penny and twopenny boxes he was soon collecting examples of the work of the most eminent printers, past and present. When his father became blind in 1864, however, Walker was forced to leave St Mark's School, Chelsea, and to find employment. In 1873 he joined the firm of Alfred Dawson, the inventor of a process-engraving technique, and there Walker laid the foundation of his extraordinary knowledge of printing and methods of graphic reproduction. Walker and Walter Boutall left Dawson in 1886 and set up their own engraving business, which was to perform so many indispensable tasks for the Kelmscott Press.

![](images/fd46728aacc1265ed6f555f5cc0ec56fb1734ec368523d220222f98611876019.jpg)  
Figure 25 Emery Walker with his friend and fellow-socialist, Bernard Shaw. Walker was Morris's unofficial partner at the Kelmscott Press and to a large degree responsible for the Press's creation.

By a happy coincidence, Walker was Morris's neighbour (the latter's daughters, seeing Walker frequently hurrying past Kelmscott House, nicknamed him 'the brown velveteen artist' because of his coat) and found himself drawn into the activities of the Socialist Democratic Federation. By 1883 or 1884 Walker and Morris were close confederates in the socialist cause. The two men quickly discovered the bibliophilic interests they had in common, and the relationship blossomed. Walker'description of the friendship, though far too self-effacing, is worth quoting: 'I have lived since I was a child in Hammersmith, and since 1878 a few minutes' walk from Mr. Morris's house. Mr. Morris possessed in his fine library a considerable collection of early printed books. Mr Morris finding I had some knowlege of the practical side of printing and was thus able to explain certain details of the methods of the early printers, a subject which was beginning to occupy his mind, he invited me to visit him, which, as we had other interests in common, I was glad to do. From this time until his death in 1896 I was a frequent visitor and learned much more in buo him  teric   tg than I cul te hi of the simple elements.'

Such a modest estimate of Walker's contribution, however, does not tell the whole story. As Sydney Cockerell testified, the Kelmscott Press simply could not have existed without the technical expertise of Emery Walker. It was he who was able to tell Morris where to find good paper and ink, skilled printers, presses — anything, in fact, that Morris needed: 'the universal Samaritan, Walker, comes, from his nae, t beaidne te wa,  eo't py e a r him,' Philip Webb observed whimsically in a letter to May Morris. And it was not only Morris who was served in this fashion by Walker; until his death in 1933 he was connected with many of the most influential experiments in fine printing in England and Germany, infinitely knowledgeable adviser.

It is surrisigy ult tassss hoa  the tyrl views associated with Morris and the Kelmscott Press came originally from Walker. Both argued for the necessity of close word-spacing and litaoh acoics bot ma;

both believin the superiorityoldac types. What sees lkely is that, during those long talks in Morris's study, they worked their way together towards a radical  and at the same time conservative — understanding of typography based on the practices of the earliest printers. ach had his distinctive its to offer to the parterhip: Morris possessed a strong, intuitive grasp of the principles of desgn, and Walker had a craftsman's first-hand knowledge of how books were actually printed. Yet it must not be forgotten that there were differences between them as well. Walker collected primarily sixthe Doves Press show a decidedly Renaissance influence. Morris, of course, looked back always to themanuscripts and printed books of the Middle Ages. Their attitudes towards technology also diverged. Walke, s aprosial posraver, as fmiar wih,anwas willing to use, the latest methods ofreproduction (though, like Morris, he favoured wood-engravings for illustrations in books printed by letterpress), whereas Morris distrusted nearly all forms of Victorian technology, and it was only with difficulty that Walker was able to persuade Morris that the ornaments of the Kelmscott Press should be electrotyped.

Despite their occasional disagreements, Morris knew that he had found in Walker the gifted artisan who could help him print books at last as he had wanted to print them since the Earthly Paradise scheme of the 186os. One is still left wondering, though, why Walker's Arts and Crafts Society lecture in November 1888 had such a profound effect upon Morris, for they had, after all, long been discussing together its key propositions. Unfortunately the lecture itself was never published, but it was reported in the press, and Walker's notes have survived (see Appendix B below); what these sources make clear is that the most striking element of the lecture was a series of approxinately two dozen lantern-slides, several of which were of books and manuscripts that Morris himself owned. In a letter to his daughter Jenny, written two days later, Morris offered this account of the lecture: 'Thursday I was at the Arts & Crafts at Walker's Lecture on printing; he was very nervous and ought to have written down his words; but of course he knew his subject thoroughly well: There were magic-lantern slides of pages of books, and some telling contrasts between the good and the bad.'7 (Walker was in fact extremely shy and had agreed to give the lecture only at the urging of Cobden-Sanderson.) Walker's own recollections of the occasion also laid emphasis on the slides: 'To demonstrate my point, which was that the main factor in a well printed book was the type, I selected many examples of earlier and later printing and had lantern slides made from them. This method of bringing home a point to the audience was then comparatively novel and the lecture, in spite of my bad delivery, was generally considered a success.'\*

Walker began by demonstrating the close connection between types and handwriting, comparing several early printed books and manuscripts of the same period. When he showed a page of one of the Renaissance writing manuals owned by Morris, Arrighi's Il modo de temperare le penne (1523), his listeners paid tribute to its beauty by bursting into applause, and Walker put beside it a passage about handwriting from Ruskin's Fors Clavigera (1875) set in three Victorian typefaces. Walker's method throughout the lecture was the comparative technique that had been perfected by Pugin, Carlyle, Ruskin, and Morris: he attempted to open the audience's eyes to the horrors of Victorian typography by juxtaposing it against the most beautiful and dignified books of the past. The lecture ended anticlimactically with slides of Bodoni's type, Miller and Richard's Old Style, and a page of the Century Guild Hobby Horse, which Walker used to demonstrate the sin of excessive leading. A final page from the Hypnerotomachia emphasized again the poignant contrast between fifteenth-century and nineteenth-century typography. (When Walker delivered essentially the same lecture again on later occasions, he was able to conclude on a happr note with a slide  The House f the Wofgs.)

The publisher Charles Kegan Paul had publicly suggested in the Fortnightly Review (April 1883) that there could scarcely be a better work for the artistic future of books than that which might be done bya  eivr, eMr. WilMor great firmof typefounders in conjunction, would they des some new types for our choicer printed books' Morrs had more than once remarked casually to Walker that he would like to 'have a shot' at type-designing, but that evening of 15 November 1888, as Morris made his way back to Hammersmith with those huge pictures of early types still vivid in his memory, he turned to Walker and said, 'Let's such a proposal led at once to its corollary  the founding of his own press — and within a few weeks he asked Walker to join him in the venture. The exact form of Walker's polie demurral is not known, since there are several recorded versions of it: Cockerell noted in his diary (after a conversation with Walker) on 1o December that 'his engagements compel him to decline the tempting offer'; Cockerell lateasserted that it wasbecauseWalker had nocapital tooffer; Walker tion'" According to May Morris's amusing version of the conversation, her father said, 'Will you go into partnership with me? . . . but mind I shall want to do everything my own way." Walker's refusal was a purely technical one, for, as Cockerell said, 'he was all the while a virtual partner, and no important step was taken without his advice and approval'.3

Morris had long owned a small but attractive collection of incunabula (part of which he sold in 188o), but now, in search of models, he began to buy books from all the principal presses in Germany, Italy, France, and the Low Countries during the fifteenth century. Within a few years he put together an extraordinarily fine library: Paul Needham has declared that, if we exclude men such as William Beckford and Robert Curzon, who are known primarily as collectors rather than authors, it may be claimed that William Morris possessed a library of higher quality than any other major English literary figure'." An inventory madé by F. S. Ellis and his son a week after Morris's death appraised his manuscripts at about  12,0oo and the printed books (excluding modern works) at about £7,300.'5 Ironically, Morris never thought of himself as a collector. These books and manuscripts were merely his working library, and in an ideal world, Morris said, they would be made available to everyone - with 'a public library at each street corner'. 'I should not then have to buy all these old books, but they would be common property,' he told a journalist in 1891, "and I could go and look at them whenever I wanted them, as would everybody else. Now I have to go to the British Museum, which is an excellent institution, but it is not enough. I want these books close at hand, and frequently, and therefore I must buy them.' Morris's protests are not persuasive: despite the evident guilt about his acquisitive instincts, there can be no doubt that he found deep, almost sensual pleasure in the ownership of beautiful books.

It should also be noted in passing that Morris's library contained a substantial collection of books about printing, typography, and manuscripts.17 The Kelmscott Press is too often treated as a mysterious phenomenon, completely unrelated to the achievements of other Victorian printers or to Victorian typographical scholarship. This misconception may be seen as an indirect tribute to Morris's genius and

##

'I am really thinking of turning printer myselfin a small way, Morris announced to F. S. Ellis in November 1889; 'the first step to that would be getting a new fount cut. Walker and I both think Jenson's the best model, taking all things into consideration. What do you think again? Did you ever have his Pliny? I have a vivid recollection of the vellum copy at the Bodleian.'"

From the late 188os until the end of his life, Morris's restless mind was increasingly preoccupied with the possibility of creating handsome books of his own. Before the Kelmscott Press was actually in operation, he conducted his final experiments with commercial methods of production in The House of the Wolfings, The Roots of the Mountains, and The Story of Gunnlaug the Worm-Tongue at the Chiswick Press. In the autumn of 1889, after a conference sponsored by the National Association for the Advancement of Art and Its Application to Industry (at which Morris spoke on dyeing and Walker on printing), a small socialist group visited Glasgow in order to deliver some further lectures, and at Glasgow's Station Hotel, according to Walter Crane, Morris talked to his friends with warm enthusiasm about his plans for the Press.9

By this time Morris had begun designing the first of his three typefaces, which was to be known as the Golden type, and early in 1890 Edward Prince started cutting the punches. The technique by which Morris created his types is worth examining in some detail, if only because it illustrates his considerable dependence upon both Walker's expertise and the Victorian technology which Morris professed to despise. This is how Walker remembered the aftermath of his lecture in 1888: 'On the way home after the lecture Morris renewed the subject we had oten talked about before, of starting a small printing office from which he might issue limited editions, or as many as he thought he could sell, of his own works, and a day or two afterwards he decided upon making the venture. The first business was to get a type cut to his own design. He supplemented his collection of Early Printed books by buying examples of every fine type - gothic or roman - obtainable. These we, i.e., my firm, photographed upon a large scale to enable him better to distinguish the actual shape of the letters apart from the excess of ink by which in early printed books the shape is often obscured. . . . After a prolonged study of these types lasting throughout the year 1889 Mr. Morris began to design the models for his own letters.'"20

Given Morris's well-known disdain for mechanical devices, why was he willing to make such heavy use of Walker and Boutall's camera in designing types? The simplest answer, no doubt, is that all of us are guilty of deep inconsistencies in our behaviour and thinking. Had the question been put to Morris, however, he probably would have been able to offer a plausible-sounding explanation. He was not (he might have said) opposed to machinery per se: he rejected it only when it threatened to enslave human beings rather than lift their burdens. The camera was merely a tool — quiet, unobtrusive (not, after all, operated by steam power), unthreatening - that simplified the task of the designer. It may also be significant that the Pre-Raphaelite painters, with whom Morris was closely associated, were fascinated by photography and were among the first artists to employ it for making studies: some of Rossetti's prints have survived, including several striking photographs of Jane Morris. For more than a decade Morris had used the camera to enlarge the small sketches that Burne-Jones produced for stained-glass windows to be manufactured by Morris and Company. And of course Morris was undoubtedly much influenced by Walker, who placed the full resources of his process engraving firm at his friend's disposal. Still it is important to recognize that the Kelmscott Press, the quintessential example of an arts-andcrafts longing for the pre-industrial age, was paradoxically built upon a foundation of photography, one of the most sophisticated forms of technology in late-Victorian England.

The photographs made for Morris by Walker's firm are now in the St Bride Printing Library, London, and, as Walker indicated, they include pages of a wide range of fifteenth-century books, each enlarged to a scale of five times the size of the original. 'By instinct rather than by conscius thinking it over,Morris wrote, I began by getting myself a fount of Roman type. And here what I wanted was letter pure in form; severe, without needless excrescences; solid, without the thickening and thining of the lie, which is the essntial fault of the ordinary modern type, and which makes it difficult to read; and not compressed laterally, as all later type has grown to be owing to commercial exigencies."2" The chief models upon which Morris base his Roman type were a fount used by Nicolaus Jeson in Pliny's Hisr,u e

# LIVERE EFON fioretina: gia libe arbitrio. Et narreome elgouerno fu glifu tolto : Et couo ordine : & altre o chefu eltyranno ite dimeno ella ha

Figure 27 Emery Walker made this photograph of a page of Historiae Flti puli Vc, 17) r Mor to s am i  hs Golden type.

employed by Jacobus Rubeus in Leonardus Brunus Aretinus's Historiae Florentini populi, also published in Venice in the same year. As Morris's critics have often pointed out, his Golden type (which he first called "the regenerate type or Jenson—Morris'²2) differs from Jenson's Roman in several respects, most notably in its darker colour. Morris admitted to Walker that designing the Golden type was the most troublesome task he had ever undertaken; admirable in appearance though it is on the pages of many of the Kelmscott books, one senses that Morris was not wholly at ease in producing a typeface associated historically with the Mediterranean world rather than northern Europe. Even Emery Walker acknowledged that the Golden type was not as elegant as the Venetian faces after which it was patterned.²3

Morris emphasized in an interview in 1895 that the typefaces of the earliest printers could scarcely be improved upon, and 'my own types' he added, 'hardly differ from theirs in essentials. They are not the same, of course; but they are not altogether original. It would be impossible to obtain absolute originality where the sphere of action, so to speak, of the designer is so very limited.'²4 Morris was also to argue, with justice, that he had sucded in emulating without directly imitating Jenson's fount. And here Walker's camera came into play once more. Having been supplied with photographic enlargements of the types, Morris traced them repeatedly until he felt he understood the essence of the letterforms. The next step was to make a group of free-hand drawings, still to the same large scale. Since it is hazardous to design type (or anything else, for that matter) on a scale too far removed from its ultimate size, Walker then photographed the best of the drawings and producd prints of them to the cale f the riginal type. In at least one instance he also made aline block of a drawing in the smaller size so that Morris could see how his design looked when actually impressed on paper. At each stage Morris continued to modify, touch up, and revise the drawings. At last he felt ready to turn them over to his punch-engraver.

Those familiar with modern type-designing will recognize that this procedure is still use, with minor variations, although of course nowadays the final stage involves the manufacture of a film or electronic matrix rather than a metal punch. When the Monotype Corporation launched its great series of historic type revivals in the 19unhi an Mw co steps laid down by Morris and Walker in the previous century. What s y arg bout a th is that th  rey type-designer before Morris using the camera as an essential tool: Morris and Walker seem to have stumbled upon a technique that is now considered standard in many fields of design, and Morris managed to do this despite some unusually strong prejudices against technological shortcuts. Altogether it is a very odd achievement for a man who sighed after the Middle Ages.

![](images/91242681a245c324889a9291d533c4f4730055c5fa19d0425ae0560fcdc26b72.jpg)  
Figure 28 This group of trial designs for the Golden type reveals that Morris first traced the letterforms from photographs of incunabula, then gradually modified thcm bcforc thcy werc again photographically reduced by Walke. In theuppe let is Morristracing or drawig of the pag own in Fig. 27.

![](images/faa4a3eea6a115f7acf6372c1fa60e5bbba9dce5527a914d6516634fa957b553.jpg)  
Figure 29 Edward Prince cut the punches for Kelnscott Press types as well as the proprietary typefaces of most of the other English privatc presscs at the end of the last century.

Edward Prince, to whom Morris now turned for the cutting of his punches, was a sober, old-fashioned craftsman destined to perform a similar task for many of the most celebrated private presses of the following generation, including the Doves, Ashendene, and Vale Presss.Indeed, at the time of his death in 1923 he was the last living independent punch-cutter in England, for by then the process of engraving punches had become thoroughly mechanized. Frederic Goudy and others have suggested that Prince's contribution to the creation of the Kelnscott types has been underrated, yet it must be said that Prince also engraved types that were superlatively ugly (such as C. R. Ashbee's Endeavour face) with the same skill that he lavished on Morris's founts. Moreover we have the testimony of Herbert Horne, whose Montallegro type he engraved, that he was 'an excellent workman, but very British; and the difficulty of making him perceive the finer qualities of form is enormous'.26

Once the punches were engraved, it was possible to make 'smoke proofs', which Morris tended to carry about with him in a matchbox so that he could inspect them from time to time. Even at this stage a few letters — the lower-case 'h', for example  were rejected and engraved a second time. For the casting of the type, Morris sought the services of the respected typefoundry of Sir Charles Reed and Sons, established in the mid-eighteenth century, and now under the management of Morris's friend Talbot Baines Reed, the author of a scholarly history of English typefoundries. The actual casting, however, was done by machine; Sparling records Morris's wry comment that 'from all I hear, there wasn't much fun in it [hand-cast ing] for the poor devils who jogged and bumped the moulds about' The Golden type was completed by the winter of 1890I.

It was cut in the generous size then known as 'English' - i.e. 14 point. (The point system for measuring type-sizes had not yet been adopted in Morris's day.) Morris was to insist during the 189os that most books were printed in excessively small type: 'Long Primer' (10 point), he suggested on one occasion, was the ninimum size accept-haps,  o ere, i sSmal i ).

It is not accidental that Morris's favourite among his own types was also the largest, the Troy, a 'Great Primer' (18 point). The reduced version of the Troy type, the Chaucer — a 'Pica' (12 point) — seemed too small to Morris, and only with the greatest reluctance did he accept it as an economic necessity in the enormous Kelmscott edition of Chaucer. For a number of years Morris toyed with the notion of producing a larger version of the Golden type as well, though nothing came of the wish. He did, however, have his binder, J. and J. Leighton, employ several sizes of an enlarged Golden type (with dies produced from line blocks rather than new punches) on the spines of many of the Kelmscott books. Cockerell also records the significant fact that the first character of the Golden type was cut by Prince in 189o in Great Primer, but Morris then decided to adopt instead the English size.29

Morris was not alone among the Victorians in his admiration for the types of Jenson. Henry Noel Humphreys, in A History of the Art of Printing (1868), a work which Morris owned, had praised Jenson's first four books for being printed in 'Roman characters . . . more perfect in form than those of any previous printer' (p. I12). In the United States, Theodore Low Dé Vinne, while acknowledging in 1886 that Jenson's letters might appear 'rude and coarse' to modern eyes, was still able to commend the simplicity and beauty of his design'³ Talbot Baines Reed, when lecturing to the Society of Arts in April 1890, showed a slide of one of Jenson's books and discussed his types in complimentary terms.' Horatio F. Brown's The Venetian Printing Press 1469180o (1891), published just as the Kelmscott Press was being launched, offered a detailed and mostly admiring account of Jenson's work as a printer. Emery Walker, it goes without saying, held Jenson's typography in high esteem and turned to it again for inspiration when he subsequently designed the Doves Press type. Morris himself remarked that, ´when it is so obvious that this is the best and clearest Roman type yet struck, it seems a pity that we should make our starting point for a possible new departure at any period worse than the best'.3² (This is undoubtedly a reference to Miller and Richard's Old Style, which was a pallid imitation of Caslon.)

The Golden type was not, in its final form, a mere facsimile of Jenson's fount: Morris insisted that he 'did not copy it servilely', and Walker admitted that Morris took only 'the general proportions' ofhis type from Jenson and Rubeus. 3 The chief modifications introduced by Morris were to thicken the strokes and make the serifs more slab-like, producing a printed page that was much darker than Victorian readers were accustoned to. The heavy, bold Golden type represents his reaction to the anaemic typography of late-nineteenth-century England, and it also reflects his search for a typeface that would match the broad lines of wood-engravings cut in the medieval style. 'Crane when he saw it beside Jenson thought it more Gothic-looking: this is a fact, and a cheerful one to me,' Morris said in a letter to Ellis.34 Another anachronistic quality of Morris's fount is that he did not design an italic alphabet or certain other characters that most modern printers would regard as indispensable (e.g. brackets, dashes, small capitals, bold face). This decision was to produce a grand simplicity and uniformity of texture in the Kelmscott Press pages, but at the same time the lack of an italic made it almost impossible for Morris to print any editorial apparatus such as footnotes or bibliographies (which he was reluctant to include in his books for other reasons as well). One notable departure from the practice of early printers was Morris's avoidance of abbreviations and ligatures — except of course for the ualones, f' 'f , f f,  and ' Writig in Septer 1890 to Ellis about The Golden Legend, which was then envisioned as the first Kelmscott book, Morris declared, 'In one thing I think I differ from you a little, i.e., about the joined letters or queer signs:since our book is to be a reprint, not a fac-simile, I do not think that we need reproduce these: indeed I should extend the abbreviations in order to make the book more readable.'3s

Morris's Golden type evoked a mixed response. Professional printers were quick to observe that, because of the great breadth of the vial t    i  woy unsuitable for books produced under commercial conditions. The adjective used by De Vinne in writing about Jenson - coarse copeut   oly the lg biquitous, ifeitatisMordn, ofhar a  . Sany Mon,  xap, to the Golden type in private as 'positively foul', and in a famous essay i T  Ty hs chie at ttupon Jo' pal et Examined individually, the Golden type characters, especially the capitals, do indeed seem heavy and stiff, yet they fit well together page of a Kelnscott book is undeniably attractive. If the Roman type ofthe Doves Press, created only a few years later and based on the same model, strikes many of us today as a more satisfactory adaptation o Je hGy with the enerous tolerance that is accorded a pioneering effrt.

Having produced a Roman type more or less to his satisfaction, Morris — even before his Press was in operation — began to lay plans for a Gothic type as well. Morris 'talks of setting to work at once on ablackletter fount! Cockere recorded with astonishment in his diary on  February 1891. It was not in fact a surprising decision, for Morris's deepest aesthetic instincts always drew him back to medieval forns, and his own library was filled with the finest German and French illustrated books of the fifteenth century, mostly printed in Gothic types. Lecturing on 'The Ideal Book' in 1893, Morris made a strong case for the possibility of reviving blackletter: 'I have hitherto had a fine and generous Roman type in my mind, but after all, a certain amount of variety is desirable, and when you have once got your Roman letter as good as the best that has been, I do not think you will find much scope for development of it; I would, therefore, put in a word for some form of Gothic letter for use in our improved printed book.'37

Morris acknowledged that such a proposal would 'startle' some of his listeners; in another lecture delivered a few months later he offered the even more startling suggestion that 'if he had his own way he would have all books printed in it [i.e. Gothic type]'38 This may be a characteristic piece of Morrisian exaggeration, but it does reveal his extraordinarily strong preference for Gothic rather than Roman types, a preference based ultimately on their respective connections with the Middle Ages and the Renaissance. Here again Morris was swimming against the current, for, outside Germany, there was an almost universal consensus in the nineteenth century that Gothic typefaces were dead. In The Printer's Grammar (18o8) Caleb Stower offered this pious graveside homily: 'We may reasonably hope, from the pleasing aspect of the present age, when mankind have dared to burst the fetters of prejudice and antiquity, and have determined to exercise their better judgement, and adopt plans more congenial to true taste, that the Roman character will be universally enployed in all civilized states' (p. 35). The assumptions behind Stower's assertation are faniliar, because they echo the charges that were still being made in his day against medieval churches and cathedrals: that Gothic types, like Gothic buildings, belong to an earlier, more barbaric period of European history, whereas Roman type, like neo-classical architecture, is associated with order, rationality, enlightenment — with, in a word, civilization. Romanticism, the Oxford Movement, and Victorian ecclesiology were to turn such assumptions on their heads, but English typography was surprisingly late in responding to these new intellectual and aesthetic forces. By the 189os English cities and towns were crowded with examples of Gothic Revival buildings, and the Pre-Raphaelites had successfully aroused public interest in medieval styles and subjects in paintings; yet, apart from a few isolated experiments by the Chiswick Press and other 'old-style' firms, there had been no serious attempt to restore Gothic types to a central position in the world of printing.

Morris was to describe his Troy type — named after The Recuyell of the Historyes of Troye (1892), the first book in which he used it - as asemi-Gothic type designed . . . with special regard to legibility' What he attempted to achieve in the Troy was a modern adaptation of the best fifteenth-century typefaces of Germany that would not seem exotic or illegible to Victorian readers: 'the task I set myself was to redeem the Gothic character from the charge of unreadableness which is commonly brought against it,' Morris declared in the Note . . . on his Aims in Founding the Kelmscott Press. 'And I felt that this charge could not be reasonably brought against the types of the first two decades of printing: that Schoeffer at Mainz, Mentelin at Strassburg, and Günther Zainer at Augsburg, avoided the spiky ends and undue compression which lay some of the later type open to the above charge. . . . I entirely eschewed contractions, except for the "&," and had very few tied letters, in fact none but the absolutely necessary ones. Keeping my end steadily in view, I designed a black-letter type which I think I may claim to be as readable as a Roman one, and to say the truth I prefer it to the Roman.'4°

Morris found pleasure in designing the Troy type. Emery Walker recalled that 'he drew the whole alphabet straight away — more or less "out of his head" However, the documentary evidence shows that Morris went throug the e paitakng rure  tg, ng the Golden type, except that in this case Morri's fnal desig wasa freer and more skilful translation of his models. Even so, several of Morris's lower-case letters are remarkably close to the examplars he found in the books of Schoeffer $( { ^ { \mathrm { ' } } \mathrm { a } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { \mathrm { d } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ' } } \mathrm { e } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ' } } \mathrm { f } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { \mathrm { m } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ~ } } \mathrm { n } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ~ } } \mathrm { o } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ~ } } \mathrm { p } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ~ } } \mathrm { r } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } \cdot } { ^ { \mathrm { ~ } \mathrm { ~ t } } } ^ { , \mathrm { ~ } } )$ and Zainer $( \mathrm { ^ g _ { \ell } , \bar { \ s } ^ { \prime } } )$ for the Troy type to Edward Prince until late summer of 1891; the completed fount was in Morris's hands by the beginning of 1892. 'Morris had trial proofs of pages of prose and verse set up in his new modification of blackletter type,' Cockerell wrote in his diary on 14 January 1892. 'They looked extremely beautiful.' A week earlier (7 January) Cockerell had participated in a lively discussion among some of Morris's friends at Gatti's restaurant (where the officers of the Society for the Protection of Ancient Buildings - known as 'Anti-Scrape' in Morris's circle - normally retired after their comnittee meetings): 'Had talk as to whether the new Morris type was really medieval in character, I maintaining that it was, and [Philip] Webb asserting that it was nineteenth century.' The Troy type is in reality as characteristic a product of the Gothic Revival in Victorian England as Pugin and Barry's Palace of Westminster or Butterfield's All Saints Church, Margaret Street; like them, it must be judged not merely as a scholarly replica but as a splendid artistic achievement in its own right. It is of course, given its size and character, even nore gloriously unsuitable for ordinary commercial use than is the Golden type, but Morris was right in seeing the Troy as his greatest success in type-designing. Printed on handmade paper and accompanied by Morris's lively, graceful ornaments, it produces an unforgettable effect.

![](images/2834172b04e50785a7a07faa7ae254ad6b29327b22aa9cc1709db6cc1430e9b4.jpg)  
Figure 31 Preliminary designs by Morris for the Troy type.

On  he    eTrye from Chaucer's 'Franklin's Tale', and it is obvious that from the beginning Morris had the type in mind for his great edition of Chaucer. Morris may have been a crusading idealist, but he had a firm grip on financial realities, and he saw that printing the collected works of Chaucer in a type as large as the Troy was out of question. In December 1891, therefore, Emery Walker made several photographic reductions of passages (both prose and poetry) set in the Troy type to help Morris envision the best size for his third fount. Morris wrote to Walker on 20 December: 'Thanks for the photos: which look very well indeed. I must say that I am in some doubts as to which I shall have cut, the English or the Pica; I am alnost inclined for the Pica, as it will give us more variety. Since the Golden type was an English (14 p Moall tt   p)r Ce, a decision that always left him uneasy. He was 'driven by the necessities of the Chaucer (a double-columned book)' to create this smaller version of the Troy type, Morrs said;43 the Chaucer type was cut by Prince between February and May 1892 and was ready for use in June.

There was another historical model towards which Morris was drawn, a typeface used by the German printers Sweynheym and Pannartz in three books produced at the monastery of Subiaco near Rome during the 146os. Morris described it as "an exceedingly beautiful type' and 'a transition between Gothic and Roman', and when in November 1892 he purchased one of their books, St Augustine's De civitate Dei, he designed a lower-case alphabet patterned after the Subiaco fount. It was never completed, but even Morris's discarded ideas created spreading ripples in the typographical waters, for in 1900 Cockerell persuaded St John Hornby to have a private typeface cut for his Ashendene Press: Walker photographed pages of Cicero's De oratore printed by Sweynheym and Pannartz, tracings and drawings were made. Prince engraved the punches, and Hornby, to his surprise, found that an investment of less than  roo had made him the owner of the Subiaco type, one of the most successfl of all the privatepress founts.

The Kelmscott Press began with only one proprietary typeface, but by the second year of operation Morris had at his disposal a I4-point Roman (the Golden type), an 18-point Gothic (the Troy type), and a I2-point version of the same Gothic (the Chaucer type). Because the press was an exercise in deliberate simplification, Morris found these three faces more than adequate for his needs. Likewise, once he had discovered a paper that satisfied him, he had it produced in several sizes and used nothing else thereafter.

As the contract for The Golden Legend was being drawn up in September 189o, Bernard Quaritch wrote to F. S. Ellis: 'My business will be to supply the paper, - & if need be - have it specially made for you.'\* Quaritch's assumption reveals that Morris's plans for his Press were still rather vague at this time, yet we know that he was increasingly coming round to the view that he must control every aspect of production, and that meant he must have his own paper — handmade, needless to say - as well as his own types. 'As to paper,' he declared to Ellis a few days later, "I have heard of two people who may help us, one whom Walker knows and whose mill I propose to visit with Walker almost at once; and one employed by [George] Allen, Ruskin's publisher. We can do nothing with Whatman but take wat as e eOAll' pmak h s f record in the correspondence, but on 22 Öctober Morris and Walker visited the paper mill of Joseph Batchelor and Son in the village of Little Chart, near Ashford, Kent. Morris took with him a fifteenthceury book sa sample  the kinpaper  want,  e mill he characteristically insisted upon making two sheets himself, with a fair degree of success. That evening after dinner, back in Hammersmith, Morris designed two watermarks, a daisy and a perch, the former of which he sent at once to Batchelor.48

What Morris sought was not merely the appearance of handmade fifteenth-century paper but also its permanence and freedom from bleaching chemicals. Once he was persuaded that Batchelor could produce such a paper, Morris seems to have put the subject out of his mind and never visited the mill at Little Chart again. There were, however, minor vexations before the paper problem was completely solved. The first batch of paper, used in The Story of the Glittering Plain, was too absorbent becausé of inadequate sizing; some of the early paper showed variations in shrinkage; and the initial dimensions proved unsatisfactory for the larger books. There was also an abortive experiment with a paper specially made years earlier for Walker which was so hard that its razor-edged deckle cut the fingers of the pressmen.4° Ten reams of the 'Flower' paper (16 × II inches) were delivered on 12 February 1891, and, when Morris realized that he would need a bigger sheet for The Golden Legend, Batchelor sent him an additional ten reams of a paper with the same watermark but double the original size (16 × 22 inches). This arrived at the Press on 22 April. Both versions of the paper continued to be used for a time, the smaller size making its final appearance in The Order of Chivalry (1893). (That book is rather a bibliographical oddity. The second halfof the volume, L'Ordene de chevalerie, was printed on the larger Flower paper; hence the first part of the book is a quarto and the second an octavo.) The two additional sizes of Batchelor's paper that Morris eventually adopted are identical to the Flower except for their dinensions, thickness (the smaller sheets being thinner), and watermarks. Morrs: 'I find that other makers are initating our Antique Handmade paper. For our protection, and as a means of giving my friends a guaranteed genuine article, I propose calling the paper the Kelmscott Handmade, subject, of course, to your approval. This does not apply to watermarking in any way, but to the wrapping and labelling of the paper. Morris gave his permission, and thereafter the Batchelor paper was used by many of the major private presses (beginning with the Doves Press) and by a few commercial printers for de luxe editions. The Kelmscott Press also, as Batchelor's letter suggests, inspired a fashion for thick, coarse, machine-made papers which mimicked the most obvious qualities of the Batchelor product, but such palpable imitations misled only the least sophisticated reader, and most have lon sinc iscooure n cubd. TheKelsott re pa tehanas ot u nqu am u recreation of the finest papers that had ever been used in books.

Morris's search for a good handmade paper was directly linked to his decision to print books on a hand-press. The Golden Legend contract (1 1 September 189o) specified merely that Morris was to have control over the 'selection of the printer', and Sparling asserts that Morris's original intention was to have only the composition of the Kelmscott books done at Hammersmith, with the presswork to be supervised by Emery Walker at his office, No. 16 Clifford's Inn, near Fleet Street. But by January 1891 Morris owned his first press, a 'Deny Albion Press, complete with Cylinder Inking Table', for which he paid W. H. Bowden, subsequently his foreman, £,25.53 When work on T 'One Super Royal Genuine Albion Press', for £42 10s. on 26 November 1891 from Hopkinson and Cope.4 Before his death he purchased two additional presses: a small Albion which was used for printing leaflets and proofing, and a full-size Albion which was acquired from Hopkinson and Cope for £52 1os. at the end of 1894. Reinforced with steel bands, the latter was used for printing the Ce

##

With an Albion press, his own typeface, and excellent paper from Batchelor, by January 1891 Morris had gathered together most of the key ingredients he would need at the Kelnscott Press. In the corresponc f Jan Morris wih Wilrid Scawen Blunt uri th period, we hear occasional gossip about the Press in gestation. 'My husband . . . does not begin tillafter Xmas — when he will first print his "News from Nowhere". if it succeeds I believe he contemplates bringing forth quartos and folios in large numbers,' she wrote on 4 December 1890.56 (News from Nowhere (1892) was to be the twelfth book printed at the Kelmscott Press.) And on 29 December: 'he has not started yet, but will soon. he has taken a little place where he can set up the press.' On 3o January she announced: 'The printing is not yet started, some of the letters had to be re-cut, and there were various little delays. the press is in its place at last, and the type has arrived and been pronounced satisfactory so the printing will really begin. the paper is quite lovely, I shall write you next on a sheet of it. . . . we shall call the press the "Kelmscott Press"

The name was symbolically important to Morris, for it linked his Press with Kelmscott Manor, the beautiful sixteenth-century house in south-western Oxfordshire of which Morris had been a tenant since 1871 and to which he was deeply attached. Sydney Cockerell, while visiting Kelmscott, recorded in his diary on 10 August 1892: 'W.M. speculating as to whether the Kelmscott Press could not be moved from Hammers[mith] and set up in the meadow here!' The exclamation point always appears in Cockerell's diary when he is reporting Morris's most outrageous proposals: in this instance both he and Morris must have known that shifting the Press to a remote village on the edge of the Cotswolds was not really possible. C. R. Ashbee was to attempt something like this with his Essex House Press, and the result was not encouraging. The Kelmscott Press, then, would have to remain in Hammersmith, on the western outskirts of London, but the pleasant riverside atmosphere of Hammersmith's Upper Mall offered an attractive alternative to rural life which had the important advantage of proximity to the craftsmen who would be essential to the success of the Press. In fact, three of them - Walker, Cobden-Sanderson, and W. H. Hooper (the chief wood-engraver of the Press) — were only a few steps away from Morris's home, Kelmscott House; to no one's surprise, therefore, Morris chose to establish the Kelmscott Press at 16 Upper Mall, a brick Georgian cottage merely a few yards from his own front door, which he rented from 12 January 1891.

Sparling's description of the building makes it clear that, in this earliest phase, the Kelmscott Press was bursting at the seams: 'One upstairs room of the cottage was fitted with racks, cases, imposingstone, etc.; another housed the single press, bought secondhand; the ooms n ondfoor etilizd  stoTh u rent book discloses that Morris paid a rent of 8s. 6d. per week and that he gave up his tenancy on I3 June of the same year.\* In the meantime Morris had acquired the lease of Sussex Cottage, 14 Upper Mall, which was attached to No. 16, Sussex House, used by Walker and Boutall. Cockerell, by coincidence, visited Sussex Cottage for the first time on 4 April 1891, when the colophon for The Glittering Plain was being printed, and left this account in his diary: 'Emery Walker took me to see the Kelmscott Press, in which the Glittering Plain, the first book to be issued from it, is now being printed from Morrs's new type. . . . Exceedingly interesting. The house a little old fashioned one and the single hand press at the top of a winding corner stair, the compositor being in a little room leading out of that in which the press is. Printed sheets, one on vellum, lying about — all most beautiful, especially the first page with its elaborately designed border.' Sussex Cottage remained the chiefhome of the Kelmscott Press until it closed, although another cottage, 21 Upper Mall, was leased in January 1895 to house the large Albion that was used for the Chaucer.

![](images/75b601afdf3e45a6fb51f07010ff2a91c77af309213d194f67712293eb16b1dd.jpg)  
Figure 33 A nineteenth-century photograph of Sussex House in Hammersmith, occupied by Emery Walker's firm. Thc smaller entrancc on the left is to Sussex Cottage, which was the chiefhomc of the Kclmscott Press.

On 3 January 1891 Morris took the final decisive step in founding the Kelmscott Press. To William Bowden, a retired master-printer who had printed News from Nowhere for Reeves and Turner, he wrote: 'Mr. Reeves tells me that you would perhaps engage yourself to me as compositor & press-printer in the little typographical adventure I am planning. I should be glad to see you about the matter: could you call on me here?'o Did Morris really think that one man could print the large books he had in mind? Soon Bowden's daughter (Mrs Pine) and son (William Henry Bowden) joined their father in Upper Mall, Hammersmith, and the staff gradually grew as Morris's plans for printing expanded. We have Sparling's word that Morris himself never set a line of type, but, as in his earlier craft revivals, he asked questions constantly, peered over the shoulders of his printers, and familiarized himself with all the technical aspects of printing. Despite increasing ill health, Morris was in exuberant good spirits during the early months of 1891 as he watched his old dream of producing beautiful books begin to take substance. W. H. Bowden has left this lively picture of Morris at the time: 'When the type came in from the founders, he was very anxious to help lay it in the cases; but not having served his time to the business, more often than not put the type into the wrong box. It was very amusing to hear him saying to himself: There, bother it; in the wrong box again!" But he was perfectly good-humoured, and presently ran off and came back, bustling up the path — and in my mind's eye I can see him now - without a hat, and with a bottle of wine under each arm, with which to drink the health of the Kelmscott Press."

The first delivery of paper from Batchelor arrived on 27 January, and on the last day of the month the elder Bowden printed for Morris several versions of a trial page of The Story of the Glittering Plain. Enclosing one of the trials in a letter to Ellis a few days later, Morris exulted, 'I don't know what you will think of it; but I think it precious good.'" He was now impatient to get a book from his Press into print as quickly as possible. Morris had originally intended that The Golden Legend should be the first title, but the small sheets supplied by Batchelor seemed more suitable for his Glittering Plain, a book of modest size. Unwilling even to wait for some illustrations that Walter Crane had promised for The Glittering Plain, he plunged ahead with an unillustrated quarto.

Meanwhile, rumours about Morris's Press began to find their way into print. One of the earliest notices was this item in the 'Literary Gossip' column of the Athenaeum on 21 February 1891: 'Mr. William Morris is getting his press into working order. The printing of "The Golden Legend" will be preceded by that of the romantic story first published in the English Illustrated Magazine under the name of"The Glittering Plain." Á very limited number will be printed as the first issue of the Kelmscott Press, by which name Mr. Morris calls his new enterprise.' There was a further reference to The Glittering Plain in the Athenaeum on 4 April, with surprising results: 'The fact is on the first announcement in the Athenaeum there was a rush to Reeves & Turner and they sold every copy long before the price was announced,' according to Walker.63

So the Kelmscott Press was to be, after all, a commercial success; not even Morris, in his most sanguine moods, had anticipated that. Yet it always remained a small, intensely private enterprise, infused with Morris's own distinctive personality and conducted in a slightly improvisational style. The casual tone of the Press's operations was evident from the beginning. The official publication date of The Glittering Plain was 8 May. On the previous day, however, the first copy of the book was actually sold - in Morrs's dining-room. Wilfrid Scawen Blunt happened to have lunch with the Morrises that day, and what then took place is recorded in Blunt's diary: 'He o

So he fell to walking up and down the hall from buttery to dais, and his wargear atd At   l ot   ll h h which yet was too husky for the squeak of a rat. So he stayed his walk and stood still, and said: "Will any man speak to Hallblithe, a new-comer, and a stranger in this Stead?"

Then the small yoice made words and said : " Why paceth the fool up and down ol d nhg   t Ravn  coki ot  c i t war-mote and the clash of the fallow-blades?"

" Said Hallblithe, and his voice sounded big in the hall : "Who calleth Hallblithe a fool and mocketh at the sons of the Raven ?"

Spake the voice : " Why cometh not the fool to the man that may not go to him ?" Then Hallblithe bent forward to hearken, and he deemed that the voice came from une of the shut-beds, so he leaned his spear against a pillar, and went into the shutbed he had noted, and saw where there lay along in it a man exceeding old by seeming, sore wasted, with long hair as white as snow lying over the bed-cloihes.'

When the elder saw Hallblithe, he laughed a thin cracked laugh as if in mockery and said : " Hail new-comer ! wilt thou eat?"

"Yea," said Hallblithe.

"Go thou into the buttery then," said the old carle, " and there shalt thou find on the cupboard cakes and curds and cheese : eat thy fill, and when thou hast done look in the ingle, and thou shalt sce a cask of mead exceeding good, and a stoup thereby, and two silyer cups : fill the stoup and bring it hither yith the cups ; and then may we talk amidstldrinking, which is good for an old carle. Hasten thou ! or I shall deem thee a double fool who will not fare to fetch his meat, though he be hungry."

Then Hallblithe laughed, and went down the hall into the buttery and found the meat, and ate his fill, and came away with the drink back to the Long-hoary man, who chuckled as he came and said : " Fill up now for thee and for me, and call a health to me and wish me somewhat."

"I wish thee luck," said Hallblithe, and drank.

Said the elder: " And I wish thee more wits; is luck all that thou mayst wish me? What luck may an outworn elder have?"

Well then," quoth Hallblithe, " what shall I wish thee? Wouldst thou have me wish thee youth?

"Yea, certes,"said the Long-hoary, " that and nought else."

"Youth then' I wish thee, if it may avail thee aught," said Ilalblithe, and he drank again therewith.

"Nay, nay," said the old carle peevishly, "take a third cup, and wish me youth with no idle words tacked thereto."!

Said Hallblithe raising the cup: " Herewith I wish thee youth!" and he drank., "Good is the wish," said the clder; "now ask thou the old carle whatso thou wilt."

Said Hallblithe : " What is this land called ?

"Son," said the other, " hast thou heard it called the Isle of Ransom ?"

"Yed," said Hallblithe, " but what wilt thou call it ?"

"By no other name," said the hoary carle.

"It is far from other lands? " said Hallblithe.

"Yea," said the carle, " when the light winds blow, and the ships sail slow."

" What do ye who live here?" said Hallblithe. " How do ye live, what work win ye?"

"We win diverse work," said the elder, "but the gainfullest is robbing men by the high hand."

"She is not, thou young fool," said the elder.

# FOUR  MORRIS AS BOOK DESIGNER

![](images/1fe7166969827eee76ee75c26f98a57e3fafdfdf37f4ab45b1af55c94999bba7.jpg)

VERYONE knows (or thinks he knows) what a Kelmscott Press book looks like: it is a massive folio printed in dense black type with large, overpowering wood-engraved initials, borders, and illustrations. Such at least is the popular notion of a typical Kelmscott product, a stereotype reinforced by the innumerable photographs of the most heavily orna-

history of printing and book-design.

In reality, though Morris did, like most designers, develop a general typographical formula, he applied it to individual volumes with great subtlety and skill, so that there is no such thing as a single completely iel  oNonh uniformity of manner, one can find evidence of Morris's growing sophistication and self-assurance as a book-designer as the Kelmscott books gradually came off the press. The ornamentation slowly became richer and the pictures better integrated with the words. Morris showed more daring in his use of red ink: at first employed abstemiously, it eventually lent colour to colophons, notes, and his printer's mark. In Psalmi penitentiales (1894) the Latin text of each verse in red was followed by the English text in black; in Sidonia the Sorceress (1893) the red notes flowed all round the edges of the black text. Even those elements we think of as Morris's typographical signature — the shoulder-note, the engraved title-page, the all-caps, unleaded title (frequently unjustified) - evolved from a surprisingly prolonged process of trial and error.

Because the Chaucer is the most famous and widely discussed work of the Kelmscott Press, many readers have assumed that it is also typical. The Chaucer's design represents a solution (a triumphantly successful solution, some would argue) to a particular set of problems of typography and illustration; but had Morris lived to complete the Froissart, another double-column folio of similar proportions, we know that it would have been fundamentally different because of a new style of borders and initials that make their first appearance in the Froissart trial pages. And of course both the Chaucer and the unfinished Froissart are, in their amplitude, unrepresentative Kelmscott books: most of the works issued by Morris's Press were octavos, and the majority of them were unillustrated. (Even the Chaucer contains many pages, seldom reproduced in photographs, with no wood-engraved illustrations and only a few small initials.) Morris undoubtedly had a great fondness for folios — he argued, rather unconvincingly, that they are easier to read because folios lie submissively and quietly on a table - yet at the other extrene he also produced four of the Kelmscott titles in the format known as 16mo (with a page size of $5 _ { 4 } ^ { 3 } \ \times \ 4 _ { 8 } ^ { 1 }$ inches). Thes are wonderfully delicate little books which beli the stereotype of ponderous, overweight folios flowing in rapid succession from the Kelmscott Press.

Another way of circumventing the usual misconceptions about the Kelmscott typographical style is to recognize that Morris's principles could be — indeed often were — applied to commercially produced books as well as limited private-press editions. Morris contended that any book, even the most cheaplymanufactured shilling reprint, could be improved by the adoption of a few simple rules: the use of old-face types, the reduction of white space between words and lines, and the careful adjustment of margins. As for ornaments and illustrations, it was better to eschew them entirely than to adopt mass-produced or unsuitable ones. Despite his ill-concealed misgivings about the prnting press as nistrment or producngniverlitery, Morris was realistic enough to recognize that newspapers, magazines, and the majority ofbooks had to be manufactured as economically as possible. Oy thal e couldt  penhe Idal Book' a book not limited by commercial exigencies of price."

We can, therefore, see Morris the book-designer in another light when, from time to time, we findhim retng with the prolems of commercial printing in the 189os. At the very moment when he was about to found the Kelmscott Press, for instance, Morris was also carrying eoatons with Bernard Quartch bout he Sa L  . In an undated memorandum to Quaritch (probably written in 1890) Morris emphasized that 'I should wish to have some controul [sic] over the pig neealt ooksIt uld say so, be of some use to the sale of them as my public expects from me a certain style.' Specifically hc suggested 'that the books should be printed in old-style type', preferably either Caslon or Miller and Richard's Old Style. 'If Caslons were used the size ought to be consquently look rathe saller or their siz. The posion  the ty te uht olct ent, d no be plastered right in the middle as modern printers treat it.'Morris proposed supplying wood-engraved initials if necessary, but 'all nt tiley et  hold wi orrismrationor slon' type was ot ualfed, u, ie W     y available that even approximated his ideal.)

There are other letters from Morris — for example, one written to the publisher Swan Sonnenschein in 1893 about the design of the pamphlet Socialism: Its Growth and Outcome\*  in which he offers remarkably sensible advice about printing books under ordinary trade conditions. In the same vein, when approached in 1894 about producing a syllabus at the Kelmscott Press, Morris dictated to Cockerell the following reply: 'Mr. Morris suggests that you should have the syllabus set up in the plainest possible manner, without rules or ornaments, (& if possible in Caslon's Old faced type) & it will then in his opinion be certain to look well.'s As Morris said on another occasion, 'whatever the subject-matter of the book may be, and however bare it may be of decoration, it can still be a work of art, if the type be good and attention be paid to its general arrangement.'6

Books have a tendency to become beautiful objects unless we deliberately and perversely make them ugly. 'I lay it down . . . that a book quite un-ornamented can look actually and positively beautiful, and not merely un-ugly, if it be, so to say, architecturally good,' he declared.' Yet however much Morris might praise the plain, unadorned book, he in fact produced at the Kelmscott Press volumes that were anything but simple and functional; and this discrepancy between his theory and practice has led to interminable disagreements about the nature of his contribution to modern book-design. Some critics, fixing their gaze on the Kelmscott books, have seen him as the advocate of a regressive, pseudo-medieval style of bookmaking that is irrelevant to contemporary needs; others, emphasizing his writings on design and typography, have identified Morris as the unacknowledged source of the great revival of English commercial printing that took place in the 192os. Was Morris, then, an escapist who misled several generations of private-press printers into believing that they could turn the hands of the clock back, or was he rather, in Nikolaus Pevsner's famous phrase, one of the 'pioneers of modern design'?8

There is no doubt, as Morris never tired of arguing, that choosing an attractive typeface and arranging the block of text carefully on the page add little to the cost of a book. What made the Kelmscott books so expensive to produce, however, was Morris's insistence upon handicraft methods, lavish ornamentation, and high-quality materials. It is not surprising if we are sometimes baffled by the nature of his 'Ideal Book', for Morris himself harbours ambiguous aims: even as he enunciates eminently sane proposals for reforming the commercially manufactured Victorian book, we sense that he is secretly hankering after the illuminated French manuscript of the thirteenth century. In the presence of such myriad-mindedness, we need not be embarrassed by our own confusion when trying to interpret the meaning of the Kelmscott Press and the ideas that lay behind it.

In examining the techniques that Morris as a printer actually adopted, it is important to bear in mind that his primary intention, when he established the Press, was to print a few copies of books for his own pleasure and that of his friends; under these circumstances, he had little choice but to make use of the hand-press, which was ideal even for the somewhat larger pressruns (on an average, between 300 and goo copies) he eventually settled on for the Kelmscott books. The slow pace of Morris's Albion presses made it possible for his printers tinspect ac sheet individually foriperections, and, by pausing a split second when the forme came into contact with the paper, the pressman could ensure that Morris's heavy ornaments printed solidly and blackly. Indeed, all those wood-engraved initials and borders, so characteristic of the Kelmscott volumes, would have been out of the question had Morris selected a power-driven press. He was of course well aware of this issue: in 1890, writing to Quaritch about the Saga Library, he urged that the large-paper copies should be printed with the hand-press, so as to get the impression black enough.

The adoption of the hand-press was also closely connected with Morris's decision to print on dampened handmade paper, which would have been impossible on a cylinder press. 'At present there is very little good paper made, and most of it is very bad,' Morris c Ou l b ut, I thk n handmade paper  ood  cn be madeJoh eor' attractive and durable) paper was a priary ingredient in the success of the Kelmscott Press books; one consequence of this is that any photographic reproduction of a Kelmscott page invariably fails to convey the superb tactile quality of the original.

Tr papers, nearly always with ragged, untrimmed edges. The earliest Kelmscott books reflect this 'deckle-fetishism' (as John Carter has cal i),ut   nt ht Mo as bk w etally rebound nd the deck trie: in a lett writtn in November 1892 which probably refers to The Golden Legend, he explained, 'The edges could be ´easily trimmed when the book is would have been a mistake I think to have trimmed the edges."Bu Morris very soon changed his mind, perhaps because he realized that most Kelmscott books were not after all being rebound by their owners, and Biblia innocentium, which was issued on 9 December 1892, was the last volume to come from the Kelmscott Press with untrimmed edges. A few collectors of more conventional tastes found this a distressing decision.As to the cut edges of the K.P. books W.M. followed the custom of the early printers, & had them trimmed,' Cockerell wrote to Harry Buxton Forman. 'I am sorry that you think this such a blenish.'"3 Even Forman, however, may have found comfort in the fact that the Kelmscott volumes were trimmed only very slightly.

Several of Morris's pre-Kelmscott books had also been issued in large-paper editions, but by the time he set up the Press he realized that this practice ruined the proportions of the margins and introduced too much white space on to the page. 'I am clean against [large-paper editions],' Morris announced in 1893, 'though I have sinned a good deal in that way myself, but that was in the days of ignorance, and I petition for pardon on that ground only." For the sake of collectors, he printed a handful of copies of most of the Kelmscott books on vellum, though Morris and Walker agreed that they personally preferred paper copies. Finding good vellum — which is made from the skin of young calves — was not easy. Morris in 189I still owned a small supply of Roman vellum which he had imported for his calligraphic experiments two decades earlier, and this he used to print six copies of The Story of the Glittering Plain, the first Kelmscott book; unfortunately he could not obtain more from the same source, because the Vatican bought up all the best Italian vellum. Sparling offers the amusing information that Morris briefly entertained the thought of appealing directly to the Pope for some vellum 'on the ground that the Golden Legend was a book in which he ought to be interested';"s such a violation of Morrs's agnostic scruples proved to be unnecessary, however, when he found an English manufacturer of vellum, Henry Band (of Brentford, Middlesex), who was capable of meeting his exacting standards. Later Morris turned also to a second supplier, William J. Turney and Co. (of Stourbridge, Worcestershire).

![](images/2114f4b3c9f0c3d8074aa05dd39840be5d2098a1010bde44e8bfa0bbdc8d38a8.jpg)  
Figure 35 When he discovered that he could not obtain vellum for the Kelmscott Press in sufficient quantity from Italian sources, Morris turned to two English suppliers, one of whom was William J. Turney.

W. H. Bowden has left a detailed record of how the vellum copies were produced at the Kelmscott Press,"7 and his description makes it clear that printing on vellum is a formidably difficult process. Until Morris's printers perfected their procedures, there was a good deal of spoilage. They learned, for example, to print always on the rough side of the vellum first, because that was where problems often arose; and the printing in red ink was done last, after the dampened vellum had dried somewhat, since the moisture caused the ink to run. One can detect also the weary voice of experience in this piece of advice from Bowden: 'Always print one more Vellum than the number required, , ul T saving.' ,

Whatever reservations Morris himself had about vellum as a substitute for paper in printing, collectors have always admired the brilliant contrast of black on white that it produces. A vellum Chaucer is undoubtedly a dazzling sight. Of course the Kelmscott books printed on vellum also appeal to the acquisitive instincts of some collectors because Morris sold them for fiveor si times the price  paper pies and because they are considerably more scarce. 'There are three complete vellum sets in existence,' Cockerell wrote in 1898, 'and the extreme difficulty of completing a set after the copies are scattered makes it unlikely that there will ever be a fourth." When John A. Saks, an American, succeeded in this quixotic undertaking, his vellum set of the Kelmscott books realized \$660,oo at auction in 1983.

In contrast with paper or vellum, ink attracts little attention from readers; even Morris had nothing to say about it in his lectures and essays on the book arts. Nevertheless,ink is a surprisingly important elenent in printing, and the range of choices available is determined a lartn y hekin   buAs th and cylinder presses increased throughout the nineteenth century, printers were compelled to use progressively thinner inks which produced pages always more greyish-looking. By selecting the Albion hand-press, Morris was able to turn to inks that were much thicker and slowerdrying  and presumably closer in consistency andappearance to the inks employed by fifteenth-century printers. Indeed, when an English publisher recently tried to match Morris's ink for a facsimile reproduction of the Kelmscott Chaucer, it was discovered that 'the original ink has never completely dried - suggesting that Morris himself never found the perfect answer to the problems which beset him in finding an ink to meet his requirements'."9

The ink difficulties proved in fact to be more intractable than the paper problem (which had been solved as soon as Morris found Joseph Batchelor), and, although it constitutes only a minor episode in the history of the Kelmscott Press, the story of Morris's search for an ideal ink is a small but illuminating example of the smoothed-over version of events given to us by earlier historians of the Press. Cockerell, typically, offers an admirably concise but incomplete account: 'The black ink which proved most satisfactory, after that of more than one English firm was tried, was obtained from Hanover. Morris often spoke of making his own ink, in order to be certain of its ingredients, t h tenton was never carr Sparng t hand, devotes more than two pages to the matter in his book on the Kelmscott Press, quotes Morris on the 'damned chemists' who have ruined nineteenth-century inks, supplies an excellent description of the ingredients and character of Morris's ink, and in general treats the episode as still another instance of Morris's rejection of modern technology in favour of the sound, simple materials associated with traditional craftsmanship. The German ink which Morris finally adopted, he admits, was rather thick, and 'in the average press-room of those days — or in these? — a little soft soap would soon have got into it . . .; [but] quickness of working was not asked for at the Kelmscott Press'. (Sparling, in his tribute to the purity of Morris's arts-and-crafts ideals, fails to mention that the German ink was made in an enormous factory with smoke billowing from a multitude of smokestacks.)

Contrast with this the recollections of Emery Walker, who first discovered the superiority of the ink manufactured in Hannover by Gebrüder Jänecke, 'having tried [he wrote] every ink maker in England without success. Many years afterwards when Morris was starting up the Kelmscott Press the question of ink came up and I advised him to use the same ink. It was tremendously stiff and very hard work for the pressmen, and the introduction into the office produced something very near a strike, and Morris[, anxious to get on having a vast prospect of books before him which he wanted to print, rather weakly gave in and used the best ink he could get in England. This turned out to be unsatisfactory, so Morris read the riot act and said if they wouldn't use the German ink he would close the Press. As his pressmen were very well paid they naturally did not want to lose their jobs and gave way. However, they took it out of Morris another way and the number of sheets turned out per day were extraordinarily small.'22

Morris's outburst to his pressmen sounds in character (though the threat of plant closure was perhaps not entirely consistent with his socialist principles), and Walker's version of the Kelmscott Press as a tense, accident-prone operation is a useful corrective to Sparig's lyrical praise of the spritual harmony of its pressroo. Sparling, Cockerell (though he is generally more cautious in his handling of evidence than Sparling), and other disciples of Morris tended to ignore his failures, to gloss over his unsuccessful experiments and his e
