of unqualified triumphs. In fact, Morris as a printer was guilty of occasional artistic and technical bungling, especially in the early stages of the production of books; Sparling and Cockerell, in their solemn, reverential accounts of the Press, tell us almost nothing about Morris's habitual tendency to muddle through, and to extricate himself in the end from alarming (and sometimes amusing) predicaments.

It was evidently in 1893 that Morris briefly used the German ink which caused so much discomfort to his pressmen. Faced with rebellion in Sussex Cottage, he reverted to the ink supplied by Shackell, Edwards and Co., a London manufacturer. (It is ironic, in view of Morris's problems with Shackell, Edwards inks, that the firm later advertised one of their products as '"Morris" Black Ink'. Like Batchelor, though with less justification, they were attempting to exploit their connection with the Kelmscott Press.²3) Morris, uncharacteristically, tolerated an ink that seemed to him second-best until a major crisis arose in the spring of 1895, when yellow stains began to appear on some of the pages of the Chaucer, then in production. With a large financial investment in the book at stake, Morris was close to panic. On 24 May Cockerell noted in his diary, 'Worry about the ink used in the Chaucer which shows a yellow stain behind some of the heavy borders,' and the same day Morris wrote (in his diary): 'Cockerell showed me the p.p. of Ch[aucer] where the ink has gone wrong[;] don't know what to make of it.'4 There were hurried consultations with the people at Shackell, Edwards (who seem to have suggested tactfully that perhaps the problem lay in Batchelor's paper), and by 8 June Morris was writing to Gebrüder Jänecke and Fr. Schneeman of Hannover: 'Some time ago I tried a sample of your black ink, at the recommendation of Messrs. Walker & Boutall, and found it excellent in all respects, but rather too stiff and difficult to work. I now write to ask whether the quality would suffer if it were made a little softer. It is quite essential to me that it should be sound and lasting and free fron any possibility of change or discolouration. I send you a sample page that you may understand the nature of the work for which the ink is required.' Jänecke shipped a softer version of the ink to Morris, who reported triumphantly to Walker on 19 August, 'The Hannover ink was tried on Friday and worked well: I shall use no other now.' All signatures from T onwards in the Chaucer were printed with the Jänecke ink.

Meanwhile, there was the problem of the sheets of the Chaucer that walre aiThrohout  r    89

![](images/e639d660d41e66297b8feda2aee83f9a05dd0cf4680dd6056377be3a882b8821.jpg)  
Figure 37 The London plant of Shackcll, Edwards and Co., which supplicd Morris's black ink until mysterious yellow stains began to appear in the Chaucer sheets in 1895. Despite this unhappy episode, the firm later advertised one of its products as ""Morris"' Black Ink'.

Morris, Cockerell, and Burne-Jones consulted every expert they could lay their hands on, even including two 'damned chemists'. The most useful advice they received was to bleach the paper in the sun, and for two months Morris's printers were periodically laying out Chaucer sheets in the garden of Sussex Cottage. Whenever it rained (and it seems to have been an unusually wet summer, even by English standards), the printers had to dash into the garden and bring the sheets indoors. Finally, in desperation, Morris rented a greenhouse. But the trick worked: 'Re-examined the 6 sheets of Chaucer . . . from which air & light were excluded (after bleaching it in the sun of Aug. 16th')], Morris wrote in his diary on 9 November. 'Examined Sept. 1oth & 23rd & Oct. 5. No perceptible return of the yellow stain.' With a collective sigh of relief, the printers resumed work on the Chaucer.

Morris never found a convincing explanation of what had gone wrong with the Shackell, Edwards ink, nor (being incurious about theoretical questions) did he seek one once he had found a pragmatic solution of his problem. There are faint yellow stains in several of the early Kelmscott Press books (notably The Golden Legend), usually behind heavy ornaments, and, as Morris's friend Jacobi pointed out, similar stains occasionally appear in incunabula: perhaps, Jacobi suggested, the phenomenon stemmed from an imperfect fusion of the two chief ingredients of ink: carbon and varnish. Whatever the reason, Morris was content to have solved the immediate dilemma, yet the price he paid in adopting the Jänecke ink is evident in a Kelmscott Press announcement of February 1896 which informed customers that, 'owing to the strength and slow-drying qualities of the ink used, the Chaucer will not be fit for ordinary binding, with the usual pressure, for at least a year from its issue'. After Morris's death, when the production of Sire Degrevaunt (1897) was rushed so that it could be issued at the same time as its companion volume Syr Isambrace, his binder found it necessary to insert a fimsy between the frontispiece and the first text page because the ink was not yet dry; one assumes that this was done with reluctance, since it violate Morris's principle that the two pages of an opening must be seen together.

Morris also used red and (less frequently) blue inks at the Kelmscott Press. The red he employed extensively for titles and shoulder-notes, though only rarely for initials; two Kelnscott books, Laudes Beatae Mariae Virginis (1896) and Love is Enough were printed in three colours. For both the red and blue inks he turned again to commercial sources. The blue (made from ultramarine ash), supplied by Winsor and

Newton to Morrs's own specifications, is beyond criticism. On the other hand, the proofs of the Kelmscott books, as well as the books themselves, reveal that Morris experimented with several reds, and the evidence suggests that he was never fully satisfied with any of them. He objected to red inks that were too pink or orange (the ink used in Blunt's Love-Lyrics (1892), for instance, is distinctly carroty), and Shackell, Edwards's red, which he adopted for a time, showed a tendency to spread on the damp paper. For this reason, the Kelmscott pressmen always worked the red ink after the black when possible.

Behind the scenes at the Kelmscott Press there were frequent experiments with inks. Proofs of the engraved title-page of Reynard the Foxe (1893) were struck off in red ink, a practice Morris never adopted in any of the Kelmscott books; more astonishingly, a set of proofs of the illustrations for The Earthly Paradise and Love is Enough was printed in gold ink, thus lending new meaning to the phrase 'Golden type'.7 Morris bought brown inks (which he did not in the end use); he printed blue inks from various manufacturers on both wet and dry papers in attempting to achieve the effects he sought;²8 in 1891 he compared the results from applying ink to the formes with ordinary composition rollers and then with litho rollers;2² for several years he tried many combinations of hand-coloured and printed ornaments, none, evidently, to his satisfaction.

This sort of restless curiosity did not extend to the Kelmscott bindings: here Morris at once found an answer — admittedly a compromise — to his requirements and never departed from it except in the Chaucer, some copies of which were issued in a special pigskin binding designed by Morris and executed at the Doves Bindery. Though allthe other Kelmscott books were bound by an ordinary (but conscientious) commercial firm, their structure and appearance were much influenced by Morris's friend Thomas J. Cobden-Sanderson, who achieved fame as both a binder and a printer. Cobden-Sanderson had been an unhappy young barrister in 1883, with suppressed artsand-crafts longings, when Jane Morris turned to him after dinner and said, 'why don't you learn bookbinding. That would add an Art to our community, and we would work together." Two days later he apprenticed himself to Roger de Coverley, a leading bookbinder, and, with encouragement from the entire Morris family (including May, who said that his new vocation was 'much nicer than being a barrister'31), Cobden-Sanderson soon set himself up in business. 'I think it would be a very good thing for you to get a bookbinding business together,' Morris wrote to him. 'I would be very glad to give you one of my Editions to do if we could get it done at a possible price.'32 Because the Doves Press was to be the most direct and distinguished successor to the Kelmscott Press, the names of Cobden-Sanderson and Morris have always been linked in the minds of bibliophiles; but in fact their fundamentally differing personalities fixed a great gulf between them. Cobden-Sanderson was visionary, idealistic, and humourless, and it is apparent that Morris, a craftsman of more energetic and pragmatic temperament, often found his neighbour on Upper Mall a source of irritation. Cobden-Sanderson's sober accounts (in his journal) of his encounters with Morris are filled with unintentional humour: in March 1885 Morris launched into an attack upon Cobden-Sanderson's prices and suggested 'that "people would go to the cheapest market," and almost as if he approved; thought my work too costly; bookbinding should be "rough": did not want to multiply the minor arts(!); went so far as to suggest that sone machinery should be invented to bind books'.3 (Though of course Morris was deliberately exaggerating in order to annoy him, there is a ring of authenticity to these remarks: the style of bindings he adopted for the Kelmscott books was exceedingly plain, if not rough.) The tension in their relationship never dissipated. Sydney Cockerell, who usually reflected Morris's attitudes in such matters, attended a lecture by Cobden-Sanderson on bookbinding and confided to his diary (2 February 1894) that it was 'over far fetched and sentimental'. Cobden-Sanderson, for his part, was capable of writing with chilling condescension about Morris after his mentor's death: 'He should have become an architect, a master builder; he became instead  an upholsterer.

![](images/cd8851841f8a7027899b5240d8c5380a182855f9d988c960c4f1750d7e40e738.jpg)  
Figure 38 The workshop of Thomas J. Cobden-Sanderson. Perhaps the most gifted bookbinder of modern timcs and the co-founder of the Dovcs Press, he was a close friend and adviser of Morris, and his views influenced the style of binding adopted at the Kclnscott Press. Cobden-Sanderson was launched on his carecr in 1883 when Janc Morris asked him, 'Why don't you lcarn bookbinding? That would add an Art to our little community, and we would work togcther.'

It would have been out of the question for an entire edition of a Kelmscott Press work to be done in the elaborately tooled binding that had become Cobden-Sanderson's speciality, but the Doves Bindery did bind books for a number of Morris's individual customers, and there was close co-operation between the Bindery and the Press. '[Douglas] Cockerell [Sydney's brother] is not exacting in point of finish, but is all the better suited to the rougher, franker work which we propose to develop in association with the Kelmscott Press,' Cobden-Sanderson recorded in his journal in March 1893.³ (Notice that by now Morris had won the argument about 'roughness'.) The real iniportance of the Doves Bindery to the Press, however, lay in its proximity, for Morris was able to turn readily to Cobden-Sanderson whenever a technical question about binding arose.

Though in the early stages of planning The Recuyell of the Historyes he worried that goat and calf skins available through the usual trade sources in nineteenth-century England were inferior and not likely to prove durable. None of the Kelmscott books was therefore issued in a conventional leather binding. Morris instead persuaded J. and J. Leighton, an old and respected London firm,37 to provide for each of his books one of two simple bindings: full vellum or quarter linen (with blue-grey papers on boards), the latter of which he described to Quaritch as  satisfactory temporary binding' The Story f the Gl tering Plain was issued in a stiff vellum binding, with washleather ties and the title stamped in the Golden type on the spine. 'I have just received from Morris the "dummy",' Cobden-Sanderson wrote in his journal on 4 April 1891; 'it is a dummy binding by Leighton, to show how he proposes to bind The Glittering Plain.' Both Morris and Cobden-Sanderson were still searching for an appropriate binding for the Kelmscott books at this point, so that the first appearance of the vellum version displays two features that were later abandoned: Morris soon decided that he preferred limp to stiff vellum (though the stiff vellum binding is in some respects more satisfactory, as it does not wrinkle and curl), and in his second book, Poems by the Way, he substituted silk ties for washleather ties.

Despite these subsequent modifications, the Kelmscott Press vellum binding from the beginning possessed a highly distinctive appearance and was widely imitated by other private presses and commercial publishers. One reason it caught the attention of readers and collectors immediately is that vellum as a material for binding books had long gone out of fashion. Joseph W. Zaehnsdorf, in the second edition of The Art of Bookbinding (189o), declared, 'This art of binding in vellum seems to be entirely lost at the present day; its imperishable nature is indeed its only recommendation. It has litte beauty; is exceedingly The simplicity and imperishable nature' of velum were precisely what appealed to Morris (who, curiously enough, was especially fond of the occasionally darker vellum binding with untrimmed hairs and always reserved such Kelnscott books for his own library). Another characteristic feature of Morris's books, one that was not generally imitated or admired, grew directly out of a dictun of Cobden-Sanderson: 'The endpapers . . . should . . . not be pasted on; they also shuld   wh e   te bok A r h pu additional sections of plain paper should be supplied to the binder by the publisher to be bound up at the beginning and end of the volume. In a ft Mo cr th pcle e furtheb blank leaves at the beginning or end of ordinary signatures as endpapers, with the result that, if the book were rebound, those signatures would have to be mutilated. There was at least one contemporary protest against this dubious practice.4' Another deficiency of the vellum-bound books is that vellum hardens with age, making it now nearly impossible to open fully some of the Kelmscott volumes.

The Kelmscott quarter-linen binding, though more unabashedly an expedient, also set a fashion among other publishers, so that for a generation or more after Morris's death English readers were afflicted with a plague of books issued in boards covered with blue or grey paper. The paper used on the Kelmscott books was a laid, handmade stock that varies slightly in thickness and colour from volume to volume; it seems to have been supplied by Leighton,4² though the advertising of the Kelmscott Press never mentions it, presumably because Morrs did not envision the quarter-linen binding as providing anything but short-term protection for a book. As in the case of vellum, the Kelmscott quarter-linen bindings were slightly modified and improved in the later books: the binding of The Golden Legend, for example, is unmistakably inferior to its successors.

One small but annoying problem whose solution eluded Morris was the proper form for titles on bindings. As a rule, the title was gilt-stamped in one of his three typefaces (sometimes enlarged or reduced) on the spine of the vellum bindings. On the quarter-linen bindings the title was printed in black ink on the front cover if the book was slender; for larger books it was printed on a paper label which was pasted to the linen spine. Surprisingly, Morris did not always use Batchelor paper for the spine labels, perhaps because it was too thick and inflexible. The label on the Chaucer, for example, is printed on the tissue-thin laid paper watermarked 'Silver Linen' which Morris habitually used for stationery and proofs, and as a result the label has disintegrated badly on nearly every copy of the book. Even on the vellum bindings there were difficulties with titles. For The Defence of Guenevere, and Other Poems (1 892) Morris arranged for Herbert Ellis, F. S. Ellis's son, to hand-letter the title in Gothic script, and, although he praised it in a letter to the younger Ellis ('I think they are very well written & look very well indeed, and for that size and shape of book look decidedly better than the stamped gilded letters'3), it is significant that he did not repeat the experiment. Likewise Morris came to realize in 1894 that titles in Gothic capitals were exceedingly difficult to read, and he thereafter used the Golden type rather than the Troy or the Chaucer type for that purpose.44

As usual, even though Morris had settled on two standard forms of binding, he found pleasure in carrying on various curious experiments. A few of the vellum bindings were stained: after calling at Kelmscott House on 5 November 1891, Cockerell wrote that evening in his diary, 'Several copies of Poems by the Way on the dining room table with covers stained red, yellow blue & green.' These books, destined for Morris and his immediate circle of friends, were not offered for sale. There were no silk ties on the vellum binding of The Poetical Works of Percy Bysshe Shelley (1894—5), provoking an indignant letter from one of Morris's most faithful customers, the Revd Stopford Brooke. 'The ties were omitted from the Shelley as an experiment, as some people don't care for them,' Cockerell explained to Brooke, 'but except in the 2 other volumes of this work, they will appear in all future books that are bound in vellum.'45 In the summer of 1894 Tregaskis exhibited in London seventy-six copies of The Tale of King Florus and the Fair Jehane (1893) that had been sent to the world's leading bookbinders.

##

Paper, vellum, ink, and bindings were all matters of much concern to Morris, and we have seen that he struggled, sometimes heroically, to find the highest-quality supplies available; Morris's fame as a bookdesigner, however, rests less on his use of fine materials at the Kelmscott Press than on the typographical theories he enunciated in a series of lectures and articles during the 189os.+6 Those theories have had an important, albeit indirect, effect on the physical form of the twentieth-century book, and they have provoked much debate among typographers, yet it is essential to recall that Morris was not temperamentally drawn to abstract theorizing. Robert Catterson-Smith, who worked closely with Morris in the preparation of the Kelmscott Press illustrations, offered this analysis of his employer's mind: 'He hated being asked questions which meant an effort at lyHeper illin r oT was enough for his mind, he did not wish to probe the unseen." By instinct Morris distrusted any attenpt to codify a set of rules for designing books.

Still, he did formulate and lay down his own laws in splendidly . I on his earlier attempts at bookmaking (including the calligraphic manuscripts), conversations with Emery Walker (and perhaps secondarily Charles Jacobi), and his firsthand experiences at the Kelmscott Preswhc as sii ther   de h most of Morris's typographical lectures and essays were written. Had Morris lived beyond 1896, it is likely that some of his principles of book-design would have been modified, or at least that his ex cathedra tone might have softened; for those principles could not always be reconciled with what he actually did at the Kelmscott Press. One wonders, for example, whether he might eventually have realized that no one typographical style is suitable for all books. Morris of course subtly modulated the Kelmscott manner for his individual volumes, but the family likeness is very strong, and even friends most in sympathy with his aims were aware that such a reductionist approach to book-design fails to do justice to the complexity and variety of literary texts. In a characteristic piece of whimsy, Burne-Jones wrote in 1893 that 'Morris vows he will print Dickens (if he lives) at the Kelmscott Press. Oh, if I could see the words "Mrs Gamp" in floriated initials — perhaps in red  thick — [here he draws a witty parody of her name as rendered in Morris initials] . . . for to be printed so finely is equivalent to beatification from the Vatican.  When a journalist put this very question to Morris — whether Dickens might not appear odd in Kelmscott dress — he replied tartly, 'I fail to see the incongruity. That arises simply from preconceived notions of the right thing, established by vicious custom.'" He then launched into a slightly irrelevant attack upon shoddy workmanship in the modern world; but the question is a pertinent one, and Morris's response is not persuasive. The Kelmscott formula would not work for every book, and only in his less combative moments did Morris allow himself to admit this.

If the chief defect of Morris's views on bookmaking was their intellectual rigidity, his strength resides in a tough-minded pragmatism and sensible grasp of first principles. There can be no greater contrast than that between his typographical writings and Cobden-Sanderson's The Ideal Book or Book Beautiful, first published at the Doves Press in 1901.° Whereas Morris is interested in how books are actually made now and how they were made in the golden age of printing (the fifteenth century), Cobden-Sanderson constantly loses his way and strays into this sort of cosmic nonsense: 'Finally, in its wholeness, symmetry, harmony, and beauty, without stress or strain, the consummated BOOK BEA UTIFUL is one in principle with that whole of life which is constituted of the world and of ourselves, that complex and marvellous whole which, amid the strife of competitive forces, supremely holds its own, and in the language of the ages writes upon the illuminated pages of the years through the infinitudes of time and space moves rhythmically onward to the completion of its astonishing story, the true archetype of all Books, beautiful and sublime.' Morris, fortunately, was incapable of such rhetorical fights. The tone and underlying assumptions of his writings are in fact closer to those of Stanley Morison's First Principles of Typography (193o, revised 195 1): both Morris and Morison see typography primarily in historical and ethical terms; both offer closely reasoned principles that are founded, so far as possible, on the best traditional practices of earlier printers; both seem to make annoying claims to omniscience because their attitudes towards book-design are rooted in an interpretation of Western culture as a whole. Morison of course frequently disparaged his predecessor (this appears to be a classic example of what Harold Bloom has called the anxiety of influence), and Morris would no doubt have found Morison irritatingly puritanical. 'Typography', Morison wrote, 'is the efficient means to an essentially utilitarian and only accidentally aesthetic end, for enjoyment of patterns is rarely the reader's chief aim.'One can hear the ghost of Morris sputtering vigorous dissent to that proposition. Yet, if one reads between the lines of Morison's brilliant little pamphlet, there are distinct echoes of the founder of the Kelmscott Press as well. It is possible to trace a direct (if not always visible) line of descent from Morris's exposition of typographical principles in the 1890s to the most influential statement of the twentieth century.

Nowhere is Morris's rigidity more evident than in his insistence uo thc  w a be wor ndn  e. The rich effec  adene, black,heavile Kett e is too often achieved at thc expense of legibility and light: such a page may create, in some readers at least, a feeling of overwhelming claustrophobia. Morris's explanation of his preference for darker coloration sounds entirely plausible; he praises the sturder typefaces of the early p  ha   gl their Victorian counterparts, and makes an effective case against the spny y hat u boksBu  h ranab umt n usps t precnal, atavistic yearning for darkness on the printed page that cannot be accounted for in purely typographical terms. The same preoccupation with deeper tones and the exclusion of light is evident in the later stained-glass windows of Morris and Company.

The imnediate source of Morris's strong convictions about compact word-spacing was Emery Walker, who already in the 1880s was arguing for a return to the practice of the fifteenth-century printers. Both the theory and practice of Victorian printing favoured placing what were known as 'thick spaces' or (at the very least) 'middle spaces' between words, with even larger spaces after colons and full stops."² Insisting that such white patches destroyed the texture of the printed page, Walker advised compositors to remove the thick spaces from their cases completely and replace them with 'thin spaces', which he thought should be adopted as the norm. By the time The House of the Wolfings was published in 1889, Morris vowed to 'enter into the conlict on this side also',yet he found it surprisingly diffcult to lter the deeply engrained habits of his printers. One can see evidence of this in the series of trial pages of The Story of the Glittering Plain which were struck off at the Kelniscott Press on 3 1 January 1891, a few days after the Golden type had arrived from the foundry. The first of these trial pages4 is, except for the use of the Golden type, a typical piece of Victorian book-composition: the word-spacing is extremely loose, and indeed after full stops there are em quads (the largest space normally used by a compositor, the width of a capital 'M'). Only in the third and fourth versions does the trial page begin to look like an authentic Kelmscott page with smaller intervals between words. Morris, in over-reacting to the splotchiness of the usual Victorian printed page, sometimes huddled the words together so tightly as to interfere with legibility; English commercial printers, meanwhile, continued in their bad old ways until the 192os, when there was a gradual but general acceptance in the trade of the Walker—Morris attitude towards word-spacing.

Morris's crusade for the elimination or drastic reduction of leading was less successful. Nineteenth-century books are leaded far more generously than most readers today would wish, but once again Morris went to the opposite extreme by trying to abolish spaces between lines altogether, thus making some (though not all) of the Kelmscott volumes difficult to read. De Vinne later offered the sensible observation that medieval scribes and the first printers probably pushed their lines closely together for the sake of economy." Morris,

The old man answered not a word, and he seemed to be asleep, and Hallblithe deemed that his cheeks were ruddier and his skin less wasted and wrinkled than aforetime. Then spake one of those women: Fear not, young man ; he is well and will soon be better.' Her voice was as sweet as a spring bird in the morning; she was white skinned and dark haired, and full sweetly fashioned ; and she laughed on Hallblithe, but not mockingly; and her fellows also laughed as though it were strange for him to be there. Then they did on there shoon again, and with the carle laid their hands to the bed whereon the old man lay, and lifted him up, and bore him forth on to the grass, turning their faces towards the flowery wood aforesaid; and they went a little way and then laid him down again and rested; and so on little by little, till they had brought him to the edge 'of the wood, and still he seemed to be asleep. Then the damsel who had spoken before, she with the dark hair, said to Hallblithe :  Although we have gazed on thee as if in wonder, this is not because we did not look to meet thee, but because thou art so fair and goodly a man : so abide thou here till we come back to thee from out of the wood."

Therewith she stroked his hand, and with however, was not inclined to compromise: 'The only leading I have all mysel e we   es h e Goh  : in a theubo bok I av   o even this in the 16mo books.' Since unleaded type does make a visually pleasing block o colour on the printed page, Morris's emphat preerence is n exampleo the confc, described by Stanley Morison, between pattern-making and legibility; and Morris, in this i the comfort of his readers' eyes.

Apart from word-spacing and leading, the question which most engaged Morris's attention as a book-designer was the proportions of margins. The usual practice of Victorian printers was to place the text very nearly in the centre of the page; Morris, with much rghous inigation, argued that the two pages of an opening, since they are always seen together, nust be regarded as a single unit, and that therefore the two adjoining inner margins should be narrower than the outer margins. 'I think you will very seldom find a book produced before the eighteenth century, and which has not been cut down by that enemy of books (and of the human race) the binder,' declared Morris, in which this rule is not adhered to: that the hinder edge (that which is bound in) must be the smallest member of the margins, the head margin must be larger than this, the fore [i.e. the outer] larger still, and the tail largest of al. I assert that, to the eye ofany man who knows what proportion is, this looks satisfactory, and that no other does so look.'8 As an historical description of the habits of early printers, Morris's statement is more or less accurate; however, real difficulties arise when, on a later occasion, he attempts to formulate the proportions more precisely: 'A friend, the librarian of one of our most important private libraries, tells me that after careful testing he has come to the conclusion that the mediaeval rule was to make a difference of twenty per cent from margin to margin.'

In an article published in 1933, Alfred W. Pollard (who, incidentally, identified Morris's anonymous source as J. P. Edmond, librarian to the Earl of Crawford) subjected Morris's views on margins to close scrutiny and was able to show that the historical evidence is more ambiguous than Morris supposed. Pollard himself, on many winter afternoons, stayed on at the British Museum after the conclusion of his official duties in order to measure the margins of 'fine books of the fifteenth-century and others fron modern presses'; he concluded that most printers, including Morris, allowed for less than a 20 per cent difference between the inner and top margins, and that thére were many other unexpected variables.® Jan Tschichold, one of the most thoughtful book-designers of the twentieth century, had also done a systematic study of the history and theory of margin proportions, and, like Pollard, offers a revisionist interpretation, based this time on the assumption that late-medieval printers arrived at their margins not by trial and error or scribal precedent (as Morris seems to assume) but by the application of the Golden Canon."

Morris's own practice at the Kelmscott Press is not entirely consistent with his public pronouncements on margins. For one thing, Emery Walker had convinced him that headlines (or running titles, as thy al wk  n  p . Morris therefore always used shoulder-notes instead of headlines. This meant, however, that his outer margin had to be much larger than his theory might indicate, and that in turn necessitated an unusually broad expanse of blank paper at the bottom of the page. On the other hand, the inner margins of the Kelmscott books are frequently so slender that they create problems in rebinding. The meagreness of the inner margin was not accidental: Cobden-Sanderson reports that ´'Morris wanted Another source of inconsistency in Morris's application of his formula to margins is that he was reluctant to continue a line of poetry on to a second line, and in order to avoid what printers call a 'turnover'he would allow a long line to drift far into the margin.

Pry in act provide  vecallenge sveral Ms typographical theories. Though a poet of some reputation himself, he obviously — as a book-designer — preferred to print prose, because it lent itself more readily to that solid, dark block of type which Morris always sought. However, his habi  placing an eormous decoraed initial at the beginning of a poem (or sometimes at the beginning of each section of a poem) produced headaches for the Kelmscott Press compositors. Since Morris preferred to set the opening lines next to the initial in capitals (and yet also wanted to avoid turnovers), his printers faced aninsolubledilemma. Morri's usual formula was to run the opening lines of the poem together, separated only by small leaf ornaments. In Tennyson's Maud (1893) he discarded most of the leaf ornaments and simply printed the first page of the poem as prose a dcision that sees  have tifd  e t Morris followed the practice in everal later volue as w

Nowhere in any poem did he indent turnovers, in disregard to usual typographical convention; for that matter, he was also reluctant to indent paragraphs in prose.

Morris as Book Designer

It comes as a surprise to discover that Morris had nothing whatever to say about the designing of title-pages. The explanation of this anomaly is that the fifteenth-century printers whom he most admired did not supply titlepages, or at least nothing beyond a simple label on an otherwise blank page. The Kelnscott Press title-pages are sometimes slightly more elaborate than that, but one can see that Morris's heart is not really in them. With no medieval precedent to guide him, Morris made the brilliant decision to supply, in many of his books, a second title-page (facing the first page of text) on which a handlettered title was engraved against a background offloral or vine

Since we have already (in Chapter Three) traced the process by which Morris designed his types, it is not necessary to linger very long over his views on that subject; but it may be useful to touch on a few points briefly. First, Morris's decision to restrict himself to the kind of fount available to fifteenth-century printers imposed limitations on the Kelmscott Press that probably became obvious only after several books had been printed. In particular, the lack of italics made it difficult, if not impossible, for Morris to include any scholarly apparatus with medieval texts, even had he wanted to do so. This may not have troubled Morris, but it was a source of constant frustration .ESc Mo hb   t, separated only by the paragraph symbol or by one of his smaller leaf ornaments, was an unfortunate decision. It did, needless to say, shape the text into the black rectangle which Morris desired, but it also disfigured the page with measles-like spots and made impossible that uniformity of colour which he himself sought. Third, the generous breadth of Morris's individual letters, which made them unsuitable for ordinary commercial purposes, likewise necessitated a broad page that strikes some readers as clumsy: the tall but narrow page which became the trademark of the Nonesuch Press book is unquestionably more gracefully proportioned.

Finally, something needs to be said about one aspect of the Kelnnscott books that has aroused much controversy: the considerable depth to which the type is impressed into the paper. Accustomed as we are now to offset printing, which makes no indentation whatever in the page, we may be tempted to conclude that Morris has taken an accidental characteristic of the hand-printed book and carried it to excessive lengths. Certainly some critics of the Kelmscott Press have accused Morris of obsessive archaism in this matter, and in support of their charge they have been able to cite a note signed by him and inserted in The Golden Legend: 'If this book be bound the edges of the leaves should only be T r im m ED, not cut. In no case should the book be pressed, as that would destroy the "impression" of the type and thus injure the appearance of the printing.' Yet the true explanation of

Morris's attitude probably lies at a deeper level. The deliberate removal of the type-impression by pressing would have been a dishonest act in his eyes, because it represents an attempt to give an artificial smoothness to a product by concealing the real nature of its material. Furthermore, we need to remenber that the unsurpassed presswork of the Kelmscott Press, which has been universally praised, was dependent upon a deep, slow impression of the forme into the fabric of the dampened paper. In short, the 'embossing' of the Kelmscott page, far from being an affectation, was an integral part of a superior system of inking.

Examined minutely, Morris's typographical theories and practices do not display the sort of monolithic uniformity that a merely casual acquaintance with his books might suggest. There are ragged edges to his ideas, and there is some cvidence in the later Kelmscott books — and still more in the trial pages of those books - that his views continued to develop until the time of his death. But, even when we have acknowledged the deficiencies and inconsistencies of Morris's work as a book-designer, we are in the end left with a large, impressive body of theoretical writings and fifty-two books which have had a considerable effect upon the books we buy and read today. Exasperating and obstreperous, Morris is none the less stilla force to be reckoned with in the world of bookmaking. At the turn of the century Emery Walker felt able to make this large claim for the influence of his friend: since the establishment of the Kelmscott Press in 1891, and especially since its close in 1898, the principles which Morris laid down in more than one article and lecture, and which were practically demonstrated in the work of his printing office, have been increasingly accepted throughout the world.'" That was a brave assertion in 1901, when Morris's reputation as a printer was temporarily ebbing, but today we are better able than Walker's contemporaries were to recognize the compelling truth of his claim.

![](images/f4ca22aa034d72b93981045bbd8a22ef0597d67cc185bdb2a6d8eefb2462a272.jpg)

ILLIAM MORRIS argued with characteristic zest that an austerely simple book, completely unornamented and unillustrated, could please the eye if it were printed in good type skilfully arranged on the page. Yet the Kelmscott books were ostentatiously decorated, with initials, borders, and smaller ornaments creeping sinuously around and within the text, and Morris's tone of voice, when he speaks of such matters, is surprisingly apologetic: 'It was only natural that I, a decorator by profession, should attempt to ornament my books suitably.What was perhaps a faint misgiving in Morris's mnd has become, in many other minds, the most serious objection to the Kelmscott Press books: that they are (according to this view) so overburdened with ornament that they suggest heavily laden ships likely to be capsized by the gentlest breeze. Another, more jocular figure of speech was often invoked after the turn of the century. The volumes of the Kelmscott Press it was said, bore an unhappy resemblance to his wallpapers.

In fact, the debate about the Kelmscott ornaments has been carried on to a remarkable extent in metaphoric language. Holbrook Jackson, who was outspoken in his reservations about Morris's achievement as a printer, offered this vivid (and, one assumes, admiring) characterization in 1913: 'The Kelmscott books look not only as if letter and decoration had grown one out of the other; they look as if they could go on growing." Beatrice Warde, on the other hand, suggested in a well-known essay that good printing should be invisible: like a crystal goblet, it is a transparent medium which encloses the wine of literature.³ Such argument by analogy has had a powerfully damaging effect upon the reputation of the Kelmscott Press in the twentieth century. Of course the 'crystal goblet' figure, though undoubtedly a brilliant image, evades some important questions about the proper relationship between text and ornament in the printed book. Behind it lies the assumption - which by Beatrice Warde's time no longer had even to be expressed - that elaborate ornamentation of any kind is not only ill-âdvised but also immoral. In the 192os the new doctrine of functionalism had persuaded many that the lingering influence of Victorianism could be eradicated only by declaring war on all historicist, non-functional ornament, whether in buildings, paintings, household furnishings, or books. The volumes of the Kelmscott Press, so thickly encrusted with initials, borders, and ornamental illustrations, inevitably found themselves caught in the crossfire of this aesthetic battle.

Attitudes towards book-ornament had been very different when the Kelmscott Press was stilltaking shape in Morris's head. During the final decades of the last century there were influential voices urging a revival of ornament and illustration (wood-engraved, if possible) that would blend harmoniously with the typefaces used on the same pages. Gilbert R. Redgrave concluded an historical survey published in the periodical the British Bookmaker (October 189o) with these words: 'It is, we think, a matter of regret that the glory of the early days of woodcut initial letters has been lost to us, and that in the place of the graceful designs which once headed every chapter we have now too often to rely upon the meaningless scrolls and flourishes which the typefounder supplies us. We can only hope that more careful study of the work of the early printers and the growing fondness for all that was best in the days of the masters of the craft may lead to a revival of the use of book-ornament, such as is to be found in the infancy of the art of printing.' In another essay with which Morris was more likely to have been acquainted, published in the catalogue of the second Arts and Crafts Exhibition (1889), Reginald T. Blomfield treated the question 'Of Book Illustration and Book Decoration' by urging the paranount necessity of concord between typeface and decorative elements: he believed, as did Morris, that such harmony could best be achieved if the ornaments and illustrations were wood-engraved. Perhaps, he hinte, the ideal solutn was or both text and illustration t blocv by a single artist.4

Walter Crane had adopted this technique in some of his books, drawing lettering and pictures alike, which were then reproduced by photomechanical means; and Morris publicly praised the results. Because he was unwilling to use process engraving, Morris himself turned to sympatheiccraftsmen who could translate the decoative designs for the Kelmscott books into wood-engravings that would not clash with his typefaces. His solution was different but the aim was al  Ti other words,' said Morris, '[that there must be] the designer of the picture-blocks, the designer of the ornamental blocks, the woodengraver, and the printer, all f the thoughtful, painstaking artists, and all working in harmonious cooperation for the production of a work of art.This is the ony possible way in which you can et beautiful books. Having designed the Kelmscott Press typefaces, Morris also assumed responsibility for all the ornaments used at the Press. The actual wood-engraving was done primarily by W. H. Hooper, assisted by C. E. Keates, W. Spielmeyer, and (for a short time) George Campfield. Both Cockerell and W. R. Lethaby have written lively descriptions of Morris in the act of designing Kelmscott ornaments: sketching faintly in pencil, he then applied India ink to paper with a brush and made occasional corrections with Chinese white.' The design was transferred to the surface of the woodblock by photography, though the engraving was done by hand. Under pressure from Walker, Morris allowed the ornaments to be electrotyped by Walker and Boutall after he was persuaded that it was virtually impossible to tell the difference between an electrotyped initial and the original printed on the same proof." (As a result, most of the larger woodblocks containing several Kelmscott ornaments, once they had been electrotyped, remained intact and were never cut apart.) This decision - a grudging concession by Morris to modern technology - had important consequences, for it meant that he had at his disposal a

Figure 42 A proof pullcd by Emcry Walkcr (whosc notc appcars on thc proof) to demonstrate to Morris that it was difficult, if not impossible, to distinguish betwecn initials printed from the original blocks and the same initials printed from electrotypes.

![](images/f9b7f6bfb02052bbc66af85734dc16edba039c1c1a5ab2fdd870be3961ab0d54.jpg)

more abundant supply of initials and borders than if each ornament had been available for use only once on each side of a sheet. A very large book like the Chaucer or a book with an unusual text like Laudes Beatae Mariae Virginis (which required only initial 'A's, since each stanza began with 'Ave') would have been exceedingly difficult to produce without electrotyped initials.

Morris's desire to ornament his books is scarcely surprising: quite apart from medieval precedent, he was impelled by an innate decorative impulse which revealed itself in every aspect of his career. As a schoolboy Morris was already embellishing his books. Both the aborted Earthly Paradise and Love is Enough were intended to be decorated as well as illustrated; Morris's manuscripts of the 187os are at least as notable for their ornament as for their calligraphic distinction. During the following decade, he was unconsciously drawing floral borders on many of the scraps of paper that lay before him at public meetings (some of the duller socialist conferences were especially fruitful in producing this sort of doodling); what is perhaps more revealing is a dummy copy of The Roots of the Mountains in which he had made a pencil sketch of a half border."

Though al the ornaments in the Kelmscott Press books were woodengraved (or, in a few instances, cast from matrices like type), Morris was fascinated by the possibility of decorative elements added by hand x . The Story ofGunnlaug the WormTongue, his last pre-Kelmscott experiment in bookmaking, was produced with blank spaces left for initials, as were a few copies of some of the Kelmscott books. There are also two surviving proofs of Kelmscott initials printed in blue with red superinposed by Morris's brush." The person to whom Morris turned most frequently for hand-illumination was Edmond G. Reuter,"² a Swiss artist employed by an English pottery firm, who decorated books in his spare time. When he submitted an illuminated manuscit to the second Arts and Crafts Exhibition in 1889, Morris was so impressed that he commissioned Reuter to provide ornaments in a TheRo hM e won at the next Exhibition the following year (though Reuter did not cople  work  e bok nt vl y later). R Morris's invitation, also decorated both a paper copy and a vellum copy of Syr Perecyvelle of Gales. By giving him these commissions, Morris hope o persude Reuter toremai in England, but in 1895 Reuter returned to Switzerland, convinced that he would find more demand for his talents there. Had Reuter not emigrated, he might well have played a minor role at the Kelmscott Press, perhaps illuminating a few copies of the Kelmscott books for Morris's special customers.

![](images/b014e52d7143b4e023cd1781c120b7185bb7b9f1dd6e656c1441b9313e4ef4e5.jpg)  
Figure 43 An accompanying note by Sydney Cockerell reads: 'These were notes made by Morris during the discussion following a lecture by him at Kelmscott Housc, Hammersmith, I think in 1886.'In thc midst of his lis cvih dee Mo' ieuenu t forms and book ornamentation.

Compelled, therefore, to use printed rather than painted ornaments, Morris found inspiration especially in the work of an early German printer, Erhard Ratdolt of Augsburg, whose books were adorned with attractive large borders and initials in a matching style of patterns on a black ground. (Morris, however, was uneasy about the comparison: 'I do not profess merely to follow precedent, or to imitate Ratdolt,' he grumbled."3) As the Golden type was being cut by Prince in 189o, Morris began working on initials, some of which resemble those in two incunabula, Günther Zainer's sixth German Bible and Johannes Friburgensis's Summa confessorum (1476)."4 ' Also I have been designing ornamental letters, — rather good I think,' he wrote to Ellis on I7 October of that year.' The first attempt at wood-engraving, however, was not wholly successful. When Morris sent a design for an 'N' to Hooper, he cut it as requested but also engraved a second version (which Hooper preferred) of the letter, with the colours reversed, and supplied a stippled background - of the kind very popular among Victorian 'old-style' printers."6 One would like to have heard Morris's comment - no doubt explosive - on this odd transformation. Eventually Hopper became more attuned to Morris's style (though disagreements between the two men persisted), and in the following year we find Morris praising him for helping out 'my trembling hand a little' and for cutting the initials 'prettily'" ockerell, calling occasionally at Kelmscott House, registered his approval of Morris's designs: 'Saw some newly designed letters with vine pattern which I liked as well as any that W.M. has done' (diary, 5 November 1891). 'Morris had . . . some newly designed capitals for his small blackletter type [the Chaucer type]  black with a thin white border [] very beautiful' (28 July 1892).

Cockerell also records that earlier that year, on 17 March, after his usual dinner with the committee of Anti-Scrape' at Gatti's restaurant, Morris, in jovial humour, passed his 'bloomer book' round the table. The book (now in the Bodleian Library) consists of numbered proofs o hi nborer whic i g tes for individual Kemscott books. Of course he continued to design new ornaments until his death; Cockcrell renarked astutely that such proofs and the books themselves 'will show the developnent of Morris's designs for capitals, as hc kecps using the new ones as soon as they are cut' (diary, s November 1891). What the 'bloomer book' also reveals is that, because many of the early initials were too dark for even his own deeply coloured types, Morris used them only once. (This is signalled by their being crossed out in the book.) Another weakness evident in the initials is that Morris sometimes designed them too large for the page. This was notably the case in The Story of the Glittering Plain, because the initials used there were originally intended for a book of more ample dimensions; but it is a recurrent problem in other Kelmscott books as well. Occasionally one hears a tactful protest from Morris's compositors. On a proof of the first text page of The Defence of Guenevere, one of the printers has scribbled this note about the narrow space to the right of a ten-line initial: 'I tried caps. here, but they fell very awkwardly indeed."8 In time Morris became more proficient in matching ornament with text, partly because he adopted

t ecty

Borders, by their very nature, are used more sparingly than ornamented initials: the same simple border appeared in the first five books of the Kelmscott Press (always on the first text page), and only later did Morris introduce a profusion of full, three-quarter, half, quarter, and corner borders into his books with splendid but sometimes confusing effect. The History of Godefrey of Boloyne (1893) must be the most over-decorated of the Kelmscott books, surpassing in this respect even the Chaucer; the other end of the spectrum is represented by the earliest books (when Morris did not yet have the resources of á 'bloomer book' at hand) and the latest ones (after Morris's death). Like the initials, the borders consist mainly of stylized foral and vine patterns in white line against a black ground, though Morris also did a few in outline style. Their obvious resemblance to some of Morris's wallpaper designs has struck nearly all readers, and the analogy is not as unflattering to the Kelmscott volumes as some wits have supposed.

On rare occasions Morris employs a border that reflects the text in some fashion, as when a rose border surrounds the opening of Chaucer's Romaunt of the Rose; but for the most part he believed that borders should be ornamental rather than illustrative. (Hence there is not one historiated initial or border in any Kelmscott book, even though Morris could have found many examples of them by ong no further than his own library.) Various sources have been suggested for his borders, including the Windmill Psalter (which he owned), sculpture in the porch of Beauvais Cathedral, and of course Ratdolt's books. In fairness to Morris, it must be said that Ratdolt's borders, while vaguely sinilar, are much less successful and more static than those in the Kelmscott books. This appears to be yet another instance of Morris improving upon, rather than directly imitating, a medieval model.

Even the most severe critics of the Kelmscott Press have acknowledged that Morris's borders are things of extraordinary beauty. The borders have inspired prolonged debate, however, about whether they distract and dazzle the eye and thus call too much attention to themselves. Morris's full borders, like Ratdolt's, reflect the classic proportions of the book page — that is to say, the border progressively increases in breadth from the inner margin to the lower margin. R. B. McKerrow justly complained that such a pattern produces an unpleasantly heavy concentration of black ink at the bottom of the page.

It is a pattern that ultimately derives from the medieval scribes and illuminators, but they had the advantage of being able to produce borders of lighter texture." These broad borders also produced technical problems for Morris: they were in part responsible for the discoloration of the Chaucer sheets, and in fact the press on which the Chaucer was printed had to be reinforced with steel bands because of the unusual size of the ornaments.

We have already seen that one of the chief decorative elements in the Kelmscott books, the engraved title facing the first page of text, was apparently Morris's own invention, since the first two generations of prnters used at most, label tites that allowed no scope or ra mentation. Morris's distinctive engraved title evolved slowly, perhaps accidentally. The first five books from the Press had only simple titles set in the Golden type, but the sixth Kelmscott volume, A Dream of John Ball (1892), included a frontispiece by Burne-Jones (the first with large Roman lettering underneath designed by Morris. Probably this gave Morris the idea of placing an engraved title (within a border, lik he Jon Balillutatini th posin, becue he rst suce appears in the next Kelmscott book, The Golden Legend.²° Of the fifty-two books printed at the Kelmscott Press, twenty had woodengraved title-pages, though naturally there were no more after the Chaucer because of Morris's death in the autumn of 1896.

Morris took pains with the lettering, even consulting Arrighi, one when he was designing the John Ball frontispiece caption; yet the Roman titles which he created leave much to be desired. Rather than resembling any of the graceful hands recommended by Arrighi, Morris handlettered titessuggest a crude version o Caslon t worst, the wood-types used on Victorian posters. They are nevertheless redeemed by Morrs's consistently fine foral and vine back-T surprisingly, are much more satisfying. Walter Crane writes that Morris 'was asked why he did not use types after the style of the lettering in some of his title-pages, but he said this would not be te ive spacs, an culd ot  ue m

Following the examplc of his medieval predecessors, Morris deth ie rr  ct ess, alhc 'Kelmscott | William Morris', was contained within a small rectangle anw   e  qar q printed on the first Flower paper, which produced a book the same size as an octavo printed on the later version of the Flower paper). It made its début in Poemns by the Way, the second Kelmscott book, and one senses that Morris was pleased with it, for he used it twice in the same volume. The second printer's mark - reading 'Kelmscott' against a leafy background rendered in outline style  was first printed in The History of Godefrey of Boloyne and generally used thereafter in quartos; Morris's drawing²³ discloses that he originally placed his name at the bottom of this design but then changed his mind and covered it with Chinese white. A third printer's mark (Kelmscott') is a long rectangular device which appears only in the Claucer. Each of the three designs blen mirably with thextureMorriyp,boreranls.

All the ornaments described so far were cut on wood; but there was also a smaller category of ornaments, very useful to Morris, that were engraved on punches and cast like type. Morris first revived the paragraph symbol, which he employed frequently in the early years of the Press, as a substitute for a paragraph indentation. After News from Nowhere he abandoned it, no doubt wisely, though Charles Fairfax Murray persuaded him to revive it briefly in Epistola de contemptu mundi (1894). Morris in effect replaced the paragraph symbol with three other small ornaments, which produced the same unfortunate effect of destroying the even colour of the page. Morris's passion for filling every white space can also be seen in the considerably larger 'line-fillings' (as Cockerell calls them) which made their initial appearance in The Life and Death of Jason (1895). Curiously, Morris never made use of the simple ornament that is the favourite of most printers, the rule — either plain or swelled  though in the proofs of The Nature of Gothic (1892)²4 there is a rule between a border and the enclosed text that was eliminated before the book was printed.

##

Book-ornamentation and illustration may seem to be two separate subjects, but Morris believed that illustrations — except in the most baldly functional books such as reference works and textbooks - should be primarily ornamental and must, like ornaments, be designed in reference to the book as a whole and to the typeface in particular. This was a subject that much exercised the minds of Morris's contemporaries, because the advent of zincos and halftones, which were printed most effectively on glossy paper, posed a new threat to the structural unity of books in the late-nineteenth century. Alfred Dawson, whose firm had introduced photomechanical engraving to England, offered a radical but plausible-sounding solution: in a paper entitled 'The Most Artistic Book', read at the Annual Meeting of the Library Association in 189 1, he suggested that the illustrations and text should be produced on separate presses and brought together only at the binding stage. The illustrations, under this scheme, would be printed on a copperplate press with dry paper; the text, set in old-face type, would be printed on dampened paper, if possible handmade.²s

But this was an admission of defeat in the struggle for unity, and Morris would have none of it. His answer was to bring harmony to a book by returning to the older method - printing wood-engraved the same paper and ink. Morris, in a lecture given a year after Dawson's, summarized his conception of the ideal book with pictures: 'An illustrated book, where the illustrations are more than mere illustrations of the printed text, should be a harmonious work of art. The type, the spacing of the type, the position of the pages of print on the paper, should be considered from the artistic point of view. The illustrations should not have a mere accidental connection with the other ornament and the type, but an essential and artistic connection. They should be designed as a part of the whole, so that they would seem obviously imperfect without their surroundings. The designs must be suitable to the material and method of production, and not offer to the executant artist a mere thicket of unnatural difficulties, prung oresul when fishev the hibtin  toure The executant, on his side, whether he be the original designer or someone else, must understand that his business is sympathetic translation, and not mechanical reproduction of the original drawing.

It was perfectly natural for Morris, given his passion for the medieval book, to prefer wood-engravings to photomechanical reproductions; as early as the 186os he had made an ambitious effort in The Earthly Paraise  restore broad splifie style engraving to its former pre-eminence. Both he and Walker were convinced that an engraved line was somchow 'sweeter' than one reproduced by a camera. And of course Morris's insistence upon the artistic freedom of the wood-engraver, who is not to obey thedictates of the deser servilely, is wonderfully Ruskinian and consistent with Morris's own billus houl  Inpch el Press, alas, all was not so simple.

PathiffyyheWi Harcourt Hooper, who engraved most of the Kelmscottillustrations. Described by Walter Crane (n 1897) as almost the only first-rate fac-simile engraver on wood left — and a good artist and craftsman besidesooper  graved thewg  rt iFr Walker, Millais, du Maurier, Tenniel, and Leighton for the Illustrated London News and Punch. A neighbour of Walker's and a committed socialist (and, in earlier days, a pupil of Rossetti at the Working Men's College), he was persuaded by. Morris to come out of retirement to work for the Kelmscott Press; but almost immediately there were disagreements between Hooper and his new employer. Morris, like Ruskin before him, believed firmly that the craftsman ought not to be merely the tool of an artist—designer. In practice this meant that the engraver should participate in the creative process by adapting and modifying the artist's design as he transfers it to the surface of the woodblock. Hooper's entire career, however, had been devoted to producing exact facsiiles of the drawings given to him. This fidelity to the design was in fact a matter of pride to engravers like Hooper: he was delighted on one occasion when Ruskin was unable to distinguish one of his proofs from the drawing on which it was based.²8

During the first year of the Kelmscott Press's existence, Hooper engraved only initials and borders, but there were even then occasional outbursts of indignation from Morris about his work, as in this letter to Walker of 4 February 1892: 'Thanks for note and proof of Hooper's cuts. . . . I think these not at all good: in fact I think them downright bad, and chiefly of use as illustrating every fault that woodcut can have. Of course I know that he can do better, and that he is doing this out of perversity. Anyhow, anybody can do them as well as this, as they are quite un-intelligent in cutting; and Leverett [who was to engrave Walter Crane's illustrations for the second version of The Story of the Glittering Plain (1894)] may as well cut them at a lower price. But I daresay with a little talking to I can bring Hooper round and get him todobetter: ifnot I must in future go toomeone else."Morris's age was usually short-lived, and Hooper was not replaced, but it is clear that the discord between Morris's ideal of the creative craftsman and Hooper's ideal of facsimile engraving never diminished. In another letter from Morris to Walker, written three months later, we hear the same lament: 'Looking again at the proof I see one place which will have to be altered, where in the pursuit of paradox H[ooper] has cut through a line which ought to have joined: however 'tis not in the "Corner" [probably one of Morris's corner borders] which is the one I want most. - Ah I see he has altered ny drawing to its disanvantage [] in the "Corner";o his principle of exact copying has gone by the board, and I can chaff him."30

Hooper's 'principle of exact copying' was in conflict with more than Morris's Ruskinian doctrine of the dignity of the workman; it was also incompatible with the habits of Edward Burne-Jones, the usual illustrator of the Kelmscott books. In all the designing that Burne-Jones had done for Morris and Company he exhibited a reluctance to provide finished drawings to scale, and in an interview published in the Studio (1894) Morris explained how his firm had got round this problem when producing textiles. Morris's remarks are worth quoting at length, because they shed considerable light on both Burne-Jones's technique as a draughtsman and Morris's use of photography: 'The original studies are not above I5 inches high. The figures are grouped and drawn from carefully prepared studies: for the rest there is but little mel; enyynTha erm in which they come into our hands. We have to have them enlarged by photography, in squares varying in size and number according to the full dimensions required. The enlarged sections are then fitted together, and the whole, now of the proper size, submitted, together with a small coloured study, to the artist for his revision and approval; and on these enlargements he does a great deal of work, especially to the heads and hands. The ornamental accessories, the patterns of are drawn, for the most part, by Mr. H. Dearle. We know that from the mid-187os the same procedure was used at Morris and Company for translating Burne-Jones's small sketches into suitable designs for stained-glass windows; and in the end something not unlike this had to be done to his Kelmscott Press illustrations as well.

The most distinctive characteristic of a wood-engraving is that it can render only blacks and whites; intermediate tones must be suggested by parallel lines (which Morris believed should be used sparingly) or cross-hatching (which he thought should not be used at all. But the designs which Burne-Jones provided for the Kelmscott illustrations were pencil sketches flled with delicate nuances of shadingOviusly  great dealoranslationhad to e done be they could be made into wood-engravings. According to Morris's understanding of craftsmanship, Hooper should have been the person t dp teke  the  thevi practice a veteran 'facsimile engraver' like Hooper was incapable of bridging the gap. Nor was there much rapport between Hooper and Burne-Jones, the latter of whom wrote in September 1894: 'Sometimes men come at half past eight in the morning for instructions. . . . I have the Chaucer engraver man one day in every week, andhave o ben at the beiig, at the wor  e ylabl th them. . . . But I am so dejected that I have a mind to say to them when they come "Good morning to you, I hope you are welland your wife and family, for, judging by your impoverished appearance, and the lac  yu t, I k  n u hav bo e and family. I hope you are well. Now to begin about this engraving. Once upon a time darkness was everywhere, and matter mingled with water. Then came a great wind." — and so on by slow degrees leading them to the Tower of Babel  to the beginnings of the Egyptians, and so through the Greek pots to Albert Durer. It[']s always like that.

If Hooper was too uninstructed and too rigid in his loyalty to the ideal of facsimile engraving (not to mention his shabby clothes which were so offensive to Burne-Jones), then soneone else would have to be found who could convert thé Burne-Jones sketches into a form suitable for wood-engraving. One obvious candidate was Charles Fairfax Murray, an artist, collector, and connoisseur who had done similar copying tasks for Rossetti and Burne-Jones in the past,3³3 and in fact Murray 'touched up' photographic copies of both Burne-Jones illustrations in The Golden Legend before Hooper engraved them.34 Whether he adapted Burne-Jones's other early Kelniscott illustrations is not known; but when work began on the Chaucer it became evident that Murrayonewas otequal the sk latinhyvn drawings. Walker, perhaps exaggerating, claimed that it took him nearly three months to complete one illustration.³ An early state of the picture which appears on page 9 of the Chaucer has survived,36 and it is a remarkably inept piece of drawing, the faces and flowers are lifeless, and the shading is unsatisfactory. This is probably not the engraving that was adapted by Murray,37 but its lacklustre quality does at least suggest the seriousness of the problem facing Morris and Burne-Jones.

The first Kelmscott Press illustration, the frontispiece of A Dream of John Ball, was an adaptation of a gravure reproduction which had appeared in the first edition (1888) of the book, and it is significant that at about the time of publication Robert Catterson-Smith, a young

![](images/5693cf18db025f3e819f636ffebe39b81f42a3052aef88ee04ef38462b8d6cdf.jpg)
