Walker and Catterson-Smith have givcn detailed accounts of how the latter mediated between Hooper and Burne-Jones;4° and an ingenious procedure it was. Once Walker and Boutall had photographed the pencil sketch, Catterson-Smith covered the print with a thin wash of hinese white so that the lines wcre just barely visible. He then traced over the drawing 'with a fine round sable brush and very black Chinese ink', thickening the strokcs as he did so and converting Burne-Jones's grey tones into engraving-style shading. 'When difficulties arose in the trcatment of passages I consulted B-J,' Catterson-Smith recalled, 'Sometimes work by Albert Dürer was consulted to see how he had dealt with passages. Some of the drawings were done over several times.' Since most of the communication between Catterson-Smith and Burne-Jones was oral, we have few written records of the negotiations between them; but we catch an interesting glimpse of the difficulties faced by Catterson-Smith in a letter written many years later when he was redrawing some unfinished designs by Burne-Jones once intended for the Kelmscott edition of Biblia innocentium. 'One of my greatest problems', he declared, is to keep the compositions unchanged, to keep line for line — and the second problem is to get rid of the rawness which arises from the change from pencil to black line: without introducing too much of my own.4' In this instance Catterson-Smith also struggled with angels that had too many wings and a sword's edge that was awkwardy y why w.

Catterson-Smith's ink tracings of Burne-Jones's designs were transferred photographically to the surface of the woodblocks and engraved by Hooper. Throughout the process the artist played an active role, and both the redrawn designs and the proofs of Kelmscott illustrations are heavily marked and annotated by Burne-Jones. 'Slight alteration in position of base of second column,' he writes on one. Or: 'Have taken out a buttress between Troilus's leg and post.' 'Ought there to be a nimbus on angel?' 'Please engrave as far as possible the accidental changes of tone in wicker-work.' 'Mail on left arm | Sword hilt | alter proportion of soldier's head | Change hand on sword hilt | Increase size of child | Best for general work yet | Perspective of stone stairs wall not true.'4² Proofs were first pulled on India paper (Burne-Jones and Morris, however, declined to look at these earliest impressions), then on thicker stock, and finally on Batchelor paper.

The relationship between Burne-Jones and Catterson-Smith appears, on the whole, to have been amicable, though Burne-Jones always adopted a patronizing tone in speaking of the Birmingham artist. 'I have had to give a drawing lesson to the lad,' he wrote. 'The lad isbot but sill adasi art e is aad t ahu Catterson-Smith, for his part, professed great admiration for Burne-Jones but complained that he was out of touch with the ordinary realities of life.44 The nature of Catterson-Smith's contributions to the Kelmscott Chaucer was not widely known or publicly discussed until 1898 (since his name did not appear in the book's colophon), when an anonymous contributor to the London Daily Chronicle - probably Joseph Pennell, the American artist—journalist — suggested that Morris had unfairly (and perhaps dishonestly) failed to acknowledge Catterson-Smith's role as collaborator of Burne-Jones in the Chaucer.4s Such a claim elicited an angry letter to the editor of the Daily Chronicle from Cockerell (15 November 1898) and a somewhat milder epistle from Catterson-Smith himself (25 November), who wrote: 'Fingers, eyes, and sympathy I brought, but Sir Edward was responsible for every line and dot in the eighty "Chaucer" drawings which I did under his guidance. I worked at his very elbow for months, often spending wholedys seking ut sipleneressivreentps, and in many cases doing drawings over and over again, until he was satisfied that the treatment or convention (he used to call it his "shorthand") expressed him.' Burne-Jones, adopting a similar but less flattering metaphor, described Catterson-Smith as an efficient tool in his hands.46 That it was an extraordinarily successful collaboration there can be no doubt; yet at the same time Morris must have been uncomfortably aware that the use of a craftsman as a mere 'tool'of an artist undernined the ideological basis of the Kelmscott Press. It illustrates once again the chasm that sometines appeared between Morris's theories of bookmaking and what actually took place at the Press.

The wood-engravings created by the joint endeavours of Burne-Jones, Catterson-Smith, and Hooper are a curious hybrid of the engravings, because they are cut on the end grain of boxwood with associated with an engraving. The lines are broad, the wood is e     h by most engravers after Bewick. The methods of shading are similar to those of a woodcut. What Morris and Burne-Jones produced, through their craftsmen, was an engraved version of the woodcut as it aper n te illusra book tefteprinters of Ulm and Augsburg.

Once they were completed, Morris found that wood-engraved illustrations are technically difficult to print. They require exceptionally careful presswork, with overlays and underlays, and, because they bite deeply into dampened paper, problems arise when large engravings appear on the verso of a page of text. In fact, not until the Chaucer was published in 1896 did Morris succeed in fully integrating words and pictures. In the illustrated Kelmscott Press books published before the Chaucer the wood-engravings always appeared alone on leaves that were blank on the verso; some were printed in an entirely separate operation and the leaves merely inserted when the book was bound, a procedure perilously close to the formal dichotomy of text and illustration that Morris always condemned in the books of others. (Another reason for Morris's adoption of this unhappy expedient is that Burne-Jones was often so slow in producing his designs that they were engraved only after the rest of the book had been printed.) There were occasional proposals that the Kelmscott Press illustrations might be issued by themselves, in a form suitable for framing, but here Morris drew the line. Writing to the bookseller Frederick H. Evans in November 1894, Cockerell declared: 'I have spoken to Mr. Morris about the proposal in your letter . . . & I am sorry to say that he does not see his way to printing the pictures [of the Chaucer] apart from the book. He thinks that if this were done it might injure the sale of future issues, as some people would abstain from buying the books themselves in the hope of being able to secure the illustrations separately.'47 Cockerell's letter was expressed in appropriately commercial terms, yet Morris was no doubt more troubled by the theoretical implications of Evans's proposal. He might be willing to compromise in his search for unity of word and picture, but to publish the Chaucer illustrations separately would have made a mockery of his ideal of integration.

Another symptom of the obstacles that Morris encountered in furnishing illustrations for the Kelmscott books is the surprisingly large number of false starts and abandoned plans. Probably the most spectacular example is The Wellat the World's End (1896): Morris frst announced that Charles Fairfax Murray would illustrate it, then assigned the task to Arthur J. Gaskin, and finally - but only after Gaskin's designs had been engraved and several leaves printed - persuaded Burne-Jones to rescue the book. A map was prepared for Utopia (1893) and a line block made, but it was scrapped. During 1893 and 1894 Charles M. Gere produced a series of drawings for a projected Kelmscott edition of The House of the Wolfings which was not in the end published. In 1892 Aymer Vallance visited Morris at Kelmscott House, accompanied by Aubrey Beardsley, who had prepared an illustration for Sidonia the Sorceress. Morris, while conceding that young Beardsley had a good eye for drapery, declined the drawing - and not very politely, according to Vallance Beardsley was so deeply offended that for the rest of his short life he nursed a grudge against Morris and sought revenge by creating a witty parody of the Kelmscott style in his ornaments and illustrations for the Dent edition of Malory's Le Morte Darthur (1893—4). Beardsley's encounter with Morris appears to have been an exceptionally unhappy one, but the truth of the matter is that every book-illustrator found Morris nearly impossible to please. It is a significant fact that no artist except for Burne-Jones illustrated more than one Kelmscott Press book.

##

When he was planning the first Kelmscott volume, The Story of the Glittering Plain, Morris hit upon the notion of enlisting the services of his friend, Walter Crane, a fellow-socialist and one of the leading illustrators in Victorian England. In the autumn of 89 he wrote t a formal proposal specifying that 'The illustrations [are] to be drawn by Walter Crane, & cut on wood by Mr Leverett or others to Walter n c It w gre thath ul hay from the book, an arrangement Morris never again offered to an ill. But Mo wa pait    snt that he s nilling to wit r Crane'desns, and The Glittg Plain was issued in 1891 in unillustrated form; to atone for his haste, Morris published another version of The Glittering Plain at the Ke , the rel was acc, r te Gltg Plai illut , quite simply, the most feeble that appeared in any Kelnscott book.

![](images/09e97ba4f2a29a6626a3df8a4de11ab8451a9a0153887acfc3abe1012191e9cc.jpg)

the Kelmscott Press. The sccond version (1894) was illustrated with woodengravings dcsigncd by Walter Crane, which Morris found unsatisfactory.

What had gone wrong? It is evident that Crane, whose own books are characterized by great delicacy and grace, found Morris's dark, medieval page uncongenial; an examination of his designs reveals that he worked mainly with white ink on a black ground, thus producing a suffocatingly black picture which was then engraved in the most nechanical facsimile fashion by A. Leverett. 'I think your woodcuts look delightful,' Morris wrote to Crane, trying to put the best face on the matter; but Crane later doubted 'if I was ever quite Gothic enough in feeling to suit his [Morris's] taste' Privately Morris offered a more blunt assessment of Crane's work. The Glittering Plain illustrations, he told the artist Edmund New in October 1895, 'are poor in execution'; to Cockerell he acknowledged that he thought this volume his one Kelmscott Press failure' Philip Webb fired offa letter to Morris filled with indignation at the inadequacies of Crane: 'do not be so rash in trusting the picture makers in future,' he implored.

But if not even the distinguished Walter Crane could be counted upon to come up to what Webb called 'the Thames-side mark', where in England was Morris to find the artists who could satisfactorily illustrate the Kelmscott books? The answer, surprisingly, was Birmingham, where the arts-and-crafts movement flourished more vigorously than anywhere else in the provinces, mainly through the influence of the municipal School of Art.54 Three youthful artists associated with the School — Arthur J. Gaskin, Charles M. Gere, and Edmund New - were doing attractive book illustrating during the 18, ad Morris was aware the and their work.Gaski, a young Birmingham artist, called in the afternoon [at Kelmscott House] with a number of very pretty drawings for an edition of Hans Anderson which Geo Allen is going to publish,' Cockerell noted in his diary on 3 December 1892.5 Nine days later this entry appears in the diary: C. M. Gere, another nice young Birmingham artist, called in the aft[ernoo]n with some drawings, and W.M. asked him to have a shot at illustrating the [House of the] Wolfings.' Both men also visited Kelmscott Manor on later occasions, as did Ednund New, though in New's case Morris offered him no assignment for the Kelnscott Press.

Morris's attitude towards Gaskin and Gere was ambivalent: he was grateful for their loyalty to the arts-and-crafts ideals, yet his praise for their work was often cautious and qualified. In an interview in the Daily Chronicle (9 October 1893), he was quoted as saying that there is a great quantity of excellent art, but the only thing that is new, strictly speaking, is the rise of the Birmingham school of book decorators.   thee young men of the Birmingham School Art Mr. Gaskin, Mr. Gere, and Mr. New — have given a new start to the art of book decorating.' In another interview, published in Bookselling two years later, however, Morris remarked:'I think they have, in Gaskin and New, two very good men, who have ideas and originality. For the most part, however, they follow too slavishly the opposition to conventionality. . . . but you must remember that the Birmingham people have not yet found their feet. They will do good work yet, I am sure.' This was faint praise indeed. The same ambiguous note is struck by Morris in the introduction he wrote for Good King Wenceslas ham 8)illa ask h] have i  yret peasue, both hiets h and as giving hopes of a turn towards the ornamental side of illustration, which is most desirable.'57

As Cockerell's diary indicates, Gere was the first of the Birmingham artists to be asked to illustrate a planned Kelmscott Press book, The House of the Wolfings. Gere - who in later years achieved a reputation as a painter as wel as an illustrator for the Ashendene Press made a number of designs for the book during 1893, submitted them to Morris, and for his pains received a bundle of letters from Morris filled with the most severe criticism. The execution was wrong, said Morris; the lines were too thin. 'I am sorry to say that I do not think that you have done yourself justice in them' (1 July 1893). This went on for some months, and finally the correspondence faded away. Though Morris had two trial pages printed at the Press, The House of the Wolfings was stuck forever in limbo.

But in the meantime Gere had undertaken a more modest task for the Kelmscott Press that proved to be a considerable success. At Cockerell's suggestion, Morris asked Gere to do a drawing of Kelmscott Manor to serve as a frontispiece for News from Nowhere, and in this instance he was able to supervise Gere's work much more directly. (The engraving, in fact, seems to have permanently fixed the image of Kelmscott Manor in the public mind: sone years ago the trees and bushes in front of the house were rearranged and in some cases replanted so that they would appear exactly as they do in Gere's illustration.) Gere's style — simple, unfussy, honest, and linear - is markedly similar to that of New and Claude Napier-Clavering, the director of the Birmingham Guild of Handicraft, whose work also appeared frequently in the Guild's intensely Morrisian magazine, the Quest, and in the November 189s number of the Quest, the News from

Nowhee frontispice was reprined from the riginal block (something Morris normally did not allow) to accompany an essay by Morris on Kelmscott Manor, 'Gossip about an Old House on the Upper Thames'.

Gaskin's relationship with Morris was, if anything, even more turbulent than Gere's. Subsequently well known as a designer of jewelry,Gaskin was scheduled to illustrate a Kelmscott Press edition of The Roots of the Mountains that never materialized; Morris also arranged for him to design the pictures for The Well at the World's End and The Shepheardes Calender (1896). The twelve designs for the latter book are impressive — Gordon Ray has called them 'perhaps the most successful of Kelnscott Press illustrations', a judgement in which Colin Franklin concursbut one suspects that they were not entirely to Morris's liking, and Morris would probably also have disapproved of the use of process blocks furnished by Walker and Boutall. The Shepheardes Calender, however, was printed shortly after his death, when Walker and Cockerell felt a desperate urgencyin trying to finish Morris's uncompleted books at the Press and thus were inclined to adopt short cuts.

The story behind Gaskin's illustrations for The Well at the World's End is longer and more complicated. The earliest reference to Gere's cion ith thebo pears,  sual, iCckre i April 1893): 'Gere to illustrate The Wolfings, Gaskin The Roots & The Well at the World's End.' Gaskin produced some designs in July 1893, but Morris confided to his daughter Jenny that 'I am sorry to say that I did not think they would quite do' Morris sent back detailed criticisms, Gaskin offered more drawings which Morris in turn criticized, and so it went for month after month: The Well at the WE on cha  e Altogether Gaskin submitted nineteen designs, some of which were lyo pulle  s.Fnal Far Mo iz  ul temporize no longer. After consulting Philip Webb, who confirmed his own unfavourable opinion of the designs, Morris wrote to Gaskin that he would be unablé to use them. The artist protested and begged to be allowed to do four new illustrations; Morris declined. The Well at the World's End, when it eventually came off the press in 1896, was embellished with four designs by Burne-Jones. What is puzzling about all this is that Gaskin's drawings are in fact beautiful. One can only guess that Morris objected to them because he feared their graceful delicacy would not harmonize with his sturdy Chaucer type.

![](images/712e92c7c4ef10d6f66095100b8d4e6ad2f0d672fa7f429c64173137f3a25dfd.jpg)  
Figure 50 At Morris's request, Arthur J. Gaskin produced wood-engraved Th W   Wr   u o ay with them and substituted four designs by Burne-Joncs.

And so, once again, Morris fell back on the support of Edward Burne-Jones. In reality, Burne-Jones's illustrations were the only ones that satisfied him, and Morris's attempts to recruit other artists to work for the Kelmscott Press were always rather half-hearted. Whatever the merits of the designs by Crane, Gere, and Gaskin, it is the name of Burne-Jones that will forever be associated with the Press, for not only did he illustrate more Kelmscott works (twelve) than any other artist, but his visual imagination also matched perfectly the typography and design of Morris's books. Reflecting their deep personal rapport, the aesthetic harmony of those volumes serves as another reminder that Morris's and Burne-Jones's ideas about book-illustration had evolved slowly, and in unison, since their Oxford days. In the evening at Exeter College they read Chaucer aloud to each other; during the day they examined illuminated manuscripts at the Bodleian. The Oxford experiences established a psychological pattern for life: until his death Morris spent Sunday mornings in Burne-Jones's studio, reading and I week for both of them. Burne-Jones sent to Lady Horner this account of one of those Sunday communions: 'To-day at breakfast came Morris, and he talked hard all morning whilst I drew — why the mediaeval world was always on the side of the Trojans. We quarrelled a good deal and often he said "Look here, Ned, that's all gammon, you know," and I said, being more polite,"Pardon me, you are quite wrong there — the fact of the matter is," and sometimes we were both polite, and he would say, "There's a good deal in that," and I would say "Yes, you are quite right. All morning through we never mendone livig person. We never got to speak onye latr than the ninth century.63

![](images/882092d69d1385ca7687865e5ee25172249647331bc1f77afc4301c08c143dc6.jpg)  
Figure 51 This photograph of Edward Burnc-Jones and William Morris was taken in 189o, shortly before they began their memorable collaboration at the Kclmscott Press. Though Morris occasionally turned to other illustrators, their work failed to satisfy him as fully as Burne-Jones's did.

As Burne-Jones's words suggest, he and Morris hardly thought alike on all subjects. There were in fact two great sources of tension between them. The first was that, whereas Morris was drawn to the art, literature, and values of the Gothic North, Burne-Jones (for all his medievalism) felt an equally strong attraction to the Mediterranean world. 'I think Morris's friendship began everything for me, everything that I afterwards cared for,' Burne-Jones wrote to J. Comyns Carr. But he added: 'I quarrel now with Morris about Art. He journeys to Iceland and I to Italy, which is a symbol.' When Morris returned from Iceland in 1873, Burne-Jones made a delightful drawing of his friend seated in the snow and gnawing a fish. "Mr. Morris has come back more enslaved with ice and snow and raw fish than ever,' Burne-Jones declared in a jesting tone that only partially conceals the seriousness of his complaint — 'I fear I shall never drag him to Italy again.'64 This painful division between their loyalties to North and South is sometimes reflected in the Kelmscott Press illustrations, most conspicuously in the Chaucer.

Morris's commitment to socialism also threatened at times to divide him from his closest friend. Burne-Jones acknowledged the corruptions of the capitalist system, but unlike Morris he did not rejoice in its impending collapse: violent change, which seemed to him a bad thing in itself, would have separated him from what he described as 'the dream country where my real home is'.6"s On his side, Morris expressed strong disapproval when Burne-Jones was made a baronet in 1894. 'W.M. much vexed thereby. A great pity I think also,' Cockerell recorded in his diary on  February. Jane Morris has left a more vivid record of the incredulous response in the Morris household to the news: 'my husband refused to believe it at first, but afterwards when the plain fact was known, he said"Well a man can be an ass for the sake of his children".66

At a more profound level the relationship between Morris and Burne-Jones could have been undermined by Burne-Jones's conviction that his friend was too emotionally self-sufficient. 'I suppose he [cares?] for me more than anyone, yet the day I go he will lose nothing, only he will have to think to himself instead of thinking aloud - no more than that,' Burne-Jones wrote with a trace of bitterness.67 But this was only a passing mood: more characteristic is his comment about Morris recorded in T. M. Rooke's diary on 24 May 1853: 'Oh! he's most beautiful, he's a darling, he's the queerest thing that ever existed on this earth, there's nobody like him, there isn't anyone that exists that in the least bit resembles him.' Contrary to Burne-Jones's expectations, Morris preceded him in death; the experience left him broken in spirit, and within two years he died as well.

Apart from his Kelmscott Press work, Burne-Jones's achievements as an illustrator were slender. In 18 54 he was asked to provide pictures and ornaments for Archibald Maclaren's book, The Fairy Family, which was to be published by Longmans. Burne-Jones's designs, rather in the style of Dicky Doyle, do not at all suggest his mature work, and he never completed them, apparently because of his own dissatisfaction with any work begun before he was exposed to Rossetti's illustrations; The Fairy Family was eventually issued wihout his drawings.6 There was much talk during the 186s about Burne-Jones's great promise as an illustrator, yet little came of it. Holman Hunt in 1861 sent the Dalziel Brothers a strong endorsement, for, he said, 'I feel very strongly [Burne-Jones] might be of great value to you in the illustrating of Good Words. . . . He has yet, I think, made but y tas   n w with the point both with pencil and pen and ink on paper, and so would have no difficulty with the material. I have not seen him lately, but remember that he has sometimes said that he should like to try his hand at drawing on wood.Though Burne-Jones did a hadful ofdesigns for magazines during the decade, his heart was not in it, and he was not temperamentally or aesthetically drawn to the 'sixties' version of wood-engraved illustrations; only Morris was able to provide the proper environment for his talents.

One reason Burne-Jones was able to produce so many illustrations fr te time span is that he borrowed heavily from his own earlier work. A large proportion of the Kelmscott pictures are derived from paintings lh oB Jon w tersirwny recurring motifs, which Penelope Fitzgerald has ably summarized as "the enchantment of the willing victim, sleep, waiting, imprisonment, l h helpless, flying, sea-crossing, clinging together, the ritual procession and dance, love dominant and without pity, the haunting angel, the enry into lifeYet he did not offer Morris mere mechanical adaptations of earlier designs. The Kelmscott illustrations based on specifc paintings by Burne-Jones are always transposed into a new, more hauntig ky. Human fures are hown  raeul but sa poses l each other (and past the reader) with strangely hypnotic expressions. It may be significant that most of Burne-Jones's Kelmscott Press illustrations, except for the Chaucer and The Well at the World's End, are upright rectangles, often quite narrow, filled with emphatically vertical and slender figures (usually female), for the pattern reminds one immediately of the scores of stained-glass windows that he had designed for Morris and Company. As in the windows, the human beings seem divided from each other, though here it is an invisible barrier rather than a mullion that separates them. The darker side of Burne-Jones's imagination is much in evidence in these illustrations.

His distrust of deep perspective, in the paintings as well as these designs, has often been commented on, and of course the comparative flatness of Burne-Jones's drawings goes well with the markedly shallow depth of Morris's ornaments. What is perhaps less commonly noticed about the Kelmscott illustrations is that Burne-Jones, in accordance with the theories that he and Morris had evolved, made certain that his pictures were above all ornamental. Whether they told a story was beside the point; he confessed that he always evaluated his illustrations by looking at them upside down. The real tests, in the end, were the decorative quality of the pictures and the extent to which they contributed to the mise-en-scène of the printed page.

Burne-Jones's view of his own role as illustrator of the Kelmscott Press books is nowhere more beautifully expressed than in this eloquent letter to Charles Eliot Norton in 1894: 'And so you don't like

Chaucer — that is very sad — for I am beside myself with delight over it. I an making the designs as much to fit the ornament and printing as they are made to fit the little pictures — and I love to be snugly cased in the borders and buttressed up by the vast initials — and once or twice when I have no letter under me, I feel tottery and weak; if you drag me out of my encasings it will be like tearing a statue out of its niche and putting it into a museum — indeed when the book is done, if we live to finish it, it will be like a pocket cathedral. . .  This was the splendid vision that beckoned Morris and Burne-Jones onwards at the Kelmscott Press, and, whatever else may be said against their books, they did indeed find a way. of reconciling type, border, initial, and picture. It was no mean achievement.

![](images/7408676361aabedf45b5d29f335f073da4214e46c592a879ab46c9511da3048a.jpg)

OT larks!' Morris would exclaim to his daughters as he plunged into another of his adventures. The play impulse was strong in his temperament, and one is constantly amazed by the degree to which he was able to combine pleasure and work in all that he did. It is useful, and no doubt essential, to see Morris as a serious social reformer who sought a transfornation of Victorian life and labour; but we overlook a vital clue to his mind if we fail to take into account the sheer zest he brought to every task at hand The Kelmscott Presitself was begun in this joyous spirit. Even though Morris had brooded over the problem of how to print beautiful books for several decades, he made the decision to design his own types on impulse, in a moment of enthusiasm, when he heard a stimulating lecture by Emery Walker. Throughout those final years of his life, Morris's pleasure in his typographical adventure never diminished. Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, visiting Kelmscott Manor, noted in his diary on 5 July 1892: 'Morris was in fine spirits and inexhaustible energy over his new hobby of the printing press.' I do so like seeing a new book out that I have had a hand in,' Morris commented to Jenny.' The Kelniscott Press was pleasantly intertwined with Morris's family life; snatches of conversation with Walker and others in the library foated up the stairway into the drawing-room, proofs and trial pages lay scattered about Kelmscott House, and in time the books themselves drifted into the house in great piles.

But the Keltt Pre whic bean sark, ur intoge and complex business operation as the months went by. There are signs of this change in the growing number of titles printed each year. In 1891 only two books were issued, whereas in the following year that figure increased to seven; in 1893 it became twelve. Though thereafter the number of books produced each year tapered off slightly, that is a misleading statistic, because the works actually printed were in some instances very ambitious undertakings, such as the Chaucer and the eight-volume Earthly Paradise. Even at the beginning the Kelmscott Press was never really a one-man operation, since Morris had the assistance of Walker and various craftsmen. It must have soon become evident, however, that Morris needed to build up a staff to help him in business and editorial natters as well as the printing itself.

![](images/2fa4af702cc82d643032d567568c1b7eb6ecb33a572773b92a9e6f1d750a6b46.jpg)  
Figure 52 Morris's daughter May and hcr husband Henry Halliday Sparlng, whorv as he frst Secreary th Kelsott Press until 84, T that hc was not an effectivc manager.

The person who initially bore much of the responsibility of the day-to-day operation of the Press was Henry Halliday Sparling, the husband of May Morris. Sparling assumed the title of Secretaryo the Press, a position he held until July 1894 when he was succeeded by Sydney Cockerell; but, whereas Cockerell was a born archivist who left behind enormous collections of papers recording his activities at the Press, Sparling is a far more elusive figure about whom we know little. Everything we can learn about him comes from a few scraps of crepondencseytns cr ppl ye obituaries, and the passing comments of his contemporaries. These observations by those who knew him, it must be added, are uniformly unflattering. Sparling seems to have struck everyone who met him as a weak, unremarkable young man. Cockerell, who was normally discreet in writing about the Morris circle, described Sparling as 'a rather second-rate Socialist'. Joseph Pennell and his wife discovered Sparling talking like a romantic schoolgirl' at the meetings of the Hammersmith Socialist League; Mrs Pennell declared that Morris's future son-in-law was 'disagreeably young and pleased with himself. Yeats, who met Sparling at the British Museum, recorded his fatuous remark about May Morris, to whom he was then engaged: ""She is very beautiful," he would say, "Morris says so."

Sparling kept himself occupied during the 188os as a socialist lecturer, literary journalist, and co-editor of Morris's Commonweal. Exactly what functions he performed at the Kelmscott Press in the following decade is not clear, because the documentary record is so skimpy. We know that he edited three of the Kelmscott books (The Recuyellof the Historyes of Troye, The History of Reynard the Foxe, and The History of Godefrey of Boloyne), did most of the proof-reading, and handled some of the correspondence and perhaps the bookkeeping as well. There is no evidence that he carried out his clerical duties especially well. In fact, when Cockerell took over, he found the records in some confusion and had to exercise his formidable organizational skills to put the Kelmscott Press on sounder footing as a business.

Sparling's marriage to May Morris (which took place in June 1890) sees to have beenfragilfrom the start, and it was finally broken up, if Bernard Shaw's account can be trusted, by the prolonged presence of Shaw as a guest in their home.³ 'May's married life has come to an end,' her mother announced to Blunt in May 1894, 'and although we always expected some catastrophe in that direction, the blow is no less heavy now it has come.' Writing again two days later, she added that

May 'has been seeing a good deal of a former lover [Shaw?], and made her husband's life a burden to him'.4 On 25 May Cockerell noted laconically in his diary: 'W.M. asked me to take up the Secretaryship of the Kelmscott Press, which Sparling is resigning.' Sparling officially stepped down as Secretary in July when the following note was inserted in a Kelmscott Press circular: 'Mr. Halliday Sparling, having been compelled by the pressure of other work to resign the Secretaryship to the Kelmscott Press, has been succeeded by Mr. S. C. Cockerell. Correspondents are asked to note the consequent change of address.'

Thereafter Sparling fades mysteriously out of the picture. He fled to France, returning to England only for Morris's funeral, and a divorce was granted in 1899. He continued to live on the Continent in obscurity and possibly also poverty, though in later years we hear of his delivering lectures on 'William Morris as Prophet of Reform' and 'The Glory that is France' to wealthy ladies in Pasadena, California. His last act before his death in 1924 was to complete a volume entitled The Kelmscott Press and William Morris Master-Craftsman, published in the same year. Though Emery Walker dismissed it as 'not of much value', the book is a useful, detailed account of the Press which supplies some factual information not preserved elsewhere. It is weakened, however, by poor Sparling's need to protest his constant loyalty to Morris's ideals despite the collapse of his marriage to Morris's daughter: 'an adoring and eager disciple throughout, I may claim to be especially qualified as an interpreter of his teaching,' he declared in the preface. It seems fair to assume that the tone ofunctuous piety which pervades Sparling's history has had a detrimental effect upon the reputation of the Kelmscott Press in our century.

It is difficult to imagine a greater contrast than that between the first and second Secretaries of the Press. Sydney Cockerell possessed a keen, inquiring mind, was an efficient administrator, and in later years achieved a position of considerable eminence as director of the Fitzwilliam Museum, Cambridge. What is especially remarkable about Cockerell is that he sensed at once the historical importance of the Kclmscott Press and from its founding collected every scrap of paper, every piece of ephemera, associated with the Press. If it were not for Cockerell's acquisitive instincts, our knowledge of Morris's work as a printer would today be greatly inpoverished. One particularly valuable document preserved by Cockerell is the series of small pocket until his death; thanks to thiese diaries, the great masses of correspondence that he accumulated, and his large collection of Kelmscott Press proofs and trial pages, it is possible to reconstruct - at least after July 1894 - the activities of the Kelmscott Press in vivid detail. Cockerell claimed that his diary, straightforward and simple as the entries are, 'has not only been of daily service to me for casual reference to events that may only have been a few days or a few weeks old, and for verifying names and dates, but, after a longer interval, has formed a sort of ladder on which recollection could cimb with sure feet' Cockerell's later published recollections of Morris and the Kelmscott Press were in fact based directly on his diaries (as early as November 1896 he was copying out the references to Morris).

![](images/7607f4fc362fafc495b1d6bc0f0a67ba17ea80ee9a5975d3df1d0e003b9d002c.jpg)  
Figure 53 'I haven't a spark ofimagination,' Sydney Cockerell once complaine, ee after Morris's death.

Left fatherless and not well provided for, Cockerell reluctantly joined the family firm of George Cockerell and Company, Coal Merchants, in 1884, and when he received a legacy of £2,ooo from his grandfather in 1889, he invested it in the company only after much soul-searching; two years later he decided to sell his share in the business and seek his fortune in the world. But what was he to do? Though he considered the possibility of Cambridge, London seemed to hold more attractions, for by now, with his socialist and artistic leanings, he had drifted into Morris's circle. He temporarily postponed the haecbou uu i ns (especially Ruskin, with whom he was corresponding), visiting museums and art galleries, and attending lectures sponsored by various socialist and arts-and-crafts groups. It was a heady experience for a coal merchant's son still in his early twenties. In 1892 he applied for the curatorship of the Soane Museum; in 1894 he was offered (but declined) the editorship of the Studio magazine. And regularly, sometimes several days each week, Cockerell dropped in at Kelmscott House, where Morris showed him the latest proofs and books from the Press and they talked about Morris's exciting plans for printing. In August 1892 he visited Kelmscott Manor for the first tine.

At the beginning of his diary for 1892 Cockerell wrote the following note (dated 10 January 1893): 'Out of my stays at Kelmscott work of cataloguing W.M.'s books, a truly enviable task, on which I have been engaged for 2 months. It willtake me some time longer, and having done it I shall the more readily obtain other employment. I already begin to kow ehing of the varus th cetury tyes. Morris had engaged Cockerel's services for 'two guineas a week and a copy of the Golden Legend! (diary, 3 November 1892), and, aided by the advice of F. S. Ellis and Robert Proctor, Cockerell, though inexperienced, found his way with surprising agility through the bibliographical thickets of incunabula. The catalogue of Morris's library was nevercomplete,but onecan see an indication othe form it would have taken in one of the last of the Kelmscott Press books, Some German Woodcuts ofthe Fifteenth Century (1897), whereCockerell printed, as an appendix, the result of this painstaking analyses of the woodcuts in Morris's collection of early books. 'I haven't a spark of imagination, and am only good for dry-as-dust cataloguing,' Cockerel protested; but it was clear to many of Morris's friends that this unusual young man possessed the excellent organ of orderliness' (as Ellis calléd it) to an amazing degree.'

The lesson was not lost on Morris, who increasingly gave Cockerell other clerical assignments, some of which were connected with the Kelmscott Press. His name appears occasionally, for example, on receipts for the Kelmscott books during the period when Sparling was still nominally the Secretary. Once it became obvious that Sparling's marital arrangements were crumbling and that he could no longer serve as Secretary, Morris turned without hesitation to the studious, meticulous young man already working in his library. Cockerell at once made himself indispensable. He was able to relieve Morris of administrative responsibilities at the Press much more efficiently than Sparling had ever done, and Morris's standard response to questions now became 'Ask Cockerell'. As Philip Webb remarked to Cockerell, 'you know every body, almost tripping up Walker in that cpcitye Morr death he was effectively iarg Press during the last year and a half of its existence.

What Cockerell brought primarily to the secretaryship was resourceful intelligence rather than experience, though for several years before 1894 he had been discussing typefaces and early printed books with Morris and Walker, and wood-engravings with Walter Crane and W. J. Linton. About printing, as about everything else, Cockerell displayed an insatiable curiosity. Consider, for example, this typical entry in his diary (1o June 189o): 'Called on Mr Jowett, the manager of Hazell Watson & Viney's [the firm that printed Ruskin's books], and was shown over the works, where I learned a great many things — and saw the processes of type casting, electrotyping, handpress and machine printing, sewing, binding etc etc which I had been very anxious to be acquainted with.'

This is, if not the first, yet one of the first of Gunther Zainer's wood cut books, & consequently one of the carliest of the Augsburg woodcut books: it is an carly example of Zainer's second Gothic type. The woodcuts, designed clearly by an carlier designerof his, and not by the artistof his Golden Legend, arerude, buthave much character, though they are not so decorative as some others of the Augsburg and Um books. The colouring of them in this copy is clearly original, and is systematic and good, the brownish lake; especially, is very good colour, and contrasts well with the transparent yellow. The book lacks the added beauty of Zainer's large woodcut etters, which were probably not then done. The biginitial, very skilfully composed ofsimplestrapwork, is one of a few letters which (I believe) occur only in Gunther Zainer's books.

Thc technical bibliographical dcscription in thc first two paragraphs is by Sydncy Cockcrell; the third paragraph is by Morris. The catalogue was ncver coiplcted, but material from it was absorbcd into Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century (Kelmscott Press, 1897).

Another conspicuous trait of Cockerell was his loyalty to the Morris family: after 1896 he became a confidant, financial adviser, and surrogate son of Jane Morris, and in later years stepped into a similar role (though now more like a kindly older brother)inhis relationship with both Jenny and May Morris. There was something about his manner that inspired confidence; he appeared to be everyone's choice for executor or trustee. In old age, laden with honours, a bedridden invalid in Kew, he became a formidable character indeed. With undiminished force of personality, he held court in his bedroom for those who came to pay their respects, surrounded by the most astonishing relics of Morris, Rossetti, Ruskin, and the other great Victorians. The slightest disparagement of Morris in print brought him roaring out of his den with a fierce letter to the editor. When he died in 1962, he seemed to many the last vestige of the Victorian era, an improbable survivor of an inconceivably distant age.

##

Apart from Cockerell and Walker, the person who exercised the most direct influence on the Kelmscott Press was Frederick S. Ellis, a retired bookseller and publisher, who edited no less than fifteen Kelmscott books and became Morris's chief literary adviser at the Press.° Morris was introduced to Ellis by Swinburné in the 186os, found him a pleasant, knowledgeable man (as did everyone), agreed to let him publish most of his books from 1 869 onwards, and was joint-tenant of Kelmscott Manor with Ellis for a decade. By the time the Kelmscott Press was in operation, Elis had sold his business and was living in Torquay, but he frequently visited his son at 7 Hammersmith Terrace and dashed in and out of Kelmscott House in his inimitable, breezy fashion.

One of Ellis's major contributions to the Kelmscott Press was his untiring insistence upon the importance of accurate proof-reading. There was an amusing rumour at the time that Morris was unaware of the existence of printers' readers; this of course was not true, but for the first three years proofs seem to have been read very haphazardly at the Press. Morris's attitude towards such matters was decidedly casual. When Poems by the Way, the second Kelmscott book, was in production, he wrote to Walker from Folkestone (where he was convalescing from an attack of gout): 'Yes, I think it would be best to print that sheet again; a misprint of that kind, though it does not otherwise matter, is such a defacement of the book, - and 'tis a 2 er.r themselves at first handled the proof-reading, but this was soon seen to be an unsatisfactory arrangement. Morris appears not to have read against copy, and many of his alterations in proofs were intended to improve the word-spacing ofloose-fitting lines rather than to elimina ypographicl errors. Es certainly hado illusins about Morris's capabilities as a proof-reader. While he was supervising a reprint of The DefencGnvere n hwro Cckr ow ing the K.P. [edition] but strange to say though dear old Morris corrected that much as to punctuation - he has not only left the old &c &c.'"

![](images/35c2b39321f1fb322e14d2f24a535fe03d584b6409d17a5920d50c7d2534c88b.jpg)  
Fiure  F. S. El, a retird bookseller who had once been Moris's plishr, ee  kfure at th Ke ress, wher  rv editor and proofreader. He brought pressure on Morris to improve the textual accuracy of the Kelmscott books.

Sparling reports that 'as time went on and the work grew in volume nodinary professional reader was employed whose identiy he unfortunately does not record. An article by 'W.H.H.' (a proof-reader at the Chiswick Press) in the Printers' Register - Supplement (December 1897), however, offers a few more tantalizing details: the Kelmscott Press reader, he says, though he was treated in a most liberal manner by Mr. Morris', resigned when he contracted a serious illness resulting from the dampness of the riverside cottage in which the Press was housed. Morris must have been immensely relieved to discover that Cockerell, in addition to his many other talents, was also an excellent proof-reader. 'Mr. Cockerell will read for literals,' Morris wrote in the proofs of The Life and Death of Jason,"3 and that short sentence serves as a summary of the solution he eventually adopted at the Press. After her father's death May Morris also gave Cockerell some assistance.

Nevertheless, the Kelmscott Press was bedevilled by careless proofreading throughout its history (though the standard of accuracy certainly improved after Cockerell became Secretary), and Ellis frequently reminded Morris of this sad state of affairs. In January 1894 he sent him a stern letter about Sidonia the Sorceress: 'there are certainly more misprints than there ought to be,' declared Ellis, offering his own services as areader; the same was true, he addedominously, oGodfry ofBoloyne. It was in 1893, during the last full year of Sparling's tenure, that sloppy proof-reading became a problem of major proportions. Three books published consecutively in 1893 were disfigured by serious errors. In a copy of Utopia Ellis inscribed the following plaintive note: 'I cannot help feeling vexed at my name as Editor [in the colophon] opposite a palpable mistake of the first printer's name.

I did correct it, but my proof was lost and the misprint left." Maud contained so many typos that Bertram Dobell, a leading London bookseller, complained, and Morris ssued four cancel leaves." Jacobi marked the errors in his copy of Gothic Architecture, which corrections Morris then incorporated in the second and third impressions of the book.'7

The surviving Kelmscott Press proofs indicate that Morris was not very receptive to editorial suggestions from his proof-readers, and when he made changes he did so with bad grace. In the proofs of Beowulf (1895), responding to a reader's query, he protested indignantly, 'I cant have my best lines spoiled!'" Another reader, probably Sparling, offered a more persuasive argument in the proofs of A Dream of John Ball: ? to W.M. Is there not a better word than surplus"? It smacks of Fabian essays!" Morris substituted the word 'overplus'. Even Cockerell, in whom Morris had more confidence than in any other assistant, occasionally provoked the famous Morris temper: 'When I read the proofs for him', he recalled, 'I often used to point out things, which he altered (as a rule) as a matter of course. On one occasion I remember an outburst & a grinding of teeth, & then the alteration after a few minutes, & a confession at Gatti's the same evening by way of amends to myself. But this was over a sentence that he had designed on the first page of the Chaucer, & his irritation was natural a some word had to be taken out & desgned ain. 920

Ellis was aware that he was treading on dangerous ground when he disagreed with Morris about textual matters, but he nevertheless did so frequently, though always with self-deprecating good humour, as i   e o TTaK F I h may go for press. There are singularly few typographical errors. I daresay you will despise some of my queries & questions." The notes i costtu tenbao  mater  pel Ell, oKe cott books should include introductions, notes, bibliographies, os n the  wes Mo w  pt e tet th as little aparatu  possibe.Elornstance persded Eward Gordon Duff to prepare a list of English-language editions of The Golden Legend to serve as an introduction to the Kelnscott Press version, yet at the last monent Morris balked at printing it, and Ellis found hinself in the embarrassing position of having to explain to

Duff, 'The author-printer kicks much against introductions, and there is some practical difficulty as to bibliographical description in that thee r ut two set o type[] cps.or eadings & that ued in the text. The most that Morris would permit was a brief essay by Elis entitled 'Memoranda' at the end of The Golden Legend.

The text of Psalmi penitentiales, which was written in Middle English, provoked a good deal of wrangling between Morris and Ellis. When Ellis suggested in the proofs²³ that the phrase 'caris colde' was a corruption, Morris replied, 'Of course it is caris colde[,] a most common expression.' They disagreed about the form of the glossary. At times they both had to admit defeat when confronted by a puzzling text. 'I cannot make very much sense of this line,' wrote Ellis, 'but it is exactly to Ms. & I daresay the old boy who wrote it knew what it meant.''All right,' responded Morris wearily. But Morris was unbudging in his refusal to admit footnotes to the Kelmscott books. In the proofs of The Nature of Gothi² one can see him experimenting with side-notes, then placing notes within brackets, and finally relegating Ruskin's annotations to an appendix.

One editorial issue on which Morris and Ellis were in substantial agreement was that the original spelling of medieval and Elizabethan texts should be preserved. In his foreword to the Kelmscott edition of The Poems of William Shakespeare (1893), Ellis called attention to the question: 'After a lapse of about three hundred years Shakespeare's Poems are in this edition printed with the original spelling. That the orthography of an author of the 16th or 17th century cannot without detriment be translated into that of the 19th, seems a proposition too evident to leave room for controversy. The fact of being able to study these poems in the language in which they were written, ought we thi, to matially othe pleasue of thereader.This was a p of view long advocated by Frederick J. Furnivall, the founder of the New Shakspere Society (which Shaw wittily described as the Old Spelling Society) and numerous other literary and philological groups; Furnivall was a friend of Morris — it was at his suggestion that Morris planned an edition of Shakespeare's plays - and Ellis was active in his societies. Like Ellis, Morris believed that to alter the spelling of a word was to conceal its history: he no doubt saw modern-spelling editions as the literary equivalent of insensitive Victorian restoration of medieval churches.

Yet, if Morris was generally scrupulous in his fidelity to an author's orthography, he was in other respets remarkably free in his treatent of literary documents. He never hesitated to alter punctuation, and he offered emendations when he believed the text was corrupt. The liberties that Morris took with the Caxton books in particular were described by Sparling: 'Those edited by F. S. Ellis, the Golden Legend and the Order of Chivalry, in deference to the editor's tastes and desires, were, as nearly as might be, textual and literal reproductions of Caxton's editions. The three others, the Historyes of Troye, Reynard the Foxe and Godefrey of Boloyne [all edited by Sparling], were differently treated, as Morris wished them to be regarded as Kelmscott Press editions, and therefore to be amended where this was desirable. Caxton's text was to be taken as a basis, but not looked upon as archaeologically sacrosanct. It was to be collated with Caxton's originals, and corrected where need was, mistranslations being put right and omissions filled in, care being taken to preserve the style and flavour of Caxton in doing this. When we came to the Godefrey of Boloyne, Morris decided that the original spelling need not be rigorously adhered to, as Caxton was an erratic speller, following no discernible rule, and that we were consequently free to retrench or add avoided thereby."26

On the other hand, when it came to modern writers, Morris worried a good deal about textual accuracy. Following his usual practice o arefully inspecting the frs sheets that came off the press, Mors s o o er htThe P Jo Kea 8 included a slightly garbled version of 'La Belle Dame sans merci' and insisted with some passion that the poem be reprinted at once.² What is especially fascinating about this episode is its revelation that Morris hadnotipe the text beor it was set up in type,and this in c was equenty the case at the Kelscott res Depenigheavy n the literary advice of Ellis, Cockerell, and others, Morris focused his own attention mainly on the designing of the books.

The basic question that faced Morris when he founded the Kelnscott Press was what books he should print, and his initial short answer was 'Caxton's books' Thc Press was created, in part, as an outlet for Morris's antiquarian enthusiasm for England's first printer (which of course did not extend to Caxton's typography): The Golden Legend was the first book planned, and it was only its length and minor technical problems that prevented it from being the first book printed at the Kelmscott Press. Morris never completely lost his intercst in Caxton, yet as time went by he came to speak of the Caxton reprints as books of mainly archaeological interest — surely a somewhat dismissive phrase — and, encouraged by Elis and Cockerell, turned his attention to writers of the nineteenth century as well. 'The books that I would like to print are the books I love to read and keep,' he explained in a intervi.That meant, not srisigly, wn boks above all; as early as November 1891 he was expressing the hope that he could reprint all his works at the Kelmscott Press, beginning with The  e c i wa books issued at the Press, in 1898). Morris also wrote especially for the Press several prose romances with vaguely medieval settings. These struck Bernard Shaw as a lamentable form of escapism that failed to advance the cause of socialism: 'Morris, to fill the maw of the Kelmscott Press, began to pour out tale after tale ofknights in armour, lovely ladies, slaughterous hand-to-hand combats, witches in enchanted castles changing humans into beasts, lovelorn heroes going mad in the mountains, haunted woods, quests after impossibilities, all under medieval conditions. . . .' Shaw claimed that he attempted without success to 'check the voracity of the Kelmscott Press' by suggesting to Morris that he turn his attention to manufacturing musical instruments to the old standards of craftsmanship.3°

About some of the other writers he published at the Press Morris displayed a conspicuous lack of enthusiasm. He did not particularly care for Tennyson's Maud and would have preferred to print his earlier poems, but Macmillan, the publisher, insisted upon Maud.'Coleridge', wrote Morris to Ellis, 'was a muddle-brained metaphysician, who by some strange freak of nature turned out a few real poems amongst the dreary flood of inanity which was his wont.'3 Of Herrick he remarked languidly, 'I like him better than I thought I should: I daresay we shall make a pretty book of it.' The truth is that Morris's deepest literary preferences lay in the Middle Ages, and medieval texts, whether by Caxton or others, were the real raison d'être of the Kelmscott Press, despite his efforts to enlarge the range of titles he printed. Something of the intensity of Morris's passion emerges in a note he composed about The Recuyell of the Historyes of Troye for Quaritch's catalogue: 'For though written at the end of the Middle Ages and dealing with but is purely medixval. . . . Surely this is well worth reading, if only as a piece of undiluted Mediævalism.'

Morris received editorial advice at the Kelmscott Press from a small inner circle of friends — Ellis, Cockerell, Furnivall, Bernard Quaritch, and perhaps Sparling - and one suspects that he would have been pleased if the books could have been printed by an equally small group of craftsmen. It is typical of Morris that he initially hired three members of one family (the Bowdens) to work at the Press, for it reflects the atmosphere of intimacy and familiarity that characterized the Press, particularly in its early years. William Bowden, the masterprinter brought out of retirement by Morris,³ found the work of the Kelmscott Press too burdensome for one pair of hands and enlisted the aid of both his son W. H. Bowden and his daughter Mrs Pine, who appear to have joined their father in February and April respectively (see Appendix C). W. H. Bowden soon became Morris's foreman; Mrs Pine, who was paid the same wages as the men (contrary to the usual practice at the time), achieved the distinction of becoming the first woman to join the London Society of Compositors.34

Mrs Pine, her father, and a pressman named Giles soon retired from the Kelmscott Press, but Morris's staff grew as the output of the Press increased. A photograph taken in September 1895 shows eleven printers working for Morris at that time; the size of the establishment obviously fluctuated according to how many books were in production, and it seems probable that Morris's staff reached its maximum size in 1895 and early 1896 when work on the Chaucer was approaching completion. By the time the final sheet of the last book was printed in March 1898, there was only one pressman, Stephen Mowlem, still employed at Sussex Cottage.

Though W. H. Bowden himself was not a socialist, several of Morris's printers — John Tippett, Stephen Mowlem, H. Howes, and Thomas Binning — had been associated with either (or, in Binning's case, both) the Hammersmith Socialist League or C. R. Ashbee's Guild of Handicraft.3s Binning, who had been the foreman printer of the Commonweal and became father of the chapel (the printers' equivalent of shop steward) at the Kelnscott Press, was a stern, unbending old trade-unionist who intimidatcd even Ashbee, for whon he later worked at the Essex House Press. 'I never nict a man so solid, so upright, so honest, so reliable, so obstinate,' said Ashbee. 'For the 10 ycars he has been with me he has never missed a day[,] not an hour, he has never shirked or loitered at his work, never made a nistake that I know of, he hates the world and its institutions and he loves his craft i mut detal It s a pont o with i to touch his hat to anyone ("least of all to me', added Ashbee), but he l Ml  ly.

It was probably Binning who, through the London Society of Compositors, arranged for the unionization of the Kelmscott Press. Though the presence of Mrs Pine at first created obstacles, in time all were enrolled as members of the LSC. When initially approached by the union authorities, Morris was able to reply truthfully that his printers were already well treated. Precisely how much they were paid is not known: Emery Walker implied that their wages were high, and H. Gage-Cole, who was an apprentice at the Kelmscott Press before working at the Doves Press, claimed that the Kelmscott pressman were paid five shillings a week above the standard rate.37 W. H. Bowden testified that 'Mr. Morris never tied his men down to any certain number of hours. He was most generous in the matter of holidays. On every Bank Holiday they had two days instead of the usual one day. When any o his men were ll i it was only a short time, they always rcvedfull pay; iit as or lie the ha full pyr h and half pay afterwards.'38

The Kelmscott printers must also have enjoyed the odd sensation of being the centre of attention in the world of English book-lovers. Journalists and other curius isitor decended upo Suse Coe regularly to see the celebrated Kelmscott Press. 'Press invaded in the aft[ernoo]n by 6o members of the Librarians' Association,' Cockerell wrote in his diary (22 May 1895) on one such occasion. 'Went off all right however.' In the autumn of 1893 one of the Kelmscott books, Morris' Gothc Architecture, was ctually rinted at theArts and Cras Exhibition in front of crowds that, according to Sparling, "imposed a severe strain upon the pressman Collins's Celtic modestyAnd there were pleasant socialoccasions. On the day of the Universiy boatrace in 1893 Morris invited his printers to tea at Kelnscott House (Cockerel diary, 22March 1893), while nce a year he and May joie them o teugkow in he trade s WayzeoseAftersusl dinner at a hotel or inn ncar London (the Dunb Bell Hotel at Taplow, Berkshire, in 1894, for exanple), the printers celebrated with a lengthy programme of toasts, songs, and recitations. Figure 57 Morris took his printers on an annual outing known in the trade as a 'Wayzegoose', where thcy enjoyed luncheon, port, and cigars. After dinner thcre were recitations and songs.

This all sounds rather jolly, and no doubt was, yet it is important to bear in mind that Morris's relationship with his printers was not always serene. The conflict over the use of the Jänecke ink, described in Chapter Four, resulted in a serious rebellion by the pressmen in Sussex Cottage, and only by threatening to close the Press altogether was Morris able to persuade them to adopt the ink he wanted. Afterwards 'the number of sheets turned out per day was extraordinarily small, according to Walker's sarcastic summary of the episode.4' The perspective that Walker is able to offer on Morris's printers is helpful in this case because, although he became strongly identified with the private-press movement, Walker, unlike Morris, was also accustomed to producing books under normal commercial conditions. The Kelmscott Press, notwithstanding its modest fnancial success, represented a special kind of experiment that would not have been possible in any ordinary printing establishment. When Morris was unhappy with the appearance of a trial page, he sent it back to be redone for the sixth time. When he discovered an error on the first sheet off the press, he had the passage reset while the pressmen stood idly by.

This pursuit of perfection both impressed and horrified visitors to the Kelscott Press. Sparling rather complacently, tells the story of a head of a large commercial printing firm, who watched Morris's cirs eullyg n imoceully ui after line; look wih a disontented eye t the presen efully pulling sheet tr sheet, minuely xaminig c  t se wheher it were up to the mark; and as he left, summed up his impressions: We-e-ll? That's all very well for Mr. Morris, but there isn't a man here that would be worth a penny an hour to me after he'd been here for a weck!" Sparling finds obvius pleasure in reciting th anote, whic h eeas a symbol of the iquities of the printing t;y it my itakitnh  l which printing had to be done in the world outside Sussex Cotte.

There was another person employed at the Kelmscott Press, not metioned by Sparling, who also played animportant role: Mrs Sarah A. Peddie, Cockerell's secretary. In the later years she coped with much of the voluminous correspondence which flowed incessantly into the Press, and Frank Colebrook (editor of the Printing Times and Lithographer) claimed that she transcribed all the Caxton books xT

##

Journalists, after visiting the Kelmscott Press, often rhapsodized about the extreme simplicity of the equipment used by Morris's printers: 'It was like stepping into Caxton's shop' (or words to that effect) was the usual response. One writer, so overwhelmed by the medieval splendour of the Press, even made Kelmscott House four hundred years older than it was.\* In fact, as we have seen, Morris was using nineteenthcentury hand-presses - Albions - that had been commonplace fixtures in nearly every English printing office until the middle of the century. The hand-press did of course seem slightly archaic by 1891, but it was still widely used for pulling proofs, printing posters, and producing limited editions; hence we must avoid the vulgar error of admiring Morris's reversion to medieval technology. It should be observed that Morris had no great difficulty in finding printers who were skilled in the use of the hand-press, for what he revived at the Kelmscott Press was a handicraft whose survival had been threatened only within the previous three or four decades.

Nevertheless, Morris was engaged in a deliberate simplification of the printing process when he founded his Press. But, unless one returns to scribal methods (which Morris always longed to do), books cannot be manufactured without machinery of some sort; even an Albion press is a machine, simple though it may be. The choices that Morris made in acquiring equipment for the Kelmscott Press were complicated by his well-known aversion to mechanical devices, yet his hostility appears to have been directed prinarily against what he called 'the great intangible machine of commercial tyranny'rather than 'this that tanbl el nd bas acie's type e t mechanically; his inks were manufactured in large factories; he used photography constantly as a labour-saving device at the Kelnscott Press; Frank Colebrook records that Morris even visited and adnired the modern printing establishnent of R. and R. Clark in Edinburgh, and that a Linotype salesman who called at the Press 'positively escape[d] with a whole skin Morris's attitude towards Vicran technology was altogether more ambivalent than some of his doctrinaire disciples supposed.

The small cottages strung along Hammersmith's Upper Mall in which Morris housed his Press were as unpretentious as the equipment the printers used. The rent book48 for 16 Upper Mall, the first home of the Kelmscott Press, shows that Morris's tenancy began on 12 January 1891; it ended in June of the same year, because in the meantime (from 2o May49) Morris had begun leasing Sussex Cottage, 14 Upper Mall, which remained the primary building of the Press until its closing. In 1893 Morris persuaded Cobden-Sanderson to lease 15 Upper Mall, opposite Sussex Cottage, for the Doves Bindery, with two rooms to be reserved for the Kelmscott proof-readers: one of Cobden-Sanderson's workers recalled that 'this brought Morris in and out many times a day, and with his marvellous interest in the detail of all craft-work, he made himself a familiar figure to the employés  This, however, was only a temporary arrangement, for on New Year's Day 1895 Morris took possession of still another small house, 21 Upper Mall (Sunderland Cottage), which was used for the large Albion press printing the Chaucer and for Cockerell's office. W. Irving Way, the American publisher, visited the Kelmscott Press in April 1895, and the vivid (albeit slightly sentimentalized) description he offers is probably of Sunderland Cottage: 'No sign marks the Kelmscott Press, . . . but . . . the right one is finally reached, and here Mr. S. C. Cockerell . . . greets the visitor with a smile, and at once puts him at ease. A quiet, tidy, orderly place it is, but with nothing modern about it. No noise of machinery, escaping steam, or hum of electric motor, distracts one. No mahogany furniture, Axminster rugs, or click of type-writer reminds one of nineteenth century progress. The furniture is plain deal like the floor, and with a "gray goosequill" the visitor registers his arrival in the guest-book.'

##

The modesty of the Kelmscott Press's machines and buildings, though it had an ideological basis, also reflected Morris's limited goals. His original intention had been to print only a small number of books - just enough of each title, perhaps, to distribute to his friends — and he did not at first envision becoming his own publisher. Morris's publisher during the 1870s and early 188os was F. S. Ellis, but in 1888 he turned to Reeves and Turner, who operated a bookshop and small publishing establishment in the Strand.² Thus there was nothing unexpected about Morris's decision to allow Reeves and Turner to publish most of the early Kelnscott books.

Nevertheless, those close to Morris grumbled occasionally about the inefficiency they detected in his publishers. 'You don't see [Morris's] books on bookstalls and I think it's for want of a businesslike publisher,' Burne-Jones commented; Jane Morris, in a similar vein, described them as ´the most unbusiness-like publishers but extremely honest'.³3 Reeves and Turner published eleven of the Kelmscott Press books, the last of which was More's Utopia, issued in September 1893, and Morris's connection with them was officially severed in June 1896.4 His relationship with Reeves and Turner, while it lasted, seems to have been happy, but he was uneasy about any suggestion that the Kelmscott Press books were less than completely his own productions. Reeves and Turner were merely the distributors for the Kelmscott Press, not the publishers in the usual sense, and Morris emphasized this in the formula he adopted in the Kelmscott colophons: 'Sold by Reeves & Turner.' (In the proofs of The Defence of Guenevere that line read 'Published by Reeves & Turner' but was changed.s) 'I don't mind having a publisher' Morris declared, long  he has nothing whatever to do with any question except purely business ones.' One possibility that he considered briefly was to print a book and then sell tein n, thua tee at all.56

Four of the Kelmscott Press books were published by Bernard Quaritch, who, following his arrival from Berlin in 1842, had rapidly made himself London's leading bookseller. To his first English employer, Henry G. Bohn, Quaritch announced, 'Mr. Bohn, you are the first bookseller in England, I mean to become the first bookseller in Euroe.Energetc, gressive, learnd, Quaric was a ve ferent person from nice, inoffensive old William Reeves, and there were serious misunderstandings between him and Morris from the L  ot the contract, Quaritch raised it on the day of publication and which Mr. Q. offered to supply them, did not push the book at the time, & the result was that a good many copies remained on Mr. Q's hands,' Cockerell wrote two years later." There was other evidence of what Morris took to be sharp dealing by Quaritch, and early in 1893 he reached a decision that infuriated Quaritch. 'Mr. W. Morris wishes v l  o  e   s son in America on 8 February; 'he will retail himself the ["]Godfrey of Bouloyne"  he will be a loser by it, but it will be a nuisance to me on account of the Chaucer.'" Morris was at first conciliatory: 'It is true that I shall probably publish the Sidonia myself, as I have done with the Godfrey, but I do not suppose I shall generally publish the Kelmscott books myself,' he declared in a letter to Quaritch the following July." Yet this was precisely what he did do. Quaritch, who had obviously hoped to be the publisher of the Chaucer, was furious. Morris's decision therefore had the unfortunate effect of poisoning the later negotiations with Quaritch about the number of copies of the Chaucer — and on what terms — he would be supplied.'Mr. Wm Morris will call on me tomorrow,' Quaritch informed his son on 20 August 1894; 'he is laying down the law to me about the Chaucer, — and I must submit.

![](images/06a402f75134bde825bc9dd8d9d951a870d05d71b7bb8b71ede6d752d7cefb16.jpg)  
Figure 59 Bernard Quaritch, London's leading bookseller, commissioned The Golden Legend, the most ambitious book issued by the Kelmscott Press during the early years of its existence. Later he and Morris quarrelled about publishing arrangements for somc of the other Kelmscott books.

This has cooled my ardour to obtain the transfer of his publications from Reeves & Turner. Mr. Morris's success has turned him into a despot.' The following day Quaritch reported that 'W. Morris did not dare to show his face' but instead sent Cockerell (a very sharp fellow') as his representative. 'My friendship with Morris is at an end,' lamented Quaritch. In the autumn they reopened negotiations, and at last Quaritch felt satisfied with the trade discount he was to receive on the Chaucer. 'Mr. William Morris & I are on the road of being reconciled, he sees now how hastily he acted,' Quaritch announced triumphantly to his son on 12 November 1894. But it was a partial victory at best, because Quarch was never allowed to publish another Kelmscott book.

Several publishers — Ellis and Elvey, George Allen, Macmillan, and Way and Williams — commissioned books to be printed at the Kelmscress, and, at F.S.El's sggetin, Ellis anElvey even pro that they be allowed to succeed Reeves and Turner. Yet there was little inducement for Morris to seek the services of another publisher, for J. and J. Leighton, his binder, performed some of the same funs  Reve nTurner he bok w  rty and book dealers from Leighton), and Morris was ager t repace, a much as possible, the middlemen between the craftsman and his customers. Acting as his own publisher, as Sparling pointed out, also gave Morris 'exact information with regard to the real and immediate sale of each book, and thus placed him in a position to guard against over-printing'.62

Many of the Kelmscott Press books, especially the 16mos and octavos, were completely sold before they were off the press. Any advertising of the books was at first done by Quaritch, and Reeves and Turner, but in May 1892 Morris issued a simple, two-page list indicating the titles already and soon to be printed at the Kelmscott Press, and periodically thereafter he announced his books by means of these lists (which grew longer and more elaborate) and similar circulars describing individual volumes, soe of the latter containing specimen pages. 'We only send out special announcements of books which are not nearly sold out when they go to the binder,' Cockerell explained.63 They proved to be an effective, low-key method of advertising: in all fyne uc crclars we issu byh Kelscot Pres athy are prized by collectors today.

Because the Kelmscott Press was operated in the glare of publicity the British Bookmaker (July 1892) summed up a popular attude when it declared simply, 'The doings of the Kelmscott Press are of Mo  ot have  ere  boo lyEvey bibliophile, bookseller, and librarian in the English-speaking world seemed to be aware of the existence of the Kelmscott Press. Even when the Press was attacked in print, its fame increased. And strong attacks there were: someone writing in 1895 under the pseudonym of 'Arthur Pendenys' addressed an open letter to Morris which began, I presume that the Kelmscott Books are published for your own amusement, because I have inquired extensively and find they do not amuse any one else. They are very dull books, much more dull than Law books and Medical books, and there are only two on your list, the Shakespeare Sonnets and the Keats, which I should prefer to a Post Office Directory.The letter went on to condemn Morris's prices, types, and every aspect of the Kelmscott books. Morris was presumably enraged when he saw this abusive yet amusing document, but he might have found consolation in the commonsense principle - subsequently elevated to a divine truth in Hollywood — that bad publicity is better than no publicity at all.

None the less, it was necessary for the Kelmscott Press to communicate with its potential customers in some systematic fashion. The Press's list of ' Addresses to which announcements were sent [in] 1897' has survived (in the Morgan Library), and it makes instructive reading. The document includes more than 8oo names - mostly book dealers — of whom twenty-one were Americans ('We have at present very few private customers in America,' Cockerell wrote in 1894"5), with several other nationalities represented; among the names of individuals
