are Bernard Shaw, Ellen Terry, George Meredith, A. Edward Newton, Holman Hunt, C. R. Ashbee, F. J. Furnivall, and Luther A. Brewer (identified as 'State Inspector of Illuminating Oils, Cedar Rapids, Iowa'). Walker was later to make use of this same list at the Dove PresMorrs nd Burne-Jones ofte proteted that handful of people had any interest in the Kelmscott books ("I do the books mainly for you and one or two others; the public does not really care about them a damn,' Morris confided to Philip Webb67), but the size of the address list and the actual sales of the books belie this claim. Morris offered a fairer appraisal when he wrote to Quaritch before the publication of The Golden Legend: 'for my part I feel sure there will be a moderate public who will like it very much.'68

There was a small group of customers who were prepared to buy anything Morris printed at the Kelmscott Press. In March 1898, when he was closing down the Press, Cockerell sent Jane Morris a list of twelve names (ten British, two American) of persons who owned either all the Kelmscott books or all those written by Morris. And Morris always took care to see that his closest friends received complimentary copies: a letter from Cockerell specifying those who were to be given the second volume of The Poetical Works of Percy Bysshe Shelley mentions Burne-Jones, his wife Georgiana, Philip Webb, Emery Walker, Elizabeth Burden (Jane's sister), Theodore Watts, Swinburne, Kate Faulkner, May (Morris) Sparling, and F. and R. Smith (the last two employees of Morris and Company).° Review copies were, according to Cockerell, posted to less than half a dozen of the London newspapers, though the Daily News and the Daily Chronicle, whose political views Morris approved, were consistently on this short list.71

Despite the commercial success of the Kelmscott books in general, there were naturally variations in this pattern from one volume to another, and Morris never ceased to worry about the possibility of printing too many copies. The first Kelmscott book, The Story of the Glittering Plain, stunned everyone when the entire edition was disposed of long before publication - indeed, long before even the price was announced by Reeves and Turner - and Morris found himselfshort of copies for his friends.7² Another of the early books, The Poems of William Shakespeare, was probably the most popular volume ever issued by the Press. At the other extreme, The History of Godefrey of Boloyne, as glumly predicted by Quaritch, sold so badly that Jane Morris had todonate the overstock to British public libraries in 1897.

Morris abandoned one of his announced books, Vitas patrum, when the advance subscriptions proved to be too meagre. The last circular announcement distributed by the Kelmscott Press - dated 25 February 1898 - reveals that, as the Press was winding down its operations, it still had on hand copies of the following books: The History of Godefrey of Boloyne (two), The Well at the World's End (eleven), Sidonia the Sorceress (six paper, two vellum), and The Tale of Beowulf (thirteen).

Morris was distressed to discover that many of the Kelmscott books were being bought by dealers who held them in stock until their value rose — as it did almost immediately. A contributor to the Contemporary Review in 1898, praising the Chaucer, adopted an unfortunately apt figure of speech when he wrote that the book's paper was 'firm and crisp to the touch like the paper of a Bank of England note'.7³ Works printed in the Golden type generated real gold in the sale rooms, though none of it found its way into Morris's pocket. Periodicals such as the Connoisseur and the Scottish Review began to publish league tables in which the escalating prices of Kelmscott books were charted in stupefying detail.74 J. H. Slater, an experienced observer of the book trade, commented in 1899 that the Kelmscott books 'will soon be unprocurable except at a ruinous price' Though, like other examples of fine printing, the Kelmscott Press volumes have fluctuated in market value from time to time (especially during the first two decades of this century), the trend to this day has been upward: one could not reasonably expect in 1987, for example, to pay less than ,6,500 for a paper Chaucer or $\mathcal { L } ^ { \mathrm { ~ I ~ } 3 }$ ,ooo for a vellum copy. (And these figures continue to calateictably, rs h boo went t s newspapers throughout the world were trumpeting the astonishing price of \$330,000 realized by a vellum Chaucer sold at Christie's, New York, in May 1989.) P. H. Muir once renarked that 'anyone well well informed on book-collecting history could venture a shrewd guess at the state of the money-market from the current price of a Kelmscott Chaucer'.76

As an old bookseller, Ellis professed to be bewildered by this development but could scarcely conceal his professional excitement. 'Chaucer $\oint 2 7 ~ -$ at Sothebys last week. We shall (some of us) live to see it bring $\mathcal { L }$ 100 — . ,' he wrote to Cockerell in 1899. And in eratthat g:'TheK..okeeu l way! Shelley £23  & Keats £26! And we were told they were dear at $\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { - } 3 . 1 5 - \& \ 3 \mathrm { o } \big \vert - . } \end{array}$ What a coedy we live in.While Ellis secetly rejoiced, Morrs - who saw this happening in his lifetime — was fu ly l tb ats.I tat an interview in 1895; 'but although I don't like that, I don't see what's to be done to ae s things  The two namiiar ter o condemnation that Morris used are medieval words meaning, respectively, those 'who bought up produce to hold it for a rise' and those who bought and sold in the market or within five miles of it' But although Morris would not tolerate the freewheeling entrepreneurial style Quaritch (aulure Cockerell callehimin private, odoubt reflecting Morris's opinion\*), he could not prevent his books from falling into the hands of other, even more unscrupulous, forestallers and regraters.

Another form of capitalistic exploitation of his goods that Morris resented was the pirating of the Kelmscott Press types and ornaments, particularly in the United States where he had no legal protection. Morris insisted that he was concerned not about preservation of private property but about the improper use of his designs, which often appeared in books printed in weak modern typefaces on cheap paper. To G. W. Jones he remarked, 'Men have a perfect right to use my designs, they have a perfect right to copy me if they can use my designs rightly, but not if they cannot use them. Every man has a right to everything he can use well in this world, but only to what he can use well.' In practice, these high-principled sentiments translated into a consistent refusal by Morris to allow his types and ornaments to be employed by another printer. He reluctantly authorized the periodical Bookselling to reproduce several of his borders but grew agitated when E. F. Strange requested permission to use them as an illustration in a book as well. Morris "allowed them to appear there as it is an ephemeral publication, but he does not think them very satisfactory, & would not like to see them in a permanent book', Cockerell declared in a letter to Strange.82 Even when George Allen reprinted three of the Kelmscott Press books under the title Old French Romances Done into English by William Morris (1896), Morris declined to let him make use of the enraved title-pages whic hadappeare i the Kelmscott editins.

But the Kelmscott Press had scarcely issued its first book before trouble began to brew in the United States. Roberts Brothers of Boston, who in 189o had published an apparently unauthorized edition of News from Nowhere, wrote to Morris in 1891 ordering some copies of the Kelmscott Glittering Plain and requesting permission to produce an American edition of Poems by the Way from early sheets. 'I will send them sheets on the definite understanding that they are not to attempt to imitate the style of the typography,' Morris informed Reeves and Turner.84 What Roberts Brothers did instead was to create a photographic facsimile of The Glittering Plain, which they issued in a limited edition of goo copies in October 1891.

News of still more alarming developments came from across the Atlantic. In early 1894, while Morris was preparing an edition of Rossetti's Sonnets and Lyrical Poems for Ellis and Elvey, the latter unexpectedly received from another Boston publisher, Copeland and Day, a version of The House of Life done in elaborate imitation of the Kelmscott manner. With three borders and 114 initials designed by Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue, The House of Life sold for \$2.50 (ordinary edition) and \$5.oo (Michallet paper, rubricated initials). 'However much we may disapprove of this Publication, & the bad imitation of the Kelmscott Press we fear we cannot help it,' Ellis and Elvey wrote to William Michael Rossetti, who was seeing the Kelmscott edition through the press. 'They do not tell us what the price is but from their letter we infer it is comparatively cheap.' The only consolation Ellis and Elvey could offer him, a few days later, was that it is not likely to interfere with the Kelmscott edition'The reaction at Kelmscott House was less restrained. Morris hinself was 'very scornful' of the American imitation but temporarily tricked his wife into believing it was one of his own books; Jane, once enlightened, described it as "an impudent swindle'.86

The Roberts Brothers and Copeland and Day volumes were merely the first trickle of the great food of Kelmscott-style books to be produced in the United States during the last decadof the century, most of them printed in types closely patterned after Morris's. 'Have you heard of the wrong they've [i.e. the Americans] just done him. . 'Burne-Jone demanded hi studio aistant in Jauy 1896. 'Some wicked wretch over there has been photographing and copying all his letters and ornaments, and those he reproduces exactly he cals the Jenson letters, and those he mommocks [sic] and alters and l hecllMotesThat g, i'i He' age, and it's no wonder. The fame of the Kelmscott Press had travelled rapidly across the ocean, and photoengraving techniques made it absurdly easy for American typefounders to reproduce — and modify to any extent they wished - Morris's designs. Either Joseph W.Phinney f the Dickion Type Foundry (Boston)or H. L.Bullen

![](images/25175ef9873dc4e1b134a11740c0b48bd0f4d2c3c51853525739ff97bdb2bfc7.jpg)

THE JENSON OLD-STYLE SERIES, MADE BY AMERICAN TYPE FOUNDERS' CO. (DICKIN-SON TYPE FOUNDERY BRANCH), BOSTON.

![](images/46b932c374c85d97139122d3d1307050b806f08f83f463ee28f5db6ad201d84d.jpg)

N THIS specimen the Dickinson Typefoundery (Boston) exhibits a variation of the types of Nicholas Jenson, a Frenchman, who at Venice, in 1470, founded the true Roman, which thereafter dominated European typography. William Morris, of the Kelmscott Press, has modified these shapes from the originals, but in all the essentials they belong to the Jenson font. The form is round and clear, with great boldness and dignity; the lines are full of strong black and white effects, adapted to old-style work. Jenson derived his Roman characters from a mixture of alphabets of various countries, modelling these letters into marvellous perfection. The original font is on about a 14-point size, and consists of twenty-three capital alphabets (J, U and W then not being in use); twenty-three lower-case characters, fifteen contractions, six double letters and three points (period, colon and interrogation), making a total of seventy-three punches. Aldus Manutius finally inherited these punches, matrices and types, and later became famous for the fine alphabets designed by himself. The attempt to lessen Jenson's fame as the originator of the present forms of Romans by crediting their use to the Weidenbach monks, at their monastery, in 1465 (five years before Jenson's publication), has not succeeded with thoughtful students.

Figure 61 Jenson Old-Style type, patterned after Morris's first typeface, had a considerable vogue in the United States and elsewhere during the last decade of the nineteenth century.

Under various names many of them became available throughout Europe.

Eventually some of these reproductions reached England, where Morris and Cockerell struggled to keep them off the market. Early in 1896, for example, Cockerell was consulting a solicitor 'about the piracy by an American firm of a great quantity of Kelmscott initials &ornaments' which were being sold in England (Cockerell diary, 15 and 20 January 1896). A more serious problem arose in December 1897 when Cockerell discovered that Sir Charles Reed and Sons, Morris's own typefounders, were advertising an American imitation under the name 'Kelmscott Old Style' in their latest specimen sheet. Cockerell, always an alarming adversary when he thought Morris's interests were in jeopardy, moved against the Reeds at once. Threatened with legal action, they agreed to withdraw the offending specimens, to announce that the name of Kelmscott could not be used by any customers who had already purchased the type, and to surrender the matrices to Cockerell. It was total capitulation. 'Reeds have climbed down & are reprinting their spec[ime]ns but I don't feel very warmly towards them,' Cockerell wrote grimly.89

The anxiety that Morris felt about American imitations of the Kelmscott types and books indicates more than an artistic concern that his designs might be used inappropriately. Like the Victorian industrialists that he condemned, Morris was trying to protect his market against cheap foreign imports. This point is worth emphasizing because, whenever one attempts to assess the business side of any of Morris's activities, the evidence suddenly becomes obscured by a smoke-screen of socialist piety. Jeanette L. Gilder, for instance, interviewed Morris in the last year of his life and recorded this characteristic exchange: 'I asked Mr. Morris to tell me, if it was not an impertinent question, whether it paid him pounds, shillings and pence to print such handsome books. He answered that it did; but his secretary [Cockerell], who had just entered the room, begged leave to contradict this statement. "You have not," said he, "counted your work and your time, Mr. Morris. You are thinking only of the mechanical part; but if you put in your time and your skill, at what you would be paid for them by another person, you could hardly say that your publications made money." Mr. Morris smiled but said no more on the subject. The profit did not seem to interest him very much: it was the artistic results which he accomplished that pleased him."

Cockerell's point is of course well taken: Morris's own invaluable contributions were never appraised in calculating the profits and losses of the Kelmscott Press, and we must never forget the genuinely impressive idealism that lay behind the whole of Morris's career, including the Kelnscott Press. Nevertheless — whatever Miss Gilder may have supposed  he was indeed preoccupied with the profits made by his books. This is not to say the profit motive was his main concern at the Press, which really was, as he said, a typographical adventure and therefore financially risky, but Morris never lost sight of the need to sell books and to pay his employees. This unexpected combination of hard-headed business sense and Utopian idealism has puzzled many admirers of Morris, and it has prompted others to accuse him of hypocrisy. Morris himself, as one inight expect, had thought long and deeply about the problem. He argued that, until the socialist revolution came, the employer was obliged to work within the capitalist system, no matter how much he might personally despise it." What Morris failed to take sufficiently into account was how tainted even the most pure craft activity might becone through commercial considerations; this, after all, is what happened to the Kelmscott Press, which began as an attempt to restore aesthetic (and moral) integrity to bookmaking and ended as the publisher of expensive books purchased mainly by wealthy collectors. Morris's friend and fellow-socialist, Walter Crane, saw very clearly the extent to which the achievements of craftsnen and artists were coloured by the corrupting ethos of the maplace.'My difulty h e Ase 89w to etablish any o ysteworking r taching whil the preent economic system exists to work its destruction ultimately. Do what one will it seems one is bound to be devoured by commercialism in some form or another.'92

Another sourceodifficulty in analysing the ncial state the Kelmscott Press is that Morris, despite his business acumen, took a decidedly casual attitude towards record-keeping. When he sent Crane a cheque for his share of the profits from the second version of The Glittering Plain, Morris promised to 'get an account made out which I shall take care is not too much humbugged by the accountant, who (any one of them) could easily make out that there was no profit I ill is effectually.' There was a confusing overlap between Morris's personal bankaccount and that ofthe Press, and during Sparling's time as Secretary the financial records, like much else at the Kelmscott Press, were in disarray. 'In future we may be able to keep a closer record of expenditures than hitherto,' Cockerell reported to the Press's account ant in August 1894, shortly after replacing Sparling.4 In addition to invoices and various other kinds of scattered records, there exists a ledger (in the Morgan Library) describing the cost of production of all the individual Kelmscott books printed between 1893 and 1896 as well as other profit-and-loss accounts and inventories; but the ledger has unfortunately not yet been subjected to careful scrutiny by scholars familiar with nineteenth-century bookkeeping conventions, and so it is not now possible to say with absolute certainty how often, and to what degree, Morris realized a profit on his books. The safest generalization one can offer at present is that he nearly always broke even (bearing in mind, again, that his services were unpaid) and that sometimes he came out slightly ahead. Selecting one title completely at random, for instance, one can readily calculate, based on the most conservative assumptions, that Morris's gross receipts from More's Utopia, an octavo published in an edition of 30o paper copies (thirty shillings) and eight vellum copies (ten guineas), could not have been less than £300, whereas it cost only about £255 to produce.

Morris was eager to explain to journalists that he was not a bloated capitalist: 'You have, no doubt, heard a great deal of the enormous proit I must be making on these books,' he told an interviewer in 1895, bee of the high pris I ask for them. I assre you that, if the people who go about talking of my profits were to see my balance sheet, they would speak quite differently.' The two examples he cited were Beowulf, which he said he sold at a loss, and the Chaucer, which represented Morris's largest investment and thus caused him much apprehension before it was finally published. When he feared that word about his ink troubles with the Chaucer might spread to the public, he wrote in great agitation to F. J. Furnivall (who had a notoriously loose tongue): 'I must beg you not to say a word about it anybod  you illoe very es injuryOnlyt pressrun of the Chaucer was increased from 325 to 425 copies did Morris feel secure from financial disaster. On the other hand, Morris sold his smaller books without difficulty and, as a matter of deliberate policy, used them to finance the printing of the folios and quartos. 'I count rather on these little ones for getting cash to carry on the Chaucer, as the little books pay me much better than the big,' Morris admitted to Quaritch.7 There is also evidence to suggest that Morris squeezed every penny he could fron publishers who commissioned Kelmscott Press books: Maud, for example, cost him only £ 202 14s. Id. to produce (according to the Press ledger), yet he charged Macmillan £400 for the paper copies alone.98

It is obvious that the slow, careful methods of design and production followed at the Kelmscott Press increased the cost of the books, and there are thirteen known instances of alterations being made in the Kelmscott volumes after printing had begun, usually involving the resetting and reprinting of a signature or a pair of conjugate leaves. Another unusual expense at the Press was Batchelor's paper, for which he charged about five or six times the price of machine-made book paper; Walker pointed out that the cost of the paper in a single copy of the Chaucer was approximately 25s. On the other hand, Morris recovered some of this expenditure by allowing a smaller trade discount than was usual, a policy which caused occasional friction between him and the booksellérs. For Reeves and Turner, and Quaritch, the discount was 20 per cent; for all other booksellers, and for a few friends like Robert Proctor, it was I5 per cent.

There is an undated Kelmscott Press circular printed in the Golden type and addressed 'To the Trade only' which reads as follows: 'We send you the enclosed, and shall be glad to receive your orders. The discount to the trade will be 15 per cent., and we must ask you to understand that these ters are strictly for cash. We shall write you when the book is actually ready or delivery, and on receipt o your cheque your order will be executed.' The tone of this communication would not have been uncongenial to Dickens's Mr Bounderby. Bt e hard, finty foundation of commercial shrewdness. When we are tempted to burst into rhapsodical praise for Morris's triumphant revivalomedieval cratsmanship, it  useful toremember tha.

# SEVEN  THE STORY OF THREE BOOKS

![](images/835bf430e965e3b1f7668197250fc010ea3040ef3c064e0ebe56f8417d58d250.jpg)

HE Kelmscott Press books, as we have seen already, come in a variety of sizes and forms: some are long (Chaucer, 564 folio pages), some short (Epistola de contemptu mundi, sixteen pages); a few are heavy with ornaments (Godefrey of Boloyne), while others are decorated only with a few small initials and a border on the first text page; a minority are

typographically elaborate (Atalanta in Calydon (1894) has three t p, o y simple in design. Despite Morris's preference for medieval texts, there s lit diversy in the Keltt oos, whic rt works written over a span of more than a thousand years.

For these reasons it is difficult, indeed dangerous, to offer broad generalizations about how the books of the Kelmscott Press were produced. The process by which Morris designed and printed the Epistola, for example, was very different from that followed in manufacturing The Well at the World's End, which was in production from 1892 to 1896 (the Interninable', Morris called it). If we wish to move beyond glib truisms, therefore, we must look carefully at particular instances of Morris the printer at work and try to understand the ordinary, day-to-day problens with which he struggled at the Press. In this chapter we will examine the production history of three Kelmscott books: Morris's Poems by the Way (1891), Wilfrid Scawen Blunt's The Love-Lyrics & Songs of Proteus (1892), and The Golden Legend (1892). Because they are all early works of the Press (the second, third, and seventh books issued, respectively), there is an unusually large amount of documentary evidence available, and though unfortunately the Press's ledger does not cover the period when they were printed, it is possible to reconstruct financial information from other sources. These particular books also possess the merit of representing the three major types of literary texts that Morris printed: his own books, the writings of other Englishmen (nainly poets), and medieval works.

##

Even though it was the last of these three books to be published, The Golden Legend deserves to be considered first since it was the earliest book planed for the Kelmscott Press. The following notice appeared in the 'Literary Gossip' column of the Athenaeum on I3 September 1890: 'Students of English mediaeval literature will be gratified to learn that an exact reprint of "The Golden Legend" as originally set forth by William Caxton is in preparation, under the joint editorship of Mr. William Morris and Mr. F. S. Ellis. If it is found practicable to obtain the use of a copy of the first edition it is proposed to reproduce that text with scrupulous accuracy, supplementing it with a glossary and index. In place of the black letter, to the use of which there are manifold objections, a fount of types newly designed by Mr. Morris after the fashion of those employed by Nicholas Jenson will be substituted. . . . Some time must necessarily elapse . . . before the work can go to press, though it will be proceeded with immediately. The editors have agreed to give their labours gratuitously in consideration of Mr. Quaritch bearing all expenses of production. As the impression will be a limited one, subscribers would do well to send their names to the publisher forthwith.' This announcement, which came from a remarkably well-informed source (probably F. J. Furnivall), constitutes in fact the earliest reference in print to any of the Kelmscott Press books.

During the summer of 189o Morris, as he was designing his Roman type, pondered the possibility of reprinting one of two Caxton books either The Golde Legend (1483) or The Recuyell f the Historyes f Troye (1474) - which would be published by Bernard Quaritch. With this in mind, he purchased on 21 July 1890 a copy of a slightly later edition of The Golden Legend (Wynkyn de Worde, 1527), and, when Cockerell saw Morris on I4 August, he found that 'he thinks of reprinting [it] when his new type is all cast' (Cockerell diary). To Ellis, who had offered to edit the book, Morris wrote on 29 August: I did want to weigh between the Golden Legend and the Troy book for reprinting: now I have borrowed a Recueil of the Histories of Troy (the Wynkyn de Worde of course) from Quaritch, and have no doubt the      the (Eventually Morris printed the Troye as well.)

What was the nature of The Golden Legeud's appeal to Morris? A collection of saints' lives compiled by Jacobus de Voragine (or Varally bi e  Lu torum, sive Lonbardiea historia (Strasburg, c.14724), it struck some Victorian readers who did not share Morris's antiquarian enthusiasms a     o  e. In his 'Memoranda, Bibliographical & Explanatory' which appeared at the end of the third volume of the Kelmscott edition, Ellis offered this justification for reprinting it: 'Among the books which serve to illustrate the religious life and mode of thought that prevailed in the middle ages, none holds a more important place than the "Legenda Aurea." Compiled and put into form in the latter half of the 13th century, the welcome with which it was received by the clergy & the lettered world is attested by the great number of manuscripts o it is eencd by the fct that within a short tme s apearance it was translated into the vulgar tongue of most of the nations of Europe, frequently with the hagiological preferences of the people of the country for which it was designed' iii. 1282).

Quaritch, always the sly businessman, saw this venture as a means of snaring Morrs. 'You are no doubt aware of the high standing, as a poet, of William Morris, he remarked to his son on  June 189o; I have sold to him lately several early printed books. I also told hin I should like to publish some future book of his. I am in a fair way of getting such a new book from him; - and this may lead much further afterwards.² In early September 189o letters were flying back and forth among Morris, Ellis, and Quaritch as they negotiated a contract for the book. Quaritch was surprised to discover that the only modern reprint of The Golden Legend was an incomplete facsimile published by the Holbein Society in 1878 (a book that Morris owned). Furnivall's Early English Text Society, however, had printed several other Caxton translations during the 187os and 188os, and David Nutt, a London publisher, issued a limited edition of Caxton's Le Morte Darthur between 1889 and 1891; the latter was, like the Kelmscott Golden Legend, an exact reprint, but its typography was undistinguished and two of the three volumes were devoted to learned analyses and bibliographical studies of the text. Quaritch, who did not yet fully grasp Morris's new preoccupation with book-design, inquired innocently whether he perhaps intended to make The Golden Legend uniform with Nutt's book.³

'My interest in the book is double,' wrote Quaritch to Ellis, 'first I think every man of culture should be acquainted with the alleged Lives of Saints, and second I am aware of the philological value of all books printed by Caxton.' But then he offered a typical aside: 'Some of the naughty Saints stories will be relished by the numerous readers of Burton's Arabian Nights.'4 The contract’ (which was dated I I September 189o) specified that Morris and Ellis were to contribute their

By the end of 189o it was clear that Morris would print the book himself.

In selecting the edition he would reprint, Morris displayed less hesitation — it would be Caxton's first version, not the Wynkyn de Worde edition that he owned  but this meant finding a copy that could be borrowed. The Cambridge University Library agreed to lend its Caxton Golden Legend, heavily insured, to Ellis, whose daughter Phyllis (later Mrs Payne) then laboriously transcribed the entire book, so that the volume itself would not have to be touched by the inky hands of Morris's printers. It was an heroic undertaking, for which she never received the public recognition she deserved, and the few surviving scraps of her manuscript confirm W. H. Bowden's testimony that not one word of it was illegible." Later her father read the Kelmscott proofs directly against the book. Though the contract with Quaritch referred to Morris and Ellis as co-editors, Morris was only nominally involved in editorial decisions, thus setting the pattern for many later Kelmscott books. Ellis established the text, directing the occasional query to Morris; Morris agreed or disagreed with Ellis's proposals. That is how it always went between them, with much joking on Ellis's side and some blustering on Morris's. But in his concluding 'Memoranda' Ellis rather exaggerated Morris's participation: 'That I express myself in the first person throughout this notice, and sign it, is only because I am responsible for the statements as to matters of fact which it contains, & which I could not ask Mr. Morris to guarantee with his name, but it should be understood that he has gone carefully over the whole book, and nothing of any importance in the editing has been done without his advice and sanction' (iii. 285-6). The colophon is less equivocal, listing Ellis as the editor and Morris as the printer.

As usual, Ellis worried a good deal about bibliographical and textual matters while the book was in production. He found a French version of the Legenda aurea which he believed was Caxton's source, and corresponded enthusiastically with Francis Jenkinson, University

Librarian at Cambridge, about the matter. He also struggled to persuade Morris to include a glossary and a bibliography of English thy r of Some Obsolete or Little Used Words', but Morris declined to use the bibliography prepared by Edward Gordon Duff. In September 1 T types or the small fount which it would be desirable to use. He said he would get it printed by Whittingham [i.e. the Chiswick Press] as a separate paper to be given with the Golden Legend but whether he will think of it again or not I cannot say - but seeing the many irons he has in the e I am farful of its ettig forgotten & do not like to bother him further on the subject.'7 The real reason for its omission, however, was, as Ellis had admitted in an earlier letter, that the author-printer kicks much against introductions'.

The colophon of the Kelmscott Press Golden Legend describes it as a reprint in which there is no change from the original, except for the correction of errors of the press, & some few other amendments thought necessary for the understanding of the text'. Ellis's textual alterations in this and other Caxton books he edited for Morris have displeased some twentieth-century specialists, but to assess his work fairly we have to see it within the tradition of energetic amateur scholarship which flourished in Victorian England, particularly among the members of the societies founded by Furnivall. Ellis, though infinitely more scrupulous and careful in his treatment of of historical and literary documents was not yet solely the preserve of professionals as it mainly is today. Ellis's aim in preparing the text of The Golden Legend for the Kelmscott Press was not only to achieve verbal accuracy but also to anticipate the needs of non-specialist readers. Although much has been written about the typography and design of Kelmscott books, historians of the Press have failed to observe that Morris and Ellis were also engaged in a pioneering attempt at intelligent popularization of literary works drawn, in some instances at least, from obscure or remote sources. Measured by that standard, Ellis's achievements as an editor are impressive.

While Ellis concentrated on the text, Morris began to think about the physical shape of The Golden Legend. Ellis was eager to reproduce the general appearance of Caxton's book, but Morris had misgivings about the wisdom of this, because he recognized that the Roman type he would be using was different in nature from Caxton's Gothic, and perhaps also because he had learned from The Story of Gunnlaug the futility of such mock-quaintness. 'Of course I should like the reprint to be of the same form as the original if the Roman type can do it, which I doubt, as black letter takes up less room: in any case some kind of folio it will have to be,' he reported to Ellis in September 189o. 'In one thing I think I differ from you a little, i.e., about the joined letters or queer signs: since our book is to be a reprint, not a fac-simile, I do not think we need reproduce these: indeed I should extend the abbreviations in order to make the book more readable.'" By December Morris's ideas about the dissimilarities between Roman and Gothic types had been taken a step further: 'One thing may disappoint you - to wit, that we cannot make a double-column page of it, the page will not be wide enough. For my part, I don't regret it: doublé column seems to me chiefly ft for black letter, which prints up so close. Jenson did not print even his Pliny in double column. But it is a case of a fortiori in modern printing: because we have no contractions, few tied letters, and we cannot break a word with the same frankness as they could: I mean we difficult, and a wide page desirable."

'But I can sympathize with my pardner's anxiety,' Morris commented; 'and dingly ed him  [ral] pae th G[itig] [ain], ure ful e, un  wi they. Io' wa you will think of it; but I think it precious good."

It would be gratifying to know exactly how Morris went about designing a book, but, although he wrote at length about the broad principles of book-design and left us a legacy of fifty-two books at the Kel , h tl p n . Bruce Rogers or Jan Tschichold, say - he seens not to have nade elaborate preci rawings  the pags e visioned. Des o onaents, types, andlettering or ropies we have ibu from Morris's hand, but not one drawing to show the layout of a Kelmscott page. (There is, in the Bodleian Library, a rough sketch by MRT

Wel  h Wr n,u  sot v  n patterned after the title-page of the Reeves and Turner Poems by the Way.) Probably this dearth of evidence reflects the way in which Mo i artist, because he felt that they inhibited the freedom of the craftsman who would execute them. No doubt the instructions he gave his compositors at the Kelmscott Press were generally delivered orally, with Morris often at their elbow, and he never hesitated to demand that they produce one trial page after another until he was satisfied. Trial pages of The Golden Legend, which survive in particular abundl that yh e th w arch 8 Morris had settled on an approximate page size, $\mathbf { I } \mathbf { I } _ { 4 } ^ { 1 } \times 8 \frac { 1 } { 1 6 }$ inches, and the length of the line of type, 58 inches (though he continued to experiment with the latter), but these dimensions were largely determined by the paper size. Morris was much more preoccupied with the proportions of the margins, on which he made countless small adjustments. Once the general appearance of a page was established and the margins were deemed satisfactory, Morris's compositors began stting the text, usually providing him with galley proofs, though sometimes, as in volumes of poetry, page proofs were pulled immediately. (This was probably unwise, since last-minute alterations frequently shifted lines on to following pages.) Spaces for initials were left blank, and in the proofs Morris specified which initials and borders were to be inserted by writing in their numbers from his 'bloomer book'.

The Golden Legend was by far the largest book issued by the Kelmscott Press in the first two years ofits existence — more than 1,30o pages printed on a ton and a half of paper4 — and progress was correspondingly slow. Morris had begun designing initials for it even before the first supply of type arrived in January 1891, and he hoped to ornament the book profusely: 'I think I shall make some scratch of a border to each life or section,' he wrote in April. 'I want to make it grand."s Such aheme was r beyond the resources f thePress at this eary age, but Morris was able to design four full borders (the first one drawn at Folkestone in April), corner borders, and a large number of six- and ten-line decorated initials. A book of this size strained the capabilities of Morris's small group of printers; to make matters worse, in late April a slab holding fifty pages in galleys collapsed, with the type scattering in terrible confusion on the floor. By the following December, responding to the pressure of The Golden Legend, Morris had eight or nine men working for him in Sussex Cottage.17

![](images/7b1129007acff6930a4a59e6e434bddda87d1629830fba74e914864b6825a68d.jpg)  
Figure 65 A proposed design by Burne-Jones for the second illustration in The Golden Legend, which differs from the published wood-engraving. In 1892 Burne-Jones was still developing the distinctive style of book-illustration that came to be associated with the Kelmscott Press.

Quaritch began to express uneasiness about the slow pace of the book. On 25 July Moris wrote to him, st as to the extent of the book I 50o p.p. about (this is pretty certain) 2nd as to time of completion. probably next June (1892) 3rd the cost: from 1200 to 1400 including doing up in boards covered with grey hand-made paper, which I think a satisfactory temporary binding. Of course if you please you might have some copies done up in a more costly way, at an increase in cost, which would mean an increase in selling price. I should add that I propose to give my work on designing the ornament, just as Ellis does his transcribing.' He also offered a further piece of news which must have encouraged Quaritch: 'Mr. Burne Jones has promised to design me a frontispiece or the book.Eventually Burne-Jones suppli two designs, which had to be inserted on separate leaves because the artist was so late in delivering them; and as a substitute for a frontispiece Morris designed the first of his lovely engraved titles. Burne-Jones, shown proofs of the book in September 1891, 'went crazy with pleasure'." The two illustrations and the engraved title were not cut by W. H. Hooper until the summer of 1892, when the third volume was nearing completion. 'The editing of the Golden Legend has been the greatest pleasure to me,' Ellis commented that summer. 'It was with a heavy sigh of regret that I returned the last proof for press.

The colophon was dated 12 September 1892; the books were delivered to Quaritch from Leighton on about 4 October; and the official date of publication was 3 November. There were 5oo copies printed on paper — but none on vellum - at a price of five guineas. On 7 October Morris sent Quaritch his receipt for the printing and binding costs (1,35o) and this valediction: 'I must needs say that, looking at the finished book, I am proud of it, and of having pushed it through so promptly. It is just 2 years ago that Ellis & I began talking about doing it in this very house where I am writing [Kelmscott Manor]."2 Towards the end of the month Morris and his friends celebrated its publication at a dinner attended by Ellis and his son, Furnivall, Duff (whose bibliography never saw the light of day), Joseph Knight, Richard Garnett, William Blaikie, William Michael Rossetti, and Walker.

Reviewers and readers responded enthusiastically to the book. The Times (6 October 1892) described it as 'truly magnificent' and considered it a bargain for judicious bibliophiles'at its price. The Library, the journal of the Bibliographical Society, suggested that The Golden Legend marked 'a new epoch in the production of beautiful books in this country'. ##

The production history of Poems by the Way, the second Kelmscott Press book, is much briefer and simpler than that of The Golden Legend. In appearance it is similar to its predecessor, The Story of the Glittering Plain: its distinguishing characteristics are a page size of about $7 _ { 8 } ^ { 7 } \times 5 _ { 8 } ^ { 5 }$ inches (the first Flower paper folded twice), the Golden type (the only typeface Morris then had), a full border on the first page of text, and a scattering of six- and ten-line initials originally designed for The Golden Legend (and thus too large). Unlike the earlier book, however, Poems by the Way is printed in two colours, with red ink being used for a few lines and the shoulder titles — a pattern thereafter followed by Morris. Except for the second colour, it is an unimposing little book (224 pages), one ofseveral works Morris pushed into print during 1891 and 1892 in order to use up the smaller paper which now seemed unsatisfactory to him. For a time Morris had thought of entitling the volume Flores atramenti.

Morris announced his plans to Reeves and Turner in June 1892: 'The title of my new book will be Poems by the Way; the format the same as the Gittig Plain. It will be print in e nd black. The poms will include some recently written and some written many years ago. Some have appeared in magazines, but with the two exceptions of a little piee out of the Jason and one out of the Ogier [Ogier the Dane', theyil h e book of mine." In preparing a manuscript for his printers, Morris found it necessary to seek the assistance of Charles Fairfax Murray, who, according to May Morris, 'had preserved a number of unpublished poems of early date . . and who also had kept a record of those that had appeared in periodicals from time to time' In order to make the book slightly longer, Morris wrote one poem, 'Goldilocks and Goldilocks', especially for it on 2o May 1891.

Printing began in July but was delayed when an error was discovered in one of the sheets, which then had to be reprinted. Blunt, after lunch at Kelmscott House on 7 August, strolled with Jane Morris over to Sussex Cottage and (as he recorded in his diary) 'saw the printing. Morris's own poems were being struck off, most beautiful they are with their rubrics. The sheet I saw being printed contained the Ballad of John a Wood.Despite some last-minute alterations in the colophon (for the purpose of improving word-spacing), printing was completed by the following month. I expect the book will be all printed to-morrow,' Morris wrote to Jenny on 23 September, 'and will go to the binders on Monday. They are printing the colophon sheet to-day, whch is exciting.The dat f publication s 20 October 1891; the next day Cockerell, who had been watching the progress of the book with keen interest (though he was not yet employed by Morris), noted in his diary: 'J R[uskin]'s Poems, edited by  G.] Collwod, publishe as we Morr "oe y the Way" beautifully printed in red and black at the Kelmscott Press. Of course no mention of the latter in the papers. . . The edition consited proximately twelve guineas); all were bound in stiff vellum covers, a few of which were experimentally stained red, yellow, blue, and green by Morris and Company at the Merton Abbey works. The book was listed as out of print in the Kelmscott Press circular of  December 1893, but as late as September 1897 Leighton still had eight vellum copies in its warehouse.²7

##

The name of Jane Morris does not appear frequently in this or any other history of the Kelmscott Press. She took little interest in the activities of the Press and had almost nothing to do with its daily operation. However, in the case of Wilfrid Scawen Blunt's The Love-Lyrics & Songs of Proteus she read and criticized both manuscript and proofs, conveyed messages between her husband and the author, and in general carried the responsibilities that were later to be borne by Cockerell in overseeing the work through the Press. The explanation for this departure from her usual behaviour is that she had been, for nearly a decade, Blunt's mistress — a fact that lends peculiar colour to the story of how the Proteus volume came to be printed at the Kelmscott Press.

The relationship between Blunt and Morris's wife was first revealed in a biography of Blunt by Lady Longford published in 1979 and was decibed more fully  year later by Peter Faulkner n lecture he Will o S. voluminous diaries in the Fitzwilliam Museum, to which scholars had earlier been denied access. Despite the massive quantity of words recorded in the diaries, Blunt strikes one as an enigmatic figure whose motives and inner life defy analysis. He was in many respects an ohT ler, at the zenith of the British Empire. And he was devastatingly handsome and charming. Initiated as a young man into the mysteries of sex by Catherine Walters ("Skittles), the most celebrated Victorian courtesan, Blunt thereafter romped amorously through life, leaving beind hitrauwo,uan r divorce suits, and numerous illegitimate offspring. These adulterous affairs, even when they produced some of his most serious and intense love poetry, often had comic overtones: when Blunt was visiting Kelmscott Manor, a jealous Morris would from time to time burst into the room where Blunt and Jane were sitting, and midnight assinations at Kelmscott were made more hazardous than usual by creaking foors. Blunt's bizarre career as an amorist inevitably calls to mind the adventures of Byron's Don Juan, and it seems wonderfully appropriate that in 1869 he married Byron's granddaughter, who mysteriously tolerated her husband's erotic adventures until they at last separated in 1906.

![](images/a4fd85c1433238d0568dd3aa01e3ee63c0a5686b76079a050a4f78950aecf882.jpg)  
Figure 68 Wilfrid Scawen Blunt as a parliamentary candidate for Camberwell in 1885. Politician, cxplorer, poet, and womanizer, Blunt persuaded Morris to publish his pocms as the third book printed by the Kelmscott Press.

It must be emphasized that Blunt was not a cold-hearted villain who had stepped out of a Victorian melodrama. He was unusually kind to women and wrote high-minded poems about his affairs, which in time were generally transformed into solid friendships — as was to be true of his relationship with Jane Morris. Throughout his life Blunt oscillated dizzily between manic hedonism and devout Roman Catholicism. After his worst debauches he often went on retreats or tried to confess his sins to any eminent Catholic churchman he encountered: Manning, Newman, on one memorable occasion even Pope Leo XIII himself. Despite frequent introspection, especially during his more religious phases, Blunt was never able to justify or explain to himself the sexual obsessions that dominated his life. The nearest he came to doing so in his diaries is this revealing passage, in which, significantly, he falls back on a gambling metaphor: 'Friendship plain and simple with a woman is to me an impossibility. It is not that in theory I should not delight in such a friend. Ihave often wished for one, but in practice and in spite of all possible good will I find it freezes me. If I do not make love, I don't know what to do or say and she is bored and I am bored and insensibly the friendship and all else withers away. I lose my interest in a game where there is nothing staked at all to win or lose. It is not that I want much but there must be just something."9

Blunt met Jane Morris in 1883 and her husband a year later. Exactly when she joined Blunt's stable of mistresses is not clear, nor do we know precisely when their sexual relationship subsided into a generous, mutually satisfying friendship. Blunt preserved the 145 letters Jane wrote to hin between 1884 and 1913, but for the most part they are oddly formal (the two lovers never address each other by first name) and tell us only what is self-evident: that she felt great affection for Blunt and was fattered by his attentions.³° Blunt's diaries - our other chief source of information - are slightly more helpful and contain accounts of his 'love-making' (a phrase that he uses in both the Victorian and modern senses) in the Morris household. It is obvious that Blunt was attracted to Jane partly because she was one of the legendary beauties of the nineteenth century, partly because she had earlier been the mistress of his hero Rossetti, and partly because she was the wife of a man he admired very deeply. Seducing the wife of Morris may seem a peculiar way to honour him, but Blunt's mind always moved in a mysterious way.

Faulkner has suggested that the romantic entanglement reached its pk; cBt  J the 189os are elegaic in tone, speaking of both her beauty and their love in the past tense. When Morris died in 1896, Blunt called at Hammersmith to pay his respects (5 October). The coffin lay in Morris's bedroom on the ground foor; upstairs he found Jane reclining on the sofa as usual, not  he emphasized - wearing black, and he kissed her 'which I had not done for long and comforted her as wel as I could'. Jane' words, as reorded by Blunt, were chilling: 'I a not app . . though it is a terrible thing, for I have been with him since I frst knew everything. I was 18 when I married - but I never loved him.' Ble  J him in Egypt that winter — something he had been urging her to do f amorous encounters.

After the drama of that scene at Kelmscott House, the expedition to Egypt, was an anticlimax, for Jane brought along May, whom Blunt found awkward and intimidating. On 29 November Blunt ma this unhappy, self-mocking entry in his diary: 'Mrs. Morris's visit has been rather a disappointment - her daughter May is an can ride, even donkeys, though Mrs. Morris has made an attempt and life without riding here is impossible. One cannot walk on account of the e n   ors y. I but with May it is impossible to keep up any conversation and I am now at my wit's end how to amuse them for I cannot make love to h h on until early April.

When we turn to the productin history  The Love-Lyri&Songs of Proteus, we are constantly aware of this strange triangular relation ship in the background. In November 189o, before the Kelmscott Press was officially in operation, Blunt convinced Morris to print a volume of his poems, and Jane's comment on the decision (in a letter to Blunt dated 4 December 1890) captures perfectly Morris's mood of euphoria in the early days of the Press: 'My husband wrote to you consenting to print the poems with his new type. he does not begin till after Xmas —"when he will first print his "News from Nowhere". if it succeeds I believe he contemplates bringing forth quartos and folios in large numbers.' Another letter (29 December) also reflects Morris's naïvety about the costs of the kind of printing he proposed to do at the Kelmscott Press: 'About the book, my husband says he thinks the cost of printing will be very little above the ordinary cost. he has not started yet, but will soon. he has taken a litte place where he can set up the press.'

Morris's letter of agreenent to Blunt (19 November 189o) was brisk and businesslike: 'I have put off writing to have more news first of my type-founding adventure. I think now that I shall be ready to set to work in about 3 months perhaps earlier. I shall of course be very pleased to print your book, if I may have my own way about the get up - which, I take it is what you want.' There are in fact only two known instances in which Morris succunbed to outside pressure in designing the Kelmscott books: Charles Fairfax Murray claimed that he was responsible for the format of the Epistola de contemptu mundi,32 and Blunt, who had found the rubrics in Poems by the Way extremely attractive, persuaded Morris, against his better judgement, to print the initials of the Love-Lyrics in red. Morris's restrained comment, when he saw the first sheet of Blunt's book, was that 'it looks very gay &

Morris announced to Blunt on 23 February 1891 that his type had now arrived and he could begin printing Blunt's poems. A few days la    f  r  T [of The Story of the Glittering Plain] is now fairly started, though the work goes very slowly with our two men, soon we shall take two more and get on much faster, your book I believe will be the third produced by the "Kelmscott Press"' Blunt's book - the partial manuscript of which he delivered to Morris in January - was to consist mainly of poems previously published in Sonnets and Songs by Proteus (1875) and The Love Sonnets of Proteus (188o), 'with [as the Kelmscott title-page phrased it] many sonnets omitted from the earlier editions'. The expressive pseudonym 'Proteus' had also been employed by Blunt in an exchange of letters about religious belief published in 1878, and in the preface to the first edition of The Love Sonnets he remarked, 'The author of these sonnets, styling himself Proteus, acknowledges thereby a natural mood of change.'

However, in February 1891 Blunt completed an additional sequence of thirty-one sonnets entitled 'Natalia's Resurrection' which he submitted to Morris for inclusion in the Kelmscott volume. It is a peculiar group of poens, flled to overflowing with surrealistic allegory, exotic imagery, and supernatural events. Young Adrian falls in love with a Roman matron (the setting is ambiguous but probably medieval), who rejects him because she feels an obligation to bear a child for her decidedly unpleasant husband. The baby is stillborn, Natalia dies in childbirth, and Adrian falls into deep mourning. In a dream Natalia comes to him declaring her love, for she says (and here the logic of the sequence becomes rather tenuous) that she is now free of the legal restraints of marriage; strangely, even her husband, who is also present in the dream, agrees. As Natalia and Adrian are about to marry, he awakens to discover that her funeral procession is at that moment passing by on the road. That evening Adrian goes to Natalia's grave and opens it, whereupon Natalia rises up, very much alive, and they dash off to get married. 'Natalia's Resurrection' is not an impressive poem (though on other occasions both Yeats and Pound expressed admiration for Blunt's verse), but it requires no great psychological insight to understand why it appealed so strongly to Jane Morris. Writing to Blunt on 13 April 1891, she commented, ´'Yesterday I finished reading the 2nd part of your M.S. poems. I like the "Natalia's Resurrection" so very much. I should like to see that placed first in the book. What do you say?' A comment on the sonnets by Blunt's wife Anne in her diary offers a different perspective: 'Poets' theories of "love ignore honourable feelings. Her husband, following Jane's advice, agreed to put 'Natalia's Resurrection' at the beginning of the book, and on 21 Ápril, from a ship bound to Naples, he posted the completed manuscript to Morris.

Printing of the Love-Lyrics went much more slowly than Morris had anticipated: he was still naïvely optimistic about the amount of work his few printers could do on a hand-press, the rubrication required double presswork, and The Golden Legend was in production at the same time. Typesetting did not begin until the summer of 1891, and proofs of Blunt's volume reached him in the country by early September, but his pleasure in seeing his words set in the beauty of the Golden type was considerably diminished by an unexpected letter from one of his former lovers.

Nearly all of Blunt's love poetry reflects his relationships with particular women  Esther, for example, offers a fictionalized account of his prolonged, unhappy passion for Skittles — and the evidence in Lady Longford's biography suggests that Blunt's ladies were usually flattered to find their amours celebrated in graceful verse, with of course no real names or incrininating circunstances nentioned. During 1890, howevr, Blunt had carrn an affair with Margaret Talbot, the wife of the Military Secretary at the British Embassy in Paris, that ended abruptly when Mrs Talbot confessed everything to her husband; uncharacteristically, there were bitter recriminations on both sides.

During the summer of 1891 Mrs Talbot learned about 'Natalia's Resurrection' and concluded, no doubt rightly, that it contained indirect references to her. Like Natalia, she was married to an older man of considerable social standing but was childless; she too had turned away a lover because of loyalty to her husband. Whatever message she now sent to Blunt was sufficiently perenptory to force him into action at once. On 28 September, Blunt, his wife, and daughter spent the day in London: Blunt went to his club while the towomen shoppThat evening, whe Blunt did not return home,

Anne surmised correctly that he had decamped to Kelmscott Manor, where he had an urgent conference with Morris. Blunt made the following entry in his diary on 30 September (though part of the explanation he offers here appears to be a later interpolation): I have been away for two nights to Kelnscott to see Morris about the printing and to wish Mrs. Morris good bye for the winter. It was very perfect weather, and we did our gudgeon fishing and walks as usual, and I made a little love to Mrs. Morris, poor woman, for the quite last tine. . . . We have arranged that Natalia is to be suppressed and instead of it that the old Proteus Sonnets shall be reprinted as the second part of the Kelmscott volume with certain new sonnets and some of those omitted from the earlier edition. The suppression of Natalia was decided on in consequence of a wild letter from Margaret who imagined that the poem described the relations between herself and her husband and me. And, although the poem had really nothing to do with her, she was so urgent that I could not refuse. It put Morris to great inconvenience, for Natalia was already in print, and I was obliged to explain the circumstances to him, when he at once and most good naturedly consented to the change.'

In a volume of proofs of the Love-Lyrics Cockerell added one of his characteristic notes indicating that it contained a portion of the cancelled 'Natalia's Resurrection' — 'with which', Cockerell wrote, 'the K.P. volumes was to have opened - but a lady, so WSB told me, pretended that the poem was about herself, and, to please her, he removed it'.3s Though Cockerell is usually a reliable witness, we must recall that he always displayed a fierce loyalty to his friends and turned a blind eye to their faults: asked once whether he was aware of Blunt's infidelities, Cockerell replied, 'I realized that there was usually a lady in the offing. But Blunt always behaved like a gentleman: he never told a dirty story.The proofs preserved by Cockerell show that eighteen pages of type for the Love-Lyrics had been set in vain, and, despite his complacent note, we can assume that Morris and Blunt did not make this decision lightly or merely on the basis of an unfounded accusation.

How much did Jane Morris know about the 'Natalia's Resurrection' episode? Since she proof-read Blunt's book (something that she did for no other Kelmscott Press volume), she must have learned what had happened. Yet she never mentions it in her letters; what does emerge in her correspondence with Blunt during 1891 is her great admiration for a group of twelve poems entitled 'A Woman's Sonnets' in the Love-Lyrics: 'the "Woman's Sonnets" I liked immensely', she wrote to

Blunt on I1 December, 'and I think them some of the best, though I don't agree with all the sentiments therein contained.' The sequence is obviously different from others in the volume, for it describes the breakup of a love-affair from a woman's point of view. What Blunt apparently never told Jane - or the readers of the Kelmscott Press edition — was that the sonnets were in fact written by Lady Gregory, another of Blunt's former mistresses, about the change in their relationship. Lady Gregory also read the proofs.

The Love-Lyrics & Songs of Proteus — a 264-page small quarto — was finally issued on 27 February 1892 in an edition of 300 paper copies (two guineas), and it sold out very quickly. On 26 April, having received an inscribed copy from Blunt, Jane sent him her final evaluation of it: 'I think it is beautiful in all ways. I spent much time alone with it yesterday, and read again and again with the greatest pleasure my favourite poems. I admire more than ever the "Women's Sonnets" series, they flow so sweetly and smoothly, no hesitation anywhere. . .. I had no copy of your book. We bought two and gave one away, then w sent r noher, but all had been old withi the fortht o publication.'

Jane's husband, meanwhile, was supplying financial reports to Morris for the printing and providing a fee of ten shillings to Reeves and Turner for each copy sold. On 12 March 1892 Morris was able to offer him good news: 'The 22o copies offered for sale have all been sold, and Reeves has handed me £20o on account for you[.] . . . I am charging you  15o for the printing which was a good deal more than I thought it would cost: but the book finally was much longer than I had estimated and the red printing, there being a great dealofit nearly doubled the cost of the press-work. . I am glad that you will make something out of the book; for when Reeves pays up the whole there will be a good balance in your favour, without reckoning the copies you keep. So I think we may congratulate ourselves on a success.' A more detailed account, sent by Morris to Blunt on 3o April, indicates that the total cost of production was 19 and that Blunt's net proft was £142.

In the second letter Morris rejoiced that the book had been well should ot ey at hismakig h ivod yhe nlyt e understands.' But there were also mutterings ofdiscontent fromEllis, whotok a dim view Blut' poeic pretensns 'Theac Mors is  gau that e oes ot  yout is pri book surely against the grain. The great unanswered, perhaps un answerable, question (which lies beyond the scope of this history of the Kelmscott Pres but is irresistibly fascinating nevertheless) is whether 'good natured' Morris was aware, as he designed and printed The Love-Lyrics & Songs of Proteus, that he was making a book for his wife's lover.

##

What general conclusions, if any, can we draw from the story of these three richly diverse books? The Golden Legend illustrates Morris's weakness for the large, sumptuous volume that he was not yet really capable of producing; impressive as The Golden Legend may be, it is not the book that Morris envisioned or the sort of book that he was later able to create at the Kelmscott Press. Poems by the Way, a much less grandiose undertaking, shows the slow evolution of Morris's typographical style while the Kelmscott books were still in an experimental stage. The Love-Lyrics & Songs of Proteus gives off echoes of very odd psychological resonances from the Morris marriage. If the three books have anything at all in common, it is a certain note of improvisation (sometimes urgent, sometimes amusing) in the history of their productio; if we grasp this, we will perhaps avoid thetemptation of treating the Kelmscott Press, with reverential solemnity, as merely a portentous episode in nineteenth-century cultural history.

# EIGHT \* THE KELMSCOTTCHAUCER

![](images/9f10434ea3a1e57b21a7fc7316a69314a9bac425d9da4fd85baf6d747cfd3db6.jpg)

ONG before it came off the press, the Kelmscott Chaucer was famous. One of the earliest references to the book (in the Athenaeum, 22 October 1892) struck the note of admiration bordering on adoration that recurs in nearly all future allusions to it: 'Lovers of Chaucer on both sides of the Atlantic will rejoice to hear that [Edward Burne-Jones] has

made very great progress with a series of designs, fifty or sixty in all, which are to be cut in wood under his own superintendence, and intended to illustrate the "Canterbury Tales" and the other poems of Chaucer. These designs promise to be charmingly graceful and beautiful in execution. Indeed, we are confident that since, precisely a hundred years ago, Flaxman . .. designed for the first Countess Spencer the famous "Illustrations to theTragedies ofAschylus,"poet and aris have never been better fitted to each other. The text to which these designs are adapted will be collated with the best manuscripts and carefully edited by Mr. W. Morris and Mr. F. S. Ellis. The typography will be worthy of the occasion, and the volume a stately quarto [sic].' In 1892 The Works of Geoffrey Chaucer was still in embryo, but as its birth neared there was widespread gossip about the astonishing folio in production at the Kelmscott Press. A New York periodical, the Book Buyer (18 November 1895), published a facsimile of the first page of The Canterbury Tales and declared that the Chaucer 'will be without question the grandest book of the century'; in 1895 some sheets of the Chaucer were shown at the Arts and Crafts Exhibition in Dublin, prompting the Studio (January 1896) to describe the book, not yet published, as holding 'the place of honour among the other productions of this press'.

Morris's associates did not hesitate to bestow superlatives upon it. It' theestbook v n than keyhv J a oament and illustrations cobine thChaucer wshe raet boha s s Many outside the Morris circle have repeated this extravagant language: a reviewer in the Library (1896), for example, described it as the finest book ever printed', and the Athenaeum (3 October 1896) called the Chaucer the crowning achievement of the Kelmscott Press

Even in the sond halthewenti century ther is siltendency to abandon all rhetorical restraint when discussing the Kelmscott Chaucer, as evidenced by this passage from an American library exhi-a  T of the Christian community proclains by its very presence the gospel of Christ, so the Kelmscott Chaucer on vellum stands surrogate for all that was important in the book world before it was completed in 1896. . . . While there have been many litanies of praise heaped upon William Morris, and upon the Kelmscott Chaucer on vellum in particular, none of them have been adequate. Not until one has had the opportunity to go through a copy does the full majesty of the Kelmscott accomplishment appear. . . . The ultimate limitations of words are reached when one tris to describe the Kelmscott Chaucer on vellum. And so on for several more breathless paragraphs.

Naturally such ecstatic approbation invites a cynical response, and there have always been some readers willing to argue that the Kelmscott Chaucer is a ponderous, overdecorated non-book which threatens the eyesight and physical safety of anyone foolhardy enough to hold it on his lap (a vellum copy in the pigskin binding weighs more than thirteen pounds). We have seen that the Chaucer is far from being a typical Kelmscott volune, but it is certainly the most celebrated and frequently photographed, and it represents, in a sense, Morris's tendencies in book-design and ornamentation carried to their logical conclusion; thus, rightly or wrongly, the Chaucer has become a litmus test which measures one's response to Morris's work as a printer.

##

That the most ambitious, richly ornamented book of the Kelmscott Press proved to be an edition of Chaucer came as no surprise to any of Morris's admirers, for he and Burne-Jones had counted Chaucer as one of their great discoveries at Oxford, and throughout his life Morris's poetry was constantly being compared by critics with that of his medieval predecessor.³ Though Morris himself protested occasionally against the injustice of the analogy, he had only himself to blame for his identification in the public mind with Chaucer: he had, after all, addressed Chaucer as 'my Master' in both Book XVII of The Life and Death of Jason and the 'Envoi' to The Earthly Paradise, and the latter poem was plainly patterned after The Canterbury Tales. Still, Morris remained slightly uneasy about the conparison, and it is noteworthy

that he declined to write an introduction to Chaucer for T. H. Ward's The English Poets in 1878. Not only did he feel ill-equipped to supply such an introduction, but Morris also objected to Ward's plan to print merely a small selection from Chaucer's works.4

Behind the Kelmscott Chaucer lies a long lineage of Chaucer manuscripts and printed editions, many of them illustrated, with which Morris, as a collector and antiquarian, was well acquainted.® The practice of wedding picture with Chaucer's text extends back to the earliest manuscripts, the most celebrated of which is the fifteenthcentury Ellesmere manuscript of The Canterbury Tales (now in the Huntington Library) containing twenty-three portraits of the individual pilgrims, each mounted and placed in the margin near the decorated initial at the beginning of his tale. The tales themselves are not illustrated; this convention, which may seem rather odd to us today, was observed by all llustrators of Chaucer until the late-eighteenth century, and only in the Kelmscott edition is there at last a serious attempt to represent the action of the pilgrim's stories beyond a few simple tableaux. Burne-Jones's departure from this iconographic pattern is so complete, in fact, that he shows us none of the pilgrims except Chaucer himself (and he is not mounted), nor does he draw the pilgrims in procession or at table in the Tabard Inn, two otherconven tions of Chaucer's early illustrators.

The portrait of Chaucer in the Ellesmere manuscript is one of only two presumed to be reliable likenesses; the other is known as the Hoccleve portrait, also found in an early manuscript. A third likeness o Chauer whichas cpture the aination atr enins is an early fifteenth-century painting of Chaucer reading his poems to theyreha VneRe epreentation  Ford Madox Brown. It irarkable thatevn though these images of Chaucer had been frequently engraved and were familar to Victorian readers, Burne-Jones scarcely alludes to them inhis llustrations or the Kelscott Chauer. His portt Chaucer does show a general indebtedness to both the Hoccleve and Ellesmere likenesses; there are broad similarities in pose and costume, the outstretched hand, even the forked beard. Yet Burne-Jones's Chaucer is more gaunt and solitary, more inward-looking (he is reading a book), and more visionary. One notices that by the end of The Canterbury Tales he has aged perceptibly, and by the last page of the volume he looks quite decrepit.
