Caxton in 1478, followed by a second edition with illustrations and an improved text in 1484. The crude woodcuts in the latter, the fourth illustrated book published in English, followed the pattern of the Ellesmere manuscript except that each mounted pilgrin appears twice: once in the General Prologue, and once at the beginning of his alCxt  uhnury seated at an enormous round table presided over by Harry Baily, landlord of the Tabard Inn. Another fifteenth-century edition of the Tales (Richard Pynson, 1491 or 1492) contains a smaller and less striking representation of the same scene, and it reappears — in a very Augustan style, with Harry Baily looking like Dr Johnson in a spirited discussion at a coffee house — in John Bell's edition of Chaucer (1782). Caxton's other Chaucerian woodcuts were also imitated and adapted by later printers for several centuries.

![](images/02cc491b96b8764d8a474bd5605a6142fa66c7cc8edb06aad9c23327f55922c6.jpg)  
x C Ta this woodcut of Chaucer's pilgrims seated around a table at the Tabard Inn. Burne-Joncs ignored all such prccedents when he created his ilustrations for the Kelmscott Chaucer.

John Urry's dition (171) brings together, for the first time, all the mounted pilgrims and displays them at the moment of their departure from the Tabard. This editon appears to have been nancil and crical failure: readers disliked Urry's textual medations and — as a symptom of the growing nostalgia for ll things medieval  they regretted the abandonment of Gothic for Roman type. (One of the sponsors of the book, Christ Church, Oxford, was able to dispose of its remaining copies only by forcing every undergraduate to buy one I by Thomas Stothard of a small group of pilgrims on horseback, and early in the nineteenth century both Stothard and Blake produced large-scale engravings - not intended as book illustrations - of Chaucer's pilgrims leaving the Tabard.

What is most noticeable about all the eighteenth-century editions ofChaucer is how abruptly they depart from the medieval spirit of the earlier blackletter versions. To read The Canterbury Tales in, for instance, Urry's Roman type, with neo-classical initials and ornaments and an extraordinarily anachronistic Squire, is a very different experience from that of studying a typical sixteenth-century page in Gothic type, illustrated with a woodcut of a pilgrim who is lineally descended from Caxton and the manuscripts before Caxton. Bell's fourteenvolume edition of Chaucer has been praised for the excellence of its typography by Stanley Morison, and it deserves our attention also as the rst attempt to illusrate the storis of The Canterbury Tales; but however admirable Bell's printing may be, one must recognize that it was precisely this eighteenth-century rendering of Chaucer — made over into the image of an Augustan gentleman of impeccable manners - that Morris attempted to obliterate at the Kelmscott Press. Morris's aim was to recapture, without direct copying, the simplicity and vitality of fifteenth- and sixteenth-century editions of Chaucer that were closer in time and spirit to Chaucer's own age. Morris believed that only by peeling off the Renaissance and neo-classical layers of cultural interpretation could he recover something like the Chaucer of the Middle Ages, and this involved both a careful restoration of Chaucer's text (including his spelling) and a return to a more medieval style of typography and ornamentation.

The Kelmscott Chaucer is in fact the splendid culmination of the Victorian rediscovery of Chaucer and of early English literature in general. The person who was largely responsible for this revival was Morris's friend F. J. Furnivall, who founded, among others, the Early

English Text Society (1864), the Ballad Society (1868), the New Shakspere Society (1873), and the Wyclif Society (1882). Furnivall created the Chaucer Society in 1 868 in order (according to its prospectus) 'to do honour to Chaucer, and to let the lovers and students of him see how far the best unprinted manuscripts of his works differ from the printed texts'. The Chaucer Society was never a large organization — Furnivall frequently complained of its meagre membership in the annual reports, and in 19oo Ellis observed sarcastically that by then only sixteen private individuals belonged, 'about one in every two million and a quarter of the population of the British Isles'6 — and it was disbanded shortly after Furnivall's death in 191o. Nevertheless, during the four decades of its existence the Society laid the foundations of modern Chaucer scholarship by publishing early manuscript versions of Chaucer's works and other primary materials, mostly edited by Furnivall himself, a man of unflagging energy. For one of these publications, a Chaucerian manuscript in the Cambridge University Library, W. H. Hooper, who was to engrave Burne-Jones's designs at the Kelmscott Press, made facsimile engravings of the original illustrations.' The most important achievement of the Society, issued in instalments over several years from 1868 onwards, was Furnivall's parallel-text edition of the six Chaucer manuscripts judged to be the most trustworthy. Like Morris, Furnivall believed passionately in the importance of digging down through the centuries of accumulated debris in order to find Chaucer's actual words rather than the glosses and conjectural emendations of later editors; like Morris, too, he took a relatively uncomplicated view of Chaucer, emphasizing what he called 'the most genial and humourful healthysouled man that England had ever seen'. Morris, of course, never remoulded Chaucer into a Muscular Christian as Furnivall did, but both men tended to ignore Chaucer the subtle Italianate poet, sometimes introspective and melancholy, seeing him instead as a cheerful realist whose value to modern readers lay chiely in his portrayal of medieval life and manners.

During the 186os the University Presses of both Oxford and Cambridge were laying plans for scholarly editions of Chaucer, and, alg uriao tha ee mi pnt  -ext version, Oxford decided instead to publish a single text based on a collation oall the authoritative manuscripts, to beedited by the Revd Walter W.Skeat, Professor  AngloSaxon at Cambrie keat was by no means an ideal editor: following the example of Furnivall (who was in many respects his mentor), Skeat constructed a text based on intuitive, eclectic principles and drawn exclusively from Furnivall's six-text edition. Both Ellis and Burne-Jones commented on the donnish humourlessness of Skeat's notes. 'Every student of Chaucer must be grateful for the labour and learning bestowed on his works by Professor Skeat, but to be a parson without any sense humour is not an advantage for an editor of Chaucer,' Ellis remarked wryly. Burne-Jones said to Sebastian Evans, 'Skeat is a bore, I must say. He never edits a poem without saying it's dull and unimportant and long-winded. How does he know and what business is that of his  if it's unimportant what does he edit it for? He is himself always long-winded."

Yet Skeat's monumental six-volume edition of Chaucer - published under Oxford's Clarendon Press imprint in 1894, with a supplementary volume issued in 1897 - seenied a godsend to Ellis and Morris, for it was incomparably superior to all previous editions and relieved Ellis of the heavy burden of constructing his own text for the Kelmscott version. Writing to the University Librarian in April 1892, E deca I shall also take thepuy callg on Mr.Skea if he is in Cambridge to hear what he says as to the text of the projected Kelnscott Press folio Chaucer.'" Skeat shared Ellis's enthusiasm for the planned Kelmscott edition, but he could not by his own authority permit Ellis and Morris to reprint the text he was preparing for the Clarendon Press, and, not surprisingly, the Delegates of the Press displayed reluctance to allow Morris to produce an edition of Chaucer that would be potentially in competition with thcir own. The Delegates first considered the request in November 1892, and their initial response was negative: 'With regard to the Kelmscott Chaucer,' C. E. Doble wrote to Skeat, 'they do not think that you would gain anything by permitting Mr. Morris to reprint your text, and they are not disposed to recommend you to sanction the use which it is proposed to make of it. Would Mr. Pollard's text be good enough for Mr. Morris' purpose? It is difficult to believe that the adoption of your text by Mr. Morris would increase the sale of your books by a single copy.

Skeat suggested to Morris, as an alternative, that he might reprint Caxton's first edition of Chaucer, and meanwhile Ellis continued to study various Chaucerian texts; for The Canterbury Tales he intended to adopt the version found in the Ellesmere manuscript, but the other poems presented more complex editorial problems, and neither he nor Morris was willing to abandon their hope of using Skeat's text. At the meeting of the Delegates of the Press on 26 May 1894, therefore, the request was renewed, again without success. The minutes record the following action: 'Letter read from the Rev. Profr. W. W. Skeat proposing that the Delegates should grant permission to Mr. Wm. Morris to reprint the Oxford Text of "Chaucer" amongst his Kelmscott Editions on condition of a contribution being made by him to the proposed English Lectureship Funds at Cambridge. Resolved that the Delegates think that it would be injurious to the Clarendon Press edition to accede to Mr. Morris's proposal.'

Morris refused to admit defeat. Aided by the intervention of Dr Arthur Evans (Keeper of the Ashmolean Museum, Oxford), he submitted the request yet again, but this time with a fuller statement of his intentions: 'May I ask you to be so kind as to lay before the Delegates the request to allow me to make use of Professor Skeat's Chaucer under the following circumstances,' he wrote to the Secretary of the Clarendon Press on 9 August 1894. 'I am publishing a Chaucer, which is being printed at the Kelmscott Press, & will be ornamented with picture[s] designed by Sir Edward Burne-Jones, and borders &c by myself. This book I hope to make a specially beautiful one as to typography & decoration, and I naturally wish to make the text as good as possible. I ask therefore to be allowed to avail myself of the corrections of errors which Professor Skeat's learning & acumen are making public, though we (Mr F. S. Ellis, the editor, & myself) by no means intend to produce a literal reprint of his text. We should be pleased to make fullacknowledgment of our obligations to Professor Skeat in the book itself.

'I should add that it is impossible that this book could cone into competition with the text now being published at the University P n i notes or commentary. It is intended to be essentially a work of art.

Morris explained in a second letter that he was willing to wait until the October meeting of the Delegates for his proposal to be considere 'I do not thik there is ay hurry' he wrote, 'or I hav oy just begun printing, and it wll be me weeks before I am fr n n he Canterbury Tales, over which I take it no question could arise as we have the vane f go M.. (heEllee s ur fuin) a the     . It ul    he p where there is o h ctury M.. uch as the Hous f Fame where we should need help.'"4 Finally, on 19 October, the Delegates gave Morris permission 'to reproduce in an illustrated edition de luxe of the Chaucer to be printed at the Kelmscott Press a limited number

Library Edition, provided due acknowlegement the sanction o the Editor and the Delegates be made'.

Such an acknowledgement eventually appeared in the colophon of the Kelmscott Chaucer: 'The hearty thanks of the Editor and Printer are due to the Reverend Professor Skeat for kindly allowing the use of his emendations to the Ellesmere MS. of the Canterbury Tales, and also of his emended texts of Chaucer's other writings. The like thanks also the Editor and Printer give to the Delegates of the Oxford University Press for allowing thein to avail themselves of Professor Skeat's permission.' But Morris's claim that he and Ellis did not intend 'to produce a literal reprint of [Skeat's] text' was disingenuous, for their marked copy of the Skeat edition (which is owned today by the Beinecke Library, Yale University) discloses that they very rarely strayed from his textual readings. Except for The Canterbury Tales, the Kelmscott Chaucer is, for all practical purposes, a reprint of Skeat's edition, but shorn of the pedantic notes that irritated Ellis and Burne-Jones. 'And I want particularly to draw your exceptionally sweet attention to the fact that there is no preface to Chaucer, and no introduction, and no essay on his position as a poet, and no notes, and no glossary,' Burne-Jones announced to one of his female correspondents when the book was published. 'So that all is prepared for you to thoroughly enjoy him - unvexed by the inpertinence of the intruder." The thwarted intruder in this case was not Ellis but a solemn clerical professor in Cambridge.

##

It was not merely the search for a suitable text that kept the Chaucer in production for so long; all aspects of the book - paper, ink, ornaments, illustrations, binding - presented formidable technical problems, if only because everything was on a much larger scale than for any other Kelmscott Press volume. Even The Golden Legend and The Earthly Paradise were minor undertakings by comparison.

An edition of Chaucer had been on Morris's mind from the earliest period of the Kelmscott Press's history. 'He thinks of printing a Chaucer from a blackletter fount which he hopes to design,' Cockerell recorded in his diary on I 1 June 1891. In the spirit of optimism which characterized the Press's announcements in those days, Morris intended to advertise the Chaucer in the first list distributed in May 1892, but at the last moment he deleted this line from the proof:

'THE POEMS OF GEOFFREY CHAUCER. BlaCk Letter. LaRge I at last announced: 'Chaucer's Works. With about 6o designs by E. Burne-Jones.' The reason for the postponement is that Morris had relucany reacethe concusin, early i 89, that the Tr type was too large for the projected Chaucer. When the type was first delivered from Sir Charles Reed and Son in January, Morris immediately had trial passages from Chaucer set up, since he then hoped the Troy would be satisfactory for the Chaucer, but Ellis, in a letter written in March, explained Morris's dilemma: 'I enclose you a specimen of the new type in which as you see Caxton's "Recuyel" is to b & ha is tolwut a zealer ye u m pt — and in double col[umn]s. To print Chaucer in this size w[oul]d be delgtful but I fear buyer would turnreentful?] at the ze whi the book would necessarily make.'"7 Morris's own bemused observation on the Troy type was that 'it is so big that it is no use thinking of printing the Chaucer in double columns with it unless the book were to be as big as Eggestein's Gratian's Decretum [1471] — an elephantine volume weighing nearly thirty pounds.

Morris was never happy with the reduced Chaucer type, which was in his hands by the summer of 1892: I was driven by the necessities of the Chaucer (a double-columned book) to get a smaller Gothic type of Pica size,' he explained apologetically in his Note on the Kelmscott Press.Blunt, visiting Kelmscott Manor on 5 July 1892, found himself in the midst of 'a great discussion as to whether it was to be printed in single or double columns'. Double column it was, as Morris had foreseen from the beginning, and during July trial settings were struck off in the Chaucer type, at least one of which was completely undedA ne trial pa etablished the basicdesg the book al n he er pape with t xtyre. A note by Morris on one of the few surviving proofs of the Chaucer indicates that the book was to some extent modelled after a specimen pleat tKeltt res.Wih  tral page in han Mos was able to work out a remarkably accurate estimate of the book's length (544 pages, just a few pages short of the size it proved to be); the same document, on which he listed the proposed sequence of Chaucer's works, suggests that he may have originally intended to print them in their order of composition. But the arrangement that he eventually adopted was, as he said, 'purely architectural'.

Morris began designing the borders for the Chaucer on I February 1893, and for the first text page of Canterbury Tales - which is overwhelming in its richness of effect - Hooper engraved the border, the frame for the illustrations, the large initial word ("Whan'), and the hand-lettered title on a single block of wood. During the next three years Morris's prolific brush produced one ornament after another for the book, all on the same massive scale: according to Cockerell's concise summary, he designed a woodcut title, fourteen large borders, eighteen different frames round the illustrations, and twenty-six large initial words'²4 \_ a list that does not include the scores of decorated initials, since many of them had been used already in earlier books. It was a staggering accomplishment, and it took its toll on Morris's health. By the spring of 1896 Burne-Jones was terrified that Morris might die before he could complete the engraved title-page: 'the Chaucer title page is done at last,' Burne-Jones commented on t (Rooke diary).

Printing of the first sheet of the Chaucer started on 8 August 1894: 'BEGAN PRINTING THE CHAUCER,' Cockerell wrOte in his diary in uncharacteristic capitals. Morris also recognized the momentousness of the occasion: 'we printed the Ist side of the Chaucer on vellum; successfully, I think, after some trouble,' he reported to Jenny.² Towards the end of 1894 Morris acquired another Albion press — this one very large and reinforced with steel bands in order to withstand the additional pressure of printing his large borders - and rented Sunderland Cottage, 21 Upper Mall, to hasten the progress of the book. 'Morris is very busy,' Charles Fairfax Murray wrote in November. 'He is just setting up a new press to print the Chaucer faster, which he calculated would [take] him otherwise 2 years to print."6 During the spring and summer of 1895 the book fell several months further behind schedule when yellow stains began to appear on some of the sheets already printed (see Chapter Four). Burne-Jones was, as always, dilatory in supplying his designs for the illustrations, and the diffculties that arose in printing the pictures and ornaments were legion. Hooper inadvertently caused a last-minute delay when he made the engraved title too small, leaving an excessively broad white gap between it and the surrounding border, and Morris - with his strength rapidly failing - had to extend the design by about a fifth of an inch.7 The colophon was dated 8 May 1896, and on 20 May T. M. Rooke recorded in his diary Burne-Jones's relieved response: 'Mr

![](images/c8f3af5f164cb27c31935f5f5e25d6e183a4223fadec0f6f8b6802695e793560.jpg)  
Figure 7 Two ofMorris's pressmen, William H. Bowden and W. Collins, at work on the Chaucer.

Morris didn't look well yesterday, but the printing of the Chaucer's every bit of it done and in a month it'll be all dry and tightly bound. They daren't bind it heavily as yet or the print would come off.'

Most of the copies of the Chaucer were issued in the standard Kelmscott quarter-linen binding with blue paper-covered boards, but for forty-eight of the books Morris designed a pigskin binding patterned after a binding by Ulrich Schreier of Salzburg (1478) that he owned²8  which was executed at the Doves Bindery. (Cobden-Sanderson also produced a binding for two copies of the Chaucer tooled from a more delicate design of his own.) A circular distributed by the Kelmscott Press in February 1896 announced that Morris would design four Chaucer bindings in all: (1) a full pigskin from the Doves Bindery, (2) a half pigskin from the Doves Bindery, (3) a full pigskin from J. and J. Leighton, and (4) a half pigskin from Leighton. Although Cockerell believed that Morris was able to carry out only the first of these schemes, there is a dummy copy of the Chaucer in Columbia University's Butler Library that seems to match the description of the third binding announced in the circular — i.e. a full pigskin executed by Leighton and evidently designed by Morris. A note in the book indicates that it is a 'specimen copy of the Kelmscott Chaucer 1896, made up from odd sheets [mostly pages 3942, which were obviously canceled] and bound by J. & J. Leighton, to the order of William Morris; carried out in thorough 15th century style oak boards, covered with pigskin and elaborately ornamented in blind; the tools specially cut after the original designs as found on the Apocalypse Block Book and the Richel Bible, c. 1475 . . . '. The title on the spine ipl. It iyhath epv ki never produced by Leighton either because Morris was dissatisfied with it or because the dummy was completed when he was too ill to make a decision.29

The typography, ornaments, and binding of the Kelmscott Chaucer are inde suptuus the book is essentially a work of art' Morris predicted - but a special source of its appeal for many readers has always been the eighty-seven wood-engraved illustrations by Edward Burne-Jones. In the earliest announcement of the Chaucer (December 1892) it was said that there would be about 60 designs', but that number crept upwards inexorably as tine went by. In a little trial dummy of eleven leaves stitched together, on which Burne-Jones sketched out his preliminary plans for the pictures, is this note in the artist's hand: 'There are 48 planned here but I may add to the Knights wrot on another sheet,  in ll and he [presumably Morrs] won't have more'.'" Emery Walker claimed, in a later lecture on Morris, that the number of illustrations continued to grow as long as the volume ws in productin: 'When the book ws printed o one, not even Burne-Jones, knew how many pictures there were in it. He had gone on putting in as many as he liked, knowing that the more he drew the b then he did more pictures. As the volume neared its end the number of illustrations increased; when they were counted after the first two volumes were bound it was found that a copy contained 87. Was Walker exaggerating slightly? He did have a weakness for colourful anecdotes, and it seems inprobable that, even at the Kelmscott Press, where improvisation ruled supreme, no one had counted the illustrations until the book was bound.

![](images/959bd7866cce3e3a972dfa222632dec3045eefa6472fcedf87e6427377c14460.jpg)  
F e cal k i  r-ht   the cott Chaucer was designed by Morris and executed at the Doves Bindery.

![](images/1943f8b5c17f15df236e204a64a6e765a4fa931b45bf65b15858d8b58f4e92c7.jpg)  
Figure 76 Burne-Jones made some of his preliminary sketches for the Chaucer illustrations in a dummy book consisting of cleven lcaves stitched together, of which the first page is shown here. In the upper right corner he wrote this note to himself: 'There are 48 [illustrations] planned here but I may add to the Knights tale & the carly part  there may be 60 in allif I like.' By the time the book was published in 1896, there were in fact cighty-seven woodengravings.

But Walker was undoubtedly correct in his assertion that Burne-Jones illustrated those stories most abundantly which appealed to his own taste. It was Chaucer the poet of courtly love and sophisticated teller of French and Italianate tales who most interested Burne-Jones (not the sturdy medieval bard that Morris found in The Canterbury Tales), and it was accordingly the stories of the Knight, the Clerk of Oxenford, and the Franklin which attracted him; the tales of bawdy realism he passed over in silence. 'I have abstained from decorating certain of the Canterbury Tales,' he wrote to Swinburne. ". . . Morris has been urgent with me that I should by no means exclude these stories from our scheme of adornment - especially he had hopes of my treatment of the Miller's Tale, but he ever had more robust and daring parts than I could assume.'3 When his studio assistant congratulated him for ignoring Chaucer's 'base stories', Burne-Jones replied, 'I'd like to pretend Chaucer didn't do them. Besides, pictures to them would have spoiled the book. You don't want funny pictures either. Pictures are too good to be funny. Literature's good enough for that.'34 In private Burne-Jones did have a taste for ribald comedy: his notebooks and letters are filled with lively caricatures, mainly of emaciated, terrified men threatened by grotesquely obese women. But his public art was reserved for high seriousness, which means that the Chaucer is populated with the usual mysterious human figures - willowy and graceful, intense but expressionless - that are so familiar from his paintings. It is certainly not true, as one recent commentator has claimed, that Burne-Jones 'ruined' the Kelnscott Chaucer by this p,tu wood-engravings, by ignoring Chaucer's humour and irony, convey a very incomplete interpretation of the poetry.

Because the older English woodcut illustrations of Chaucer held no interest for him, Burne-Jones was compelled to turn to other visual sources. We are told that he and Morris regularly pored over illuminated manuscripts at both the Bodleian Library and the British Museum, and an exhaustive study of the manuscripts he could have seen there would probably turn up a few miniatures from which he borrowed or adapted some details in his Chaucer illustrations. There is (to choose a more remote example) a miniature in a manuscript of Boccaccio's Teseida which is remarkably similar to the wood-engraving which appears on page 9 of the Chaucer to illustrate the Knight's Tale: it is not clear whether or how he saw this particular manuscript (which is now in the national library of Austria), but probably the miniature represents an iconographic tradition which Burne-Jones encountered in other manuscripts as well.36

He was also able to draw upon his own earlier work. In 1857 Burne-Jones painted scenes from the Prioress's tale on a wardrobe as a wedding gift to the Morrises, of which the left panel is almost identical to the illustration on page 6o of the Kelmscott Chaucer. (Another version of this scene appears in the last work Burne-Jones completed, a water-colour exhibited at the New Gallery in 1898.) On the right panel of the wardrobe Chaucer himsef is portrayed, looking very much like the Ellesmere miniature and not yet as gaunt and melancholy as he was to become in 1896. Chaucer also reappears in a series of cartoons Burne-Jones did for Ruskin in 1864 to illustrate The Legend ofGoode Wimmen; Ruskin intended that the girls at Winnington Hall should embroider a set of hangings under Georgiana Burne-Jones's supervision, but the scheme came to nought, and a water-colour of 1871 - on which the Kelmscottillustration on page 416 is based was the only tangible result of the aborted project.

Burne-Jones insisted that he was trying to see Chaucer's poetry led to a peculiar sort of literalism in his interpretation of the text: the exa noi b rer  theCae s theexty image of the House 'made of twigges' as a large wicker basket in The Hous of Fame. Such an approach was not always easy for the illustrator. 'I wish Chaucer would, once & for all, make up his unrivalled & precious mind whether he is talking of a picture or a statue  I do wish it,' wrote Burne-Jones, ' — for in this book I am putting myself wholly aside, & trying to see things as he saw them — not once have I invaded his kingdom with one hostile thought — '37 Burne-Jones has also left a revealing, albeit disjointed and impressionistic, account of the thoughts that passed through his mind while he was designing the first of the wood-engravings for The Romaunt of the Rose: like many of his epistles to female correspondents, it is a thinly disguised love-letter. 'You come today & be with me while I draw in the little garden room,' he wrote to Helen Gaskell (whose face is recognizable in some of the Kelmscott Chaucer illustrations³8) in July 1894.". . . It's the Romance of the Rose I am beginning - all new I shall make it & nothing that I have done befre shall behee all shall be be[]

The illustration described by Burne-Jones in this letter is typical of many in the Chaucer: the space is claustrophobically enclosed by the walls o the tiny bedroom, the surface of which is broken only bya tiny window and an arched doorway. Both Chaucer's gown and the hangings of the bed give Burne-Jones an opportunity to display his well-known virtuosity in drawing the sinuous folds of fabrics. Duncan Robinson, who has done an extended analysis of the Chaucer illustrations, finds a recurring pattern of a 'fat back of the room, often recessed, with a gently curved vault and pierced further with either a window or a door'; in the outdoor scenes, he adds, there is frequently a 'three-layer composition, using a wall or battlenents to divide the picture plane horizontallThe English Illustated Magazine (c Emery Walker was an assistant editor) had published in September 1884 a series of five wood-engravings designed by H. Ryland to illustrate an article by Alfred Ainger entitled 'The Women of Chaucer', and they are eerily similar to Burne-Jones's later drawings of the same scenes; but the most plausible explanation of this correspondence is that Ryland was merely imitating stylistic conventions that Burne-Jones had already established in his paintings, which had been frequently exhibited.

Burne-Jones began the Chaucer illustrations in late 1892 or very early 1893, usually working on the designs on Sunday when Morris came to his studio to talk and read aloud. As the task drew to a close, he was able to look back on it with satisfaction: 'And I have been calculating that . . . the time I have given to the Chaucer work in the past two years and a half is exactly to an hour the time I should have spent in visits from Saturday till Monday at "houses," if I had been amiable and sociable - for I haven't let it invade the week's work, but have designed only on Sunday with very little exception -. . . . I have fourteen more designs to make and then my share is done, and I  . I  py over it: it has never tired me but refreshed me always.'42

Yet this mellow retrospective mood fails to convey the genuine anxiety both he and Morris felt at earlier stages in the book's history. Many of the illustrations had to be redone several times. Burne-Jones said their rule was 'that in illustrating a story we must always choose the essential incidents', but their radically differing conceptions of Chaucer's poetry must have led to some very lively discussions between him and Morris; moreover, the elaborate procedure by which Robert Catterson-Smith 'translated' Burne-Jones's delicate pencil strokes into thick, bold lines suitable for engraving (see Chapter Five) was time-consuming and expensive. There were moments when it seemed that the book would never be done. Burne-Jones completed the last designs at the end of December 1895 and wrote one of his delightfully whimsical letters about them: 'I have just finished my Ce  My I   o    . I sincely it wil e ll the ot wan  I havi I could think of to obstruct the onward march of the world. The designs are carved in wood . . . the lines as thick as I could get them. I have done all I can to impede progress  you will always bear me witness that I have not faltered  and that having put my hand to the plough I invariably look back.'44

Burno iiesshe ll c book was $\begin{array} { r } { \oint _ { \scriptstyle \mathbf { \nu } } 7 , 2  { \mathbf { I } } 7 ~  { \mathrm { ~ I ~ I ~ } } s . } \end{array}$ , an enormous investment (extending over four years) that was a source of worry to Morris, who occasionally used the word 'panic' in his diary to describe his response to the multiple problems encountered in its production. Because of the unexpected delays and expenses, he announced to his customers on 14 November 1894 that 'the number printed will be 425 instead of $3 2 \boldsymbol { \zeta } ^ { \prime }$ and that they would be allowed to cancel their original orders if they wished to do so. There is no evidence that any customer did. Jane Morris wrote to Blunt that at first it seemed likély to be a failure financially, then an extra 10o were printed, which has made all the difference between loss and profit'.45

This increase in the size of the edition, however, produced further disagreements with Bernard Quaritch, who was still sulking because he had not been allowed to publish the Chaucer. The terms under which Morris sold the book to dealers were a Is per cent discount on the paper copies, with an additional s per cent allowed for cash payment within a week of delivery, and a flat Is per cent discount on the vellum copies (cash only). Morris had discovered that Quaritch, who was given a further discount, was offering the Chaucer to the trade at a lower price than he himself was, and he was therefore reluctant to sell him any of the additional hundred copies. After protracted, unpleasant negotiations Morris agreed to supply fifty of the copies to Quaritch on condition that he not undersell the Kelmscott Press itself. As Morris had remarked dryly to his wife a few months earlier, 'They are printing the Chaucer very well. Item it is all sold except 3 vellum copies & people are quarrelling over the privilege of buying it.

The 425 paper copies of the Chaucer sold for £ 20 each, though that price was rising among the dealers before it was published; the thirteen vellum copies went for 120 guineas each. The pigskin binding cost an additional $\mathcal { L } ^ { \mathrm { ~ I ~ } 3 }$ . Despite the fact that the entire edition had been sold before the date of publication, the Kelmscott Press ledger reveals that the book made no profit for Morris. As of 3 October 1896 (slightly more than three months after publication) the Chaucer revenues amounted to $\mathcal { f } _ { 7 } , 0 0 5 ~ \mathrm { ~ I ~ } 6 s$ . Iod — less than production costs — though an additional $\oint \mathrm { I } 2 3 \mathrm { ~ I } 4 ^ { s }$ dribbled in later from dealers who had taken the book on credit. Nevertheless, the Chaucer was not a financial success: it had been subsidized, as Morris pointed out on several occasions, by the smaller Kelmscott Press books which had a healthy profit margin.

Figure 8The discounts offercd by Bernard Quaritch were  genrous that Morris feared some customers would be tempted to buy the Chaucer from Quaritch rather than from the Kelmscott Press itself.

Even by the leisurely standards of the Kelmscott Press, the Chaucer was a long time in the making, and the abundant references to the book in contemporary letters and diaries make it possible for us to follow its progress with some precision. Trial pages were pulled in July 1892, and during the same month Morris began to discuss the illustrations with Burne-Jones. Morris designed the first border in February 1893. On 8 August 1894 the first sheet was printed (on vellum). Cobden-Sanderson discussed the binding with Morris in October 1894. On 21 December 1894 another Albion press arrived and was installed in Sunderland Cottage exclusively for printing the Chaucer. By April 1895 all the designs for The Canterbury Tales had been redrawn by Catterson-Smith, and in the following month the printing of the Tales was completed. Between May and August, however, there was a hiatus while Morris tried to solve the mystery of the yellow stains on the sheets already printed. Burne-Jones drew the last illustration in December 1895; at about the same time Morris began to design the p nding ruary 8  rte rave page, finishing it on 27 March. At the end of May the printing of the Chaucer was done, and Morris celebrated by giving presents to his pressmen.

Aside from the magnitude and complexity of the book, a major source of delay (at least during the final year of its production) was Morris's failing health. His friends wondered whether he would live to finish it. T. M. Rooke has left a moving account in his diary of a visit Morris paid to Burne-Jones's home on 2 March 1896: Morris appeared at the door 'very ghostlike, feeble and old looking', and Burne-Jones declared that the Chaucer was "the finest book ever printed — if W.M. had done nothing else it would be enough'. Morris responded, 'I must own I'm complacent about it - must try though not to be too conceited. And there's one thing that's not to be forgotten, that you backed me up well in it, old chap. If you'd been at all slack over it and hadn't been as much excited about it as I was, we should never have got through with it.'

The first two bound copies of the Chaucer were delivered to Morris and Burne-Jones on 2 June, the latter passing his copy on to his daughter the following day as a birthday gift. On 24 June Douglas Cockerell took a train to Folkestone, where Morris had gone on the advice of his doctor, and handed him a copy in the pigskin binding. 'Douglas came with first bound Chaucer about I. very satisfactory,' Morris wrote in his diary. When he gave May her copy, she afterwards recalled, 'the look of profound melancholy that (perhaps unconsciously) he turned on me in smiling tenderly seemed like a glimpse into a very far country. What it quite meant I don't think I realized at the time, though it moved me deeply: all was made clear not long afterwards . . . it was the look of an intensely lonely man — never to be forgotten while memory serves. The Chaucer was Morris's last great achievement: he died the following October.

![](images/70a9b759443b35c793684b651191558f8a15b3fad2b65657db8406129f1fb57e.jpg)  
Figure 81 In this characterisically light-eart caricature by Burn-Jones, Chaucer blesses Morris and Burne-Jones (who look like awkward schoolboys) for their work on the Kelmscott Chaucer.

The Works of Geoffrey Chaucer represents the culmination of Morris's lifelong search for the Ideal Book; it is the kind of book that he and Burne-Jones dreamed of creating when they were undergraduates and that they attempted unsuccessfully to produce during the 186os and 187os: 'it should have come out when Morris and I were at Oxford in our first term,' Burne-Jones wrote, "and we should have lost our senses with bliss.'49 Instead it came at the end of Morris's life, when he was able to pour into this single volume all his bibliophilic passion and his unmatched skill as a designer of ornaments; it is significant that even those readers who find Morris's pages too heavy or congested for their tastes feel compelled, in the end, to pay tribute to the Chaucer as one of the great books of the world. 'To me it is the most beautiful of all printed books,' Yeats declared;° it is a sentiment that many, sometimes grudgingly, have echoed through the years.

![](images/0bf9cb7f5667412dd5ccca94eaecc7af2e35674d29782450f16134b185511aeb.jpg)

F William Morris had not died in 1896, how many more books might the Kelmscott Press have published? We can never know the answer to that question; but it is evident that Morris had numerous schemes in mind at the time of his death, the most important of which were, according to Ellis, an edition of Shakespeare's plays, the English Bible, and Froissart's

Chronicles.' Morris on his deathbed made one last, feeble effort to prolong the life of the Press, as recorded in Cockerell's diary on 30 August 1896: 'He asked whether I should be prepared to carry on the Press after his death, with Walker, & I said that I was in favour of its ceasing - as otherwise it would fizzle out by degrees, & the books already issued would suffer by inferior ones following them. He said he thought I was perhaps right.' Nevertheless, there was much unfinished business which Cockerell and Walker had to supervise after Morris was gone. The renaining five volumes of The Earthly Paradise wiss te  s el s therr ial p w published during the last phase of the Press's history. Not until the spring of 1898 - more than a year and a half after Morris's funeral — was Cockerell able to bring the Kelmscott Press to a definitive close. For the most part the books produced during this period had been planned or even begun while Morris was still alive, but it is enlightening to discover that fully a fifth of the Kelmscott volunes were produced without his direct supervision by associates and employees who had learned to emulate his principles of typography.

Morris showed signs of deteriorating health in 1895. Although one medical diagnosis suggested that he merely suffered from the disease of "being William Morris, and working 18 hours a day', it was obvious to his family and friends that — as Burne-Jones put it — his 'enormous vitality [was] diminishing'³ By February 1896 the symptoms were alarming, and Morris was seeing doctors regularly, though Cockerell still reassured himself that all would be well in the end. 'W.M. very unwell & weak,' he wrote in his diary on 2o February. ". . . Surely it is influenza.' Throughout the spring of 1896 there were further signs of decline, but of Morris's intinate circle only Burne-Jones and Blunt seemed to recognize the gravity of his illness. Blunt's diary describes a visit on I4 May to Hammersmith, 'where I found my poor Morris looking very ill and aged, doddling feebly in front of his house. We went in together however and he brightened up and told me of his maladies in a cheerful and not too desponding way. . . .' When a fortnight later the Morrises visited Blunt at his country house, he observed in his diary (29 May) that Morris's health had worsened: he was now 'very feeble & aged. I fear from the luck of things that it is some form of consumption and that he will not recover, but his spirits are fairly good and hetalks at time as brilliantly as ever.. his ess does not make him gloomy, only it troubles him in his work.' (Blunt's amateur diagnosis of consumption was correct.) These developments plunged Burne-Jones into misery: 'now I am frightened — & can think oflittle else — steadily he seems to be consuming away - without pain — but growing weaker & weaker and I can't think about anything else,' he declared in a letter to Blunt on 6 June.4

A few days later, on his doctor's recommendation, the Morrises left London for Folkestone in search of the beneficial effects of sea air. In July Morris, again on professional advice, took a voyage to Norway, but when he returned the following month to Hammersmith, his health displayed no sign of improvement. He now withdrew to his bedroom at Kelmscott House, which he never thereafter left for more than a few hours, though during the last month of his life there was a steady fow of visitors - the Burne-Joneses, Ellis, Mary De Morgan, Charles Fairfax Murray, Blunt, and others - to the room. On 9 September 1896 Morris made a new will, naming his wife, Ellis, and Cockerell as executors. When Quaritch called at Kelmscott House on 17 Septenber, he discovered Morris 'worn out and feeble', emaciated, and staring out of the window at the river. 'I fear a catastrophe is impending,' Quaritch wrote later the same day.' To raise Morris's spirits, Burne-Jones came on Sundays and sat in the bedroom drawing illustrations for the Kelmscott Press edition of Sigurd the Volsung, and Ellis, elsewhere in the house, transcribed traditional Border ballads for another Kelmscott book that Morris hoped to publish. Morris himself, at intervals, sruggled with the design oran initial word (Empty that was to appear in The Water of the Wondrous Isles (1897). 'He sat up & was going to design"Empty". . . but only just managed to black in the spaces, & just rough in the word,' Cockerell wrote in his diary on 4 September. And two days later: 'W.M. rather tired, but decidedly no worse. He had done a little more to "Empty".' But Morris never completed the design, and after his death Catterson-Smith had to put the finishing touches on it.

On 28 Septenber Blunt witnessed Morris entering the diningroom at Kelmscott House 'like a man risen from the grave'' The following day Morris was in better spirits and insisted upon being taken outdoors in a bath chair and then walking about within the house; a short time later Cockerell, returning from an errand, found him upstairs with blood streaming from his mouth. Morris died on the morning of 3 October. The effect on his friends was devastating. Burne-Jones fell on his knees next to the body and burst into tears; Cockerell retreated to Morrs's study opposite the bedroom and wept in private; Crane paid tribute to him by placing a sonnet and a wreath next t teKelscott Chaucer that went n sply te followng Monday at the Arts and Crafts Exhibition.

But it is entirely characteristic of Cockerell that he did not indulge his personal grief for more than a few minutes. When his sobbing abated, as he testifed many years later, 'I set to work on all there was to be done." Jane Morris had little experience in handling money, Ellis was in poor health himself and lived in Torquay, and therefore almost the entire responsibility for managing the Morris family's financial affairs fell on Cockerell's shoulders. During the following months he engaged in ceaseless, almost frenzied activity. A buyer had to be found for Kelmscott House; arrangements were made for Morris's library to be sold; Jane Morris and her daughters required constant attention and sympathetic understanding. Above all, Cockerell had to avert the immediate collapse of the Kelmscott Press by pushing through to completion the books already in production and by devising a rational scheme for gradually closing down the Press.

To the discerning eye there were soon signs of Morris's departure from the Kelmscott Press. The largest book then passing through the press was The Earthly Paradise, three volumes of which had appeared before Morris's death. In the fourth volume the colophon (dated 25 November 1896) was altered so that 'Printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press' became 'Printed by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press'.8 An announcement, issued on 12 November, of The Shepheardes Calender contained this unsettling final setence: The folio editons fFroissart & f Sigurd theVolsng, described in the Juue list, have been abandoned.' Even in the books that were completed, the absence of Morris's guiding hand was evident, particularly in the lack of new borders, initials, and engraved titlepages. (The Athenaeum, on 27 November 1897, was sufficiently alert to notice these deficiencies in its review of The Water of the Wondrous

IsAo io wy much more lightly ornamented. Love is Enough, the penultimate book printed at the Kelnscott Press, is an interesting example of how Cockerell was compelled to solve book-design difficulties on his own, since no work had been done on it before October 1896; Cockerell confessed that it offered many difficult typographical problems on account of the varying length of the lines It must be said, in fairness to Cockerell, that these last books of the Kelmscott Press display no dramatic fallingoffin qualiy, but with the exceptionoftherosrt specimen pages, they represent a reversion to the simpler style of ornamentation characteristic of the very earliest Kelmscott books when Morris had not yet designed very many borders and initials.

There were countless books that Morrs had thought of printing the Bible (Wycliffe' version), Bunyan'Pilgrim's Progress, FitzGerald's translation of The Rubáiyát ofOmar Khayyám, Malory's Morte Darthur, Piers Plowman, to name only a few — and Cockerell recognized that there was now no hope of ever doing them at the Kelmscott Press. Two projected folios were abandoned, however, with greater reluctance. In 1893 Furnivall had persuaded Morris to undertake an edition of Shakespeare's plays, with either P. A. Daniel or Furnivall as editor and Longmans as publisher. Morris printed at least two trial pages in the Golden type: the design (two columns, stage directions and speakers' names in red, a six-line initial at the beginning of each scene) is simple to the point of austerity, and it is not known whether he intended to include illustrations. In any event, the project never went any farther than this, because work on the Chaucer absorbed Morris's attention entirely.

The other aborted folio was Froissart's Chronicles, which was in a more advanced state of production than the Shakespeare. Morris enthusiastically announced his plans to a journalist in 1895: 'My reprint is a full folio and will take up two volumes. I also intend to publish it in four parts . . . no book that I could do would give me half the pleasure I am getting from the Froissart. I am simply revelling in it. It's such a noble and glorious work, and every page as it leaves the press delights me more than I can say. I am taking great pains with it, and doing all I can to realise what I have long wished. I am printing it in my Chaucer type.'" Despite Morris's assertion, no sheets of the book were actually printed, but by November 1896 some thirty-six pages were in type, and Cockerell hit upon the ingenious solution of publishing a two-leaf vellum specimen (as well as a larger specimen privately

ChAt the bonorable and noble aventures of featis of armes, done &achyued by the warres of fraunce and Inglande, shulde notably be inregistered, and put in perpetuall memory, whereby the prewe & hardy may haue ensample to incourage them in theyr well doyng, I syr Johan froissart, wyll treat and recorde an byatory of great louage and preyse: but, or I begyn, I require the sauyour of all the worlde, who of nothyng created al thynges, that he wyll gyue me suche grace and vnderotandyng, that I may continue and perseuer in such wyse, that who so this proces redeth orhereth, may take pastaunce, pleaoure, and ensaumple It is said of trouth, that al buyldynges are masoned & wroughte of dyuerse stones, and all great ryuers are gurged and assemblede of divers surges and sprynges of water:in lykewyse all sciences are extraught & compiled of diuerse clerkes, of that one wryteth, another parauenture is ignorant; but by thé famous wrytyng of auncient auctours, all thyng is ben knowen in one place or other.

also in bis lyfes days welbeloued, and of the Becret counsayle with the lorde sir Johan of Haynaulte, who is often remembred, as reason requyreth,here after in thisboke; forof many fayre & noble auentures be was chiefe causer, & to the kyng right nigh, & by whose meanes the said syr Joban la Bele myght well knowe and here of many dyuers noble dedes. The whiche here after shal be declared.

ROUtB it is,thatI whohave entreprised this boke to ordeyne for pleasure and pastaunce, to the whiche alwayes Ibave been inclyned, & for that intent I haue folowed and frequented the company of dyuerse noble & great lordes, as wcll in fraunce, Inglande, and Scotlande, as in diuerse other countries, and bave had knowledge by them, and alwayes to my power iustly haue inquired forthe trouth of the dedis of warre and auentures that haue fallen, and specially syth the great batell of Poytyers, where as the noble kynge Johan of france was takyn prisoner, as before that tyme I was but of a yonge age or vnderstandyng  Howe be it I toke it on me assoone as I come from scole, to wryte and recite the sayd boke, & bare the same compyled into Ingland, and presented the volume thereof to my Lady Phelyppe of Heynaulte, noble quene of Inglande, who right amyably receyved it to my great profite & avauncement. N y b for featis of armes derely bought & achyued, the bonor therof ought to be gyuen& truly de uided to them that by prowes & hard trauayle haue deserued it  Cherfore to acquyte me in that bibalfe, & in folowyng the trouth as near as I can, I Johan froissart have entreprysed this hystory on the forsaid ordynaunce & true fundacion, at the instaunce and request of a dere lorde of myn, Robertof Namure, knight, lorde of Bewfort, to whom entierly I owe loue and obeysaunce, & God graunt me to do that thyng that may be to his pleasure. Figure 85 One of the great unfinished undertakings of the Kelmscott Press was Froissart's Chronicles, which was envisioned as a lavishly ornamented folio that would rival the Chaucer in splendour. After Morrs's death, only a few specimen pages were issued.

printed on paper as a gift to some friends of Morris and the Press). The Froissart specimen revcals a stunning new departure in Morrisian ornamentation: though the two-column text is arranged in the same pattern as that of the Chaucer, the borders, incorporating coats ofarms, are extraordinarily vigorous and spiky in appearance, an initial word is connected to one of the borders, and the initials extend into the margins. (Morris also designed some similar initials, with vines and tendrils trailing below, for the unfinished Sigurd."²) Such innovations will startle us only if we have mistakenly assumed that Morris's principles of book-design and ornanentation were static and wholly incapable of development. The Froissart specimen pages leave no doubt that Morris's comparatively early death robbed the world ofa work which would have rivalled the Chaucer as one of the finest printed books of the post-medieval era. Swinburne's astute comment was, 'Why did not Morris take up & finish Froissart earlier - instead of the less appropriate work (Shelley, for one instance) on which the Kelmscott Press was occasionally wasted.'13

Of the final group of books published at the Press, the one that caudCockerel thereatet anxity ws Morri'The Story Surd the Volsung, which was originally intended to be a folio adorned by some twenty-five Burne-Jones illustrations. The book had been planned as early as 1891, but Burne-Jones was not able to turn his attention to it until January 1896 after he completed the Chaucer designs. 'To pacify and please Morris I will do what I can to adorn the Volng Talhe wrote to Lady Leighton at about this time.But o tn  I The scenery for one thing isn't quite a painter's scenery — it's more a BJo s  u designs and still complaining that he found the assignment unconnal. It' qu wondeul hoteul the  o akng drawings of it is to me,he said. . .Terrible the tragedy of it is and the fine things in it are so much fitter for literature than for my art, and I've no turn r the dramati."As Morrs ly dyng at Kelcott Ho uo the designs to his friend's bedroom each Sunday, thus extending to the last moment the intense intellectual and artistic relationship that had existed between them since their Oxford days. Burne-Jones was plainly rlevd when thefolSiurd ws adnd, bt ven   Jany 8 Cckel nvi h  h t illustrations for a scaled-down quarto version of the book, which was eventually published in February 1898. It was his last piece of work for the Kelmscott Press, and both he and Cockerell were conscious that it was the end of an epoch in Burne-Jones's career. Cockerell had lunch with him at the Grange, his home in South Kensington, on 7 October 1897, and made this terse record in his diary: 'EB-J alone & very nice. He was working on the picture of Gudrun for the Sigurd which bids fair to be as fine as anything he has done for the Kelmscott Press - a fitting conclusion.'

![](images/202f7a81e5d0fc086e20f6c3155f204068220a61f30d2a7c42c198ff432e1cc9.jpg)  
Because of Morris's death, the ornamentation of the book had to be drastically curtailed when it was published in 1898. The style of these unfinished initials, whose tails extend down into the margin, is sinilar to that of the initials Morris created for the Froissart, another aborted project.

There were in fact two more Burne-Jones illustrations in Love is Enough, published just a few days before the Kelmscott Press officially closed in March 1898, but they had been designed earlier. As the colophon explained, 'The picture on the opposite page was not designed for this edition of Love is Enough, but for an edition projected about twenty-five years ago, which was never carried out.' Similarly, the frontispiece of the last Kelmscott book, A Note by William Morris on his Aims in Founding the Kelmscott Press, was one of the wood-engravings designed by Burne-Jones and cut by Morris in the 186os for the unfinished Earthly Paradise. And so, in these final two volumes, there were touching reminders of Morris's and Burne-Jones's lifelong preoccupation with the Ideal Book, and things cane full circle at last. Cockerell, who struck some observers as a coolly efficient technician-manager, was in reality a man of deep emotion, especially in his loyalty to Morris, and he wound up the affairs of the Kelmscott Press with evocative flourishes.

##

During 1897 and early 1898 Cockerell was also kept busy sorting through the abundant records and papers of the Press. The most interesting proofs and other documents he kept, with Jane Morris's peissn,r is  con evermeodal, he e of cancelled cheques by clipping Morris's signatures and pasting them inet k p  o Cockerell's friends, particularly Robert Proctor, the brilliant young incunabulist and Morris-admirer at the British Museum. 'You are not unique in your taste for first proofs &c — but I will see what I can fnd for you later on,' Cockerell had written to him in November 1896,17 and during the following year he supplied Proctor with a large quantity of such material. Proctor attempted to make use of it in a Morris bibliography, never published, "which he was compiling

Kelmscott proofs and related documents that survive today in both ia them have passed through the hands of Cockerell or Proctor at one time.

The gradual closing f the Kelmscott Press was punctuated by one reminder after another of Morris's death. On 24 March 1897, for example, Cockerell, Walker, and Ellis met together again at Kelmscott Houe  celebrate Morri birtday, with Els reading aloud par f The Lovers of Gudrun before they went to bed. Between then and August Cockerell found himself burdened with the melancholy task of supervising the removal of Morris's belongings from Kelmscott House, and on 18 August he sold Morris's vellum set of the Kelmscott Press books (for 65o) to Henry Gamman, an old friend from the Coal Exchange. The sane morning, by coincidence, he disposed of the last set printed on paper. In July he approached Sir Edward Maunde Thompson, director of the British Museum, about depositing the woodblocks of the Kelmscott Press ornaments and illustrations in the Museum, a transaction that took place in December. 'F. S. Ellis came in the aft[ernoo]n & discussed the future of the K.P. type & ornaments,' Cockerell recorded in his diary on 12 November 1897. 'I declined to consent to the ornaments being used after the close of the Press.' The remaining stock of Batchelor paper was transferred to the Chiswick Press, as were the founts of type.

To Jane Morris he offered this summary in December of the state of the Press: 'The winding up is very anxious work - . . . There are 5 books still to come out[:] Sigurd, Love is Enough, Sundering Flood, German Woodcuts, & the Note on the Press. The first three are waiting for pictures that are nearly ready & the 4th is at the binders. I shall have to see these books launched before I can take up other matters of a heavy kind.'"9 Because of his added responsibilities — Cockerell was in effect directing the Kelmscott Press after Morris's death, with advice and occasional assistance from Walker and Ellis — Jane Morris paid him  1oo beyond his usual salary in March 1898, extending to him also my heartiest thanks for having brought the Kelmscott Press to such a successful end, it's career has been a marvel since the beginning, but the completeness of the success was certainly owing to your very skilful steering towards the end

The last two announcements issued by the Press had a note of finality: that of 22 November 1897 declared that the Kelmscott Press "will close early in the New Year', and that of 25 February offered for

On 24 December Cockerell 'spent the day in moving from Sunderland Cottage to I4 Upper Mall [Sussex Cottage], thus closing another chapter, the last but one, in the history of the Press'. Those were the words he entered in his diary that Christmas Eve; earlier in the day, as the workmen waited to carry off his desk, Cockerell wrote a final letter to Jenny Morris from his office in Sunderland Cottage, regretting that the fog prevented a last nostalgic view across the Thames from his window. Builders at that moment were already beginning to turn the cottage into a granary.²1

The last few sheets were printed in Sussex Cottage during the early months of 1898. Following two springlike days, Cockerell reported to Jenny on 7 January: 'Our pressman, there is only one left now, says that we need a frost for the ink rollers, which get soft & difficult to print with in damp weather. I hope it will come, as we have the second Sigurd picture to do next week, & the map for the Sundering Flood - and perhaps the first picture for Love is Enough, which Mr Hooper is now engraving — all of these will need his best skill  '2 The sole surviving pressman was Stephen Mowlem, who on 19 March printed the last copies of the last signature of the last Kelmscott Press book, A Note by William Morrs. The final sheet, appropriately, was given to Walker; the penultimate sheet, signed by Mowlem and W.H. Bowden, the only other printer still on the premises, went into Cockerell's collection. The official publication date of the book was 24 March. The time had come, as Cockerell remarked to a correspondent the previous month, ´to write ExPLIcIT over the door of the Kelmscott Press'.²3 On 3 1 March Cockerell sent a benedictory letter to J. and J. Leighton (thanking them "for all your care & attention to the binding of the Kelmscott Press during the seven years of its existe id goy  Jane Morris had lunc wit Geoana Burne-Jones at the Grange, and sailed the next day to Siena, leaving behind Mrs Peddie to deal with the Press correspondence until he returned in early May.

The demise of the Kelmscott Press did not go unnoticed by the book trade or the press. 'There has been a great rush on the German woodcuts & they were all sold out last week,' Cockerell observed on 30 November 1897. ". . . A good many people have been left out in the cold, as orders keep coming in! I suppose it is the announcement that the Press  closing that as made people  ager.a

Hollings, the London dealer, trumpeted to his customers in December that 'there is no doubt that the dissolution of the "Kelmscott Press", . . . and the strictly limited number printed of each work, will cause them to rise in value by leaps and bounds', and he added with becoming modesty, 'I have a renarkable stock of these desirable volumes'.° The Academy, in a long article entitled 'The End of the Kelmscott Press' (12 December 1896), offered a balanced appraisal of Morris's achievement as a printer, concluding that 'on one thing, at any rate, we are all agreed, that for better or for worse, the folio edition of Chaucer which appeared this summer forms a great landmark in the history of printing, and were sufficiently monumental in itself, had he produced no other book, to render the names of the Kelmscott Press and of William Morris memorable for all times'.

##

Yet, when Cockerell walked out of the door of Sussex Cottage on the last day of March 1898, he did not succeed in putting the problems of the Kelmscott Press behind him. He had agreed to sell the presses and other equipment to C. R. Ashbee's Essex House Press (which will figure again in the next chapter), and several of Morris's printers, including Binning, decided to join forces with Ashbee as well. Eventually Hooper, Catterson-Smith, and Douglas Cockerell also contributed their services to the Essex House Press; Ashbee's decorated initials were freely adapted from Morris's, and in one of his books he even invoked the name of the Kelmscott Press in the colophon. Morris's old friends and associates were outraged by what they considered to be a shameless exploitation of a minor connection. To Walker it seemed futile to try to imitate the Kelmscott Press. 'The main-spring is broken,' he remarked with his usual moderation to Ashbee, 'and when the watch stops there is nothing further to be donethers used ess teperate anguage.The ress was br to a definite end, and no one has the right to pose as successor to WilliamMorW.H.Bon ca etr  . C I certainly do not think Ashbee worth taking any heed of,' he wrote to Cockerell in June 1899. 'It is only the innates or candidates for Earlswood [an Asylum for Idiots in Sussex], who are likely to confuse Ashbee with Morris, & I don't think they are worth troubling about. . . . Ashbee's initial difficulty I take to be entire ignorance of h was slightly embarrassed about the uproar, because he had earlier agreed to edit Shakespeare's poems for Ashbee (just as he had done for Morris), and he added jovially in his next letter, "Perhaps you will learn in his next circlar that he has bought me as a freeold. But I still thi him quite unworthy of powder and shot.'

Cockerell devoted a good deal of time and energy during the closing years of the century to matters of this sort. In general he was much more disturbed than Ellis and Walker were by suppo treats to the reputation o Morris or the Kelmscott Press. When a copy of the Chaucer in unbound sheets appeared in a London auction room in the summer of 1899, Cockerell became agitated because he was convinced that it must have been stolen from the presroom; a lengthy correspondence between him and Ellis ensued, with Ellis again advocating a calm response. (The mystery was never solved, though another set ofChaucer sheets was offered for sale by Sotheby's the following year; in boh s My Mor'  Robt Sel nd S appear to have been implicated.)

Walker favoured a proposal to print Morrs's collected works in the Golden type at the Chiswick Press, but Cockerell vetoed it, and only five slender volumes of lectures by Morris were printed thus. The question of the ultimate disposition of the Kelmscott types continued to vex Cockerell for several decades. When May Morrs was revising her will in 1919, Cockerell suggested that the types 'might perhaps go to Oxford University, if Walker has nothing better to propose'3 Seven years later he wrote to her that, "with Mr Hornby's approval [St John Hornby was now one of the trustees of the Morris estate], I am transferring the Kelmscott Press type from the Chiswick Press (which has now passed into hands that we know not of) to the Cambridge University Press, which is now doing very good work. Apart from the fact that I am in Cambridge for a while the Press here is a permanent concern, whereas the Chiswick Press was recently in difficulties — so I hope you will also think it a wise change.'"² Cockerell recommended to May in 1933 that she give the types to the Cambridge University Press, but it was not until 194o that they became the property of the Press by purchase.33 Morris's founts have recently been deposited in the Cambridge University Library.

Today the flotsam and jetsam of the Kelmscott Press are scattered round the world. The types are in Cambridge; the woodblocks are in the British Museum; the presses are in Hammersmith, New York, and California; Morris's 'bloomer book is in Oxford; the address book and the ledger of the Press are in New York; a smaller account book is in Japan; and huge mounds of paper — all those precious scraps of proofs and trial pages so assiduously collected by Cockerell - have floated away to several continents. Confronted with these sad reminders of the impermanence of all human endeavours, we may perhaps find some consolation in the thought that the more durable monument to the memory of William Morris and the Kelmscott Press is, as the Academy suggested, the fifty-two wonderful books, particularly the Chaucer, printed in Hammersmith between 1891 and 1898.

# TEN  THE LEGACY OF MORRIS AND HIS PRESS

![](images/319b0998fc309e364a19409473abd605e99bc4eeffc7ff1e6218a2544c00b924.jpg)

HERE must have been moments when Morris, deafened by applause for his brilliant and famous adventure in printing, wondered privately whether the Kelmscott Press measured up to his own standards of achievement. I aim at the idea and the ideal, and never get beyond the collector"Cobden-Sanderson lamented, .and Morris might well have echoed the complaint.' In 190o C. R. Ashbee, visiting the Caxton Club, a collectors'organization in Chicago, heard the members speak of Morrises' as though they were particularly lucrative shares: one man expressed his enthusiasm for the rising value of the Kelmscott books by declaring that no trust that ever was formed has paid such a rate of interest'² In News from Nowhere Morris envisioned a socialist Utopia in which all men and women would have the opportunity to createbeautiful objects, and the fruits of their labour would be gener ously distributed according to need; but, as Morris acknowledged in his evocative, bitter-sweet last chapter, such a world exists now only in our dreams and visions, and for the moment the ugly reality of 'dingy Hammersmith' (or greedy Chicago) prevails. The Kelmscott Press books, intended to symbolize a protest against the ethos of Victorian industrial capitalism, became themselves, in all their opulent splendour, an example of conspicuous consumption. The irony of this improbable transmutation was not lost on Morris.

If Morris found it difficult to evaluate the success of his Press, it is not surprising that others, from 1891 onwards, have offered remarkably contradictory appraisals of the Kelmscott books. Probably the majority of bibliophiles have always been willing to accept the proposition trenchantly expressed by Emery Walker in 1930, that 'Morris showed how a printed book might be on its own plane a work of art'; but the Daily Chronicle was undoubtedly also speaking for a vocal minority when it declared in 1896, 'Truly it is awful to contemplate the terrible effect that the Morris doctrine of decorative illustration has had upon the world.' What is puzzling is that evidence to support either of these views lies abundantly at hand: the immediate consequence of the Kelmscott Press was to produce a horde of imitators who lacked Morris's skilland sensitivity as a book-designer, yet the ultimate impact of the Press has been so powerful and pervasive that it is not easy even now, a century later, to assess. The issue is further clouded by Morris's insistence that his typographical adventure was linked with a root-and-branch rejection of modern life. If we demand to know, therefore, what Morris's experiment in bookmaking 'means' to us today, we are asking a difficult question for which there are no unambiguous answers.

##

The most ardent imitator of the Kelmscott Press typography in England was C. R. Ashbee, who had established the Guild of Handicraft in London's East End in 1888.4 Ashbee saw himself as merely putting Morris's principles into practice, though with greater emphasis on the workshop rather than on the individual craftsman, but he received little encouragement from Morris, who suspected rightly that Ashbee wished to reform, not overthrow, the capitalist system. Laurence Hodson, a wealthy young businessman with an interest in Pre-Raphaelitism and the Arts and Crafts movement, visited the Kelmscott Press in December 1897, and early in the following year he provided funds that made it possible for Ashbee to buy the Press's equipment. Ashbee was overjoyed, since the transaction offered him a symbolic claim to discipleship that had been denied while Morris was still alive: 'the great event of the week had been the purchase of the Morris Press by the Guild of Handicraft,' he wrote at the end of March 1898. 'Great jubilation! . . . So next Monday there will be vans of presses and gages, and chairs and tables and paper and machinery - lock, stock and barrel moving East from Hammersmith. We are thinking of a musical procession. We engage Morris's printers and carry on the great tradition.'"

To Cockerell's annoyance, Ashbee emphasized the Kelmscott connection by adopting the same ink, paper, and vellum as Morris had used at the Kelmscott Press; illustrations were to take the form of wood-engravings, with, in some instances, lluminations supplied by Florence Kate Kingsford (who later became Mrs Cockerell). Since Morris's types did not constitute part of his purchase, Ashbee printed the carliest books of the Essex House Press in Caslon, but he was eager to follow Morrs's example by creating his own typefaces. Ashbee's e Er   e in which it appeared, An Endeavour towards the Teaching of John Ruskin and William Morris, published in 1901) and the King's Prayer Book type, a larger, modified version of the Endeavour. 'In the design of the founts I sugt tofollw pon the es id down by Mos, Ashbee wrote. 'Development appeared possible in the direction of combined letters, and my bias was towards a black (in preerenc to a eccentric, and, if one wished to argue the case that Morris's influence on subsequent type-designs was in the main pernicious, the Endeavour and King's Prayer Book types would probably constitute Exhibit A.

The Essex House Press printed eighty-three books over a period of eleven years, and often they rise above the limitations of Ashbee's types and wood-engraved initials (the latter more art nouveau than Morrisian): the illustrations, some of them by Ashbee himself, are undeniably attractive, and even at their weakest the books now have a certain period charm, not unlike that of an awkward Victorian building which has mellowed with age. In 1902 the Essex House Press, along with the Guild's other craft workshops, moved to Chipping Campden in the Cotswolds, and there, for six years, Ashbee and his men strenuously searched for the simple life far from the Modern Babylon. They sang newly revised texts of old folk-songs (one stanza of 'Joan's Ale'celebrated a compositor who joins the 'jovial crew' and declares, The printer's worth his pay | Especially if he come Kelmscott way!'), explored the countryside together, shared their profits, and attempted to revive a somewhat sentimentalized version of the medieval guild. It was a noble experiment, but, as Morris had predicted, the guild ideal was difficult to sustain in a society dominated by a hostile economic system. The Essex House Press found itself increasingly in competition with machine-press books patterned after the Kelmscott Press style, and the remoteness of Chipping Campden interfered seriously with efforts to market the Press's products. Ashbee later insisted that all the English private presses were victims of an unfounded optimism: 'What we failed to understand was (a) that our public was limited; (b) that the competitive system was so devised that the various presses which should have been co-operating for the maintenance of standards were at times forced into competition; and (c) that ultimately "the Trade", seeing there was money in it, would set up sham presses to catch that money."7

As early as the summer of 1904 Ashbee's printers were put on half-time, yet he was reluctant to close the Press because of its potent symbolism suggesting that Morris's mantle had fallen upon his
