shoulders. In August 1906, however, Ashbee and Binning, the stubborn socialist compositor who had worked under Morris at the Kelmscott Press, bore the sad responsibility of stock-taking before Morris's presses and other equipment were sold: 'old Binning and I entered them up and chalked them down and set out value on them — so much for the working price and so much for the sentiment, except when we came to sizing up the Press on which the Chaucer was printed and we raised that a fu above its market value.

If the Essex House Press books represent a debased rendition of the Arts and Crafts typographical style, there were other English private presses at the turn of the century which displayed the influence of Morris more positively, and three of them deserve separate consideration; the Doves Press, the Ashendene Press, and the Vale Press. The Doves Press, founded in 190o by Cobden-Sanderson and Emery Walker, was the most distinguished successor of the Kelmscott Press and was patterned after the Kelmscott model very closely indeed. The Batchelor paper and vellum bindings were virtually identical; the Doves type was based on the same photographs of pages printed by Jenson and Rubeus that Morris had used in designing the Golden type;9 and a Kelmscott page stripped of its ornaments does not differ fundamentally from a Doves page. Given the close relationship of both its proprietors to Morris, the similarities are of course hardly unexpected. One of the pressmen at the Doves Press had even served an apprenticeship at the Kelmscott Press, and J. H. Masn records that a portrait of Morris was pinned over the fireplace in the Doves composing room.

But it is misleading to emphasize too strongly the parallels between Hammersmith's two celebrated private presses. Cobden-Sanderson did not share Morris's bias in favour of medieval typography and ornament; his own preferences (and Emery Walker's preferences too, for that matter) led him to sixteenth-century French and Italian books as exemplars, and the volumes he produced at the Doves Press were severely simple in design, unadorned by ornament or illustration. The only form of decoration that Cobden-Sanderson would permit himself was the occasional calligraphic initial, the most-photographed example of which is Edward Johnston's beautiful 'I' on the opening page of the Doves Bible (1903-s). 'Candidly, I do not think that William Morris himself is a great printer,' Cobden-Sanderson wrote to Cockerell in 19o9: 'that his work is remarkable and in itself constitutes an epoch, is, again, another matter. William Morris came to

important question. The main question is the aspect which the llutration hall be made to take inoder to fit it into and amid a page of Typography. And I submit that its aspect must be essentially formal and oe  ex  t spek s the etes. It should have a set frame or margin to itself, demarcating it distinctly from the text, and the shape & characteroftheframe,ifdecorative,shouldhaverelationto the pae as well as to the illutrative cntent; an te illustrative content itself should be formal and kept under so as literally to llustrate, and not to dim by over brilliancy the rest of the subject matter left to be communicatéd to the imagination by the letterpress alone.

## THE BOOK BEAUTIFULAS A WHOLE

FINALLY,ifthe Book Beautifulmay bebeautifulby ab el, eiul, by te of allto the production of one composite whole, the consummate Book Beautiful. Here the ideato becommunicated by the book comes first, as the thing of supreme importance. Then comes in attendance upon it, striving for the love of the idea to be itself beautiful, the written or printed page, the decorated or decorative letters, the pictures, set amid the text, and finally the binding, holdig the whole in itstrong rp d for very love again itself becoming beautiful because in company with the idea.

This is the supreme Book Beautiful or Ideal Book, a dream, a symbol of the infinitely beautiful in which all things of beauty rest and into which all things of beautyoltmately merg

¶The Book Beautiful, then, should be conceived of as a whole, & the self-assertion of any Art beyond the limits imposed by the conditionsof its creation should be looked upon as an Act of Treason. The proper duty of each Art within such limits is to co-operate with all the other arts, similarly employed, in the production of something which is distinétly Not-Itself. Thewholeness,symmetry,harmony,beautywithout stress or strain, of the Book Beautiful, would then be one in principle with the wholeness, symmetry, harmony, and beauty without stress or strain, of that WHÓLE OF LIFE WHICH IS CONSTITUT EDOFOURSELVES&THEWORLD,THAT COMPLEX AND MARVELLOUS WHOLE WHICH,AMIDTHE STRIFE OF COMPETI TIVE FORCES, SUPREMELY HOLDS ITS OWN, AND IN THE LANGUAGE OF LIFE WRITES, UPON THE ILLUMINED PAGES OF THE DAYS, THE VOLUMES OF THE CENTURIES, & THROUGH THE INFINIT UDES OF TIME & SPACE MOVES RHYTH MICALLY ONWARD TO THE FULL DEVE LOPMENT OF ITSASTONISHINGSTORY THE TRUE ARCHETYPE OF ALL BOOKS BEAUTIFULOR SUBLIME,

Figure 89 The typography of the Dovcs Press was directly modelled on that  th Kelott r, partculary  hass  cloan word-spacing. The sentiments of this essay on The Book Beautiful by Cobden-Sanderson, however, would doubtless have struck Morris as too pretentiously phrased.

printing with a mind set on decoration, and with a mind overscored with tapestry and woven effects, all which he reproduced where they were not wanted, on the pages of his books. And many of his effects, as I told him at the time, are "typographical impertinencies", and utterly destrüctive of the page as an expression of the author's thought.'"

Cobden-Sanderson brought his partnership with Walker to an end in 1909, and the Press itself closed in 1916; in order to prevent Walker from using the Doves fount in future books, Cobden-Sanderson melodramatically tossed the punches, matrices, and types into the Thames under cover of darkness. The antagonism that erupted between Walker and Cobden-Sanderson is, however, only a partial explanation of the ultimate collapse of the Doves Press: a deeper reason lies in the rigidity of Cobden-Sanderson's vision of what he termed 'the Book Beautiful' (a phrase sometimes erroneously attributed to Morris but presumably derived from Oscar Wilde's 'House Beautiful'). The unhappy truth is that the Doves books are boring in their cold perfection, each one an immaculate clone of the other. Albeit stunningly lovely, they disclose none of the vitality that one finds in even the least satisfactory Kelmscott books. Yet this extreme simplicity of the Doves Press volumes mediated between the ornate pages of the Kelmscott Press and the more austere tastes of twentieth-century typographers. By demonstrating that Morris's principles of book-design could be applied with equal success to a completely undecorated text, Cobden-Sanderson and Walker translated the Morrisian Ideal Book into terms acceptable to printers and publishers who found Morris's own style intolerable. Giovanni Mardersteig, whose Officina Bodoni has been regarded by some as the most outstanding private press of the twentieth century, is only one notable example of a young man who first became excited about the possibilities of fine printing through the example of the Doves Press."² What we must always bear in mind, however, is that the Doves Press could not have existed without the theories and practice of Morris lying behind it.

C. H. St John Hornby's Ashendene Press, on the other hand, had already been launched before Hornby met Morris, and his first books resemble, if anything, the Revd Daniel's little private productions at Oxford. Hornby began printing on a modest scale in 1894, but in March 1895 he made a pilgrimage to the Kelmscott Press, where he saw the Chaucer in production, talked to Morris in his study, and had tea with both of the Morrises in their dining-room."² Hornby, like

Cobden-Sanderson and Walker, was sufficiently strong-minded to avoid a direct imitation of the Kelmscott books, for, as he said, the Chaucer, though 'in many ways the greatest example of a printed book of all time', is "inimitable'."3 'But', Hornby observed in I935, 'we can follow the spirit of his work, and that spirit has without a doubt exercised an enormous influence during the past 4o years upon printing throughout the world. Even those who profess to make light of Morris as a printer are themselves largely influenced by what was best in the Morris tradition. . 9I4

In 19oo Hornby met Cockerell and through him Walker (Cockerell was at this time a partner in Walker's business), and they persuaded him that it would be possible to create his own type fount for the Ashendene Press. After consulting Robert Proctor, they decided to turn to the semi-Gothic of Sweynheym and Pannartz as their model: Hornby was of course aware that Morris had once begun designing a type based on the Sweynheym—Pannartz face, and this no doubt added to its appeal. The resulting Subiaco type was so successful that he continued to use it regularly until the Ashendene Press ceased operation in 1935. The Press had a long, illustrious history, much of it falling outside the boundaries of this book; but what Hornby's career as a printer especially demonstrates is that it was (and is) possible to be a disciple of Morris yet to chart one's own course in book-design. The Ashendene volumes convey the impression that Hornby still speaks Morris's language but has translated it into a new and unexpected dialect.

The Vale Press (1896-1904) of Charles Ricketts offers another instructive example of how the Morrisian influence could be diluted by other forces. Ricketts admired Morris's books immensely, but he recognized that it was only too easy for the Kelmscott style to be debased by less talented designers. In an essay entitled A Defence of the Revival of Printing (1899), Ricketts commented on the 'ornamental tendency' in Morris which led him to create books 'ornamentally conceived and of ornamental use. With such pomp of circunstance', he observed, 'they became more and more large, necessitating ornamental type such as the Troy type; certain books like the Chaucer should be perused at a lectern.' When the designing of such a book fell into the hands of a typographer who only half-understood Morris's he   - cellence, relate it to some casual fount, throw in with this a photor the new usual ornamental book becomes unbearable; instead of a revival in the shaping of books we have a new cumbersome trade article."5

The type that Ricketts designed for the Vale Press is a bold, slabserifed Roman which openly proclaims its indebtedness to the Golden type, and Ricketts's borders also owe something to Morris's, though the general effect of a Vale page is much lighter, more delicate and open. Ricketts, like Cobden-Sanderson, found the dark coloration of the Kelmscott books unattractive and sought inspiration instead in the less cluttered pages of the early Venetian printers. 'I would define the page of a finé Kelmscott book as full of wine, an Italian book as full oflight,'he declared. Yet if Hornby veers off into a different dialect of the Morrisian language of bookmaking, Ricketts seems to be speaking in a different tongue altogether: the earnestness and solidity ofthe Kelmscott style have, through some strange sea-change, become mannered and precious.

Rickets's deepest links, despite his professed emulation of Morris, are in fact with the art-nouveau book that coexisted with the Morrisian style during the final decade of the century. John Russell Taylor, in a lengthy study of the art-nouveau fashion in typography, has offered a useful and concise distinction between the two traditions: 'Arts and Crafts [i.e. the Kelmscott-inspired style] is essentially, as the label suggests, direct, unaffected, simple (or at least simpliste); it is fairly solid and robust; and it is solidly traditional, often with a sort of revivalist fervour. Art nouveau, on the other hand, is essentially sophisticated and eclectic, accepting and assimilating influences from here, there and everywhere — Japanese, Greek, Celtic, Pre-Raphaelite. . . , Rococo, Dutch, Blake, Victorian industrial design, etc., etc. Moreover, art nouveau is above all highly mannered."7 The origins of art nouveau are obscure and complex; suffice it to say here that Aubrey Beardsley was responsible for introducing the style in book illustrations and ornaments, and, as we saw earlier, this came about as a direct result ofhis failed attempt to illustrate a Kelmscott book. When Morris peremptorily refused his frontispiece for Sidonia the Sorceress, Beardsley saw Dent's edition of Le Morte Darthur (1893-4) as a wonderful opportunity to achieve revenge by parodying the Morris—Burne-Jones conventions and modifying them in a sensual, decadent fashion. The Morte Darthur was a great commercial success, and it effectively launched Beardsley's career as an illustrator. 'William Morris has sworn a terrible oath against me for daring to bring out a book in his

Beardsley's clever travesty of the Kelmscott books was both a personal rebellion against his respectable elders and a fin-de-siècle response to Morris's medievalism, which seemed to Beardsley's generation a tiresome symptom of mid-Victorian values. When Leonard Smithers proposed´establishing a magazine with the very ninetyish name of Peacock, Beardsley wrote to him in 1897, 'On the art side I suggest that it shouldattack untiringly and unflinchingly the BurneJones and Morrisian medieval business, and set up a wholesome seventeenth and eighteenth-century standard of what picture making should be." Bear' w o wuThe r h part of the decade sponsored periodic competitions for illustrated title-pages, and the winning pages were almost always more in the Bearslyv than in  sy whic Morr  Bue-Jone would have approved.

There was another emerging typographical fashion in the 1890s, most brilliantly represented in the skilfully designed books of Whistler, which emphasized asymmetric layout, minimal ornamentation, and mechanical methods of printing: the 'Aesthetic book' (as one scholar has called it)² seems to owe almost nothing to Morris's example, except perhaps a shared preoccupation with minute and careful planning of the printed page. Some historians are persuaded that there would have been a great revival of printing in England at the end of the nineteenth century even without the Kelmscott Press; this overstates the case, since Morris went well beyond the achievements of artists like Beardsley and Whistler by reaffirming the primary importance of fine materials (paper, ink, bindings) and by asking searching questions about the structure of the book that never entered their minds. There is no doubt, however, that other views of bookmaking were clamouring to be heard at the time, and that in many instances people accepted only selected portions of the typographical gospel according to Morris. Bernard Shaw, for example, was strongly impressed by The House of the Wolfings and The Roots of the Mountains (Morris's two pre-Kelmscott Press experiments at the Chiswick Press), became a Caslon-addict, and for decades badgered his printers whenever they failed to produce the closely spaced, evenly textured page that Morris had advocated.²' Shaw never aspired to emulate the Kelmscott Press volumes, yet he had an intelligent grasp of Morris's typographical principles, which he attempted to folow while at the same tine ignoring the private mannerisms of Morris's own books.

Shaw's case exemplifies the difficulties in tracing the reputation and influence of the Kelmscott Press during the final decade of the century, for Morris's ideas (which seem distinctive enough in retrospect) about book-design mingled subtly and confusingly with the notions of other practitioners of bookmaking. Nor was there any unanimity of opinion about the Kelmscott Press, which evoked astonishingly varying responses from readers throughout the decade. The earliest comments on the Press that appeared in newspapers and magazines were on the whole very favourable, and even the printers' trade journals printed occasional enthusiastic notices about Morris's activities in Hammersmith. The death of Morris in 1896 and the closing of the Kelmscott Press in 1898 stimulated further public praise of his work as a printer. Herbert P. Horne offered a judicious and lengthy appraisal of Morris's achievement in the Saturday Review (24 October 1896), finding the key to his success in the ideal of good craftsmanship; the Library, in commenting on an exhibition of the Kelmscott Press books mounted at the British Museum in 1898, remarked that ´'the Kelmscott books may fitly take their place with those of Gutenberg and Schoeffer, of Zainer and Koberger, of Jenson and Wendelin of Speier, among the maserpi f th printer rA.W.Pollarrviig the m exhibition in the Guardian (8 June 1898), found encouraging evidence of the spread of Morris's ideals, for 'three great firms — the Chiswick Press, Messrs. Constable, and the Clarendon Press - have already begun to use richer founts of type than have been seen in English books since black-letter went out of fashion, and with admirable effect'. When Henry R. Plomer wrote A Short History of English Printing for the English Bookman's Library (190o), the terminal dat  his survy was, significantly, 1898, since he regarded the 'brilliant but short-lived career othe Kelscott Press theculminating episode in astory that had begun with Caxton in 1476.

Ye tho et s ucalleTM grew out of an interview with him entitledMaster Printer Morris" A Visit to the Kelmscott Preswhich was published in the Daily Chronicle on 22 February 1893; shortly thereafter the same newspaper printed a stinging reply called 'The Kelnscott Press: A Master Critic on a Master Printer'The 'Master Critic' was apparently Morris's old adversary, Joseph Pennel,and the article was reprinted and discussed in at least two printers'jourals.² It ertainly pulle o punce: the anonymous critic proclaimed that De Vinne in New York was printing finer books than Morris, assaulted the Troy and Chaucer types, and insisted that the Kelmscott volumes 'are filled with blemishes which would be ascribed to carelessness even in printers of lesser pretensions'.

![](images/137ce47d3a56f1c02f9115bc86d1576634e4fb80f5f860f1ef601c571c12da3c.jpg)  
Figure 91 By the end of the last century, Morris had become linked in the popular imagination with Gutenberg, as the façade of the Edward Everard Building in Bristol demonstrates.

The Kelmscott Press books did not again come under such strong attack until Morris's death, when Pennell once more made a brutal asault in the Daily Chronice, the British Printerrevived the old charge that Morris divided the lines of verse arbitrarily in his books, and the British and Colonial Printer and Stationer declared that the Kelmscott volumes were characterized by poor inking and register.²4 After the hyperbole lavished upon the Kelmscott Press in the preceding years, a reaction of this sort in 1896 was not unexpected. The Academy F12 December 1896) remarked that 'certain journalist writers' had used the occasion of Morris's death to 'air their opinions as to the superiority of the Continental and Transatlantic fashions of illustration over the style [of Morris] . . .; their opinions also as to the vast superiority of process blocks to hand engraving, machine-made papers and printing to handmade papers and hand-printing, and so on'.

Other indications of the Kelmscott Press's status were equally ambiguous. The market value of the Kelmscott books — a good rough index of the esteem in which they were held by collectors — rose steadily until late 1903, when their prices inexplicably dipped. 'The truth is, collecors have tired of [the Kelmsott volumes] andwill have o hemW.G.Mezi wrote n  Conseur My1905). That they may regain some of their lost prestige is possible, but that th able.' This prediction was wide of the mark, yet the pattern of prices is suggestive indeed. Within the printing trade there were also mixed responses to the Kelmscott experiment. Trade journals that had at first welce Morr attempt t private rintin radually re more hostile as his condemnations of commercial typography became more widely known through lectures and interviews. The Caslon typefoundry was reflecting a substantial body of opinion when it declared in 1897 that it could 'only condemn the fantastic types and methods adopted by Mr. Morris' John Southworth, in his book Moe i ecn  ha hKel s ht t n,  h t much likelihood that what they regard as its peculiar characteristics il

Yet printers continued to pay at least lip-service to Morris's high standards of craftsmanship even while they scorned his isolation from commercial realities. The Printing Number of The Times published on 10 September 1912, for example, though it was largely devoted to the burgeoning technology of the trade, prominently displayed a huge reproduction of a page of the Kelmscott Chaucer. An amusing example of this ambivalence can be seen on the cover of the Monotype Recorder of April 1913, which featured a facsimile of one of Morris's decorated initials; the Recorder had of course always been an enthusiastic advocate of increased mechanization, with early covers portraying a Monotype machine rising triumphantly into the sky above an old-fashioned compositor, often accompanied by such edifying slogans as 'The old order changeth, yielding place to new'.

Paradoxically, at the very time when Morris's reputation as a printer had declined, particularly in the printing trade, there were signs on every side of his influence making itself felt in more oblique ways. Caslon and Old Style type continued to gain ground in book-work, though Shaw, Walker, and the Chiswick Press as well as Morris's example contributed to the growing revival of old-face types. Founts based on the Golden type still proliferated in the first decade of this century. An examination of any representative group of English books published at that time reveals a slight but perceptible darkening of the page through the use of heavier faces and less leading. Volumes in Everyman's Library series, launched by Dent in 1905, displayed a Venetian typeface and ornaments that were intended to evoke memories of the Kelmscott Press. The Oxford University Press began issuing a handsome series of English classical texts in 1906, printed in the Fell types on pure linen rag paper; the Cambridge University Press a few years earlier acquired a new fount bearing a family resemblance to the Golden type which it used for printing limited editions of English classics.

But whereas the two university presses had special resources at their disposal, most printers before the First World War possessed unsatisfactory collections of typefaces. Harold Curwen, at the Curwen Press, was délighted when a shortage of lead for the munitions factories in 1915 enabled him to dispose profitably of vast quantities of types accumulated since the firm's founding: he cheerfully got rid of more than 2oo founts, retaining only Caslon Old Face, Monotype Old Style No. 2, and two other faces.²6 Few other proprietors were willing to adopt such a drastic solution, if only because Caslon still seemed unsuitable for many varieties of commercial printing. As Morris had seen so clearly in 1888, English book-design could not be rejuvenated until the tired nineteenth-century modern-face types were supplanted by new founts modelled on classic oldface types. The earliest really successful effort to do this in England — and simultaneously to make the face generally available on the Monotype machine — came when Gerard Meynell and several associates commissioned an altered version of Caslon, named after their journal in which it was first used, the Imprint, with the first issue appearing in January 1913. It is symptomatic that the Imprint, while saturated with the ideal of technological progress in printing, is also pervaded by the presence of Morris: the first article, 'Art and Workmanship'by W. R.Lethaby, quotes Morris in its second paragraph; the frontispiece of the Kelmscott Press A Dream of Johñ Ball is reproduced; an editorial note declares, 'The Kelmscott Press, the Doves Press, and the Ashendene Press have done a great deal towards bringing about a Renaissance of Printing, and though the commercial printer is prone to speak slightingly of private presses, much of the improvement in his own work, especially in the e

The Imprint survived for less than a year but is a harbinger of the resurgence of fine printing, by both private and commercial presses, that was to take place between the wars, a period that deserves to be regarded as the golden age of English printing. The transformation of presses, nearly all exhibiting Morrisian impulses, continued to pour forth handsome volumes of every variety, the renewal of printing now frequently took the form of books produced under normal trade conditions yet displaying a refinement of typography and illustration hitherto associated only with small, experimental presses. Morris's principles, ter lyigalmost drmant r twdecades, sudnly emerged during the 192os into the different world of commercial publishing, and were applied, with modifications, to large editions of machine-printed books. What complicates this pattern of influence is that the yraphicl neer the  coittd pin oy,al weucan kw ties to Morris or the private-press movement; they prided themselves on being forward-thinking men of a brave new century and professed scorn for the preciusess o Kelscott-sty ventures.

Uncertainty about Morris's position in the history of printing b h

# The Imprint

ART AND WORKMANSHIP: By ProfesSor W. R. LETHABY

W E have been in the habit of writing so lyrically of art and of the pement  st taveg n wo in the street, sometimes a very mean street, is likely to think of it as remote and luxurious, not " for the likes of him." There is the danger in habitual excess of language that the plain man is likely to be frightened by it. It may be feared that

between it and common lives.

A proper function of criticism should be to foster our national arts and not to frighten timid people off with high-pitched definitions and far-fetched metaphors mixed with a flood of (as Morris said) " sham technical twaddle." It is a pity to make a mystery of what should most easily be understood. There is nothing occult about the thought that all things may be made well or made ill. A work of art is a well-made thing, that is all. It may be a well-made statue or a well-made chair, or a well-made book. Art is not a special sauce applied to ordinary cooking ; it is the cooking itself if it is good. Most simply and generally art may be thought of as THE WELL-DOING OF WHAT NEEDS DOING. If the thing is not worth doing it can hardly be a work of art, however well it may be done. A thing worth doing which is ill done is hardly a thing at all.

Fortunately people are artists who know it not-bootmakers (the few left), gardeners and basketmakers, and all players of games. We do not allow shody in cricket or footbal, but reserve it or serious things like houses and books, furniture and funerals.

If it is necessary that everything must be translated into words, our art critics might occupy quite a useful place if they would be good enough to realise that behind the picture shows of the moment is the vast and important art of the country, the arts of the builder, furniture maker, printer and the rest, which are matters of national well-being.

It is doubtful if we have it in us to form a leading school of painting at B

Figure 92 The initial numbcr (January 1913) of the Imprint, a journal that signallcd thc beginning of the revival of commercial typography in twenticth-ccntury Britain. It is significant that thc first articlc is by W. R. Lcthaby, who was decply influenced by Morris's ideals, and contains a dircct refercnce to Morris in the second paragraph.

ghost was a common activity. The publication of Sparling's history of the Kelmscott Press in 1924 served as the occasion for much energetic debunking, as in this review in The Times Literary Supplemnent (22 January 1925): 'most students of typography will find this a profoundly depressing book. The book itself - the way in which it is printed — the Morris designs and the Burne-Jones illustrations — are all of a piece, and all have the pathos of dead enthusiasms. . . . It is certain that the reaction against Morris's canons of printing is not the outcome either of moral baseness or of our system of society; it is due to the discovery that in many things Morris was wrong — wrong as an antiquary, wrong as a.practitioner.' During the interwar period Holbrook Jackson, one of this century's most influential writers on printing history, kept up a series of attacks upon the Kelmscott Press in articles, lectures, andbooks:readinga paper at the Morris Centenary Dinner of the Double Crown Club on 2 May 1934, he seized the occasion to describe Morris's books as pretentious, ill conceived, and unreadable. They were, he said, 'museum pieces, typographical luminous wings in vain At the same dinner James Shand paidtrbue to 'Morris's magnificent technical achievements, yet found that he meant nothing to the printers of today' Paul Johnston's Biblio-typo graphical: A Survey of Contemporary Fine Printing Style (New York, 1930), began: 'The fallacy that William Morris is the patriarch of modern fine printing is as common as it is inaccurate.'

Naturally Morris had his defenders after the war, chief of whom was Bernard Newdigate, the accomplished printer at the Shakespeare Head Press, who wrote a regular colunn entitled 'Book Production Notes' for the London Mercury between 192o and 1937. Newdigate repeately remided his readers of Morris's achievement at the Kelms cot Press, as in this representativ passagefro a clumn  1934: A fw  c it    e n g o a printer, or, at best, to dann it with faint praise; and this notwithstanding that in the ghtandthirty years which have pasd since his death, no books have surpassed his in excellence and sheer beauty, and none have been printed anywhere save at the Doves and the Ashendene presses which are worthy to rank with them." Newdigate was one of several typographical enthusiasts — the others were Francis Meynell, Stanley Morison, Oliver Simon, and Holbrook Jackson - who met in London during 1922 in order to establish a publishing society that would issue one book a year, set and printed by mechanical means yet produced to the highest standards. The would-be founders immediately discovered themselves divided ideologically: 'There were disputes on the texts suggested and the manner of printing them and Newdigate was felt to have too strong a tendency towards the ideas and pracc Willia MorrS ecalledThe ocity disbanded before it was even organized. Though no more than a minor episode, the stillborn Fleuron Society represented emblematically the struggle between Morrisian handicraft methods and the modern mechanization of printing.

This was in fact the very issue that rendered Morris's accomplishments so problematic during the second and third decades of our century. Among the intelligentsia of the day there were urgent calls to harness the machine to aesthetic and humane ends, emphasizing the importance of design in industrial production, and Morris's Luddite tendencies now seemed distinctly embarrassing. In England the most eloquent expression of this viewpoint came from Aldous Huxley, who Whole arnies of William Morrises and Tolstoys could not now expel it. . .  The sensible thing to do is not to revolt against the inevitable, but to use and modify it, to make it serve your purposes. Machines exist; let us then exploit them to create beauty - a modern beauty, while we are about it. For we live in the twentieth century; let us frankly admit it and not pretend that we live in the fifteenth. . . . The printer who makes a fetish of hand-work and medieval craftsmanship, who refuses to tolerate the machine or to make any effort to improve the quality of its output, thereby condemns the ordinary reader to a perpetuity of ugly printing.Huxley thus transforms Morris's pro foundly moral rejection of industrialism into a conspiracy against the reader of modest means; the same point was often made during Morris's lifetime (Talbot Baines Reed, for example, said something very like this in a letter to Emery Walker written in 18883²), yet Huxley's polemic is striking both because of its cleverness and because of its assumption that the benefits of mechanization and mass-production are so self-evident that they no longer require defence.

Even those whose fundamental attitudes were close to Morris's acknowledged the supremacy of the machine in the contemporary world. Eric Gills brilliant but eccentric Essay on Typography (1931) illuse how Morrisn and Ruskinian) ieas could, with the passing of time, be metamorphosed into mysterious new shapes. Gill, a master of reductio ad absurdum, argues that, since industrialism and handicrafts represent two separate worlds of human experience, machine-made goods should be plain and simple, not imitations of craft artefacts. Once we have decided to nake the machine our master, we must learn to accept the only kind of beauty it can create. On the Continent the Bauhaus movement had been advancing the same case, but what sets his reasoning apart from theirs is that it is coloured by intense hatred of our industrial civilization. When one probes beneath the surface of Gill's writings, his unlikely advocacy of functionalism proves to be not very different from the position of Morris, whose medievalism Gill claimed to despise.33

Morris's understanding of machinery was certainly more complex than Huxley's caricature of him as a regressive escapist might suggest. The confusion about his perspective was conpounded by the simpleminded propaganda of some of his disciples, particularly Sparling, who offered the world a one-dimensional version of Morris's ideas on handicrafts: Sparling could not even bring himself to admit that an Albion press is a machine.3 When the restoration of Morris's reputation came about in the following decades, therefore, it signalled a growing realization that Morris had anticipated many twentiethcentury notions of functionality and faithfulness to the nature of materials. The fullest exposition of this revisionist view of Morris is to be found in Pevsner's Pioneers of the Modern Movement (1936), which was retitled in the second edition Pioneers of Modern Design: From William Mor Wal Gos; e an  he e pr g t terms with Morris in the public and private pronouncements of Stanley Morison, who was largely responsible for the important programme hc tye evia t honoty CornMoro li many of his contemporaries, viewed Morris's printing with distrust and delivered a steady stream of criticism of it during the 192os and 1930s. In a 1944 lecture delivered in Edinburgh (published in Morison's The Typographic Arts: Two Lectures, 1950), however, he adopts a more Pevsnerian view of Morris, arguing that he was not really opposed to the machine-made book. Morison is able to make a persuasive case because Morris docs indeed refer occasionally in his ph to commercially produced books. Yet to make Morris a cryptomodernist who had the misfortune to be born in the nineteenth century, as both Pevsner and Morison imply, is a form of special pleading that brings us o closer to the core  Morris's ideas than do Sparling's purple passages attacking machinery.

Another issue on which Morris was seen to be in the wrong was the ornamentation of books; one of the most widespread manifestations of anti-Victorian sentiment in the 192os was the conviction that ornament, especially of the historicist kind, was morally and aesthetically inappropriate for a machine-made product. The application of this doctrine to book-design led to what one French critic later described, iiffext,yaeiz ical fear of any decoration of the text. Holbrook Jackson offered a trenchant summary of the doctrine in 1924: 'Decadent printing is always ornamental printing. When typographical imagination fails it becomes decorative.'"6 It was the Bauhaus school that adopted the idea most zealously, eschewing all ornamental patterns except for the functionalist structure of blocks of plain sanserif type. In England the hostility to ornament rarely reached such a pitch; even Morison, who espoused a different variety of typographical austerity, admitted that the taste for ornament is an essential human need.37 So long as ornament was seen as a symptom of moral weakness, however, it was impossible for Morris's contributions to fine printing to be acknowledged fully.

Whereas Morrs's contemporaries had been startled by his rejection of typographical orthodoxies, in the early decades of this century the Kelmscott books were seen as the final, overripe expression of Victorian ornamental eclecticism. Under these circumstances, how can one claim any connection at all between Morris's theories of bookdesign and the renewal of English printing that took place after 1918? There is indeed a direct line of descent from the Kelmscott Press to the 192os and 1939s, but, because Gill's and Morison's generation found it psychologically impossible to acknowledge the ancestry of their typographical views, the line is not always easy to trace; we can glimpse the connection, however, by looking briefly at the careers of two men who, with Emery Walker, were largely responsible for bringing the Morrisian vision of the Ideal Book into the market-place.

J. H. Mason, an admirer of Morris and compositor at the Doves Press, began teaching evening classes in printing at London's Central School of Arts and Crafts in 19o5, and four years later, on the recommendation of Walker and Cobden-Sanderson, he was appointed the superintendent of the Printing School.³\* In that position he was responsible for training many of the next generation of British printers. The typographical style that he imparted to his students was a simple adaptation of the Kelmscott/Doves manner: closely spaced

Caslon Old Face (almost the only type of which he approved) printed on handmade, dampened paper on an Albion press. Mason was aware that such conditions could not be duplicated in most twentiethcentury printing firms, but he argued that, in order to operate complex modern machinery, a young printer first had to master the fundamental elemnents of his craft - a principle of which Morris would surely not have disapproved.

Leonard Jay, who enrolled as Mason's first pupil and in 1912 became his assistant at the Central School, eventually grew restless under Mason's regime of Morrisian orthodoxy and in 1925 left to become head of the School of Printing in Birmingham. There he continued his master's tradition of exacting craftsmanship and careful design, but his students in Birmingham were also encouraged to study advertising typography and to make use of the Linotype and Monotype machines. Jay regarded these innovations not as a lapse from the purity of the Kelmscott/Doves faith but as an attempt to prepare students for a world in which handicraft methods no longer prevailed. In a lecture delivered in 1929, Jay declared, 'The product of machinery can only be improved when its producers are imbued with the same spirit which produced fine work in the past, and every effort should be directed towards breathing life and beauty into the products of machinery. This is a vital necessity in this mechanical age; for the more machinery that is introduced into the printing trade the greater the need for more alert, intelligent, and enlightened craftsmen. Perfect machinery is available everywhere; our saving grace lies in better quality of production.'39 In the differences between Mason and Jay one sees the process by which Morris's ideas were adapted to mechanized printing and stripped of their Ruskinian implications. Jay and his students were just as fastidious in their search for high standards of craftsmanship as Morris was, and they nearly always followed his advice on such crucial matters as wordsng, leding, ndmains; but Morrir  thedehuzig effects of machinery upon workers is no longer in evidence.

Francis Meynell's highly successful Nonesuch Press is another example of how the Morrisian principle of hand-production could be gradually assimilated to more modern methods, though Meynell, unlike Mason and Jay, thought that Morris was 'something of a calaity' to the development of printing Meynell recognized the relationship between the Nonesuch Press and Jay's work in Birmingham, because in both instances there was an abandonment of the handicraft ideal, accompanid by an biding beliin the imporance of painstaking design. In 1930 Meynell wrote, 'My previous experience had shown me quite clearly that, in order to avoid monotony and to produce desirablé editions at a reasonable cost, one must intelligently exploit the best mechanical equipment and the highest technical skill available. To-day there is more fine typographical material to be had than even the largest printing house in the country could possess; and the various "mercenary" presses have developed technical skill and variety along various lines. There was therefore no good reason, we thought, for a new private press in the old style, arrogantly selfcontained, and with but one type and obsolete "hand"' machinery. . . . In this faith we began to mobilise other people's resources; to be designers, specifiers, rather than manufacturers; architects of books rather than builders. In this faith we proceed to compel the fully mechanical processes to serve for the first time the end of Fine Book making.'41

Though this stridently anti-Morris blast sounds like a complete rejection of all that the Kelmscott Press stood for, in reality Morris's principles of book-design had simply been detached from their original intellectual context and were now being claimed as their own by a new generation which believed that machinery could be tamed and civilized.

##

Morris's impact on printing and book-design outside England was, for at least a decade or two, unparalleled, with the force of his influence being felt most dramatically in northern Europe and English-speaking countries. In the United States imitating the Kelmscott books became a temporary craze towards the end of the century, as Professor Susan Thompson has shown in her exhaustive study American Book Design and William Morris (New York, 1977). Roberts Brothers of Boston published a pirated facsimile of The Story of the Glittering Plain a few months after it was issued by the Kelmscott Press in 1891, and within a few years the New York periodical the Bookman (November 1896) was complaining that 'Kelmscott—Jenson type, more or less accurately reproduced, has spread from Portland to San Francisco, and seems especially dear to the heart of advertisers of bicycles and malt extracts'. The more responsible American printers and book-designers were alarmed by this commercial degradation of Morris's ideals. 'Everyone who cared for William Morris must regret the extent to which his name and work are being used in the matter of printing,' wrote

Theodore L. De Vinne. 'Tasteless imitations of his types and ornaments threaten us on every side, and no one seems able to start a new press or get some type without taking Mr. Morris's name in vain.'42 Bruce Rogers, looking back on the phenomenon in 1912, recalled that 'for a period of ten years the work of William Morris had an enormous influence on American printing - and not, on the whole, a beneficial one, except in its insistence upon sound craftsmanship'.43

The Kelmscott style in the United States was initially promulgated by a handful of periodicals and publishers of anglophile or Arts and Crafts tendencies. The most ambitious of the periodicals was the Knight-Errant of Boston, which survived for only four issues during 1892-3; set in Old Style type on handmade paper and ornamented with initials and tailpieces designed by Bertram Grosvenor Goodhue, the Kuight-Erraut was self-consciously Morrisian and aesthetic. In the July 1892 issue an essay by Francis Watts Lee, 'Some Thoughts Upon Beauty in Typography, Suggested by the Work of Mr. William Morris at the Kelmscott Press', provided facsimiles of three Kelmscott pages and the printer's mark designed by Morris, and described his books as 'the crowning glory of the nineteenth century' Another Boston periodical ws Mode Art, quarterly published beween 1893 and 1897. Its design, though more satisfactory than that of the Knight-Errant, was equally Morrisian (Kelmscott-style borders and initials, a replica of the Golden type, shoulder titles and notes in red), and it had the distinction of publishing 'A Note by William Morris on His Aims in Founding the Kelmscott Press' an essay reprintein the last Kelms cott book.A considerably less imposing journal was the Craftsman the first issue of which (October 190 1) was devoted entirely to Morris, with vaguely Morrisian borders and indifferent typography.

The publishing houses that fell under Morris's sway in the 1890s displayed his influence at a relatively sophisticated level, since they were responding to a variety of styles in book-design that floated across the Atlantic. Copeland and Day of Boston, for example, occasionally imitated the Kelmscott manner, most conspicuously in their edition of Rossetti's House of Life (1894), but many of their books possessed a strongly aesthetic and decadent quality reminiscent of John Lane's characteristic style. W. Irving Way of Way and Williams (Chicago) made a pilgrimage to Hammersmith in the spring of 1895 and persuaded Morris to print Hand & Soul, the only Kelmscott book copublished in the United States (though Copeland and Day nearly

Figue 9 The Boston magazine Knight-Errant gave clear evidence obeing under Morris's spell in 1892. Pages of some of the Kelmscott books were reproduced in it for the edification of American readers.

Copeland and Day, Way and Williams, and Mosher were all reputable publishers (Mosher, known as 'the moral pirate', actually sent royalties to English authors) who responded briefly and with restraint to the enthusiasm for Kelmscott-style typography in the United States; to see the Morrismania in a less inhibited form, we must turn to Elbert Hubbard, who, after meeting Morris, created the Roycrofters, a self-described crafts community, in East Aurora, N.Y. ('Take the train for East Aurory | Where we work for Art and Glory' was one of Hubbard's many slogans.) There the 'P. T. Barnum of art', as one newspaper justly called him, built the Roycroft Inn for visitors (with one of the rooms named after Morris), a printing plant, a book bindery, and a chapel, ll in a Ye Olde Worlde fashion. The Morrisian doctrine of handicrafts was oddly blended with inspirational capitalist sentiments ("I am not in the business of defaming America . . . I believe in big business and more of it,' Hubbard announced45 ), and at annual conventions in East Aurora the numerous disciples of 'Fra Elbertus' listened to band concerts on the lawn and pondered such themes as 'HEALTH, HAPPINESS, TRUTH'. May Morris, visiting Buffalo in 19o4, was handed an invitation from Hubbard to visit the Roycrofters; angrily crumbling the letter in her hand, she replied, 'I most certainly will not go to East Aurora, nor do I have any desire to see that obnoxious imitator of my dear father.'46

Hubbard, who had called at Kelmscott House a second time in June 1896 when Morris was too ill to see him, always regarded himself as Morr's chieAmerican isciple.The sentiental,slindulgent tone of the Roycroft propaganda is well represented by this typical effusion from Hubbard's son after his father's death: 'When the Roycroft workers have departed and the still serenity of deep night has settled down like a blanket upon the shops, the kindred spirits of William Morris and Elbert Hubbard often glide noiselessly through the print shop, the chapel and the book-bindery to learn how we, their earthbound successors, are carrying on with the heritage of fine craftsmanship which they have left to us.'47

The books offered a bastardized rendition of the Kelmscott style, with coarse paper, Morris-like ornaments, incongruous halftone illustrations, abundant red ink, conventional typefaces, and limp chamois covers. "Yet, unappealing as the Roycroft volumes may seem to us todáy, they had a strong influence on some young American booklovers such as Dard Hunter and Will Ransom. Through the improbable medium of Hubbard's tasteless imitations, the Morrisian idea of fine printng was tanslate into veacularasiy rasped by amass audience. 'That is how the tradition first came to America,' wrote Ransom, 'was expressed in terms of the national characteristic, publicity, by a master of that delicate art, and set many of us on the road to our own goal.'49

The usual superficial indications of Morris's presence in American book-design at the turn of the century, apart from a predilection for the ATF Jenson type, were title-pages surrounded by dark Kelmscottlike borders, often printed separately on coated paper and merely inserted in a book that had been produced earlier. Such affectations do not suggest a deep understanding of Morris's views on bookmaking, and, if we wish to assess his more permanent contribution to American typography, we must consider his influence on the most accomplished printers and designers of the United States. To cite only a few random examples, Frederic Goudy, the type-designer, bought Morris's Chaucer press, issued Morris and Walker's essay 'Printing' as the first publication of the Village Press, and made his Village type remarkably similar to the Golden fount; John Henry Nash, the celebrated California printer, commissioned a portrait of Morris for his library; Carl Purington Rollins, for many years the director of the Yale University Press, began collecting Kelmscott books when still a young man and,

One can trace this evolution of attitudes in the lives of Daniel Berkeley Updike and Bruce Rogers, the most distinguished printer and book-designer respectively that the United States has yet produced. The first ambitious book undertaken by Updike during the 189os was the Altar Book for the Episcopal Church, and from the beginning it was conceived as a homage to the Kelmscott Press. In August 1893 Updike wrote to Morris, "I am engaged in the printing of a devotional book which I sincerely desire to make the most perfect piece of printing that is possible. And to this end I take the liberty of addressing you to know if through you, I can get a Roman type for the printing of it that has enough blackness and strength to allow the use of decoration of a solid yet brilliant kind.' Would Morris sell him founts of the Golden type or allow it to be recut in Great Primer size?"1 It is not known whether Morris replied to this request, and if he did his answer was no doubt negative. Updike therefore turned to the unauthorized adaptation of the Golden type produced at the Dickinson Type Foundary in Boston but decided not to use it when it was supplied to another printing house. The only remaining solution was to commission his own design, a task put into the hands of Bertram Goodhue. The Altar Book, published in 1896, was perhaps the most stupendous tribute to the influence of the Kelmscott Press ever published in the United States (or in any other country): an enormous folio, it was embellished with ornaments by Goodhue and illustrations by Robert Anning Bell (an Englishman) and was printed by De Vinne in New York.

This remarkable pastiche signals the high-water mark of Morrisian enthusiasm in both American book-design and Updike's career, for the majority of the early books printed at his Merrymount Press in Boston are set in Caslon and resemble the exercises in puritanical severity produced by Mason's students at the Central School of Arts and Crafts. Updike share his dislike of machinery. He did not hesitate to install cylinder presses, though he operated them at slower speeds than most printers did, and he was an advocate of Monotype composition, especially after more attractive typefaces became available from Monotype in the 192os. Updike, in short, moved from a youthful phase of uncriticaladulation of Morris to a more judicious, mature appreciation of his achievement. Updike in later years criticized Morris's types and his failure to follow always his own typographical principles, but the American never abandoned his conviction that Morris 'was a great printer because he was a great man who printed greatly, as he did much elsee'2

Never as bewitched by Morris as Updike was, Bruce Rogers none the lesdiscovered his vocation as a book-desiger when J. M. Bowles, the founder of Modern Art, showed him several Kelmscott volumes. Only in one of Rogers's youthful productions, R. B. Gruelle's Notes: Critical and Biographical.Collection of W. T.Walters, published by Bowles in 1895, did he attempt a frank imitation of the Kelmscott about Morris's books. Though he remained primarily a 'limited edition' designer, Rogers faced directly (and solved) many typographical problems — such as the designing of footnotes and indexes that Morris, with his disdain for scholarly apparatus, never had to confront, and he went beyond Morris also in his ready acceptance of mechanical typesetting equipment and power presses. Rogers's work occasionally rather reminds one of Francis Meynell's books at the Nonesuch Press, for in both instances it is the sheer versatility and inventiveness of the designer that astonishes. And, as in Meynell's case, the ipac  Morri is etie iffult oc n theit surface of Rogers's books, yet, as Beatrice Warde suggested, we can discover just beneath that surface the surprising truth that Rogers mnl hivi Fhhe books, an somehow o be the frst to bring it down t arth' a phrase which adroitly summarizes the transformation of Morrisian principles in both the United States and England during the first half of the twentieth century.

In Germany there was rapid recognition of the importance of the Kelmscott Press, which inspired a flourishing private-press movement in the first decade of the new century. As early as 1898, when the Press closed, an account of Morris and his ãccomplishments as a printer was published in Zeitschrift fiir Biicherfreumde which included numerous cts.T German interest in the Kelmscott Press was Weimar, where in May 1905 there was an exhibition of the Kelmscott and Doves books and where Count Kessler, an admirer of the English private presses, set up one of his own, the Cranach-Presse. J. H. Mason was brought over to itruct the German compositors; he service  Edward Johnstn were enlisted; Emery Walker gave advice and assistance. A few years earlier Walker, Johnston, and Eric Gill also contributed their skills to Ge la  I VerL commercial publisher. The Morrisian influence, as mediated through his assciate and discples, was sronginGermay unil 914,an the war, in a backward fow of ideas, some English printers received their frst exposure to Morris's typographical principles through German books.

Other European countries also reacted enthusiastically to the Kelmscott ress booksSwitzerland, at this tmeunder German culturalway, tended to reflect the typographical fashions of its larger neighbour. In 1894 visitors to the 'Libre Esthétique' exhibition in Brussels were able to see the Kelmscott books assembled for the first time, and a correspondent for the Studio (April 1894) reported: 'It is with the most lively admiration that connoisseurs are arrested before the books of William Morris — The Defence of Guenevere, The History of Troy, and A Dream of John Ball, of which the frontispiece has been designed with such distinction by Sir Edward Burne-Jones. One recognizes in them the perfection of quality; and the first appearance of these works of the Kelmscott Press before the Brussels public, one can say, has been a real triumph.' For the opening of a similar exhibition in Antwerp the same year, Emmanuel de Bom delivered a lecture on Morris and his influence on typography which was later published. On the Continent, however, the art-nouveau influence was strong, and at that distance from England both readers and book-designers found it difficult to distinguish between the rival styles; thus Henry Van de Velde, the most influential figure in Belgian book-production in the early years of the twentieth century, may seem a very Morrisian figure, printing in Caslon on a hand-press and designing all his own ornaments, but the latter prove, on closer inspection, to be more in the manner of Beardsley than of Morris. In the Netherlands there was also a Morris-like personality, Dr P. J. H. Cuypers, an ardent medievalist architect, not to mention a book-designer of thé first rank, S. H. de Roos, whose earliest volume was a translation of some Morris essays (1903).

Morrisian ideas were spread in Scandinavia primarily by a few printers such as Waldemar Zachrisson in Sweden and Simon Bernsteen in Denmark. Frederik Hendrikson, a Dane, had even anticipated Morris by writing an article on the deficiencies of modern book-design for the newspaper Politiken in 1884, and in 1888 he founded Forening for Boghaandvaerk (the Danish Society for Book Craft). But if northern Europe was receptive to Morris's theories, this was not the case in France and Italy, both of which were dominated by strong native traditions of typography and illustration. The French especially had their own views on the Ideal Book, and their ideal bore scarcely any resemblance to Morris's. French bibliophiles, moreover, were so convinced of the superiority of their own éditions de luxe that they were not at all receptive to an Anglo-Saxon standard. Octave Uzanne, in Nos amis les livres (Paris, 1886), declared unhesitatingly: 'The art of producing beautiful books is the only one that the foreigners have not been able to initate. Essentially it is a purely French art and one could parody Villon and say:"Inst bon livre que de Paris!"Pritig, illustration, stitching, binding make us the absolute superior of all others in Europe or America, and this applies to everything, not only to the talent of ourartists, but to the eye and perfect taste of our main publishers.'ss In Italy the only minor evidences of Morris's influence are some volumes published by the Fratelli Treves of Milan early in the century in an imitation of the Golden type, and a temporary revival of darker typefaces and wood-engraved title-pages.

##

A. W. Pollard, long an astute observer of the contemporary printing scene as well as a bibliographer of early English books, in 1934 offered this summary of the meaning of Morris's typographical adventure: The beauty of all these books [by the Doves, Vale, and Ashendene presses and Robert Proctor] reinforced the influenceof the Kelmscott Press ones, by proving that what Morris had done on his own lines cold be done by lesser men with the rtios ggeted by their individual tastes. They reinforced also the proof which Morris had given that solong as it i regarded as a hobby or in a commercial ouse as an advertisement) the production of really fine specimens of printing is not an impossibly expensive one. Morris made no profit from the Kelmscott books as a publisher. But he proved the when print and paper were the best which money could buy.'" '56

Morris's discovery ofa market for limited-edition books was indeed significant, and his customers were for the most part the same middleclass bibliophiles who were caught up in the first-edition mania fostered at the end of the nineteenth century by T. J. Wise and Harry Buxton Forman; it is no accident that thefirst (and only) full-length bibliographical account of Morris's books was written by Forman. In o  ia  v nd V rur to be demonstrated that there was a substantial number ofbook-lovers who were prepared to purchase handsomely printed volumes. The success of the Kelmscott Press proved this was so. At the same time Morrissupplied an ideology, rooted in Ruskinian thought and the Arts and Crafts movement, which justified the hand-labour required by such undertakings.

The nature of Morris's contribution to modern book-design may iu   a nearly all twentieth-century private presses that have emphasized fine printing. Naturally these presses have evolved in various ways, and few of them today are similar in every respect to the Kelmscott or Doves model. After the economic collapse of 1929 the bibliophilic market shrank dramatically, making it unthinkable to produce works on the scale of the Kelmscott Chaucer, the Doves Bible, or the Ashendene Dante. Many of the private presses in Britain and the United States in the period following the Second World War embodied, in fact, a temporary reversion to the Daniel ideal of the hobbyist printer distributing slender little volumes to his family and friends. But that pattern has changed yet again in recent decades, and today the private presses seem to be prospering once more, flourishing with amazing vitality in such centres as Leicester, Iowa City, and San Francisco.

One major difference between the earlier Kelmscott-type presses and contemporary private presses, however, is that the latter are less likely to have any direct influence on the world of commercial printing. The reason for this appears to be that their respective printing technologies have diverged so abruptly in the last two or three decades that they no longer have much in common except certain problems of designing. The hand-press methods still used by the majority of private printers and the automated realm of offset presses and computerized typesetting simply represent two separate worlds, and, despite occasional attempts to bridge the gap - such as the University of California's admirable lithographic reprints of private-press books i the Ari ess Moby icn he eyal lic l it seems unlikely that any private press in the future will ever have the impact on printing and publishing at large that the Kelmscott and Doves Presses and their ilk once did. This is an unhappy development, since there is unnistakable evidence of deteriorating typographical standards at the moment as a result of the new technologies.

Except for Blake, who rejected letterpress printing altogether, Morris was the first English artist of major stature who succeeded in controlling every aspect of the printing of a book from types to bindings. He did so not only because he had a passion for thoroughness but also because it seemed to him the only way to achieve that unity of effect which he and Walker regarded as the primary characteristic of fine printing. Two centuries earlier Joseph Moxon, in his Mechanick Exercises on the Whole Art of Printing, had offered a definition  often since quoted - of the typographer (or, as we might say today, the book-designer) as 'such a one, who by his own Judgement, from solid reasoning with hinself, can either perform, or direct others to perform from the beginning to the end, all the Handy-works and Physical Operations relating to Typographie'.7 With increasing automation and specialization in modern printing, there has been an ever greater need for one man or wonan to orchestrate all the multitudinous activities now required to produce a book: Morris, it might be argued, invented (or reinvented, if we take Moxon into account) the role of book-designer.

Yet such a claim on his behalf has troubling implications, because it suggests an acceptance of the division of labour which Morris, as a disciple of Ruskin, found abhorrent. Peter Floud, in a talk on Morris delivered on BBC radio in 1954 and later printed in the Listener, made the point that 'though Morris's designs [for fabrics and wallpapers] were certainly executed by hand, they are not examples of individual craftwork, but rather what one can call serial hand-production on a factory basis, which makes full use of the division oflabour and differs very little in its moral and social implications from straightforward machine-productionFloud may have put his ase  stron n guage, but there can be no doubt that he has discovered the essential discrepancy between Morris's theory and practice. A similar analysis of the Kelmscott Press would show that Morris's printers were compelled to follow the typographical patterns and designs he devisedin his study at Kelmscott House rather than to express the roughness and majesty of their own souls (as Ruskin phrased it in Th Nature of Gothic). On the rare occasions when they were allowed complete fm spicuously hideous like the Wayzegoose menus.

But it is almost too easy to point out the inconsistencies of Morris's systemoideas. Iwfind Morris thethinkeriating, we can o wha Pevsner, Morison, and others have done: ignore the intellectual foundations of his medievalism and concentrate instead on Morris the precursor of twentieth-century functionalism. But to do so is surely to ignore the richness and complexity of the man and to isolate only a sal ar ismin that hape  s roast. In hed the Kelmscott Press is important to us for reasons that go beyond the technical and aesthetic innovations described in earlier chapters of this book. The Press still fascinates us today because of the profound questions that Morris posed about the triumph of the machine.

Morris saw that more was at stake than merely the revival of attractive printing, and it is the reverberation of his ideas, quite apart from whether one is personally drawn to the Kelmscott Press books, that makes the books so extraordinarily compelling in their appeal. Although he was unable to foresee the specific technology of the next century  films, radio, television, video cassettes, computers — Morris once expressed to Burne-Jones, in a characteristically jocular way, a dark vision of the future of the printed book in modern civilization: 'he railed fiercely against the invention of printing and gave it a hundred years at most to come to an end. Already said he the magazines are driving books out of the field - presently newspapers will have killed magazines — then the telephone will come — bottles of talk  & the newspapers will be ended, & thats a comfort — as a will do it said he. . . .'59

The Kelmscott books are imperfect creations, produced by occasionally uneasy compromises with modern technology and often flawed by Morris's hatred of all post-medieval thought and art. But from our point of view today, a century later, they represent a logical and, on the whole, satisfying culmination of the art of fine printing that began with Gutenberg. Of course Morris was wrong in a literal sense: books that are in themselves works ofart are still being printed and will continue to be printed in the future. But Morris's astonishingly accurate prediction of the death of the book as a central cultural artefact in the twentieth century, with moral and social consequences we cannot yet gauge, makes the Kelmscott Press seem, more and more, like the end rather than the beginning of something good.

HE following list includes all the books printed at the Kelmscott Press but not the ephemera such as advertising circulars. My A Bibliography of the Kelmscott Press (Oxford, 1984) supplies much more detailed descriptions of each of the books.

The books were all produced in one of several buildings in the Upper Mall, Ham-

mersmith, except for Gotlic Architecture (no. 18), which was printed during the Arts and Crafts Exhibition at the New Gallery, London. Unless otherwise indicated, the books are printed in black ink only. The two sizes of Flower paper (16 × 1 1 inches and 16 × 22 inches) are designated Flower (I) and Flower (2) respectively. I have made no attempt to describe the initials, borders, and engraved title-pages in this brief list; suffice it to say that every Kelmscott book was decorated with wood-engraved ornaments designed by Morris, some much more fully than others.

# APPENDIX B \* LECTURE BY EMERY WALKER

![](images/0ba814a708878f23049e6d7d078b1a97ed39bf3f1333ffd0675a4f3e3f537b95.jpg)

HE lecture by Emery Walker on 'Letterpress Printing and Illustration', delivered on 15 November 1888 to the Arts and Crafts Society, was a turning-point in the history of modern printing, for it inspired William Morris to design his own typeface and to establish the Kelmscott Press. Since Walker spoke (rather nervously, according to Morris)

from notes, the lecture was never published in the form in which it was delivered; yet, because it had such far-reaching consequences, I have made an attempt here to reproduce the substance of the lecture. There are three important sources of information: Walker's notes (in the Humanities Research Center, University of Texas); Oscar Wilde's (unsigned) article, 'Printing and Printers', Pall Mall Gazette, 16 November 1888, p. 5; and The Art of Printing. . . A Lecture by Emery Walker' Britis and Colonial Printer and Statier, 6 February 1890, p. 94, which reports a slightly different version of the talk, delivered at the City of London College in 189o. Of these, the notes and Wilde's article are more significant, and I have therefore transcribed them in their entirety, but I have also made use of the article in the British and Colonial Printer and Stationer (which is more detailed but filled with inaccuracies) to clarify certain doubtful allusions. Two other reports of essentially the same lecture by Walker 1896, pp. 1011, and 3 1 March 1898, pp. 2056, both accompanied by illustrations based upon Walker's slides. In the later variants of the lecture Walker concluded by showing pages fron the Kelmscott Press books. An important recent study of the lecture is John Dreyfus, 'Emery Walker's 1888 Lecture on "Letterpress Printing": A Reconstruction and a Reconsideration', Craft History, 1(1988), 11830.

Since Walker used these lecture notes on more than one occasion, it is not always possible to distinguish precisely between the original text and later additions to it. The inconsistencies in numbering the slides reflect these revisions: only Walker's final renumberings have been transcribed here. Any necessary annotations of the two texts have been placed within square brackets; Walker's own square brackets in his notes are reproduced as arrow brackets.

##

## Walker's lecture notes

Although, I suppose one may not unreasonably think that every person here, has at least some idea, of the methods by w[hi]ch books are made, I hope I may be pardoned, if for the sake of impressing the essentials of the various processes on your minds, I, with the assistance of my friend here, very briefly demonstrate the rudiments of the art. Type and paper, may be said to be to a printed book, what stones or which there can be no book in the one case, and no architecture, in the other.

I do not want to dwell on the purely historical aspect longer than I canhel, but i is ey  y n what wymo, that i, type printing differs from the method which has been practised in China for centuries. Shortly it is the use of moveable letters instead of engraved blocks, and as Mr. Talbot Reed [inserted in pencil: whose book is certainly the best historical work in the language] has said, the man who invented typography, was doubtless he, who invented moveable type.

<Here show punch, matrix, adjustable mould and type, and Japanese wood block.>

<metal composed of lead[,] tin and antimony> Early printers cast their own type

Describe and names [of] various parts of the type, face, serif, feet[] shoulder[,] nick. Now generally cast by machines when large quantities are wanted

Compositor[']s Frame[.] Upper and Lower Case arrangement of letters[:] 'e'in centre of the lower case.

Composing stick. Galley. chase.

You will see that the labour of casting and 'setting up' is precisely the same whether the type is as beautiful as that of the early printers or as ugly as the latest American importation.

The earliest punch cutters naturally copied the current handwriting of the country in which they lived, and in the specimens which will now be shown, enlarged on the screen, we shall see how close the resemblance is to the written books

[Inserted in pencil:  Block book ars Moriendi.]

First edition of Bible known to Bibliographers as the 42 linel,] usually called the Mazarin or Gutenberg's[] printed at Mentz

23 [Added in pencil: House of the Wolfings]

Black letter often used in titles. It shouldn't be mixed with roman. margins. Inner margin as small as possible so that it will allow the printed page to lie flat when the book is opened. Top margin may be the same width as inner margin. Fore-edge and tail large — especially tail[.] Reasons: — that the wear comes at bottom left hand corner — and the binder might cut fore-edge and tail but can't cut inner margin. When a book is opened in w[hi]ch margins have been well arranged the two pages are seen in the middle.

Headlines may be left out; where a short running abstract is used by authors this might be set [deleted: as a marginal note] in a small square in margin.

Paper. Type looks best when printed upon a moderately smooth Hand made paper which has been damped. If a machine made paper is used it should not be too smooth. Highly glazed paper very objectionable in appearance. Fashion of retaining rough edges objectionable on machine made paper. [Added in margin: Sizes of paper & books. Foolscap & post. water marks. laid & wove paper.]

Press Work[.] Type should not merely be brought in contact with pape[] should be [deletd: driven] well mpressd into the paper and should be quite black not grey.

and block with it. Show mechanical relation.[]

Show American Wood cut full of admirable technical qualities — chiaroscuro. pull it on hand-made paper. Demonstrate want of mechanical relation.
