##

## Oscar Wilde's article

Nothing could have been better than Mr. Emery Walker's lecture on Letterpress, Printing, and Ilustration, delivered last night at the Arts and Crafts. A series of most interesting specimens of old printed books and manuscripts were displayed on the screen by means of the magiclantern, and Mr. Walker's explanations were as clear and simple as his suggestions were admirable. He began by explaining the different kinds of type, and how they are made, and showed specimens of the old block-printing which preceded the moveable type and which is still used in China. He pointed out the intimate connection between printing and handwriting - as long as the latter was good the printers had a living model to go by, but when it decayed printing decayed also. He showed in succession on the screen a page from Gutenberg's Bible, (the first printed book — date about 1450-5) and a manuscript of Colunella; a printed Livy of 1469, with the abbreviations of handwriting, and a manuscript of the History of Pompeius by Justin of 145 1. The latter he regarded as an example of the beginning of Roman type. The resemblance between the manuscripts and the printed books was most curious and suggestive. He then showed a page out of John of Spier's edition of Cicero's Letters, the first book printed at Venice, an edition of the same book by Nicholas Jansen [Jenson] 1470, and a wonderful manuscript Petrarch of the 16th century. He told the audience about Aldus who was the first publisher to start cheap books, who dropped abbreviations, and had his type cut by Francia pictor et aurifex, who was said to have taken it from Petrarch's handwriting. He exhibited a page of the copy-book of Vicentino the great Venetian writing master which was greeted with a spontaneous round of applause, and made some excellent suggestions about improving modern copy-books and avoiding slanting writing. A superb Plautus printed at Florence in 1514 for Lorenzo di Medici, Polydore Virgil's History with the fine Holbein designs, printed at Basle in I556, and other interesting books were also exhibited on the screen, the size of course being very much enlarged. He spoke of Elzevir in the seventeenth century, when handwriting began to fall off, and of the English printer Caslam [Caslon], and of Baskerville, whose type was possibly designed by Hogarth, but is not very good. Latin, he remarked, was a better language to print than English, as the tails of the letters did not so often fall below the line. The wide-spacing between lines occasioned by the use of a lead, he pointed out, left the page in stripes, and made the blanks as important as the lines. Margins should of course be made, except the inner margins, and head lines often robbed the page ofits beauty ofdesign. The type used by the Pall Mall [Old Style] was, we are glad to say, rightly approved of.

With regard to illustration, the essential thing, Mr. Walker said, is to have harmony between the type and the decoration. He pleaded for true book-ornament as opposed to the silly habit of putting pictures w    h a rr  hanhanN should be used in a book that cannot be printed in the same way as the type. For his warnings he produced Roger's [sic] 'Italy' with a steelplate engraving, and a page from an American magazine which being forid, pictorial, and bad, was greeted with some laughter. For examples we had a lovely Boccaccio printed at Uln, and a page out of 'La Mer des Histoires' printed in 1488. Blake and Bewick were also shown, and a page of music designed by Mr. [Herbert P.] Horne.

The lecture was listened to with great attention by a large audience, y  ti. W instinct that comes out of actually working in the art of which he were most well-timed, and we hope that some of the publishers in the audience willtake them to heart. Next Thursday Mr. Cobden Saunderson [Sanderson] lectures on book-binding, a subject on which few men in England have higher qualifications for speaking. We are glad to see these lectures are so well attended.

Appendix B

# APPENDIX C \* KELMSCOTTPRESS EXPENSES

![](images/4fd5cb2302a4a1f8bfff941694507f13f2249efc2109d6dce6ff27beeeadaf7e.jpg)

HE document transcribed below came to light after this book was completed. At the Sotheby sale of Sydney Cockerell's books and manuscripts, 10 December 1956, lot 99 was described as 'Kelmscott Press Ex-PENSES, I89I.Ms. AccouNT Book in the handwriting of William Morris, his daughter May, and his pressman William

Bowden, bound in at the end are some leaves from Morris's diary for 1892 with a few entries in his hand, red morocco, the original limp boards of the account book preserved, in slip-case sm. 8vo 1891—2'. The buyer was the firm of Bernard Quaritch, which in turn sold the document to Arnold Yates, an English collector. In 1971, near the end of his life, Yates allowed an acquaintance to make a photocopy of it for Dr Joseph R. Dunlap of New York, who has recently given me a copy.

A note in Cockerell's hand appears on the flyleaf: 'This was the original cash book of the Kelmscott Press. It was begun on Jan I7 1891 and the entries as far as Mar 7 are in the hand of William Morris, who was then disabled with a gouty illness. The entries for Mar I4 are by his daughter May, and those for Mar 21 & 28 by the overseer Mr Bowden. The remaining entries to July 7 are by Morris. At the end are bound some leaves from WM's diary of 1892, which was but little usOn the facing pageare three notations that frequently apper in Cockerell's volumes of Morris-related documents: Cockerels sinature; the private shelf-mark 'Morrisiana 28'; and an indication that the expense book was no. 308 in the Morris centenary exhibition at the Victoria and Albert Museum in 1934.

On the cover of the book is a label in Morris's hand that reads Kelmscott Press | Expenses  anuary 1891.Cockerel following his usual practice, has pasted a signature of Morris (probably clipped from a cheque) over a calendar on the cover verso. At the end of the expenses, on a separate page, is a list, not in Morris's hand, which consists of the names of Morris's printers, though it cannot necessarily be assumed that all of them were employed by Morrs during the first hal8.The ol m appear in the  Tibbett [roy J. Tippet], [L.] Tasker, [Thomas] Binning, [R.] Eatley and [G.] Heath, Colis  Col ork te  r

Bark, Eat ll: Fre HGa Co Wen h f of the press was photographed in 1895, all but three were still employed by Morris. The names of three other printers  Stephen Mowlem, H. Howes, and Mrs Pine  appear in the list of expenses.

The three diary entries by Morris alluded to in the Sotheby catalogue are for 20 January, 3 March, and 4 March. The latter records in stock paper | big s | reams | small 30. |& retree'. Pasted into the last pages of thdocument is Quaritch's receipt to Yates for 6 (dated 15 January 1957) for the expense book.

In the following transcription I have recorded all the information in the book but have made no attempt to reproduce its actual form. The expense book serves as a useful supplement to the later ledger in the Morgan Library, because precise documentary evidence of the Press's activities is relatively sparse before Cockerell, an unusually assiduous record-keeper, became the second Secretary of the Kelmscott Press in the summer of 1894. Perhaps the most lluminating entry is that of 2o May, which tells us that Morris bought two manuscripts of the eleventh and twelfth centuries 'for study for type' it is characteristic of Morris as a collector that he purchased early books and manuscripts primarily as an aid in designing his typefaces.

II. F. S. Ellis, 'The Life-Work of William Morris' Journal o the Socet Arts, 46 (27 May 1898), 620. It is difficult to determine precisely what manuscripts Morris saw in the Bodleian. As an undergraduate he would not have been allowed to use the Bodleian under normal circumstances, and his name does not appear in the library's records during those years. Yet Morris had vivid memories of studying the Bodleian manuscripts, and possibly he might have used the reading room by special permission, and the documents inight have been requested by a companion. Morris's name does figure in the Bodleian's 'L.P. Lending Book' after he received his degree, but here the evidence is complicated by the occasional appearance of an 'R. Morris' (probably the Revd Richard Morris, Winchester Lecturer on English Language and Literature at King's College School, London) in the records. 6 There are earlier records of 'Caxton' types being cast for this purpose, and Vincent Figgins produced a replica of Caxton's second type which was initially used in an 1855 reprint of The Game of Cliesse. Towards the end of the century this and other blackletters based — in some cases very loosely - upon Caxton's founts achieved some popularity in display work. (Bernard C. Middleton, A History of English Craft Bookbiuding Techuique (New York, 1963), 207; G. V. Ovink, 'Revivals of Fifteenth-Century Typefaces', in A. R. A. Croiset van Uchelen (ed.), Hellinga: Festschrift/Feestbundel/Mélanges. Forty-three Studies in Bibliography Presented to Prof. Dr. Wytze Hellinga (Amsterdam, 1980), 282-3.) A history of the Chiswick Press 'Caxton' face is supplied by an anonymous memorandum in the Press's archives (BL Add. MS 43985, fos. 20910).

55. The present locations of three of Morris's four presses are known. (a) The small proofing press, unlike the other three, was not acquired by C. R. Ashbee; it was advertised for sale at 3o guineas by Tregaskis in 1898, apparently without success, for it remained in the basement of the Tregaskis Bookshop in London for a number of years. In the 192os Dr John W. Bartlett of New York bought it. Theodore M. Lilienthal purchased it from Bartlett in 1939; and it is now in the Huntington Library. (See Lilienthal, A William Morris Press goes West (Berkeley, Calif., 1961).) (b) Either the first or second of Morris's presses passed from Ashbee in 1913 and through several intermediate owners to the Shakespeare Head Press; Sir Basil Blackwell gave it to the William Morris Society, and it is at present, appropriately, in the basement of Kelmscott House. (See the Morris Society pamphlet William Moris's Printing Press (1983)() The large Chaucer press, after it left Ashbee's hands, wandercd about for some years before it came into the possession of

Frederic Goudy, the American typedesigner, who used it at his Press of the Woolly Whale. It is now in a private collection in New York. (See J. Ben Lieberman, The Liberty Bell on the Kelmscott Goudy Press', Black Art, 1 (Summer, 1962), 41-9.) 56. Jane Morris's letters to Blunt are in 56. Jane Morris's letters to Blunt are in

Douglas Cockerell, who had been much more closely associated with Cobden-Sanderson than his brother Sydney was, wrote some years later: 'Cobden-Sanderson's egotism' was almost pathological. He lacked the power of co-operation almost entirely and was almost insanely jealous of any reputation, even Morris's, that might rival his own. He lived in a world of his own creation, swayed by emotional storms of great intensity, and I doubt if he was capable of true friendship' (Cockerell, p. 85).

24. FM.

Ellis offered an energetic apologia for his deciion to adot oldpelling in at to T. R. Lounsbury, 16.12.93 (Yale): 'It would be presumptuous of me to offer any argument as to the adoption of modern spclling for Chaucer, but I am quite convinced an edition so printed would not gain him one additional reader. . . . I have myself gone so far in the other direction with regard to orthography as to have printed Shakespeare's Poems, for the first time in a collected form with the author's spelling. Though the book was published at 25/ the soo copies were all sold on the day of publication. I did the same good work this year for Cavendish's Life of Wolsey, with a like satisfactory result, holding that an author's orthography is a part of his work. Furnivall I am happy to say is now engaged in editing the whole of Shakespeare in like manner - at last we shall be able to read him with the full 15th-17th century flavour.'

While there was still a chance, they should try and produce a few specimens of what was really good printing.'

# SELECT BIBLIOGRAPHY

Since the quantity of words written about Morris and the Kelnscott Press is beyond measure, the following lists are deliberately brief. The reader who wishes further information wil fnd many aditial our liste in the note bove nd in A Biblgraphy Kehmscott Press, pp. xvii-xxi.
